North of the bridge, a narrow path leads inward. There stand six clusters of cedar trees; within three hundred paces to their left and right is a deep stone chamber. Before it flows a spring, where Ying and three others now dwell.

In the *chen* year, they will briefly emerge to partake of mortal food and visit their native mountains near their hometowns, allowing their families and brothers to see them. At that time, they will inform their younger brothers of their whereabouts. They also send word to their disciples, urging diligence: if one seeks Ying at Zhuye Mountain, they may yet meet him. Ascend the eastern stone bridge of Zhuye Mountain; north of the bridge, the path is smooth and easy. Do not take the southern route up the mountain, for it is perilously steep.

Among the immortals of Zhuye Mountain are Chen Zhonglin, Xu Daoju, Yin Linzi, and Zhao Shudao—all of whom entered this mountain at the end of the Han. Shudao has already attained the rank of Lesser Perfected One. Zhonglin has just passed his great trial and will soon depart. These are the elder immortals of Zhuye Mountain.

As for Wang Shilong, Zhao Daoxuan, Fu Taichu, and Xu Ying (also called Xu Yuanyou), they arrived only four years ago. The record from the twelfth day of the fifth month to this point is based on Yang’s transcription of the imperial decree.

The venerable ones, resplendent in inner radiance, have cast off their former burdens and are no longer their old selves. Through purification and refinement, they have attained the essence of the Dao. Their spirits harmonize with their hearts, gazing up at the numinous clouds, beholding the dawn’s splendor. Their virtuous voices are as fragrant as orchids, and in another decade, they shall reach perfection.

Jì [note: should be written as 伟]举 was his given name. In all matters, he must be exhorted to diligence and never again be remiss. In the second year of the Taihe era, the year *dingmao* (47), on the seventeenth night of the twelfth month, the Supreme Perfection True Person, Lord of Destiny, addressed Mu:

By the year *bingzi*, a decade will have passed. At that time, he shall be seventy-two. By the years *haizi* and *zi*, his spirit will undergo transformation and refinement. In the *zi* year, ten years will remain. Like an axe laid aside in desolation, his heart shall harmonize and congeal in stillness. Though the Dao is subtle, riding it brings order. His form shall settle in the cinnabar void, his banner raised to the celestial ridge. His spirit shall shine with radiance, his light grow ever more luminous. Truly, this is the lofty ascension of the dawn’s attendant, the celestial array of Master Gu’s tripod [note: ancient form of 鼎]. One may call him a talent surpassing all expectations, a transcendent of profound and serene cultivation.

He must further exert himself in cultivating virtue and refining his practice, abiding in tranquil emptiness. On the seventeenth night of the twelfth month, the Supreme Perfection True Person, Lord of Destiny, addressed the Jade Axe.

[He declined an appointment as a recruited aide in the office of the Minister of Education and instead withdrew to Maoshan in his home county for five years.]

This sixteen-character annotation was added later by Rong’s younger brother. (The public office recruitment appears to be mistaken.)

Profound and wondrous, he embodied the Dao;
Unraveled mysteries and attained essence.
Emptiness within received all things;
Gentle virtue followed constancy.
Compassionate, magnanimous, and broadly learned,
He heard the Dao and put it into practice.
Wandering free, his steps took flight,
Opening sincerity with candor and equanimity.
Urging the dragon to ascend,
He drifted through mist to the Three Clarities.
Truly the leader of perfected transcendents,
A master among the elders of his village.
He was destined to govern a sacred domain,
To guard the immortal capital as its count.
Assisting the highest virtue,
His name was inscribed in crimson registers.

The above describes the allotted destiny received by Director Xu of the Bureau of Merit.

(At that time, Protector-General and Director of the Bureau of Merit—these six characters were also added by Rong’s younger brother.)

Jade brilliance blazed with pure radiance,
Concealed light veiled the true.
The two luminaries descended upon the blade,
Flying auroras trailed in coils.
Thus the Jade Youth
Gathered these twin celestial bodies.
Thereby he opened the supreme Dao,
Rightly attaining the wondrous gate.
His demeanor shone with jade-like splendor,
His jade standard gleamed with dark gold.
His name ascended to the Five Palaces,
His record hung among the Seven Primes.
Truly he was among the paired domains’ array,
A minister of the Upper Clarity.
This arose from the child’s inner harmony, his resonance with emptiness, and his single-minded devotion to truth—
Also from the southerners’ certain harnessing of the cloud-chariot,
And the Three Primes’ luminous carriage bearing him on their excursions.

(This “paired domain” refers to Master Gu Xi, the same as the earlier “Master Gu’s celestial array of the tripod.”)

The above describes the allotted destiny received by Jade Axe.

(In the preceding two instances of “the above,” the word “said” appears below. From Director’s book: “The above was spoken by the Pure Numinous Perfected…” Yang’s text omits these four characters, likely because Yang conveyed it orally to Director—this being Director Pei.)

This marks the moment of Dao attainment through diligent effort, the time of receiving the scriptures—the initial names determined and set forth. It also signifies the allotted destiny of attaining the Dao and being entrusted with its transmission.

Those who 〈rely〉 and then abandon it will find no path forward.
We must together 〈cherish〉 this.

The above five entries were transcribed by Director from Yang’s manuscript in two copies.

The *Scripture of Preserving Life* states: “Master Xu was thus able to entrust his form to the hidden transformation—his nocturnal liberation following Lord Zhang of Zhen’nan. Thereby he nurtured his hun-soul in the Grand Yin, concealed his po-soul within the earth, with the Four Numinous Ones guarding his essence and the Five Elders preserving his store. After sixteen years, he nearly beheld me in the Eastern Flower. Once attaining the hidden and unobstructed, none perceived him as unreal.”

(Master Xu here refers to the Attendant. According to records, Master Zhang served as General Zhen’nan and died in the twenty-first year of Jian’an. Buried east of Ye, forty-four years later—during the fourth year of Ganlu in Wei—his water-sealed coffin opened, revealing a body as if alive. It was lifted onto a bed, whereupon he raised a fly-whisk to cover his face, laughed loudly, and died again, necessitating reburial. External accounts describe the event thus, though the method of nocturnal liberation remains unclear. In Master Xu’s case, it appears not to have involved sword or staff.)

The above was spoken by Perfected Ying: “From antiquity to the present, life and death possess their ford; the manifest and the hidden meet differently. Concealing the past and extinguishing knowledge—those who align with the world in this—all practice the conduct of Dao attainment. As for those who ride the jade elixir once, make the ninefold blossoms take flight thrice, revel in cloud-liquor at dawn, or wander amidst flowing yellow; who swallow golden nectar and chew jade sprouts—these ascend at once, riding the light into the void, darkly ascending to the Supreme Subtlety. Yet when worldly affairs stray from the mysterious, and this work remains unfinished, one ought to tread the Grand Yin for a time, conceal life in the hidden realm, and cast off the outer form, discarding the bodily husk.”

Within the empty dwelling of nurturing the embryo, the kiln of qi is sealed, and the furnace’s key is cut off. Receiving the essence in the profound vastness, one thus transforms their countenance within the halls of the Three Yin and gains a youthful visage at the gate of the Ninefold Refinement. Only then does one know that the divine immortals are wondrous—dying yet not perishing. The matters of departure and return are principles profound indeed.

The Southerners say: those who attain the Dao and depart this world may appear or conceal themselves, entrusting their form and leaving traces behind—this is the Dao’s concealment. Yet some, after once more reveling in jade essence, knock upon their coffins; or with a single dose of the elixir’s measure, their corpses decay. The Deer-Skin Elder swallowed jade blossoms, and maggots streamed from his door; Qiu Jizi imbibed golden liquor, and the stench spread for a hundred li. The Yellow Emperor fired the Nine Cauldrons at Mount Jing, yet still left behind a tomb with a bridge’s collar. Ji Zhu consumed cloud powder to ascend in concealment, yet his head and feet lay apart. Mo Di swallowed rainbow cinnabar and cast himself into the waters. Ning Sheng took stone-brain and leapt into fire. Wu Guang cut leeks and entered the limpid, frigid abyss. Bo Cheng inhaled qi until his intestines and stomach rotted thrice. Such cases are too numerous to record in full—how subtle indeed!

The traces of those who attain the Dao—whether they embrace or abandon it—are ever inconstant. (The "Southern One" refers to the Lady of the South. These transcendents appear in various records, yet their deeds and traces vary, sometimes conflicting.)

*Preserving Life* further states: Once one has attained the subtle and unobstructed, none perceive their unreality. Such indications reveal not a true corpse.

The following four entries are recorded in the hand of the Prefect:
When a person dies, one must examine their form. If they appear as in life, it is *shijie* (corpse liberation). If the feet are not discolored and the skin remains unwrinkled, it is also *shijie*. If the eyes remain undamaged, indistinguishable from a living person, it is likewise *shijie*. If the hair falls out entirely yet the bones lose their form, it is still *shijie*. Those who achieve *shijie* in broad daylight are transcendents, not subject to the usual examples of *shijie*.

The following entry is transcribed in Jia’s hand:
If one temporarily dies and enters the Great Yin, passing provisionally through the Three Bureaus, the flesh may turn to ash and decay, the blood settle, and the pulses disperse—yet the five viscera continue to live. The white bones become jade-like, the seven *po* spirits attend, the three *hun* souls guard the dwelling, the Three Primes suspend their breath, and the great spirit seals itself within. After thirty or twenty years, or ten or three years, one emerges at will. Upon reviving, the blood is gathered anew, flesh nurtured, fluids generated, and form reconstituted—surpassing even one’s appearance before death. This is what is meant by "the perfected refining their form in the Great Yin and transforming their countenance in the Three Bureaus."

So says the Heavenly Emperor.

The refinement of form in the Great Yin surpasses the consumption of nine-times-cycled elixirs. The visage becomes upright and solemn, the complexion like numinous clouds. Ascending to the Gate of the Supreme Ultimate, one receives the scriptures and is named a Perfected.

After five or six years following Zhao Chengzi’s death, travelers passing the mountain at dusk saw his corpse within a stone chamber—flesh decayed, bones remaining. Yet within his abdomen, the five viscera persisted as before, fluids and blood entwined within, purple wrappings and networks bound without.

Those adepts of the Dao who temporarily wander the Great Yin—Taiyi guards their corpse, the three *hun* souls tend the bones, the seven *po* spirits protect the flesh, and the embryonic spirit records the qi.

---

The preceding three entries were copied by the Prefectural Secretary from the *Nine Perfected Scripture*, detailing the consumption of the Five Mineral Riches. Those who achieve liberation through the corpse by means of other medicines—not through the transformation of numinous pills—are unable to return to their native land, for the Three Bureaus seize them.

Some die and revive again. Some, though decapitated and dead, emerge from another side. Some lose their corpse before burial. Some retain their form but are without bones. Some leave their clothing behind as their form departs. Some shed their hair and vanish. Those who depart in broad daylight are called "upper corpse liberation"; those who depart at midnight, "lower corpse liberation." Those who depart at dawn or dusk are called "earthly lords."

---

The preceding entry was copied by the Secretary from the *Sword Scripture*, discussing matters of corpse liberation.

*True Records*, Scroll Four.
*True Records*, Scroll Five.

**Initiation into Destiny and Transmission of the Dao, Chapter One**

The Dao is transmitted.

*(This section was copied by both the Chief Clerk and the Secretary, with the title as given. It is unclear whether this is an ancient Daoist text or a revelation delivered during Yang’s descent. Its principles align perfectly with the true scriptures, and it is suspected to be the teaching of Master Pei. The reason for this is as follows: According to the *Scripture of Treasuring the Spirit*, the Dao states, "My master is the Red Pine of the Southern Marchmount." Moreover, the practices of the bedchamber were only received and cultivated by Master Pei in his youth. The *True Records* contain the words, "I once regretted that this [understanding] came too late." This phrase also seems to echo the speech of the pure and numinous.)*

The Master said:
The Dao is the undifferentiated origin of life. When the origin is perfected, then the Supreme Ultimate arises. The Supreme Ultimate is the parent of Heaven and Earth, the mystery of the Dao. Thus, the Dao has a great return—this is called Pristine Reality. Without the Dao, reality cannot be perfected; without reality, the Dao cannot be fulfilled. If the Dao is not fulfilled, how can its pristine nature be perceived? Therefore, it is called the great return. Perceived, it is called the Wondrous; fulfilled, it is called the Dao; put into practice, it is called nature. Nature and the Dao share the same essence. The essence delights in the perfect Dao, for the Dao causes it to be so.

*(This explains that the human body naturally harmonizes with the Dao. Thus, Heaven’s mandate is called nature; following nature is called the Dao; cultivating the Dao is called teaching. Now, using the Dao to teach and refine nature into reality is to become one with the Dao.)*

The Master said:
The Most High is the descendant of the Dao, who discerns the Dao’s root and penetrates the Dao’s foundation. Thus, they are the Perfected of the Highest Clarity and the teacher of the Old Lord.

*(This refers to the Most High, the August and Numinous, the Great Dao Lord of the Jade Dawn, who is the teacher of the Left Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate and the Central Yellow Old Lord.)*

The Master said:
The Old Lord is the disciple of the Most High. At the age of seven, he grasped the essentials of longevity. Thus, he is the Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate.

The Master said:
The Supreme Ultimate has four Perfected. The Old Lord resides on its left, bearing the talisman of the divine tiger, girded with golden bells, holding the purple-feathered staff, and wearing the headscarf of golden essence. When he travels, he is attended by the canopy of flowering dawn and rides the cloud of the Three Primes.

*(These two passages are drawn from the *Scripture of the Nine Perfected*, discussing the Central Yellow Old Lord. The Yellow Old Lord is the teacher of the Perfected of the Grand Void and the Red Lord of the Southern Marchmount. Since Master Pei studied under the Red Lord, he revered his origin and expounded upon his rank and virtue.)*

The Master said:

The Way possesses the *Eightfold Pure Scripture of the True*, the hidden writings of the Supreme Lord. In the world, the Master said:
The Way possesses the *Scripture of the Nine Perfected*, the secret teachings of the Old Lord. In the world, the Master said:
The Way possesses the *Supreme Clarity’s Scripture of Seventy-Four Methods of Transformation*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Texts for Eliminating the Six Heavens and the Three Heavens’ Orthodox Dharma*. In the world, the Master said:
The Way possesses the *Yellow Qi and Yang Essence: The Heavenly Treasury of the Hidden Moon*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Diagrams of the Three Primes’ Dispersed Scripture and the True Way*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Forty-Four Formulas of the Yellow Plain Divine Methods*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Essentials of Longevity from the Yellow Writ and the Red Realm*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Cinnabar Elixir, Golden Essence, Stone Radiance, and Water Mother*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Green Essentials, Purple Writings, and the Golden Root’s Multitudinous Texts*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *True Formulas of Jade Clarity: The Three-Nine Pure Sayings*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Stone Essence and Golden Light: The Record of Concealed Radiance*. In the world, the Master said:
The Way possesses the *Eight Arts of the Hidden Earth: Elixir Radiance and Dao Essence*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *White Tablets and Plain Bamboo Records of Attained Dao Names*. The Master said:
The Way possesses the *Purple Crossing, Blazing Light, and the Spirit Candle of Night Illumination*. The Master said:
All these are the scriptures of the Way.

The *Yellow Writings*—though many copies exist in the world—remain matters of the esoteric Way. (The Celestial Master adopted their names to propagate their teachings, with the essential purport largely unchanged, only expanding their methods. What is here called the *Yellow Writings and the Crimson Realm’s Three-One Scripture*, or the *Inner Truth of the Yellow and Crimson* as spoken by Master Juanzi, are not the same as those expounded by the Celestial Master of this age.)

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Scripture of the Seven Celestial Principles for Soaring Steps*. It exists in the world.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Scripture of the Seven Transformations and Divine Arts of the Seven Cycles*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *True Scripture of the Great Cavern* in thirty-nine scrolls. It exists in the world.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Hidden Writings of the Great Elixir: Eight Refinements and Ten Resolutions*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Three Celestial Charts of the Heavenly Pass and the Seven Stars’ Transference of Degrees*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Central Records of the Nine Elixirs’ Transformations and Embryonic Essence*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Nine Crimson Seals for Binding Mountains and Sinking Seas*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Divine Elixir of Golden Liquor and the Concealed Mushroom of the Supreme Ultimate*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Five Phases’ Secret Talismans for Summoning Souls and Beckoning Spirits*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Formulas of the Crooked and Plain* for invoking the ghosts of the Six Heavens. It exists in the world.

The Master said:

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Yellow Water and Moon Essence*. Ingesting it transforms one into the moon.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Receding Water and Jade Essence*. Ingesting it transforms one into the sun.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Hard Ring Tree Seeds*. Ingesting them transforms one into clouds.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Verdant Reflection of Yang Water*. Ingesting it transforms one into stone.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Crimson Tree and White Seeds*. Ingesting them transforms one into jade.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Cinnabar Tree and Azure Fruits*. Ingesting them transforms one into yellow gold.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Langgan Jade-Stemmed Flower Elixir*. Ingesting it transforms one into a flying dragon.

The above seventeen entries are recorded in the *Numinous Writ of the Purple Texts*, all being transformations of the *Langgan Elixir*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Nine-Cycled Divine Elixir*. Ingesting it transforms one into a white crane. (Recorded in the *Transmission of Lord Mao of Destiny*)

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Celestial August Symbolic Talismans*, used to harmonize with the Primordial. These are also found in the *Purple Texts*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *White Feathers and Purple Canopy*, used to wander the Five Peaks.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Inner Writ of the Three Sovereigns*, used to summon the spirits of heaven and earth. (Though extant in the world, these are not the true versions)

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Jade Pendants and Golden Bells*, used to ascend the Supreme Ultimate.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Divine Tiger Talismans*, used to overawe the Six Heavens.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Flowing Gold Bells*, used to subdue ghosts and spirits.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Plain Memorials and Cinnabar Talismans*, used to summon the Six Jia.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Golden Perfection and Jade Radiance*, used to illuminate the world below.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Eightfold Scenic Chariot*, used to traverse the Upper Clarity.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Wings for Flight*, used to transcend the void and tread the skies.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Purple Embroidered Kerchief* and the *Vermilion Flying Scarf*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *White Feathers and Black Plumes*, used to soar across the eight directions.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Kingfisher-Feather Robes, Golden Bells, and Azure Sashes*.

The Master said:
The immortal Dao possesses the *Curved Dawn Flying Canopy*. When mounted, the body naturally takes flight. (Recorded in the *Sword Classic*)

The immortal Dao possesses the *Thirty-Seven Colored Nodal Staffs*, bestowed upon the immortals.

The Master said:
The wonders of the immortal Dao all have their methods. One who fully masters this Way becomes a Perfected of the Nine Palaces—not merely ascending as an immortal. Yet the Dao’s methods are manifold; when each is fully prepared, this may be known. (This means that mastering even one strand of the Dao suffices to attain the rank of Perfected of the Nine Palaces. If all are fully possessed, one becomes a Perfected of the Great Ultimate.)

The Master said:
Now that you have come to study the Dao with wholehearted devotion, I shall transmit the Way to you. Yet all learners must have a teacher. My own teacher was Master Pine of the Southern Marchmount, who served as Left Immortal Duke to the Perfected of the Great Void. Master Valley-Seeker served as Right Immortal Duke. In ancient times, the Most High instructed Laozi in virtue, enabling him to attain the Dao. Master Pine transmitted the Dao to me, enabling me to attain immortality. Now that I have received the Dao from Master Pine, if you wish to study it, he will surely test you. Should you fail the trial, it would bring shame upon me. Having now revealed to you the method of attaining the Dao and awakened you to the means of examination, if you still fail the trial, it would only reflect your own folly.

Those who seek to test others devise trials from what lies beyond the reach of thought and beyond the grasp of emotion. Be vigilant, my disciple.

The Master said:

The Twelve Trials of Immortal Dao must all be passed before this scripture is conferred. These twelve matters are the great trials, all witnessed by the Perfected of the Great Ultimate. How could one not be vigilant?

The Master said:
Long ago, Liu Weidao of Zhongshan, who studied the Dao of immortality on Mount Fan of the Zhong Mountains, spent twelve years there. An immortal tested him with a stone weighing one hundred thousand *jin*, suspended by a single white hair, and bade Weidao lie beneath it. Weidao’s countenance showed no change, his heart was at ease, and his body at peace as he lay beneath it for twelve years. The immortal tested him repeatedly, sparing no trial, yet Weidao awakened to them all. Thereupon, he was bestowed with divine elixir and ascended to heaven in broad daylight. (This man likely lived in the Han era.)

The Master said:
Long ago, there was Lord Green Crow, who received the teachings of an enlightened master and thoroughly grasped the wondrous principles of immortality. He entered the Hua Mountains to study the Dao and spent four hundred seventy-one years there. Though he underwent the twelve trials, three remained unpassed. Later, he ingested golden *shuijiao* and ascended to the Great Ultimate. The Lord of the Dao of the Great Ultimate deemed that, having failed three trials, he was but an immortal and not a Perfected One—how much less could a worldly mind suffice?

The Master said:
The Dao of the Great Cavern is most refined and wondrous, being the scripture of the Guardian of Simplicity of the Non-Ultimate. None who recite it fail to ride clouds and mount dragons. In ancient times, the Yellow Lord of the Center concealed this scripture, and the world knew nothing of it. If you come to know it, guard it in secret and do not transmit it.

Moreover, long ago, the three Zhou brothers, all fond of the Dao from youth, dwelled in the Chang Mountains for ninety-seven years. Their concentrated thought moved all things without fail.

Suddenly, they beheld an old man, his head and beard snowy white. The three knew him to be a great spirit, and they kowtowed until their foreheads bled, tears and sobs mingling in overwhelming joy. They drew near to beg for the Dao. The old man then produced seven scrolls of plain silk scripture and bestowed them for recitation. The three brothers all studied them with utmost devotion.

Presently, a white deer appeared at the mountain’s edge. The two younger brothers set aside their scrolls to watch it, but the eldest, Zhou Jun, never ceased his recitation. When the younger brothers returned, Zhou Jun had already surpassed them by seven thousand repetitions. Inwardly, the two wondered if the immortal had transformed into the white deer to call Zhou Jun away, but he paid no heed. Zhou Jun recited the scripture ten thousand times, while his brothers completed nine thousand seven hundred and thirty-three. Then Zhou Jun suddenly ascended as a flying immortal.

The two brothers took up the scrolls to recite them, but the stone chamber suddenly split with a burst of flame that consumed the texts. Thus, they failed to attain immortality and remain in the Chang Mountains to this day, traversing the Five Peaks on foot. Be cautious in this, disciple.

The Master said:
Long ago, there was Zhuang Bowei, a man of the Han era. From youth, he was devoted to the Dao of long life. Each day at sunset, he faced the northwest, closed his eyes, clenched his fists, and visualized Mount Kunlun. After twenty-one years of such practice, he began to ingest elixirs.

Having entered Mount Zhong to study the Dao, he still practiced this method. After another ten or so years, when he closed his eyes, he suddenly beheld Mount Kunlun. Persisting in this visualization without cease, he eventually saw an immortal, who bestowed upon him the formula for the Golden {Water Ladle}. Thus, he attained the Dao. Yet this was merely the resonance of his essence with the Dao’s response, causing it to be so—not the subtlety of the technique itself.

The Master said:
True persons conceal their Dao’s wonders while revealing their coarse forms. Some wear tattered robes and appear emaciated, their demeanor like that of fools. When others seek to learn the Dao, they test them in this way—yet in the end, none can recognize them. Those who fail to recognize them have not passed the trial. You must ever be cautious of this.

Long ago, in the early Han, four or five children were playing by drawing on the road. One child sang:
*"Don the azure {lord’s kerchief} to enter Heaven’s Gate,
Bow to the Metal Mother, salute the Wood Lord."*
This, too, was a hidden saying. At the time, no one understood it—only Zhang Zifang knew. He went and paid homage to them. These were the Jade Lads of the Eastern King Father. The so-called Metal Mother is the Western Queen Mother, and the Wood Lord is the Eastern King Father. Immortals bow to the King Father and salute the Queen Mother.

The Master said:
Long ago, there was Master Fu...

In his youth, he loved the Dao and entered a stone chamber on Mount Jiao. After seven years, the Supreme Ultimate Lord Lao visited him and gave him a wooden drill, instructing him to bore through a stone plate about five feet thick, saying, "Once you pierce this plate, you shall attain the Dao." The man then drilled day and night. After forty-seven years, he bored through the stone and obtained the divine elixir. He ascended to the Great Clarity and became the Perfected One of the Southern Marchmount. This was a man of steadfast resolve—take note of this. If ever you face such a trial, be cautious never to say you cannot do it.

The Master said:
Long ago, there was Huang Guanzi, who also loved the Dao from a young age. His family practiced both Buddhism and Daoism, and he paid homage morning after morning, kowtowing to pray for longevity. Thus he persisted for forty-nine years. Later, he ingested medicinal herbs and entered Mount Jiao. The Supreme Ultimate Perfected One tested him with one hundred and forty trials, all of which he passed. He then ingested the golden elixir and chanted the *Scripture of the Great Cavern*. Now he serves as an immortal official, the Left Immortal Minister of the Supreme Ultimate. He was a man of utmost resolve—Buddhism could not have achieved this. His mind was firmly settled within.

(This account is similar to Fu Hanzhen’s devotion to Buddhism.)

The Master said:
Long ago, Mao Bodao, Liu Daogong, Xie Zhijian, and Zhang Zhaoqi were all men of the Later Han. They studied the Dao together in the Wangwu Mountains for over forty years and jointly compounded the divine elixir. Mao Bodao ingested it first and died. Daogong ingested it next and also died. When Xie Zhijian and Zhang Zhaoqi saw this, they dared not ingest it.

They then abandoned the mountain and departed. Later, they saw Bodao and Daogong on the mountain. The two were shocked and grieved, then approached to seek the Dao. Bodao and Daogong gave them the method for preparing and consuming *fuling* (poria). After ingesting it, they both lived for several centuries. Even now, they remain in the mountains, wandering among the Five Peaks. These men understood the attainment of the Dao through the divine elixir, yet failed to realize that trials were inherent within it. Thus, they became only earthbound immortals, with no further hope of ascending to heaven.

(There are three accounts of Xie Zhijian: one states he followed Ge Xuan; another places him in the Deer Track Grotto; and this is the third. It is unclear whether these refer to the same person or to individuals sharing the same name.)

The Master said:
In the early Jin dynasty, there was a perfected one, Guo Shengzi, who worked as a diviner in the Luoyang marketplace. At that time, the four clans—Liu, Shi, Zhang, and Zang—all wished to study the Dao. They often lamented, saying, "We have not encountered an enlightened master. Even if such a master were to appear, we would remain unaware, and all would be trials we could not pass. Thus, we would gain nothing." One must always be cautious of this. If something extraordinary arises and one fails to recognize it, it becomes a trial one cannot pass.

When a person harbors the intention to study the Dao, the heavenly net is vast yet never misses its mark—all are tested. You must deeply ponder this meaning and take heed. The Master said:

The Master said:

In ancient times, Lü Chengzi, from his youth, cherished the pursuit of longevity and delighted in studying the Dao. After more than forty years, he entered the Jing Mountains. Having lived there for over seventy years, he was tested by the mountain spirit of Jing Mountain. Chengzi mistook it for a perfected one and bowed, seeking the Dao—only to be devoured by a great serpent, nearly to the point of death. Fortunately, he awakened swiftly and preserved the visualization of the Seven Stars of the Supreme One to repel it, thereby escaping harm. Later, he was again deluded by malevolent spirits, losing his left eye. Thus, he failed to attain the Dao and perished in the mountains. You must be cautious of such trials and always remain with your master. This was due to Chengzi’s lack of single-minded devotion, as his heart harbored some impure intentions.

The Master said:
Huang Ziyang was a man of Wei. From his youth, he understood the wonders of longevity. He studied the Dao in the Boluo Mountains for over ninety years, eating only peach bark and drinking the yellow water from stones. Later, he encountered Sima Jizhu, who transmitted to him the *Eight Directions of Daoist Immortality*, enabling him to transcend the world. (This refers to the Wei of the Warring States period, not the Wei dynasty after the Han.)

The Master said:
There was Liu Fenglin, a man of the Zhou dynasty, who studied the Dao on Mount Songgao. After four hundred years, he successfully compounded the divine elixir three times.

Defeated by malevolent forces, he then moved to Mount Weiyu, where he could hold his breath for three days without respite. To this day, over a thousand years have passed, yet he has still not ascended as an immortal. This is because his trials were too numerous to overcome, and his mastery of the Daoist arts remains insufficient. This man merely consumes yellow lotus to attain undying life, yet he cannot command or employ any spiritual powers.

The Master said:
Long ago, Gaoqiuzi was a man of Yin. He too was fond of the Dao and entered the Six Radiance Mountains. After accumulating five hundred and twenty years, he only studied the *Yellow Plain Scripture of the Dao* and ingested atractylodes. Later, he compounded the *Vast Elixir*, thereby attaining terrestrial immortality. He wandered among the Five Peaks for over two hundred years. Thereafter, he obtained the *Golden Liquid* and ascended to the Great Clarity, now serving as the Perfected One of the Central Peak. (This account closely resembles the preface to the *Sword Scripture*.)

The Master said:
In pursuing the Dao, one must ensure the Three Passes remain perpetually harmonized—this is the Way to root essence and strengthen the bones. The Three Passes are: the mouth as the Pass of the Heart, the feet as the Pass of Earth, and the hands as the Pass of Man. When the Three Passes are harmonized, the Five Viscera are at peace; when the Five Viscera are at peace, the entire body is free from illness.

Long ago, Zhao Shuqi studied the Dao in the Wangwu Mountains. He would often emerge among the common folk and heard of a skilled diviner in the marketplace. Shuqi went to meet him. Thereupon, the diviner spoke to Shuqi, saying:

"To enter the Gate of Heaven, harmonize the Three Passes, visualize crimson robes, and align Kunlun. Shuqi knew this was a divine being and bowed in reverence, kowtowing to request the essential instructions. Thereupon, the being gave him a scroll—*The Record Within Embryonic Essence*. Having received this text with bows, he entered the mountains to recite it. Later, he compounded the divine elixir and ascended to heaven. These are all verifications of past events. You ought to remember these words. (The matter of the Three Passes is identical to that in the *Yellow Court*; though discussed, no method is given.)

The Master said:
One must visualize the Five Spirits within the body. The Five Spirits refer to the two hands, two feet, and the head. Envision the head as perpetually azure, the two hands as perpetually crimson, and the two feet as perpetually white—thus is one near to attaining transcendence. Long ago, Xu Jidao studied the Dao in the Huming Mountains. He, too, would often emerge among the common folk. Suddenly, he saw a man wearing leather trousers and a white silk robe, holding a peach-wood staff. He encountered Jidao, who remained unaware. This happened repeatedly. Jidao then realized and bowed in gratitude. Thereupon, the man spoke to Jidao, saying:
To study the Dao, one must don an azure headscarf, chant the *Great Calendar*, step with paired white [feet], and pace with two crimson [hands]. This pertains to the Five Spirits. His words were veiled.
The *Great Calendar* is the *Scripture of the Three Sovereigns*. (This is the matter of the Five Spirits of Primal Simplicity; separate scriptures and methods exist.)

The Master said:"

"To rectify the heart-mind, one should, when the sun has risen three *zhang*, cross the hands upon the shoulders and align the sun with the heart. When warmth arises in the center of the heart-mind, the heart-mind is rectified. It is excellent to practice this regularly.

Long ago, there was one named Jiang Bozhen who studied in the Meng Mountains. While cultivating the Dao and gathering herbs, he suddenly encountered an immortal. The immortal instructed him to stand upright at noon, but his shadow was askew. The immortal said: 'You know the value of the immortal’s Dao and are earnest in your study, yet you do not recognize that the failure lies in the heart-mind not being rectified.' Thereupon, he taught him this method. Later, Bozhen attained the Dao.

(*Dinglu Mu* records that Master Xu said: 'Jiang Bozhen’s disciples do not know whether this Jiang is the same person.')

The Master said: At midnight, remove the pillow and lie flat, clench the fists and relax the body. When the qi is harmonized and subtle, the body and spirits are complete. If they are not complete, quickly rise, burn incense, sit upright, close the eyes, and clench the fists upon the knees. Focus the heart-mind on the spirits of the body, envisioning before the eyes a white [light] the size of a chicken’s egg. Thus, one returns to the original state. Practice this once every five days."

(This pertains to the practices among the Twenty-Four Spirits.)

The Master said: Consuming herbs and plants without knowing the arts of the bedchamber and the practice of *daoyin* renders the medicine ineffective. One will never attain the Dao. Yet if one’s will is so sincere that it moves the numinous and the spirits are preserved, even herbs and plants are unnecessary. If one only knows the arts of the bedchamber and *daoyin* but does not know the method of the divine elixir, one will likewise not achieve immortality. If one obtains the golden [or "water-ladle"] divine elixir, no other techniques are required—one immediately becomes an immortal. If one obtains the *Scripture of the Great Cavern of Perfection*, even the way of the golden elixir is unnecessary. Recite it ten thousand times, and upon completion, one becomes an immortal.

The arts of the bedchamber and the practices of *daoyin* and circulating *qi* have their own classics in the world; they need not be reiterated here. (This means that merely consuming medicine and engaging in meditative cultivation while continuing conjugal relations will not lead to longevity. It does not mean one should abstain entirely—for even without such practices, those who consume medicine may still attain immortality.)

The scripture says: Those who attain the Dao all employ the method of starving the grain-devouring worms and perceive the techniques of the Three Corpses. When the grain-devouring worms die, the Three Corpses wither; when the Three Corpses wither, they naturally fall away. There are separate methods for killing the grain-devouring worms, which those who obtain them keep secret. (This refers to the *First Spirit Pill Formula* in the *Su Tradition*—other miscellaneous methods do not compare to it.)

The Master said:

Those born into the world possess a destined record in the bones, and must harbor steadfast resolve—this is the Dao’s decree. Consider the likes of Master Qingguang, Gu Xizi, Master Song of Nanyue, Master Changli, and Moyu: all were befriended by the Supreme Pole Perfected or cherished by the Celestial Thearch on High, who summoned them with clouds and dragon-drawn chariots. Thus, though they did not cultivate the Dao, immortality came to them of its own accord. Yet for those below such rank, unwavering resolve is essential. (Note: These figures all had cultivation practices and deeds recorded in scriptures, enduring hardships before their attainment. To say they achieved transcendence without effort obscures the truth. *Moyu* may refer to *Moyu* [墨羽] or *Muyu* [木羽].)

The Master said: "In cultivating the Dao, there are nine afflictions—each a grave malady of humankind. Should one discern these afflictions, immortality lies not far off. The nine afflictions are: having resolve but no opportunity; having opportunity but no companions; having companions but no resolve; having resolve but not meeting a teacher; meeting a teacher but not recognizing them; recognizing a teacher but not diligently following; diligently following but not upholding the Dao; or having resolve that is not firm; or being firm but not enduring. If one, from youth, cherishes the Dao and guards it with an unshakable heart—so that neither water nor fire can daunt their spirit, nor glory and splendor sway their resolve—and embraces authenticity while holding to simplicity, they will eventually encounter a teacher. Then they need not fear failure. Thus, one may attain success without companions and progress without external impetus. These are the essential and expansive teachings of immortal-cultivation. You should reflect upon them."

The Master said.

The Master said:
"Joy and anger harm resolve; sorrow and grief harm nature. Glory and splendor delude virtue; yin and yang deplete essence. These are the great taboos of Dao-cultivation and what immortal arts most abhor. Though one may practice essence-returning and embryonic breathing to merely replenish them, the inner void will already be thoroughly exhausted—this is still not one’s original truth. Better to know and yet not act, or act without dissipation. This is the essential Dao of immortality and the fundamental practice of life."

The Master said:
"To attain longevity, one should wash the face and purify the heart. When the sun has risen two zhang, face it directly. Exhale the dead [qi] through the mouth, and inhale the sun’s essence through the nose. When a sneeze arises, cease. This is called ‘penetration,’ and it replenishes essence and restores the embryo—a method for long life."

The Master said:
"In eating, take care not to overindulge, for excess breeds illness. After a full meal, take care not to lie down, for lying down unsettles the heart. An unsettled heart often loses its nature; overeating breeds illness. Illness renders medicine ineffective. For those who would cultivate the Dao, be vigilant about these matters before ingesting medicinal substances."

The Master said:
"The method of ritual regulation..."

The Master said:
"This method sharpens the eyes. With prolonged practice, one gains penetrating vision. On the first day of the *jiazi* ten-day cycle, take clear east-flowing water and mix it with true cinnabar to wash the eyes. Face the pure brightness of dawn and perform this twice seven times daily. Consistent practice yields excellent results. (This practice originates from the Twenty-Four Spirits, where it is called *Futong*, and is performed at midday on the *gengwu* day.)"

The Master said:
"For those who would cultivate the Dao, visualize the sun and moon in the eyes, let the ears resonate with the master’s voice, and constantly exhale dead *qi* while inhaling the living. Embody the five stars in form, walk as though treading empty space, and keep the heart fixed on long life. Be cautious with laughter and measured in speech. Always contemplate your form—this is the essential Dao."

The Master said:
"The method of the Seven and Five must always be preserved. The Five reside within the body; the Seven within the meridians."

The Master said:
"In the world, there are malevolent and fierce ghosts of the lower earth who often appear as women to delude and test people. If you encounter such beings, immediately close your eyes and visualize the *Tian Guan* star at the center of the celestial balance, along with the spirits of the body. Compose your expression, steady your will, and gaze intently at the core of the ritual."

If the pearl-like clarity in your vision dims and grows murky, it is a test by ghosts. When you recognize such a trial, visualize the Seven Stars before your face—or above your head—to repel them. If the light within the ritual’s core becomes square and radiant, it is a transcendent Daoist. Those who awaken will bow to them; those who fail remain untested. Yet to mistake the demonic for a true person is another form of failure. Be ever cautious. (For when the false and true intermingle, this is the most difficult test of all.)

The Master said:
"Do not abruptly cease eating—only gradually reduce your intake. Decrease by one *sheng* every ten days, and within half a year, you will have stopped entirely. There are methods for abstaining from grain, but as many in the world already know them, I will not repeat them here. People eat peaches to nourish the body, unaware that peach bark is even more potent. There are separate methods for using peach bark."

The Master said:
"When abstaining from grain and entering the mountains, one should consume white stone. Long ago, the Master of White Stone made stone his sustenance, and so the world called him the White Stone Immortal. He was a perfected being, now serving as the Left Immortal Minister of the Eastern Bureau. There are methods for consuming white stone. The *White Stone Method* was created by the White Stone Immortal himself. He was also skilled in the *Tai Su Transmission*, which speaks of the essence of white stone—hence his name, the White Stone Immortal." (This method exists in the world.)

The Master said:

The *Tai Su Transmission* is a Daoist text. Those who study it should revere the Most High Lord Lao and the Supreme Clarity Sovereign. This is the Way of luminous emptiness reflected within the radiance—it is not the most exalted of immortal teachings. It is what Laozi called the "Spirit of the Valley."

The Master said:
"Wangwu Mountain is a celestial realm apart, known as the Terrace of Yang. All who first attain the Dao proceed to the Terrace of Yang. The Terrace of Yang is the Palace of Pure Emptiness. For those who wish to enter the mountains, this mountain is unrivaled. Below flows the Baoji River, and within its waters dwell stone essences. Those who obtain and consume them may attain longevity." (This mountain lies in Bishui County of Henan, the very source of the Ji River.)

The Master said:
"The Great Cavern is the Divine Continent. Within the Divine Continent are three mountains, and within these three mountains are seven palaces. The seven palaces undergo seven transformations: at dawn, they become gold; at midday, silver; at dusk, bronze; at night, light. Sometimes they transform into mountains, sometimes into water, sometimes into stone—these are the seven transformations. The seven transformations correspond to seven scriptures, and twenty-one jade lads accompany these texts. Thus, it is called the *Perfect Scripture of the Great Cavern*. Reading it ten thousand times bestows immortality. This is the supreme scripture of the immortal Dao."

The Master said:

The {Men Liang} Wilds are the abode of the {Men Liang} winds. Atop Kunlun lie nine palaces, which are the nine abodes. The Supreme Ultimate is the Great Palace. All immortals are but officials of the nine abodes (the character 辽 should be 僚), while perfected beings are the dukes, ministers, and grandees of the nine abodes. The ranks of immortal officials ascend and descend, each with its proper order. Among immortals, there are left and right bureaus, and within these, left and right dukes, left and right ministers, left and right grandees, and left and right censors. Those who master the *Great Cavern* become immortal ministers; those who ingest the golden elixir become grandees; those who consume the various fungi become censors. Should one obtain and consume the Hidden Fungus of the Supreme Ultimate, one becomes a left or right immortal duke or a perfected being.

The Master said:
"There are those who attain liberation through the corpse, and these are of several kinds, all within the reckoning of immortality. Yet corpse-liberated immortals may not ride the flowered canopy, mount the flying dragon, ascend the Supreme Ultimate, or wander the nine abodes." (This refers to those who naturally attain corpse-liberation, such as the rulers of the underworld, not those who employ transformation or concealment.)

The Master said:

The ninefold refinement of the Yang Elixir is a method all in the world possess—no need to expound it further. (This refers to the arts of the bedchamber; "Yang Elixir" may instead denote the "Yin Elixir.")

The Master said:
"While studying among men, one should only ingest medicines. If you do not cut off grains, the *Great Cavern* cannot be attained. Methods for cutting off grains exist in the world."

The Master said:
"I wish to speak of the wonders of immortality and discourse on the transformations of the Dao. You must keep this secret—guard my words well." (This refers to the two matters that follow.)

The Master said:
"Long ago there was a man named Guo Chongzi, a man of the Yin dynasty and disciple of Perfected Peng. Once, he and his three brothers were traveling together when they were attacked by evil men, who injured his left arm. His three brothers grew furious and wished to punish them, but Chongzi said it was useless. He smiled and they each went their way. Later, this man entered officialdom, and Chongzi’s praise elevated him, repeatedly and without cease. The man then went to thank him, yet Chongzi’s praise did not stop. The man said, 'I am an evil man and cannot accept the gifts of a noble one.' He then took his own life. Later, Chongzi attained the Dao, but the Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate deemed him guilty of causing a man’s death and thus unworthy of becoming a Perfected Being." (This shows that even an excess of goodness may bring harm—how much worse, then, is evil? Many present-day affairs resemble this, so let it serve as a warning.)

Fan Lingzi was devoted to the Dao of immortality from his youth, persisting thus for many years. Later, he encountered Sima Jizhu, who was about to enter the mountains of Changshan. After seven years, he entered a stone chamber. In its northeast corner lay a stone *pian she*. (This is pronounced with the *ye* rhyme of *zuo*; it is a large jar. It may also be a stone *pian hu yong*.) Whenever Jizhu went out, he would tell him, "Beware—do not open this." This warning was repeated many times.

Fan Lingzi suddenly opened it and looked inside. Below, he saw his parents and family, near yet not distant, and was overcome with sorrow and longing. When Sima Jizhu returned, he sent him home. Later, Jizhu summoned him again and had him guard a bronze chest, once more warning him not to open it. Lingzi opened it again, and as before, saw his family. Jizhu sent him away, and thus he failed to attain the Dao.

(These events enter the realm of the inconceivable. Yet one must always follow this principle and take heed in similar cases.)

When accumulated merit reaches a thousand, even with transgressions, one may still attain immortality. When merit reaches three hundred but transgressions outweigh it, one’s descendants may attain immortality. When merit reaches two hundred, one’s grandchildren may attain it. If one has neither transgressions nor merits but relies on the merits of ancestors, one may still attain immortality—this is what is meant by "the lingering blessings of forebears." Those lacking resolve and burdened with many transgressions may attain wealth and nobility, but immortality remains beyond hope.

(This section sets the standard for merit and transgression—how can one not strive?)

Here ends the transmission of this scroll.

This scroll bears the calligraphy of a Chief Clerk and an Assistant Clerk.

*True Reports*, Scroll Five.
*True Reports*, Scroll Six.
*The Transmission of the Mandate*, Second Section.
*An Account of the Art of Serving the Herb*, by the Lady of Purple Tenuity.

(There are two copies of this text in the hand of an Assistant Clerk. Though titled *An Account of the Art of Serving the Herb*, it largely expounds on fundamental principles and general theories, seemingly not the author’s original intent. The Lady of Purple Tenuity is rich in talent and elegant in sentiment; her words flow with abundance and ease. She traces matters from beginning to end, ever transcending the bounds of reason. In later passages, she elucidates the subtleties of the Yellow and Red with profound clarity.)

Now, the morning’s first light gathers in primordial vastness, and the profound origins of the hidden dawn remain unordered. The Eight Directions are in chaos, and the numinous gates unopened. One may only gaze serenely into the empty void, allowing nature to take its course in tranquil stillness. Then, pure tones softly resound, and harmonious winds arise in unison. The twin luminaries shine with radiance, and the clouds of dawn await no invitation. They cradle the sprouting of the uncarved, blocking the myriad sources at the loom’s outset. All life returns to the true, and every kindred spirit conceals its beginnings. How could one wish for a lifespan of ten thousand years as mere mortality? Rather, one should long to embrace the talisman of longevity and dwell in eternal harmony. Why trouble oneself with the obscure realms of the spirit or delve into the illusory externals of esoteric arts?

For those whose forms lack the means to attain the true and whose vessels cannot bear its use, it is truly fitting to tread the wondrous subtleties of the Celestial Origin, to push the myriad essences to their utmost refinement, to seek the Nine Wefts and bring forth life, to behold the distant illumination of the morning light. Gazing upward at the misty qi, one sees the numinous clouds coiling through the void. Looking down upon the Six Pitches, one perceives the Eight Winds fanning their might. The Great Void emits the howl of the hidden depths, and the circular luminaries cast their radiance upon the empty sky. Then, purple mists gather in beauty, waves surge against crumbling peaks, floating vapors shape the landscape, and the clear light flees in flight. The Five Phases engage in mutual destruction, the Four Seasons clash and cast aside. Metal and Earth draw close, while Water and Fire form enmity. Forests and grasses halt their growth, and the hundred rivers open and close. Thunderous lightning rages and boils, thunder shakes east and west, splitting and shattering. Heaven is in turmoil—this is the calamity of the Yang Ninth. Earth is obstructed—this is the convergence of the Hundred Sixth. The excess of the Celestial Stem reaches its limit, and one sees the blood of the flock of dragons spilled across the fields of Earth. Thus, fortune and misfortune collide, and the multitude is shown omens of disaster.

Those who tread the level Way shall see the reclusive attain true felicity, while those who dwell in secluded abundance shall find no path unblessed. Yet to brave peril is to journey with corpses borne in procession; to venture northeast is to lose companions and meet with regret. Should the great rivers prove unkind, the clarity of the Kan well’s depths shall sink into ruin. All this arises when humanity loses its true nature, when things turn against our harmony. Myriad shifts unfold, and spirits and ghosts ply their schemes, even causing Heaven and Earth to lose constancy, making the common folk their concern.

Thus did the Supreme Perfected One take pity on the restless multitudes, opening the dark ford to awaken the worthy. Thereupon, guiding through a hundred transformations and grasping the principles of life in full, those who dwell in fortune and virtue remain ever whole, while those who abide in peril and harm wither and break. Those who master the Six Qi attain fixed longevity; those who ingest the numinous fungus achieve divine ease. Wondrous formulas and supreme arts unfold within the mysteries of the Clear Void. Golden tablets and jade slips are compiled upon the Terrace of Commissions and Plumes. Subtle and profound, the divine chant resonates, the true radiance merging and parting. To sing its verses is to rein in the dawn at the Grand Tenuity; to employ its Way is to wheel through the Nine Peaks. Carriages and canopies arrayed in the ranks of floating rosy clouds, gazing back at the Terrace of Literary Splendor. Some turn the furnace to refine cinnabar’s hidden essence, grinding and forging the purple nectar of gold and azure. The lush jade efflorescence surges in splendor, the Eight Precious Stones shimmering as they soar. Crimson fluids swirl in eddies, the dragon embryo hums in concealment. Tiger foam and phoenix brain, cloud-laden jade frost, the lunar elixir of the Great Ultimate, the Three Rings of Numinous Steel—with but a knife-tip’s measure, the divine wings unfurl. Then, donning the celestial scriptures of the Empty Sky’s upper texts, entering the chambers of Primordial Beginning, the jade tones of the resonant texts issue from the Palace of the Three Mysteries. Precious silks bear the gifts of the Three Origins, flowered pendants emit from the cinnabar groves’ chambers. The August Thearch bestows the purple leaps of exalted joy, the Supreme Perfected grants the fiery bells of flowing gold. Divine youths open the carriage shafts as the Nine Phoenixes cry in unison. Heavenly pipes startle the void, morning bells chime in scattered resonance. Raising the body and lowering the banner, the Eight Effulgences float in the sky. Dragon carriages and tiger banners fan out across the eight directions, ascending to the utmost tips of Eternal Yang, descending to repose in the orchid halls of inverted shadows. Moon consorts harness their teams of four, sunlight illumines the visage; spirit maidens embrace the quilts as incense smoke overflows the windows. A glance and a turn—how the circular net strides beyond! What need to speak of the ninety thousand li? Only then does one know the Way of the lofty immortals stands supreme, the subtleties of seeking the numinous are profound, the attainment of ingestion and adornment unites with the divine, and the workings of fortune and misfortune suddenly become manifest.

Had it not been for the noble scion’s *Yellow and Lao Jade Writ*, the *Thirty-Nine Perfected Scriptures of the Great Cavern*, or the *Supreme Hidden Mystery of the Seven Origins of Vast Emptiness*, none would have found peers in this art. Yet there are those who, by embodying the spirit in concentrated thought, treasuring and refining the Hall of Light, and journeying at dawn to the Six Numinous Ones, cause the Five Viscera to blossom with radiance. They guard and seal the Primal Pass, inwardly preserve the Nine Perfected, and circulate the Three Qi to irrigate the Cinnabar Field—this, too, is a secondary path.

For one whose cinnabar sincerity is as plain as coarse herbs, what need is there for the grand sacrifices of the eastern neighbor? Instead, one may augment with five-cloud cassia, water-plantain roots, yellow essence, southern candle and yang grass, eastern stone and azure-green, pine and cypress resin and fruit, sesame and poria—all implements of nurturing life, by which longevity may be attained.

I have further observed the strengths and weaknesses of plants and trees, noting those that swiftly benefit the self, yet none rival the manifold proofs of *Atractylodes*’ efficacy. Of late, killing qi has shrouded the heavens, baleful fumes have stilled the light, malevolent demons have surged forth, and a hundred ailments have converged. Whether from wind-cold binding the Passes or flowing swellings and chronic afflictions, misfortunes have assailed without warning, and illnesses have arisen beyond expectation—day by day, they gather in throngs.

Now, *Atractylodes* qi checks demonic fluids, exhaled smoke subdues and breaks the joints of evil. It fortifies the interior and gathers the hun-soul, enriches the blood and engenders the brain, expels malignity and induces the true, guards essence and defends life.

Consuming it as food renders the spirit supple and pervasive, bestowing radiance and lightness. Taking it in pills or powders dispels the hundred diseases, and the five viscera brim with fluid—thus vision endures long and grows ever clearer. The ancients called it the "crimson essence of mountain spirits" and the "quintessence of *Alpinia*." The *Tai Shang Daoist Inscription on Immortality* states: *"If you desire long life, consume the mountain essence. If you desire to soar lightly, consume the mountain ginger."* Such is its meaning.

I do not claim that all other substances should be reduced in favor of *Atractylodes* for benefit. Yet the efficacy of *Atractylodes* qi is precisely what this age requires. In this latter age, illness abounds—it is fitting to take and employ it. Though the Dao may be inwardly sufficient, one still fears the calamities of external affairs. Even when the form is outwardly replete, there may yet be the flaw of inner collapse. The extremes of Zhang and Shan serve as warning enough.

*Atractylodes* can grant long life and enduring longevity, and secondly, it can repel the myriad afflictions of malevolent demons. I have seen recluses in the mountains and forests who, having mastered this Way, live for eight hundred or a thousand years, standing shoulder to shoulder with the Five Peaks. Yet people, burdened by excessive writing, cannot even record one or two methods to demonstrate it.

Now I compile several formulas for taking *Atractylodes* to enlighten those who cherish secrecy. If one truly believes and employs them, they may avert sudden and violent disasters. When great peace arrives, the four directions will harmonize, the celestial weave will nurture life, calamitous fumes will vanish, the five poisons will conceal their forms, the two luminaries will ever watch over, and the myriad things will flourish of themselves. At such a time, you may act as you will—no more will there be untimely ruin.

What I have spoken of *Atractylodes* is meant to urge those with intent to take and use it, thereby averting these disasters and ailments.

Of late, however, lesser scholars have vied with one another in confusion, often employing indiscriminate concoctions and the methods of the *Yellow Writ* and *Red Realm*. These indeed contain the true essentials of harmonizing the two complementary forces of life. If one unites in the Dao, breaks free from the snares of the world, aligns with the six directions, follows the seasonal nodes, averts disasters, dispels misfortunes, congeals essence, and treasures the embryo—above, the spirits of the brain will not wane; below, the three fields will overflow. Advancing and retreating in proper measure, calamities will be dispelled; warp and weft will align, and health will endure. Enemies will grasp the reins without faltering, and the six armies will march far and return whole—such are its benefits. Yet this is not the grandest affair of the immortals’ tradition.

Alas, how perilous! Though these are arts of mutual generation and methods of transcendence, they resemble driving an ice chariot through the fiery regions or sailing a fire boat upon drowning waters. Without true rectitude, failure is all but certain.

Some may defy celestial patterns, harbor slanderous malice and envy, causing the spiritual root to stagnate and block, the cycles of fortune and misfortune to diverge. They violate sacred vows and offend the luminous, incurring the wrath of the Three Officials. Or they provoke enmity and chain disasters, leading royal armies to defeat. Or they bury captives alive and slaughter the surrendered, soaking the fields with blood. Or they exhaust their horses’ strength yet still press onward—if they then plunge deep into the northern frontier without resistance, will they not surely perish beneath the Xiongnu blades? Their bodies will die abroad, their families be executed at home. How can one not be cautious? How can one not be cautious?

I have witnessed many such cases—few gain benefit, only vain strivings for survival, sighing endlessly over the cycle of rebirth. How much better to guard the cinnabar truth within the Crimson Palace, summon the primordial spirit to the Mud Pill, preserve the vital fluids without loss, seal the hidden arts at the Gate of Destiny, ingest sacred herbs to nurture life, rinse the mouth with flower-streams in clear rivers, study the profound and wondrous secret formulas, and recite the hidden chapters of the Most High. Then one may dwell high among the peaks and ridges, reckoning time alongside gold and stone. What lord could claim such a one as friend? What emperor could make them a subject? Far from the Five Turbidities of the dusty world, ever in purity awaiting the true. Leisurely and carefree, I delight in my clouds.

*(Note: The subsequent prescriptions are absent in both versions, though two separate formula texts appear to correspond to this method. They have been compiled in the third scroll.)*

This passage appears in two appended manuscripts—one on yellow paper, one on azure—where the Azure Lad of the Directions remarks:

"To pursue the Dao is bitter; not to pursue it is also bitter. From birth to old age, from old age to sickness, guarding the body until death—its sufferings are boundless. The vexations of the heart and the accumulation of sins perpetuate the cycle of birth and death, its bitterness beyond words. How much more so for those who fail to live out their natural span?

To pursue the Dao is bitter: maintaining purity to preserve the true, guarding the mysterious to contemplate the numinous, seeking teachers through hardship, enduring hundreds of trials, diligently keeping the mind from lapsing, resolutely focusing the will—this is the utmost bitterness. To regard the rank of lords as transient, gold and jade as mere bricks and stones, and fine silks as rags—only then can one be said to truly inquire into the Dao."

The Azure Lad of the Directions further states: "One who cultivates the Dao and severs the roots of love and desire is like a hand grasping a string of pearls—one by one, they will eventually be exhausted. Gradually removing external evils will also reach an end. When all is exhausted, the Dao is attained."

Again, a parable likens it to an ox bearing a heavy load through the mire: utterly spent, it dares not glance left or right, hastening only to escape the mud and find respite. For the Daoist, the passions and desires are far worse than that mire. With a steadfast heart set upon the Dao, one may avoid the manifold sufferings and likewise attain the Dao.

*(A cautious note records that the Supreme Minister’s audience contained no oral transmissions—only these two admonitions and a single song-poem, which may not have been personally witnessed or recorded by Master Yang.)*

The Perfected King of Western Citadel declared: “For a being to depart the Three Evil Paths and attain human birth is difficult. Having attained human birth, to leave the female form and become male is difficult. Having become male, to possess all six senses and four limbs intact is difficult. With the six senses complete, to be born in the Central Kingdom is difficult. Having been born in the Central Kingdom, to encounter a lord of the Dao is difficult. Having encountered a lord of the Dao, to be born into a family that studies the Dao is difficult. To possess a heart of kindness and benevolence is difficult. With a benevolent heart awakened, to believe in the Dao, virtue, and long life is difficult. Having believed in the Dao, virtue, and long life, to encounter the era of Great Peace under the *ren-chen* cycle is difficult. How can one not be exhorted to diligence?”

*(The Three Evil Paths refer to rebirths where one fails to become human—namely, as birds, beasts, or insects and livestock.)*

The Most High questioned a Daoist, saying: “How many days does a human life span?” One replied, “A matter of several days.” The Most High said, “You have not yet attained the Dao.” Another replied, “A human life spans the time between meals.” The Most High said, “You may depart; you have not yet spoken of the Dao.” Another replied...

“Within the span of a single breath.” The Most High said, “Excellent! This may be called speaking of the Dao. I once heard these words, and now I tell them to you. If you diligently study the Dao, you may yet transcend this breath. Though my disciples may be thousands or tens of thousands of miles away from my [teachings]—their hearts holding fast to my precepts—they shall surely attain the Dao. Those who study the jade-classic treasured scriptures shall surely achieve immortality. As for those who stand at my left side, their intentions incline toward perverse conduct; they shall never attain the Dao.

When one pursues the Dao—reading the Daoist scriptures and performing Daoist deeds—it is like tasting honey: the entire mouth is sweet, the six viscera are delighted, and a lingering savor remains. Those who can act thus shall attain the Dao. *(The Supreme Minister has no descending Yang matters; this refers only to absorbing the radiance of the sun and moon.)*

The Perfected of Great Emptiness, the Red Lord of South Mountain, declared: “When a person commits many evils yet does not repent, suddenly restraining their heart, their sins return to them as rivers flow to the sea, daily growing deeper and wider. But if one recognizes their wrongs, repents, and turns to goodness, their sins vanish and virtue accumulates—thus may they attain the Dao.

When others bring misfortune upon you, meet it with blessing. Thus, the qi of fortune and virtue ever arises from this. The qi of harm and heavy calamity returns instead to them. This is the conduct of one who studies the Dao.”

He further declared: “When the wicked harm the worthy, it is like spitting toward the sky—the spittle does not [reach] the sky but returns to [strike] oneself. *(All instances of ‘刑’ should be ‘形’.)* To cast dust against the wind is to have it not [reach] the other but instead drench one’s own body. The Dao cannot be destroyed; calamity must perish the self.”

The Perfected of Great Emptiness said: “To feed a hundred ordinary people is not equal to feeding one good person. To feed a thousand good people is not equal to feeding one who studies the Dao. Those who endure cold and dwell in the mountains and forests—take this especially to heart.”

The Red Lord also had no further separate instructions to impart.

The Lady of Purple Prime declared: “There are five difficulties in the world. The first is that poverty makes bestowing alms difficult. The second is that great wealth makes studying the Dao difficult. The third is that controlling one’s fate to avoid death is difficult. The fourth is that obtaining and beholding the Cavernous Scriptures is difficult. The fifth is that being born in the sagely age after the *ren-chen* cycle is difficult.

Long ago, I asked the Most High: ‘By what means may one come to know one’s past lives?’ The Most High replied: ‘The Dao and virtue are formless; knowing them brings no benefit. What is essential is to guard one’s resolve and practice the Dao. It is like polishing a mirror—once the grime is removed, clarity remains, and one’s own form becomes visible. Sever the six passions, guard emptiness and purity, and you will both perceive the truth of the Dao and know your past lives.’

He further said: ‘Contemplate the Dao, practice the Dao, and have faith in the Dao, and you will attain the root of faith. Its blessings are measureless.’

The Lady of Purple Tenuity declared: “One who practices the Dao is like one who carries fire into a dark room—the darkness vanishes, and only light remains. When one who studies the Dao preserves correctness, ignorance is extinguished, and correctness alone endures. Wealth and sensual pleasure in relation to oneself are like a small child coveting the honey on a blade’s edge—its sweetness is insufficient to delight the mouth, yet it brings the peril of a severed tongue.”

The Lady of Mystic Clarity declared: “The entanglements of a man with wife, children, and treasured home are more grievous than imprisonment in shackles. Imprisonment in shackles may yet be pardoned, but the passions of wife and children—even when they bring calamity as dire as a tiger’s maw—one still willingly casts oneself into them. Such transgressions admit no pardon. Emotional attachments ensnare a person as surely as one who grasps a torch and walks against the wind. The fool who does not release the torch will surely burn his hand. The poisons of greed, anger, and ignorance dwell within the human body. If one does not swiftly remove this disaster through the Dao, peril is certain. This is what it means for the ignorant to burn their hands.

One who practices the Dao is like wood upon the water, following the current as it flows. It neither strikes the left bank nor the right bank. It is not seized by men, nor is it obstructed by ghosts and spirits. Nor does it rot and decay. I guarantee it will reach the sea. When a person practices the Dao, is not deluded by filthy desires, is not deceived by manifold evils, and advances with unflagging resolve and no doubt, I guarantee they will attain the Dao.”

The Lady of the Southern Culmen said: “From love arises sorrow; when sorrow arises, fear follows. Without love, there is no sorrow; without sorrow, there is no fear. Once there was a man who recited scriptures late into the night with such grief that his emotions were deeply stirred, and suddenly he was overcome with longing for home. The Perfected of the Most High then appeared in the guise of an ordinary man and went directly to ask him: ‘Have you ever played the zither?’ The man replied: ‘When I was at home, I used to play it.’ The Perfected said: ‘What is it like when the strings are slack?’ The man replied: ‘They do not sound, nor do they evoke sorrow.’ The Perfected asked again: ‘What is it like when the strings are too taut?’ The man replied: ‘The sound breaks, and it becomes mournful.’ The Perfected asked once more: ‘What is it like when slackness and tension are balanced?’ The man replied: ‘All notes harmonize, and the eight tones resound in wondrous concert.’ The Perfected said: ‘Studying the Dao is the same. When the mind is properly attuned, it is like playing the zither—the Dao may be attained.’

Of all great desires, none is greater than sensual pleasure. Its transgressions know no bounds, and its consequences admit no pardon. Were it to have even one more counterpart, not a single person under heaven could practice the Dao.

Those who study the Dao—no act of hidden virtue is greater than bestowing kindness and delivering salvation, and no resolve is greater than guarding the body and serving the Dao. Their blessings are vast, and their lives are firmly established. If someone bears ill will toward me, I do not accept it; the ill will returns to its source. Disaster will return to their body as surely as a shadow follows form and an echo follows sound.”

The above are the teachings and admonitions of the assembled numinous beings. Recorded here are the words of three male Perfected and two female Perfected.

The exalted Perfected Ones, in their noble majesty, descend and gather but rarely. I fear these are but accounts of those who have descended, recounted as if by the Four Lords of the Jasper Terrace chanting on the autumn equinox—not necessarily received directly from Lord Yang himself. Thrice have I witnessed changes; twice have I been told to wait. The essential matter now is to rise and build a dwelling on East Mountain, and for the time being, one may depart from protective custody.

When I inquired as to the reason, no answer was given. I asked the assembled numinous beings, “Is it perhaps thus with me?” Yet the meaning remains unclear. I wish to understand it.

*(This passage is Lord Yang’s own record, discussing the affairs of the Prefectural Governor.)*

Those who often wander in thought through mountains and marshes, entrusting their spirit to the immortals and Perfected Ones—if their qi is moved by compassion for your distant joy, and the mountain spirits rejoice in your turning toward the Dao, then the hundred illnesses cannot invade, and the hundred evils cannot assail. Those who frequently burn incense at your side will find their hun and po rectified, and ever perceive the fragrance of the wind’s qi. Over time, they will come to realize this. Realization leads to entering the Dao; entering the Dao leads to attaining immortality; attaining immortality leads to becoming a Perfected One.

*(From the preceding scroll, ten poems of waiting and verse follow the precepts, continuing to this point—altogether eight sheets. All were later inscribed by hand on ruled paper, with half a line of characters written at the end.)*

This is Yang’s writing. Upon gazing serenely at the movements of heaven and earth, one should recognize that there must have been further continuations by Yang before this. Later hands merely transcribed and appended other continuations—hence the prior omission of thirty-four characters. (What Yang originally wrote here is now unknown.)

Serenely gazing at heaven and earth, one aspires to become a flying immortal.
Serenely gazing at mountains and rivers, one aspires to become a flying immortal.
Serenely gazing at the ten thousand things, one cherishes the compassion of their nurturing and sustaining.
Constantly maintaining such a heart, one attains the Dao.

Human life is but an illusion,
a brief sojourn between heaven and earth.
Yet if one refines qi and nurtures spirit,
striving with bitter diligence to infuse the true,
one will attain the Dao for long.
When the Dao is perfected, one dwells together with heaven and earth in the Great Vastness.
If one penetrates emptiness and embodies non-being,
then one abides with the Great Vastness in utter stillness.
One who can fathom stillness
will find it invisible to sight, inaudible to hearing.
The root of life and death is easily untied,
and the pursuit of enduring years is easily found.
What is found may be grasped;
what is untied may endure.

To endure in the Dao is to nurture life.
To wander long and freely is to absorb qi.
When qi is whole, life persists.
Only then can one nurture to perfection.
Perfected nurture unites with the true,
and only then can one endure.
Ascending to the two realms of vital qi,
gazing toward the utter stillness of complete nurture—
one sees the ten thousand things, in their dark and yellow hues, as mere temporary lodgings.
