How could one not be diligent?

When qi is whole, it repels demonic spirits.
When nurture is complete, it repels the hundred harms.
Entering the army, one does not encounter weapons;
traversing the mountains, one does not meet tigers or wolves.
This is what is meant.
The heart of one who studies the Dao.

Ever maintain the urgency of remembering one’s morning meal—none who do so fail to attain their aim. Cherish qi as one cherishes one’s own face and eyes—none who do so fail to preserve it whole. Yet faces and eyes may still be marred, just as qi may still be lost. What men cherish most is ever their face and eyes, fearing defilement, and only secondarily their four limbs. Were one to cherish qi as the first and most pressing concern of the whole body, I would seldom see men wither away.

*(Note: The qi spoken of here refers to the seminal essence of the bedchamber, not the qi of breath.)*

Men, following custom, seek after florid reputations—like burning incense: all around may smell its fragrance, yet they do not realize it scorches itself to ash. When the incense is spent, its qi vanishes; when the name is established, the self perishes. Thus the lofty withdraw and smile, keeping to purity and stillness.

Life, as a thing, is like the sun, moon, heaven, and earth—these four images stand directly opposed to the arising of life. Lose life, and the four images also perish. It is not that the four images perish, but that the perishing of life destroys them. Were life to endure always, the four images would endure always—not because the four images endure, but because I can make life endure. Enduring life can even generate that which casts no shadow—what need to reckon with the four images?

Disaster strikes, calamity arises, form decays, qi scatters—what manner of affair is this? It seems to arise from much speech without holding to the One, from many schemes seeking mere escape. Thus the lofty nest collapses and its branches fall; a hundred victories are lost in a single defeat. Alas for the talent of one who might have become a transcendent—how could it be ruined by the folly of a mere stripling?

*(The phrase “ruined by the folly of a stripling” alludes to the malady in the gaohuang region that could not be cured, borrowing from the dream of Duke Jing of Jin. Otherwise, it may refer to some other childish misdeed.)*

As the Lady of South Mountain Peak said:
The swan and heron face the southern journey,
their distant wings stirring the air.
Amid the wilds of ten thousand and a hundred,
those birds deem themselves sufficient.
Yet the turtledove sighs at their meager reach,
and the great Peng laughs at the span of their flight—
a mere hand’s breadth.

Content with what is content, right and wrong settle of themselves.

Thus the three birds do not confer among themselves—why mock them?

The Purple Tenuity states:

The eight clauses above are all from Yang’s writings. The perfected of antiquity held solely to the tally of numinous unity, yielding to the transformations of dark contemplation and compliant compliance. They illuminated the level path to harmonize with change, and grasped the hidden pivot to align all things. Therefore, beyond the bounds of spontaneity, they preserved it without discourse; within the realm of governance, they discoursed without debate.

In former times, the mystic wind faded and vanished, the dusty qi thickened and spread. The great Way sank into oblivion, and the shallow and false flourished. Those who galloped and raced cast aside the true beyond the edge of the cliff; the restless and anxious thronged the marketplace of form and reputation, gluttonous for gain. Those who wielded cunning gave rise to the calamity of drifting and dissipation; those who coveted and sought brought upon themselves the burden of self-aggrandizement. They strayed from the constant in pursuit of what they fancied, and departed from the ultimate rightness for what was not right.

Which is dearer, name or self? The Daoist’s solemn *admonition* (should read *admonition*).

To tread the depths is the utmost warning of guarding the One. How lamentable indeed!

On the night of the eighth day of the sixth month, *Preserving Life* addressed Steward Xu: To know that the boundless harms one’s nature, and that the heart, swayed by likes and dislikes, disturbs the true—how could this compare to guarding the root, purifying the empty and tranquil, and dwelling in the cultivation of the Three Spirits? Thus one pervades the myriad things and merges with the mirror-like stillness of the profound, utterly dissolved and unified with the Mud Pill, so that inner and outer share in equal blessing.

This may be shown to Hu Ya.

The Lady of South Mountain Peak says:
Hasten the distribution of the elixir.

Do not allow disturbances along the River’s course. (The *dispersal* appears to be the *Atractylodes Powder*. The River refers to the Water Official.)

*Preserving Life* says: Those who neglect the cultivation of the Dao and virtue, or who study the Way without attainment, will fail to keep pace with the diligent disciples who initiate merit. Of late, I have seen none who advance day by day. The resolve to learn has naturally waned. (The virtue of seven generations is fundamentally rooted in the scholar. Without study, it can no longer be relied upon—thus this serves as a warning.) Those who grasp at the higher Dao yet remain indolent are but *lower ghosts*. (The *lower ghosts* refer to the spirits who preside over lesser understandings, not necessarily the ghosts of the palace. The scriptures also speak of this.)

When in office, remain free of affairs. *Yi the Perfected* inwardly refines; entanglements do not sully his clarity, nor do rivalries disturb his heart-mind. Such is the Daoist in office. Those who dwell in *Xiu the Mysterious* and abide in lofty solitude, though mountains and rivers collapse, do not glance aside. Those who preserve the Dao and guard the true, though cold and heat, hunger and thirst assail them, remain unguarded. This is the utmost of single-minded devotion.

When essence scatters to the eight hollows and the hun-wandering drifts through the ten thousand *waters*, some seize the moment when the wind blows to invade the gaps, while others cling to demons to bind themselves in illness.

Let all things be cast aside and unheard. The Dao of old is not the Dao of today. If spiritual awareness differs, what can the hundred gaps do to me? What I have heard is sufficient to restore simplicity.

*(The above nine clauses are all from Yang’s writings.)*

The true one is entirely free from the stirrings of emotion and desire, from thoughts of male and female. If crimson and white linger in the heart-mind, true resonance will not respond, and the numinous maiden, the supreme honored one, will not descend. Even if there are those who attain something, it extends no further than the master of the moment. The coupling of yin qi can never lead to the cultivation of the supreme Dao. I once deeply regretted how swiftly this reliance changes.

Thus the true Dao cannot be sought through opposition, nor essential words heard by chance. Where there is a pair, there is no truth. Where external things are combined, truth and falsehood intermingle. The traces of truth and falsehood are clearly discernible.

*(This clause should be Master Pei’s words.)*

*(From another text:)* Weeping is but the sound of hastening toward death; grief is the great affliction of rotting bones. I fear you have not yet awakened to this, and thus I worry for you. Those who grieve excessively will be touched by licentious qi. Though Master Lai may be skilled in your arts, what can he do against the one who stretches upon the wooden tablet? This is what Lord Dinglu cautions against.

*(The above clause is from Yang’s writings, though the latter part was cut off and incomplete.)*

Mu, in trepidation, speaks: Meeting with the mysterious fate, I have been granted the hearing of the ancestral teaching. In every matter, I will follow and yield, awakening my heart-mind. My benevolence lingers and clings, my reverence is boundless. As for the distinction between beginning and end...

The natural principle of Heaven is to delight in life and abhor death. Human sentiment is ever thus moved—weeping and mourning are but the sorrow of parting, the rites of hastening to the bier. Internally, they express emotion; externally, they accord with ritual. This is what the worthy and the common alike uphold, and none may transgress it. Though I, Mu, inwardly cultivate the Dao, outwardly I remain bound to the customs of the world. I have not yet cast off my robes to dwell in the mountains, nor girded myself with rope to wander alone. Thus I cannot but follow the rites that harmonize emotion and propriety, aligning inner and outer in perfect accord. Should I one day violate this, not only would I fail to bear the weight of affection, but I would also fear the harm that would befall me.

Your words, my lady, are tender and attuned to feeling, yet vast in their guidance of the Dao and enduring in their instruction—truly far-reaching. I shall humbly heed your admonitions, ensuring that sorrow does not turn to harm, that weeping does not exceed restraint. I will dwell in the Dao as I please, without daring to transgress. Respectfully submitted.

*(This is a reply to the letter from Youying. The matter of transmission is now lost, though it does not refer to the earlier warning about "the sound of hastening toward death," yet it remains tangentially connected.)*

---

**From the Writings of the Attendant (Yuan):**
*True Sayings, Scroll Six. True Sayings, Scroll Seven. "The Transmission of Destined Appointment," Part Three.*

Embodying the lofty workings of destiny, savoring the mysterious and masticating the real, I summon and draw near the radiant luminaries, congealing and stilling the six spirits. My luminous guidance shines forth in the eight directions of clarity. I yield to the numinous root, treasuring and refining the three measures. Nurturing the fluids and harmonizing the hun-soul, even should a rushing wind howl and rage, it could not assail my true nature. Though a tempestuous gale may surge and darken, how could it disturb my pure and unadulterated essence?

Thus I empty myself and chant in stillness, with a void heart awaiting the gods. I tend and gather the hundred absences, dwelling in the utmost truth. Let no cares or burdens intrude upon the mysterious abode; let no sorrowful thoughts disturb the crimson ford. In serene detachment and distant contemplation, I rejoice in the shared radiance of light. Then the supreme joy naturally resounds in both ears, like jade chimes ringing in the empty pavilion. My mouth is filled with fragrant winds, my eyes behold the threefold clouds, and in my reverence for the four seasons, I move and pause.

Daily attainment of the perfected truth—
wherever the gaze alights, all has already merged with the divine.
For the true person’s attainment of truth
is ever gained through this alone.
To feign truth without possessing it
is also through this that one stumbles and falls.

Should errors and ailments accumulate,
grief and sorrow fill the embrace,
worldly affairs ever burden the mind,
clinging to external paths,
seeking harmony for the multitude’s ears,
or chasing the heart’s worldly aging—
suddenly, a mournful wail arises,
like a child’s ceaseless weeping.
Long lamenting the dead and grieving the departed,
one’s essence scatters, spirit disperses,
the three *hun*-souls perish,
and in the void of perverse fate,
the *po*-soul summons *wangliang* spirits.
They seize my empty formation,
bringing forth a hundred afflictions.
How could one grasp the Way of life
only to rush toward the chamber of death,
or cast the numinous wind
to tread upon the corpse’s abode?
To hurl one’s auspicious signs
into foul and defiled realms?

I have heard of the lofty and victorious yet still proceed in ease,
often witnessed the luminous precepts yet remain unresolved in doubt.
Thus I array petitions upon the altar,
causing the stench of corruption to taint the hidden scriptures.
The four poles strike their drums,
the Three Officials brandish their golden spears.
How could one forget the sworn covenant?
The Great Beginning wanders there in vain.
The divine tiger bares its claws,
the venomous dragon displays its fangs—
the eight directions stand desolate in sincerity.

Alas, where could one flee?
Only after the body’s departure
does one realize the worth of pure companions,
and that the speaker’s words were not empty.

Yet mournful cries disrupt truth,
offending the upright.
Why does the enlightened lord not dwell always in the land of fortune,
but here shake his robes and return to his chamber?

On the night of the eleventh day of the first month, An Fei declared.
*(This passage concerns the ninth day of the first month in the year of yin, when Jiuhua addressed Yang Jun with mockery, prompting the Southern Perfected’s later discourse. Yang’s writing.)*

The perfected return their hearts to the One Correct,
and the Dao’s standard rests in eternal trust.
When the heart returns, the correct spirits harmonize;
when trust is aligned, auspicious omens arise.
This is the natural response,
requiring no reliance on dual extremes.

Delusion gives rise to right and wrong,
suspicion breeds doubt and semblance.
Lurking within, it harbors the mind of estrangement.
Outwardly, it grasps at scrutiny;
inwardly, it knots the threads of derision.
Abandoning the primal awakening at the founding,
deviating from the mysterious ladder to indulge in secret suspicion—
such are these followers.
I foresee their downfall,
never their establishment.

Those who cherish fear—
are they alone in this?
On the night of the thirtieth day of the second month, the Lady of Southern Marchmount addressed Attendant Xu.
*(This was conferred upon the Attendant, now spoken to admonish Yang Jun. Let none doubt the matter of Jiuhua. Yang’s writing.)*

Thus, gazing upon the vast waves that reach the heavens,
one knows they do not rise from the pools of *shuikua*.
Beholding the boundless black wings of *xuanhan*,
one knows they do not emerge from the ranks of mere scribes.
*(Purple Tenuity’s words:)*
When myriad blossoms gather, the dragon’s pattern forms;
when all voices unite, the *Yunshao* harmonizes.
Only when bitterness and sourness are complete
does the fine flavor blend.

*(The Lady of Zhonghou replied. These two phrases are drawn from the "Extensive Analogies" in the *Baopuzi* Outer Chapters. Later examples follow this precedent, as the assembled perfected borrow and employ them as metaphors—akin to the use of phrases from the *Zhouyi* or *Maoshi*.)*

How does that man compare to Liang Boluan?
*(The Lady of Zhonghou’s words. "That man" likely refers to the Attendant.)*

Liang’s virtue was narrow;
this one’s desolation and distant qi are far greater.
The tree that shades ten thousand *mu*
must rise from the loftiest peak.
The waves that surge to the heavens and shake the hills
must issue from the source of *Bantong*.
How vast is the worthy who accumulates hidden virtue—
he resembles the lord of *Fenyi*.
*(Purple Tenuity’s reply. *Fenyi* refers to the state of *Shanshanshi*, a comparison to King Tai of Zhou. From "shades ten thousand *mu*" to "source of *Bantong*" are also from the "Extensive Analogies," with *xiangling* altered to *zhencen*.)*

The farther north he goes, the more his wisdom closes.
*(Right Florescence’s words:)*
The wise value discerning the Dao’s resonance across a thousand generations,
grasping the traces of rise and fall.
The untamed scales roam the garments of the great wilderness,
free from the woes of snares and tethers.
The numinous wings beat upon the peaks of *Xuanpu*,
escaping the perils of nets and *sigen*.
How keenly they perceive fortune and misfortune!
*(The Immortal of Life Preservation’s reply. This again combines two passages from the "Extensive Analogies," altering *jijing* to *jiluo*, *linghu* to *lingyu*, and *zhaoluo* to *luosigen*.)*

To pursue the flight of the shadowless foot
yet fail to gallop freely at *Lüliang*;
to be one who swims the *shuiling* waves of the abyss
yet be unable to ascend perilous heights—
truly, this one’s talent is extraordinary.

How could one inwardly gather and restrain?
Those who assist the mechanism aspire to be humane—
their virtue must flourish abundantly.
Where dense forests thicken with foliage,
feathered flocks gather like clouds.
Where dark abysses stretch vast and boundless,
scaled multitudes race to converge.
If those who dwell in the heart are many,
what need is there for proximity?

*(Purple Tenuity’s words)*
The eight passages above were written by Yang. There are also copied versions. Those of the Dao should deeply study their numinous mysteries, settling the heart in matters beyond. Yet even diligent reflection on their essence—mere dregs and remnants—suffices for self-realization.

*(Perfected One of Paulownia Cypress’s words)*
For purity, nothing compares to the Eastern Mountains;
for nurturing the true, nothing compares to secluded groves.
Dwelling in the luminous scene, one ascends in emptiness,
far from the snares of worldly dust.
Thus, how could the nets of honor and disgrace ever bind the perfected scholar?

*(The two passages above were written by Yang)*
Gold and jade may pile like mountains,
yet they are not the self.
The eaves of *yuequ* and *yueqie* bamboo may endure,
yet they are not one’s own.
The splendor of glory and rank may be arrayed in full.

Yet they are not true value.
The fragrance and splendor of adorned beauty—
yet they are not the real.
If one can dissolve and dispel them,
then the heart of excess and stinginess vanishes,
and the gate of vulgar stagnation closes.
The awakening to what is truly noble gradually emerges,
and the sentiments of the worldly paths become manifest.
If one does not do so,
these four all become internal harms,
while externally, the afflictions of *Huandou* remain unremoved.
If stinginess does not disperse,
there is nothing left to pursue or arrange between them—
nor any affairs worth attending to in such a state.

*(Admonition to the Chief Clerk. These three characters were originally written in red, and should also be the words of the Lady of Right Radiance.)*
For those who pursue the Dao, there is indeed toil and hardship.
Such a person may be said to be one who will surely attain it.

*(The above two passages were written by the clerk of the Chief Clerk.)*
Those who study the Dao must focus single-mindedly on the Dao and direct their essence,
with no scattered thoughts.
They must cast aside extravagance,
preserve simplicity and purity,
and abide in stillness as if in profound secrecy, yet perceiving something,
or in tranquility as if in hiddenness, yet gaining something.
They must be wholly concentrated, as if standing at the edge of a deep ravine,
or trembling as if treading upon ice and embers.
Only then have they entered the gate of the Dao—
yet they have not yet reached the inner chamber of the Dao.

What is called difficult is the study of the Dao.
What is called easy is the study of the Dao.
To dwell in profound stillness and deep savoring,
to preserve harmony and celestial truth,
to direct the spirit and lodge the numinous,
to immerse oneself in the study of the Six Treasuries,
to cherish essence and seal the Gate of Earth,
to see nothing and hear nothing—
this is the ease of the Dao.
Yet it is precisely the inability to practice this
that makes it difficult.

*Xu Hou*, ponder this!
*Fuzi Ying*, refine yourself!

*(The above was dictated by the Lady of Right Radiance for transcription. That lord diligently attends to these matters.)*

Those who reap blessings are many—
yet those who fall into ruin accumulate misfortune and defeat.

Fan Shuai said:
*"He who knows not the Dao—"*
*(This likely refers to the ghostly commander Fan Jiang.)*

The two preceding passages were appended in a clerk’s hand.

Long ago, through the repeated white missives of the Hua clan, I reverently submitted my devotion. The numinous Way soared high, its resonance utterly transcendent, its echoes severed from the mortal realm. Gazing up toward the Nine Heavens, I fixed my heart lest it stray. Moved by compassion, it did not forsake me. By special grace, I received a response: the radiant words of cloud and blossom unfolded, jade tones shimmering forth. Gentle guidance, earnest and sincere, unlocked profound awakening. As I pored over the text, joy surged—my five faculties brimmed with delight.

For years, my aspiration has been steadfast, yet unfulfilled—
*(Two characters missing. Another version reads: "I have attained my aim.")*
Humbled by my seclusion in obscurity, I have only now glimpsed the light of heaven. The numinous perfected—
*(One character missing. Another version reads: "subtle." This may be incorrect.)*
—have entreated me. Their teachings converge, compelling me to restrain myself and amend my faults, to reflect and dissolve my vulgar stagnation. Rising early, I diligently cultivate my heart; in sleep, I awaken to my own nature. Through the accumulation of sincerity, at last I have obtained a faint resonance.

The celestial cycle has now aligned, and the appointed time for audience draws near. *(The character 想 is suspected to be extraneous.)* Truly, *(two characters missing; another version reads "to long for")* my life has been mired in worldly delusion, sunk deep in the dust’s obscurity. Unaware of the loftiest Perfected, I once believed the Dao ended here. I resolved to temper my nature through stillness, to refine *(one character missing; four characters in red ink)* and treasure it. Yet it is not that sensual desires are *(one character missing)* many, but that the difficulty lies in their mastery. *(One character missing.)* As for the warnings of water and fire, the metaphors of ice and burning coals—these now shine forth with luminous clarity. I reverently uphold the pure precepts, striving to diminish and diminish again, *(以 is likely the missing character)* until I reach the state of dead ashes.

I lament my late awakening and regret that my grasp of the pivotal moment comes so near its end. The words on the use of fire—how subtle their meaning! Reflecting on them, drawing parallels, I am filled with remorse and dread. In the past, I relied upon Master Hua, who ever urged and encouraged me, hoping I might achieve something and witness the transformative alchemy. Yet I indulged in the allure of worldly splendor, treading the Dao without steadfastness. I have always wished to share quiet leisure with you, to pour out my heart’s bitterness in full. But our inclinations differ, and intimate counsel is hard to come by. Still, the longing *(two characters missing)* in my heart is to offer my sincerest devotion. I wonder—might there yet be hope for me?

*(The following two letters are replies from the Senior Scribe. The first conveyed the intent of Hua Qiao, seemingly in response to an earlier letter. The latter mentions the words on the use of fire, but the present text is incomplete.)*

Above: Two letters.

In the fifth month, Master Mao the Younger mentioned on a lost day that Hua Qiao had leaked celestial patterns and recklessly expounded on emptiness and nothingness. Now, the Hua family—father and son—are being examined by the Water Bureau. Hua Qiao’s deviation from the Dao stems from Hua Qi’s flattery and disorder, which corrupted his resolve. As for Hua Tuan and Hua Xigu, the Three Bureaus used them for testing, but the trial proved unsuccessful. Thus, Qiao was sentenced to death, his name promptly stricken from the registers and his scalp delivered to the Water Bureau. Secretly inquire whether anyone in his household bears this name—and if so, who it is. *(The preceding all responds to inquiries about the Hua family’s affairs, though it remains unclear whether this refers to the Year of the Ox or the Year of the Rat.)*

Xu Chao brutally murdered Zhang Huan, the Merit Officer of Xinye Commandery, and wrongfully demanded a dragon-horse. These men all await opportune moments to act, having already reported to the Water Bureau. The Bureau has pressured Xu Dou to restore his ancestral tombs and observe his household, seeking the son of the declining line to appease the accusers. The appointed day of his death falls on the lost day of the lost month—by then, his retribution shall arrive. You, having received your mandate, should be capable of subduing ten thousand ghosts and restraining a thousand spirits. I wish to witness your use of hands in this. To ensure no further harm, you ought to visit Dou’s grave on this day, rebuke and summon Huan and the others, and command the Left Bureau to seek a replacement for examination, thereby eradicating the source of contention. Do not disclose this. Instead, claim that I have lent you my authority. I wonder if you might attempt to subdue and eliminate them. The traces of ghost-destruction—such matters are *{momentarily sufficient}* to meet the need.

Written on the night of the sixteenth day of the sixth month, this letter was entrusted by the Younger Master.

*(This instructs Yang to suppress the tomb litigation for the Senior Scribe’s family. Xu Chao, formerly of Nanyang Commandery, had the power to kill a man of Xinye. These three cases do not appear in Zhou Fang’s interrogations of the Master—likely the forty-third entry in the records. Though Dou ranks among the immortals, he remains pressured by the Water Bureau due to lingering baleful qi from mourning.)*

Three hundred sheets of paper. *(In recompense to Ghost Marshal Wang Yan, who recently reported in writing on the gathering of the Pestle Sect, proving his merit.)*

Three *dou* of oil. *(In recompense to Ghost Marshal Fu Huang, who recently assisted the Merit Officer’s emissary in manifesting his majesty to illuminate ghostly forms, preventing their violence.)*

Thirty feet of green silk. *(In recompense to Ghost Marshal Fan Jiang, who recently captured and executed a hundred evils, dispelling litigation and dispersing calamity, proving his merit.)*

Three silver forks. *(In recompense to Ghost Marshal Liang Wei, who recently protected the ailing, summoned souls, and settled spirits, preventing tomb litigation—rendering exceptional merit.)*

The above four offerings were deceptive pledges. On the thirteenth day of the sixth month, the Lesser Mao Lord temporarily assumed the form of a jade axe and appeared in a dream to Hu Ya, commanding the couple to clearly deliver these four kinds of offerings in recompense to the four marshals who had captured ghosts. Why do you no longer recall this so that I may ask you further? *(My question means: "Let us both be attentive." It was not that these four ghost marshals were originally Daoist libationers, who, having mastered the arts of release from below, received scriptures and became ghost marshals.)*

Since there were recompenses in the form of deceptive pledges, they would long serve one’s own purposes with diligence. The reason for appearing in a dream in the form of a jade axe was that Hu Ya’s hun and po had yet to connect with the perfected immortals. The jade axe is pure, bright, and cleansing; after prolonged fasting and purification of the po, the heart draws near to the immortals, thus borrowing its image to communicate through dreams. Though the dream was communicated, if one still fails to awaken, it may be said that faith is not sincere. Or if one awakens yet forgets the pledges, it may be said that sincerity lacks reflection.

As for deceptive vows, all receive their mandates in secret exchange—one must be cautious not to let others know. The outer scripture says: *"I have heard the mandate; it cannot be shown to others."* *(The "Yang Zhi Shui" chapter of the Mao Odes states: "I have heard the mandate; it cannot be told to others." This refers precisely to such matters.)*

The night of the thirtieth day of the sixth month.

The Lesser Lord bestowed the scripture, revealing it in utmost secrecy. The True Controller of Merit states: *"Those who exert strength over the hundred ghosts or command generals to harness celestial might must employ such deceptive vows."* The civil officials of the underworld govern the texts of the Dao for those who dissolve their bonds below; the martial generals of the underworld govern the martial texts for those who dissolve their bonds below. Civil dissolutions advance once every one hundred forty years; martial dissolutions advance once every two hundred eighty years. Martial dissolution is the lowest rank of dissolution.

Those whose hearts stir with worldly desires, who indulge in impurity while feigning clarity, who adorn their eyes to follow the world yet fear death and seek immortality—these are mostly bound to martial dissolutions. *(This is the category of martial dissolutions; many in the world resemble this.)* They should take the Five Beverage Pellets to dispel the qi of water retention. Prepare them swiftly—not merely to cure illness, but also to preserve a fair complexion and radiant qi.

On the night of the twenty-third day of the sixth month, the Lady of South Mountain Peak delivered this message. *(The Chief Archivist has long suffered from thin rheum; recently, his illness flared, hence this revelation. The Five Beverage Pellets are those known in the world.)* When the Five Beverage Pellets are refined with precision, great efficacy will follow. For now, one may rest and recover.

(Range Anyuan’s words:) Tell Chief Archivist Xu not to worry—there is no need for such deception. He may return it. *(The Lord of Life-Preservation to Marquis Xu: “Do not fret. The deception of the heir-physician—let it be returned.”)*

The Lady of the Right and Right Radiance spoke: The Young Lord said, “We two are officers and soldiers. It is fitting to offer deceptive apologies. Present the secret treasures from your person. If you do not, this junior will later refuse to exert himself for you.” (This refers to Xu Hou and his ilk.) What Xu {Quantu} relies upon is his younger brother; what Xu Ya relies upon is his father. Good deeds must not be forgotten; evil deeds must not be forgotten. Moreover, these are secret treasures—keys to penetrating the subtle. Silver, too, is a secondary treasure. Now, the more heartfelt the sentiment, the more refined it becomes. Do not repeat this often, lest it weary and deplete his spirit.

*(The Young Lord of Lesser Mao Peak {orally transmitted}:)* The lowly Xu was the daughter of Daishi Zi. She was slain by her enemy, the Xue family, along with others. The Xue also killed Xu’s embrace of her infant, Aning. Xu now resides in the Water Bureau, accompanied by her child. Her bones drift and float, and her case is also under litigation in the Three Bureaus, seeking to confront and examine the living. When Aning was killed, his head bore a sore that spread across his face and eyes. *(Upon consulting the register, Daijian is not recorded—likely because women’s names were not publicly noted.)*

On the night of the second day of the seventh month, the Young Lord transmitted this text. That same evening, the Chief Scribe also received [a dream]. (This is the character for "dream." True texts often appear thus.)

The pivot of life and death, the convergence of gain and loss—how much more pressing this becomes. Why not dwell afar in the profound savor of the Dao, delighting in emptiness and transcending the mundane? To seek near-term responses is to invite ever more [delay]. This, too, is a path leading to loss.

May you embody lofty refinement and not pursue matters down to the finest detail.

On the night of the twenty-sixth day of the seventh month, the Lady of the Purple Tenuity transmitted this composition, instructing that it be given to Chief Scribe Xu.

*(From the Young Lord of Lesser Mao Peak, a total of eighteen slips in Yang script.)*

At midday on the twenty-seventh day of the seventh month, Hua Hou, Registrar Xu, was to enter meditation. At that time, nothing further remained—this was merely to guard against what had not yet come to pass.

The reason I could not recently report my grave illness was out of caution regarding the precepts. Moreover, if worldly people knew of the distress of those not yet afflicted, they would surely violate the prohibitions of the Three Bureaus. This would bring further harm to the body, compounding illness and severing recovery.

Why then do you indulge in such excessive worry? Which is dearer, name or body? This is worth reflection.

Even if one were to substitute the small for the great—what would that avail a father? If both great and small were to come, what would that avail a mother? To weaken one’s own body is to bring litigation upon one’s own family. The Three Bureaus have their own settled affairs; worry [or grief] will not resolve them.

Only those who attain equanimity within and without may escape dread and disquiet.

On the sixth day of this month falls the Red Grandson’s Day of Severance. This was addressed beforehand. Though the matter is now suspended, there is nothing further to fear. Yet the Four Marshals have turned against me, and I am confined within the capital’s禁. On that day, they will dispatch corpse-killing messengers to observe you. Though no further anxiety remains, on that day your parents will enter seclusion. Should illness arise during seclusion, it will bring no suffering. I, too, shall watch over you on that day.

The above three matters were spoken by the Lesser Mao Lord.

*(The following three entries are recorded.)*

On the sixth day of the eighth month, when parents take their infant into seclusion to burn incense, face north, and present their pleas to the Two Lords, you will naturally witness something. What you see will bring no suffering whatsoever. At midday, the one who wore azure robes and patterned trousers the previous day will appear, lingering before the seclusion chamber—this is the Lesser Lord. You may approach and make your entreaties.

At midday on the sixth day of the eighth month, a man wearing a flat-topped cap, with long, flowing beard and mustache, dressed in purple leather trousers and an unlined jacket, will arrive leading a yellow-robed maiden. This man is Wang Yan, Marshal of the Ghostly Hosts. Yan acts for the benefit of others, bringing the maiden to be seen. On that day, the maiden may be bound. The scribe of the floral missive will, on that day, enter the well to assist the master. He will come at midday and depart shortly after.

Therefore, you must vigorously direct the wind toward the grave and present the litigation talisman to repel the onslaught of poisonous qi. Do this three times, and the examiners will conceal themselves.

The scattering of obscuring mists and the deployment of examiners all arise from one’s failure to present offerings. The afflicted, gripped by fear, thus engage in inner warfare of essence and womb. Hence, maladies spread and erupt—not confined to a single body alone. The purpose of submitting the memorial is precisely to curb the expansion and contraction of the tiger’s fangs. Thus has it been expounded in the *Prophecies of Fan Zhonghou*.

(Fan Zhonghou, whose given name is *Jinmao*, is the compiler of the *Southern Perfected Transmission*.)

Xu Hou ought to apologize for his deceit. The clerks and soldiers of the Lady of the Southern Perfected have so reported in the grand memorial.

As for the Lesser Lord: to employ the lesser in place of the greater—what further entreaties are needed? Should we petition the Most High to halt this? As for the Lesser Lord: are the fangs likewise? Hush, hush—you are but a disciple. What concern is Xu’s fang to you? How dare you pursue this further?

The maiden and Hou share a writ of water and fire. I have recently received the command of the Southern Perfected to thoroughly bind and seize them. A mere ghostly underling, ungovernable—what cause for worry? This, too, is what the Prefectural Secretary Xu has striven to overcome.

As for the Lesser Lord: Xu Hou is reproached by his father-in-law, and the reproach is severe. Yet he does not take heed of family matters or the comings and goings of guests. Such negligence is indeed deserving of censure. Why must this recur? This is the fault of the household elder’s strictures, not my doing. A solemn apology to the Dipper will surely bring release.

Marshal Fan, in recent times, has grown weary of deceit and wishes no longer to involve himself in these affairs. I did not permit it. Now, there is no need for action. The deceitful must be swiftly healed and sent away. The Dipper, ever thirsty, drinks what must not be drunk; overfed, it grows sluggish and consumes what must not be eaten. The daughter-in-law is inattentive—yet she must not be left uninformed.

Is this the reproach of a deceased father-in-law? Thus, one ought to be diligent in household affairs. Yet this persists without end. Might it lead to receiving instruction beneath the father-in-law’s roof? On the night of the second day of the eighth month, the Lesser Lord penned this letter and had it delivered to Master Fu. *(Right: ten items in Yang’s hand.)*

The messenger sent by Marshal Xu stole six dogs from another household and steamed them—*with fire*—beneath the marshal’s hearth to eat together. Why did the marshal not inspect this? The foul, rank qi tainted the meats and rice. Having eaten, they walked upon the road—this has already offended the Star of the Perfected One. There was a white dog; the common folk had prayed to the local earth deities with offerings to Xu. How could they have someone steal and boil it? The earth deity declared that Marshal Xu ordered this, not that petty men stole of their own accord. Secretly investigate this. You were beneath his roof and did not perceive it—how could such a thing occur? This is sufficient to be considered an illness. Be cautious.

On the night of the sixth day of the eighth month, the Lesser Lord of Mao wrote this to Marshal Xu. It is also fitting to address the Lady of the Southern Marchmount with words, entreating that illness be cured. Further, one ought to address the Two Lords of Life-Preservation and the Register of Destinies, with words akin to those for the Lady of the Southern Marchmount. The afflicted should personally petition the Dark Master for relief. Without this, recovery will not come.

Yiqian spoke of this recently on the Dao.

[Separate Review]
(These two characters are written on the back of the title paper)

The four clauses above were written by Yang. The petition of Nansheng Xu Yufu. Yufu, defiled by corpse impurity and tainted flesh, has received the sagely compassion of deliverance. Each edict of admonition bestowed is a profound grace upon his descendants. He constantly looks up in reverence to the numinous beneficence, forever relying on the celestial {vast shade}.

Yufu, with his dull and stubborn inferiority, is by nature difficult to instruct. Though he strives day and night, he remains plagued by transgressions and faults. This night, in a dream, he awakened to this realization. Reflecting on this, all is Yufu’s own culpability. Overwhelmed by shame and dread, he has no ground to stand upon.

The numinous Dao is lofty and empty; a man of flesh cannot yet grasp the true Dharma. He can only hold fast to sincerity and reverence, cultivating the precious mysteries. Yet he fears the numinous decree is too profound, the admonitions too subtle. Yufu, dull and benighted, cannot fully comprehend. Such sins accumulate day by day, and his violations of the precepts and commandments number in the thousands and tens of thousands.

If the Divine Mother, in her benevolence, would deign to pardon and absolve him once more, then today his anxiety and distress would be profound, his liver and gall shattered. He can only {break} his bones in remorse for his faults, though it does nothing to amend past wrongs. To repeatedly submit petitions and cause such trouble is unseemly, and his shame and fear compound.

Yufu pledges his sincerity and begs to swear an oath. From this day forward, he will purify his heart and renew his devotion, making covenant before Heaven and Earth. He will guard his form in stillness and follow the teachings with discipline. He begs for the pardon of his father Mu, his elder brother Huya, and all their transgressions, great and small. If Yufu has failed in his duties, he begs to bear the punishment himself. May all, great and small, be absolved.

If the Divine Mother would show mercy and permit Yufu to reflect on his faults and amend his errors, then his entire household, in perfect unity, would receive her grace. Thus, they would forever behold the Three Luminaries and be granted life anew.

Respectfully submitted.

Here the text records Huya’s illness—this is Yuan’s farewell address to Nan Zhen. (Yuan himself copied this version.)

Huya must be cautious never to tread again in dampness. Even seeing others newly afflicted by dampness startles the Three Origins into mourning. Dampness delights in killing men. On the twenty-fourth day of the eighth month, Nan Zhen declared:

Those who study the Dao often fail to exercise caution in their affairs and thus bring upon themselves a hundred ailments. Yet they blame the gods and spirits. Lying exposed to wind and moisture, they reproach others for failing to shelter them—all fools, unfit to be taught the mysteries.

Preserve life and warn Huya.

*(The following two entries bear Yuan’s hand.)*

Moments later, he intoned to himself:
Morning blossoms gleam by the well at dawn,
Nine canopies tilt against azure clouds.
Before this, jade scepters were commonplace—
I knew not the cedar among ten thousand streams.
Released in an instant from darkness,
Why must the guest of Yin be human?

*(Some say this is a warning—if a warning, then let him change.)*

Later that evening, Ying spoke:
Those who see form cling to their allotted tasks in Confucianism and Mohism.
The sunken wanderer treads the words of mystery and chants of emptiness.
Such men may yet awaken—late or soon.
Do the Xu brothers still have hearts?
I fear only hollow ears.
Let them try once more to comprehend—
beware the subtle path.

On the twenty-eighth day of the ninth month, Mao Baoming declared:
Whether this endeavor succeeds or fails depends entirely on you.
The Register Lord has spoken thus:
To betray inner truth is to break faith with the heart.
The three *hun* lose their authenticity;
once the true is scattered and confused,
the *po* seize the interval.
One who practices the Dao must ensure that inner and outer are as clear as mirrors,
palace and note resonate in harmony,
spirit moves within,
and divinity descends without.
This is no empty claim.
In former days, I too was thus—
all men smelted their hearts.
Now that has ceased.
Those who require smelting and reform remain in the lower ranks.
Hence my earnestness,
my hope that none linger here.
Lately, there has been some progress.

*(These are the words of the Lesser Lord.)*

Worldly affairs are not what I seek;
why heed the turns of fate?
The man of the Dao will naturally be seen.
Do not fret midway.
I wished to inquire of Yang,
and thus came to know—
so I composed this.

(Right: Qingling)
There is an opening for the perverse,
and the perverse leaps through it.
This is not illness.

(Right: An of Nine Flowers)
Thoughts ought not to be many.
When many, the correct scatters.
With the correct scattered, to seek freedom from illness
is like opening the gate to repel a fierce enemy.

(Right: Ziwei)
The condition will naturally improve.
Do not suffer.
Words of the Lord of Life-Preservation:
Why reach the point of a house in mourning?

Words of the Lord of Life-Preservation:
One may ingest talismans and drink water
to be cured at once.
For those unwilling, it shall be granted.

Words of the Lord of the Register of Destiny:
It will soon resolve itself.

Words of the Lord of Life-Preservation:
Much has been said,
and it is as stated.
The audience shall take place on the second day of the *yi* month.
The seventh matter is of no concern.

(This line is Yang’s message to the Chief Clerk.)
A jesting remark—
do not laugh at this, Chief Clerk Xu.
The expanse is unbounded, unconstrained;
high and low lose their proper measure.
Stabilize it without doubt.
If no care is taken,
do not use this alone.
Guard it from others’ eyes.
Guard it from others’ eyes.

One line from the Lord of Life-Preservation to the Chief Clerk:

(Right: Fourteen Written Instructions)
In declining years, the body grows frail,
often assailed by wind and cold.
One must deeply nurture and preserve.
In this tranquil state, free of affairs,
the supreme flavor is the Mysterious Origin;
abide in the Crimson Ford.
The body stills to utmost attainment,
the heart refines through inner contemplation.
Cast off the myriad burdens,
wash away stray thoughts.
Only then does one draw near the gate.

If worries and entanglements are many,
and worldly matters remain unexamined,
even if one rests in the spirit’s emptiness,
dwells in a heart of clarity,
the lingering coils will still not be transcended.

Tian Yu of Yuyang said:
A man who, like a worn wheel in old age,
is like a bell tolling the end of the night-watch
yet still travels without rest,
is a criminal.
This analogy applies to the old who indulge in desires,
rushing about in trivial pursuits,
keeping company with the young.
If one can now vow never to act thus again,
then a swift recovery will follow.
If not, it shall be otherwise.

Then can the ailment and the stages of age
be deliberated together with the heart?

(Tian Yu, styled Guorang, was a native of Yongnu in Yuyang. A man of resourcefulness and strategy, he served as Inspector of Bingzhou and later as Minister of the Guards. In his old age, he sought to resign his post and wrote to Sima Xuanwang: "To remain in office past seventy is like a bell tolling the end of the night-watch yet still traveling without rest—such a one is a criminal." He died at eighty-two, citing these words to urge his chief clerk to resign his position.)

Take one of the four medicinal pills each day. Conduct yourself with care in all matters—this has already been repeatedly stated.

The above three entries are from Yang’s writings.

For those troubled by nightmares, at dawn the next day one should invoke the Most High, first to rectify the hun and po, and second to dispel inauspicious omens.

Households that uphold the Dao should meticulously maintain a tranquil abode.

The above two entries.

According to ritual, at seventy one should "hang up the carriage." "Hanging up the carriage" means that as the years wane like the sun nearing Yuanyuan, the body’s qi declines, and the spirit’s vitality begins to fade. One can no longer exhaust the physical form in the dust of the world or entangle the heart-mind with external things.

Now that Attendant Xu has reached this stage, may he at last withdraw into reclusion? Returning to the Hall of Huayang to cultivate the arts of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi—while the summons from the Northern River still flies its banner and the honors of promotion remain resplendent—would this not be most joyous?

At present, this illness is on the mend. There is no cause for concern. Nevertheless, be cautious in engaging with the tumult of affairs, navigating the cold and heat of human matters. For now, do not yet stir from your place. If you do, people will say you fear ghosts.

(The "summons from the Northern River" refers to the words of Dou, as recorded in the *Book of Changes*.)

Should there appear hesitation in your resolve—this being the sixth month of the year, when I grew concerned for Attendant Xu’s condition, though it was no grave illness. This year, the ancestral spirits of the Xu clan have stirred in minor disturbances, yet no great suffering has arisen. Both Baoming and Fan Zhonghou have already made their appeals on his behalf, and Youfu of the Right Division has taken it deeply to heart. There is no cause for worry.

The juncture of departure or stay, the matter of life and death—these are secrets guarded by the Three Offices, not to be revealed to outsiders. That I speak of them now is only because you have already embraced the Supreme Dao within your heart-mind. For now, you may retain your official post; it is not yet time to resign. Whether to leave or remain—we shall deliberate further when the moment comes.

This was relayed by Zhonghou of the Right Division last night, and so I write it down to share with you.

When a household encounters illness, death, decline, or misfortune—when strange lights appear, dreams trouble the mind, or wealth dwindles—these may be averted through rites of exorcism. Yet the most urgent matter is to resolve disputes over graves and ancestral curses. If these remain unresolved, calamity will persist without end.

This is the reply from Baoming to Attendant Xu.

*True Instructions, Scroll Seven*
*True Instructions, Scroll Eight*
*Transmission of the Destined Mandate, Fourth Chapter*

The wanderings of spirits and the illusions of the unseen may not be trusted, yet excessive toil, excessive affairs, excessive thoughts, and excessive distractions wear down the spirit, scatter the true self, and disturb the Three Passes, thereby harming one’s luminous virtue. Thus, for those like Master Xu, to contain the inner radiance and restrain the four excesses, to refine emptiness and infuse the numinous—only then may immortality be hoped for. The Register of Fixed Destiny declares:

Purify your bed and its surroundings, keep them clean and serene. Arrange your clothing and bedding with care, assigning a constant attendant. Burn incense regularly, letting its fragrance remain pure and untainted. The Perfected of the Southern Marchmount will often inspect your inner chambers—attend to your daily life accordingly.

Let only grasses and quince be used. Your hands have long suffered from wind affliction—this is why the root of wind-qi has now reached your ears. Many say acupuncture and moxibustion bring excellent benefit, freeing one from worry. (This was spoken by Yi Qian to inform the Chief Clerk. The "grasses and quince" likely refer to the names of attendants responsible for clothing.)

You may summon Huang Min to come forth. Min’s servant wishes to come as well. It is also said that around the end of this month, you will suffer a minor illness. If you summon him here, the ailment will be dispelled. You must urgently prepare a quiet chamber and secure a room for rest. This year, you intend to gather grasses, but you should set them aside for Min’s servant. This year, the grasses themselves face their own life’s tribulation and should not be taken. Have him beg for a talisman to wear for self-protection.

Behind the bed hangs a solitary wind-chime—place a screen to the north of it. (This should read as the character *zhang*, meaning "screen.") Alternatively, position the bed near the northern wall to avoid leaving it exposed. Morning and evening, focus your mind on reverent stillness, mentally performing the Daoist rites. (Since your body is unwell and you cannot bow or rise, maintain this mental practice without fail.)

Fu suffers from cholera. Do not let him eat cold food. This child is not well-suited to staying here, but no other harm will come to him.

The above was relayed by Yi Qian, who came twice in one night, speaking alone at the fourth watch. When he first arrived, he was accompanied by Bao Ming. (This appears to be the instruction given at the county office, ordering the clerk to return to the mountain and send Huang Min home. Yi Qian was the clerk’s mother, who after death entered the Yi Qian Palace and was thus called by that name. The previous appellations were also hers.)

Fu’s pursuit of the Dao is like digging a well. The deeper the well goes, the harder it is to haul the earth out. One must first lay bare one’s heart and rectify one’s conduct—only then can the source of the spring be glimpsed. Someone told this to Zhonghou; let him know of it. (Li Zhonghou, whose given name was Zun, was the author of *The Biographies of the Three Lords of Mao*.)

A person’s study of the Dao is like a journey of ten thousand li. As one approaches each place along the way, cold and heat, good and ill, grasses and trees, waters and soils—nothing will not be encountered and witnessed. This, too, is a trial within it. Recently, I have heard others speak of this, and only now have I come to understand.

That gentleman’s thoughts and longings are many. (The character *qu* should read *ju*.) Can he truly fulfill his distant aspirations? In times past, he often harbored many regrets, only now realizing how difficult it is to become a true person—and that virtue must never be lost.

It is said that those who study the Dao must first remove their faults and reproach themselves for them. Examine this carefully and be diligent.

The above was spoken by Lady Yi Qian.

When the qi of mountains and peaks is disturbed, mighty birds cry out in the forests. When rivers and streams are blocked and stagnant, dragons and serpents grow desolate in the marshes. This is the natural pattern of things.

Thus, when the powerful and flourishing show the slightest sign of decline, it is as if beasts warn of their coming calamity. Subtle omens in the mysterious reckoning, like the cries of heralds, foretell order or chaos. This is the waxing and waning of mountains and rivers—it is not the fortune or misfortune of human affairs.

If tombs cling to hills and mountains, they truly share the same waves as the peaks. Yet should one’s choices and rejections stray from principle, the workings of the myriad winds will differ. Without stillness and compliance, one cannot grasp humility; without empty dwelling, one cannot achieve profound union. Thus, the pivot of life and death—even the wise do not presume to foresee it. Yet if one’s thoughts remain free of deviation, no misfortune or harm will arise.

Fifteenth day of the fifth month.
The above was spoken by Lady Ying in reply to [Kong’s inquiry].

*(Later hands obscurely wrote "Xie An" in place of the name. Kong refers to Kong Mo. It appears to be Kong Yan, his brother. The Prefect’s father had once served as vanguard commander under Yan’s elder cousin Tan during the campaign against Shen Chong. Given this connection, this exchange was possible.)*

Regarding the matter of tigers roaring near graves in recent times—if one has not yet attained harmony of spirit and stillness of form, or bowed to nurture hidden essence, might the source of illness accumulate with the years? For those yet unable to tread the path of *jiaoyu*, why hasten so urgently toward medicine?

Record of the Immortal’s reply to Kong’s request for a prescription:
I trust your elder brother has been buried early. Now that the smoke of the offering has swiftly dispersed, though it does not [impede] the living, it is essential to obtain burial goods to pacify the three springs below, so that the baleful *qi* may dissolve and grow still.

A remedy for the child’s illness will be sent shortly.

Seventeenth night of the fifth month.
Words of the Life-Preserving Immortal Lord.

To the one whose surname may yet be Kong, who inquired of illness and suffering—

*(To the right, nineteen entries from recent years, all bearing the seal of the clerk’s office.)* With reverent heart and fragrant incense, you have turned with feeling toward medicine. Thus there are words of "dissolving and grinding" to cure illness—might you, upon hearing this, seek life’s mandate? *(The Perfected Immortals all call medicine "dissolving and grinding," hence the term "Dissolving and Grinding Sutra." Reciting it may also dissolve illness.)* Yet you rush south while your heart races north—mouth and mind already at odds.

To cast aside the water-chestnut and feast on the bitter herb, to scorn the ninefold elegance of courtly music while delighting in the rustic tunes of the northern wilds—*(From "cast aside the water-chestnut" to this point echoes the parables of the* Baopuzi*)*—I know you lack the discernment to hear the harmony of the true tone. May you ever remain a fortunate man, extending this grace to your mother and servants.

Yet the root of this lies in learning without reflection, digging a well without clearing its depths—merely a minor flaw in one of flesh. Is this not the case? Now the matter is bound by water taboos, though there remains room for appeal. If Prefect Xu can, in stillness, act as one who rescues, then the entire household will be preserved. Is this not also a fitting request for the Master of Ceremonies and Libationer to bestow as a virtuous favor?

Twentieth night of the fifth month.
Written by Youying for the Prefect.

*(Liu Zunzu was skilled in discourse; Yin Hao once praised him to Guangyu Liang. Yet in a later gathering, their discussions diverged entirely.)*

Thus it was named "Master Sheep's Crane." *(Here the name is lost.)* Liu Zun’s heart was ever set on cultivation. Why not have his mother take the *Far-Reaching Aspiration Pill*?

On the seventh night of the seventh month, the Lady of the Purple Tenuity spoke. She was asked whether the prescription could be obtained. After a long while, she answered: "It already exists in the world. Seek it in secret and use it." The Lord of Life Preservation asked the Lady of the Purple Tenuity: "This formula requires bezoar and silver filings—without these, it would be difficult to compound." *(This is the present-day Great Heart-Settling Pill. First, mercury is used to grind the silver filings until they dissolve, which makes the process difficult.)* The Lady of the Purple Tenuity replied: "Simply nod in assent."

*(The above three entries are from Yang’s writings.)*

The Lu brothers, Na and Shi, were pure, sincere, and undivided, possessing the manner of Jiang Bozi. Knowing of their desire for *far-reaching aspiration*, they rejoiced. Their grandfather had accumulated hidden virtue, and blessings were to flow through seven generations. It was known that Lu Gouzi had vowed to undertake a year of ascetic fasting in order to receive the scriptures. *(The words "you yourself" were later added by someone—spurious.)* "Consider it further." Liu Zun had a good heart and early self-awareness.

The Lord of Life Preservation answered Prefect Xu. *(Lu Na’s elder brother was named Shi, and both were of virtuous conduct. Their grandfather was named Ying.)*

Lu served as Prefect of Danyang Commandery in the state of Wu. *Gouzi* was likely his childhood name, though his full identity remains unclear. Na later became President of the Imperial Secretariat and died in the twentieth year of the *Taiyuan* era.

Yu Zhao handled affairs for his *nephew* (these two characters were later altered to "ancestor" by someone—though the original remains discernible). Entangled in official documents, he was arrested and detained, the case dragging on without resolution, nearly beyond all reason. Fortunately, he had accumulated some merit and virtue in his lifetime, and his family possessed strong blessings. Petitions piled up, all reaching the throne, and the matter was eventually dismissed. Soon after, he was pardoned and sent home. Though his illness was severe, he felt no distress. Lord Xu was deeply concerned for him and secretly conveyed his support.

The Lord of Life Preservation answered Prefect Xu:

Guangyu Daoji, holding high office in the world of light, failed to align with Heaven in applying the law. Arrogant and reckless, his heart and conduct were at odds. From youth to adulthood, he performed not a single good deed, amassing evil without repentance. His offenses are already recorded. Now, facing the end of his life, he seeks to cultivate virtue to save himself—wasting a thousand gold in vain. Is it not too late?

Guangyu Yuehe, styled Daoji, was the second son of Liang. From childhood, he possessed talent in rhetoric and literary composition. During the Shengping era, he served as Prefect of Danyang, submitting memorials to abolish over sixty corvée labors for the various marquises. At the beginning of the Taihe era, he became Commandant of the Army. Such conduct does not resemble that of an evildoer. Perhaps he heard warnings and cultivated goodness, thus securing his reprieve.

Yet Xiyi Hui’s father, without cause, slaughtered hundreds of people and seized their wealth and treasures. His transgressions were grave and his retribution severe. The aggrieved spirits (originally written as *yuan*, meaning "resentment") constantly lodged complaints, and the celestial bureaus had long since settled the case. By law, Hui’s entire family should have been exterminated. However, since his cultivation of virtue was profound, he alone escaped. How could his descendants be spared? Hui may preserve his natural lifespan, but the affairs of the immortal Dao are far beyond his reach.

The Perfected One of the Great Primordial answered Prefect Xu:

Xiyi Hui’s father, Jian, was frugal and upright, with noble aspirations. He should not have committed such killings and plunder. Perhaps this occurred when he first crossed the river...

This was likely the result of his entanglement in the chaos of war. Otherwise, it must have been during his time in the capital when he killed bandits indiscriminately. Jian lived to over seventy before passing away, having attained an official position.

[The following four clauses, transcribed by hand, were brought by Lu Na from the right:]

Balanced and serene in substance, profound and wondrous in spirit,
His divine craftsmanship surpasses all bounds—
He dwells in truth and nurtures life.
In the Great Mystery, he plants his bamboo slips;
In the Great Simplicity, his name is inscribed.
Within, his radiance illumines the Golden Court;
Without, his luster polishes the Jade Splendor.
With crimson chariots drawn by four steeds, he summons the host of spirits,
Galloping through the Dark Realm of the Dragon, soaring on clouds through the ethereal void.
Surely he shall befriend the lords of the Leisure Terrace above,
And oversee the governance of the Great Purity below.

On the night of the seventeenth day of the eighth month, the Lady Wang of the Purple Tenuity bestowed this teaching, instructing Prefect Xu to convey it to Hui.

Hui, your brilliance shines afar, your hidden resonance congeals in mystery.
The lofty melody ascends, yet you linger in dreams, fleeing from truth.
Gaze upward to soar through the clouds and mist,
Look downward to scatter the roots of spirit.
With flying steps, you traverse the Four Awakenings;
Inwardly, you contemplate the Seven Conditions—
Then you shall surely receive the vermilion script and elegant bamboo slips of the Emperor’s chamber,
Your dark voice resounding in eight directions,
Your body dwelling upon the Five Peaks.
Thus, bathe the embryo and pay homage to the Origin,
Exhale and inhale the Six Fluids,
Move with ease through the Three Paths,
And instruct this luminous blessing.
Above, you may ride the empty vastness of the chariot of space;
Below, you may reverse the blossoming of youth and transform blackened hair.
Yet if your form is ensnared by the net of glory,
If you churn the wheels in the garden of splendor,
If you ride the waves to chase after things,
If your jade hairpin chimes in the wind and dust of the world—
Outwardly bearing the name of one who seeks the Dao,
Inwardly beset by a hundred worries—
You will merely exhaust your heavenly years in restless thought,
And in the end, return your bones to the Three Bureaus.
If your fasting is not wholehearted,
Your awakening will be in vain.
Can this be called a mind that does not exhaust its words?

Now, to practice truth:
One must exhale the Three and inhale the Four,
Ride the Seven and swallow the Nine.
Yet now, your breath is insufficient,
Your steps disordered.
The spirit leaks, flowing back to its source;
Essence scatters, the embryo disturbed.
Though you may fast for days,
You know not the Dao, and your years are lost.
Do you wish to ascend this crossing?
You have not yet grasped it.

Those who seek long life are many as the ford’s waters;
Those who tread the path of the numinous are countless as the sands.
Why must you use an ice vessel to hold fire,
Or hasten to repay debts to the Three Bureaus?

The words of the Central Lord, conveyed by Prefect Xu to Hui.

The Lady of the Purple Tenuity says: If Hui attains the Dao, he shall become a supervisor of the Great Purity. Should he heed the essential teachings and practice diligently, he may reach this rank. But if he is not single-minded and steadfast, if he clings to delusion and strays from the Dao’s methods, abandoning them outwardly—then all will be lost.

[The preceding words of the Purple Tenuity differ markedly from those of the Great Primordial, as seen in what follows.]

Is it that Hui’s delusion prevents him from diligently pursuing the essential Dao? This is why the Director of Destinies reveals his ultimate fate. Only when no other thoughts remain does the Dao draw near. Is Hui’s turning still insufficient to discuss the supreme Dao?

(The Lesser Lord says:) Hui’s paternal grandmother, Ding Shuying, possessed the hidden virtue of aiding the destitute. When she encountered Zhao Fu’s calamity, she did not speak of it. Her inner compassion arose from within, and the numinous responded to her humanity—thus her blessings extended to Hui, inclining him toward the love of immortality. Her tomb lies in Dongping. Shuying now serves as a Consort of the Vermilion Mound, often wandering the Three Peaks, where the Director of Destinies has also permitted her to attend to governance.

(These two were likely Hui’s great-grandparents, though external records do not make this clear.)

Hui’s forebears, Qu and Xue Chunhua, devoted their hearts to the ancestral lineage, never concerning themselves with worldly affairs. Yet litigants were many, and they could not restrain them all. Whenever they saw those who came to remonstrate or lodge grievances, they treated them with utmost care. Should seasonal rites be offered to them? These two are the protective spirits of Hui’s household.

(External records also lack mention of these two; their relation to Hui is unknown.)

Hui’s elder brother, Xiong, and his wife, the daughter of Yan Qu, remain bound to the labor of carrying stones—a debt unfulfilled to this day. They delight in assaulting the ancestral lineage.

Their hearts ever bent on slaughter and annihilation—these two are the calamity-spirits of Hui’s household. Hui (this name cannot be deciphered beyond this) and Yin Wuji were subjected to judicial torture for burning and killing Zhu She and Li Jian, thereby inviting disaster. They left no descendants, yet still sought substitutes to suffer in their stead. They further conspired with Gao Feng, vigorously aiding the litigation of Matou against Shi Gong. No favorable outcome could be secured, and the matter seemed boundless. Why, then, did they persist in deceiving their wives? Though they came with earnest pleas, one might attempt to suppress and rectify their ways—but none of these individuals exist in the records.

The following is the response of the Protector of Life to Prefect Xu:
The Lady Minor reports: Hui Jian now resides in the Three Offices, sued by Liu Jijiang over a dispute concerning the Three Virtues. Zhou Matou, in the Water Office, litigates against her son-in-law, presenting a most grievous argument. Hui Lang and Yi Xiang—these two are now sought by the girl Ya Nuqi. Marshal Fan recently received the complaint of Jiang Luo. Hui Xiang is presently detained by the clerks of the Grand Court; his wife, Xing Yingtao, remains entangled in the case, still seeking a substitute from this household.

[The preceding was merely an informal hearing of the Lady Minor’s words and was not to be recorded. It is noted here only for personal reference.]

(These twenty-two characters are Yang Jun’s personal note to the Prefect:) In his advanced years, Gao Ling has reverted to childlike frailty. The Dao and qi no longer intermingle; his spirit lacks a guiding presence. Thus, a host of malevolent forces assail his essence, hastening calamity. Why not embrace the principle of *shengsheng* (generative vitality)? For me, aiding him holds karmic affinity. His wife, too, speaks urgently of household matters—these must be resolved, for no outsider may intervene.

Now, the Six Heavens’ tyranny runs rampant, while the Great Peace’s influence wanes. Spirits insufficient to aid the upright instead summon a throng of {treacherous women}. Thus, divine radiance scatters, and evil usurps the rightful path. Gao Ling’s lack of virtue has long been evident; the entanglements of ghostly litigation have piled high.

Prefect Xu—(Huang Shi writes this as “clerk”)—do you intend to set this aright? If you promptly submit to Master Zhang Hui’s teachings, I shall grant you a talisman to ingest. Thus, he will surely recover and {harm be dispelled}. Otherwise, proceed to the Water Office.

Alas, what sorrow! (Zhang Hui is the name of the Celestial Master; Yang does not wish to write it explicitly.) When pernicious qi invades the body and ghosts congest the chest, how can one turn from delusion to discern the true and false? How then could one uphold the Orthodox Unity amid the qi of equilibrium? In such a case, my method for treating illness may well prove unattainable. (The Orthodox Unity and qi of equilibrium refer to the transformation wrought by the Celestial Master’s libationers.)

If he departs, his son will also leave—why must this one life pass forever? On the night of the nineteenth day of the eighth month, the Lord of Life Preservation secretly addressed Prefect Xu: the litigation over the tomb grows ever more severe, and I fear it will not cease. Ling once defaced the inscriptions on ancient steles to aggrandize himself, deceiving spirits and gods. He {obscured} virtue to exalt himself, and thus the plaintiffs have multiplied their grievances, placing this matter first.

On the night of the twenty-fourth day of the eighth month, the Lord of Life Preservation spoke again: he wished to appoint Xie Feng to the post of Gentleman of the Expectant Gate, but another has already been assigned to the position. The Northern Emperor has temporarily suspended the appointment. Recently, Wang Yunzhi was appointed to act in the role and has since been replaced. If Feng ingests the *Atractylodes* wine, he need not yet comply with the summons. Gao Qi may also take *Atractylodes*. The litigation over his family’s tomb has likewise become chaotic. *Atractylodes* repels ghosts, so there will surely be no further trouble. Fan the Marquis of the Interior spoke of this. (Xie Feng, styled Hongdao, was a native of Kuaiji who rose to become Prefect of Wu Commandery, Governor of Danyang, and Minister of Personnel.)

Wang Yunzhi, of the same clan as his cousin Wang Shu’s son, was a man of wisdom and administrative ability. He served as General of the Central Army of Henan and Governor of Jiangzhou, later promoted to Defender-in-Chief and Governor of Kuaiji, enfeoffed as Marquis of Panyu. He died at the age of forty and was posthumously honored as the Loyal Marquis. Gao Qi was also known as Gao Ling.

(Note on *Gentleman of the Expectant Gate*: a central court office, though this title does not appear in the records—only *Gentleman of the Xiumen* is attested.) Fourteen entries have been transcribed from Pingning’s compilation.

To observe things and assign them their proper roles, to inwardly align with the luminous spirits, to entrust one’s nature and life to the lofty perfected, and to commit one’s form and *qi* to divine oversight—this is how one strengthens the far-reaching and preserves the authentic for an autumn’s span. Now that the master of virtue has settled his spirit upon trust, clarified his mind in serene stillness, and embraced reverence with perfect compliance, he is surely favored by Heaven. Yet his descendants are few, and some may yet fade away—perhaps the numinous gateway has lost its weft, and the hidden mechanism remains unsettled.

In this era, the five *qi* radiate their brilliance, ever illuminating the chambers of Wen Chang. The Three Stars weave their splendor, each time adorning the *jade pivot* within. Thus the mysterious moisture nurtures the embryo, and its roots flow forth. According to the *Inner Records of the Nine Unions*, the bamboo is the essence of the Northern Mechanism, receiving *qi* from the constellation of the Dark Chariot. Its hollow roundness and inner purity, its layered yin enfolding whiteness—all arise from deep roots spreading their fruit, binding abundance in profusion.

Command the lord to plant bamboo beyond the northern chamber of the inner residence, that the fair ones may wander beneath it. Thus shall Heaven stir the numinous mechanism, and descendants greatly increase. Pregnancies shall be preserved whole, and births shall be long-lived. The subtle and manifest shall flourish, ever guarding propitious purity. This is the secret rule of the profound ones, and those who practice it find it most efficacious.

On the twenty-third day of the sixth month, the Lady of the Mid-Candidate addressed the lord:
*(Emperor Xiaowu was born in a renxu year—this should correspond to a xinyou year. Later, it is said: "The seat of the Grand Minister stirs," and thereafter he ascends the throne, which would be in a houwu or wei year. This discrepancy is vast.)*

The numinous herb and the *Guangyin* esoteric method, gazing upward, receive the essence of the revolving luminaries. *(The characters "洗洗" appear to be a marginal note beside "竹," likely a variant of "言先," echoing the meaning of the *Mao Odes* line: "The locusts’ wings flutter—may your sons and grandsons be as numerous.")* Flourishing and luxuriant, its sprouts abound; layered virtue must surely prosper. Composed by the Lady of Purple Tenuity: "Fortune and Harmony shall bear two sons, their abundant virtue destined for the age." *(Fortune and Harmony seems to have been the humble childhood name of Lady Li; in the *Book of Jin*, she is called Junrong. The two sons are Emperor Xiaowu and his younger brother, Daoji.)*

That same night, the Mid-Candidate spoke again:
*(The following three entries are in Yang’s hand, later transcribed by an aide.)*

"Since the virtuous artisan has already concentrated his spirit and the profound model descends from Heaven, matters of safety or peril ought not yet be inquired into. The lord’s devotion is most earnest; that I have not yet reciprocated is because foreknowledge of events is difficult to discuss."

Of late, the celestial *qi* has surged wildly, and the shadows of yin have shifted repeatedly. Venus has sundered its form between the two celestial markers, its turbulence seething beneath the Purple Chamber. The king detests this; the Son of Heaven has cause for concern. The seat of the Grand Minister stirs. Now I offer a cryptic verse in secret counsel:
*(The following passage and the cryptogram were transcribed by an aide, appended to his own record of revisions on the same sheet. The original text is woven in a circular pattern, making it difficult to decipher. Here, the lines are extracted and arranged sequentially, though the transmission was likely oral, with Yang later altering the interpretation. All pertain to affairs of the Jin dynasty, with clear corroboration.)*

---
*Is deception the subtle essence of the Way?*
*Must I reveal Heaven’s secrets?*
*There is an art of ten thousand gold measures—*
*Seek a master swiftly to expel the rebellious and evil child.*
*From this, govern the barbarians and pacify the age;*
*Heaven’s mandate rides forth, the treasure borne aloft.*
*O wondrous dragon, how sorrowful!*
*Between the ages, one must ponder what is fitting.*
*The divine radiance reverses its course;*
*The reckoning lies here—whether there is or is not, none can say.*
*Who shall quell the surging armies and stabilize the imperial house?*
*Be cautious of the earth, for the royal chamber draws near.*
*The elder son shall perish first, his metal flawed;*
*Fivefold chaos arises, virtue in disarray.*
*The fire’s number misses its time; the throne shall shift—*
*From afar, omens of misfortune appear, the artisan’s design unravels.*
*Threefold the rule, yet the artisan’s footing is unsure;*
*No need to fear distant perils—pluck them out, secure the fief.*
*Inch by inch, its depths are unfathomable;*
*Liu Xiang alone discerns the signs of what is to come.*

*Three and five, the auspicious portents of Heaven’s era—*
*Prosperity and change alternate, cycling in succession.*
*Essence, qi, and spirit—subtle and wondrous, they join the Two Modes.*
*Beware of transmitting them to the common or the wise;*
*To the worthy alone should they be bestowed.*

*Seal Heaven, govern Earth—nothing is amiss.*
*If you, child, can treasure and conceal it, Heaven shall know.*
*Is the essence of my Way subtle and wondrous? How could it deceive?*
*There is a marvelous divine plan for war’s onset.*
*Fire, inch, three, five—the auspices of Heaven’s era.*
*Prosperous ages alternate, cycling in succession.*
*The luminous artisan guards virtue—beware of reckless thought.*
*Drive out evil, expel rebellion—swiftly seek a master.*
*The art of ten thousand gold measures, the dragon’s radiance.*
*The reckoning surges; the third son’s loss is fated.*
*None can fathom its source—Liu Xiang alone knows.*
*With misfortune plucked out and disorder quelled, the imperial foundation is restored.*
*Riding Heaven’s mandate, the age is pacified and leveled.*
*Who shall curb the rebellious? Who shall fix the appointed time?*
*The artisan’s resources are insufficient—fear not distant peril.*
*Within five generations, truly, sorrow abounds.*
*Alas! The reckoning is set; the imperial seat falters.*
*Soon, strange omens shall arise in profusion, the golden chamber at hand.*

Place a musk sachet at your neck,
to ward off the onrush of water’s curse.
Nightmares shall cease utterly.
Ever guard the Three Passes—this is most excellent.

The Right Radiance spoke to the Duke.
(Wherever "Duke" appears, it refers to when Emperor Jianwen served as Prime Minister.)

The following is a passage from Yang’s writings,
(the five characters written in red):

The Perfected of the Great Prime spoke to Director Xu:
(What follows is not the true teaching.)

I once met Le Zichang of Nanyang,
a man of unadulterated simplicity.
He neither sought a master nor received instruction,
but followed Heaven and entrusted himself to fate.
He knew nothing of the methods for cultivating life,
yet his conduct never transgressed evil.
His virtue harmonized with the natural,
and though he did not attain longevity,
at death he ascended to the Hall of Blessings.
Refining his spirit and receiving qi,
his name was recorded in the Emperor’s register.
Thus he was appointed Attendant of the Gate of Cultivation,
ranked just below the immortals.
This was due to his inborn destiny,
as well as the accumulated virtue of his ancestors,
whose flowing blessings had shaped him.

Had he but roughly understood the principles of nurturing life
and received even a single word of the Supreme’s secret,
then in seeking the Dao,
nothing would have eluded him.

The reason people do not fulfill their allotted years,
but often meet untimely ends in the world,
is that some encounter the calamities of war,
cutting short their glory and emolument,
or leaving their descendants without posterity.

Those who aspire to the Dao,
though they harbor lifelong devotion
and ceaselessly seek with diligence,
may still encounter true scriptures,
delving into the profound and subtle mysteries.
Many admire such pursuits,
