Those of volatile and violent temperament are thieves and maladies to the whole body, yet also the sturdy ladder for seeking the Dao. Those who abandon such ways draw near to the true; those who reform them invite the Dao to come. In every matter and its kind, one must embody gentleness and slowness, exhausting the principles of purity and refinement—thus does one approach the Dao. (This passage seems addressed to Attendant Yuan.)

The Lesser Mao Lord said: Cinnabar, realgar, and orpiment—every household possesses them, yet not a single person compounds the elixir. All are like the imperial seal, passed from father to son, from son to grandson. Those who love the Dao but lack devotion, whose will is weary and unsteady, whose hearts are clogged with vain reputations and whose eyes are dazzled by superficial allure—ten thousand times ten thousand are like this. Such ways only exhaust the governance of my Three Offices. (This passage seems addressed to Lord Ya.)

Let Xu Fu bathe repeatedly to cleanse the qi of water-borne afflictions and dissolve the accumulated infirmities of long toil—this too is a step toward attaining the true.

The Perfected One of Purple Yang stated: Infrequent bathing is the nature of the *po*. To defy the *po* and return to the true is to refine away one’s turbid filth until it vanishes of itself.

The Lady of Purple Tenuity said: This is the method of the Supreme Dao.

Let garments and headscarves not be lent to others, nor shared with those who use different utensils, carriages, robes, beds, or bedding. This is to block the pathways of filth and grime and to guard against the comings and goings of perverse winds. Such practices are exceedingly easy to follow, yet they are deemed difficult—this is because one’s faith in the Dao is not steadfast, and those who seek to ascend instead sink deeper. Why speak of what the heart encounters? They will naturally come to know their own conduct. *(These three precepts are addressed to the Chief Clerk.)*

The Lady of Southern Marchmount said: *(The above sixteen precepts are all from Yang’s writings, with additional miscellaneous copies by clerks.)*

When one lies upon a bed, it should be elevated, for if it is high, earthly qi will not reach it, and demonic breaths will not assail. Demons that invade a person always rise upward from the ground. *(High means three feet or more.)*

When one rests in a chamber, it should be kept pure and full. If it is full, it will receive numinous qi; if not, it will receive stale qi. Thus, when qi disrupts a chamber, undertakings will not succeed, and endeavors will not stand. The same applies to the body. One must frequently bathe and cleanse; otherwise, there is no hope. *(The character "full" here carries the meaning of "pure." In the Central Kingdom, there was originally no character for "pure," so "full" was used instead. This is consistent throughout the scriptures.)*

Do not speak of the Dao while studying the Dao. To speak of the Dao while studying it invites demonic interference, prevents attainment, and causes illness—this is because the body has not yet become true. *(The Azure Lad also said: Every word, every deed...)*

When qi leaks and diminishes from the {bamboo instrument}, one must be cautious of this. These three principles originally appear in the {Palace Records, Yang’s Writings, and Yuan’s Writings}. The *Highest Scripture of the Great Cavern: Inner Chapters for Suppressing Demons, Supreme Incantation of the Upper Method* states:

Whenever traveling treacherous paths or passing between demonic shrines—wherever the mind harbors doubts, difficulties, or subtle taboos—one must first command all that is traversed. Begin by curling the tongue inward and swallowing saliva three times. Then, with the second and third fingers of the left hand, press thirty-six times at the root of the philtrum beneath the nostrils, at the inner edge of the nasal septum. Press swiftly without lifting the fingers to count. The edge of the nasal septum is called the *Mountain Source*; it is also named the *Demon Well*, the *Divine Pool*, the *Root of Evil*, and the *Terrace of the Hun-soul*. After pressing, knock the teeth together seven times. Then advance the palm to cover the nose, close the eyes, and softly recite the incantation.

The Vermilion Bird soars through heaven,
divine might unfurls within.
The Mountain Source seals the four quarters,
the Demon Well flees in rout.
The Divine Pool exhales qi,
the Root of Evil cowers in hiding.
The Terrace of the Hun-soul shines on all sides,
jade chambers resound with tinkling jade.
The Jade Perfected stands majestic,
enthroned to guard the Hall of Light.
Hands radiate purple auroras,
the head crowns itself with sacred luminance.
Grasping and chanting the thirty-nine chapters of the *Cavern Sutra*,
within them are demon-expelling dragons and tigers,
mountain-severing blades and cliff-cleaving axes,
fierce beasts and charging oxen,
swords in jaws and {golden armor} devoured.
Uprooting mountains and seizing the sky,
divine birds and venomous dragons,
six-headed fire-spewing beasts,
devourers of demon kings,
lightning boars and thunder lords,
comets trailing across the heavens,
{stone owls} ablaze with dappled flames,
marching against the wind.
Celestial birds arrayed in formation,
all stand beside me.
Spewing flames a thousand yards,
to dispel all misfortune.
Hosts of spirits open the Way,
sealing off mountain villages.
A thousand gods and a hundred spirits,
all clasp hands and bow in reverence.
Marshals bearing lanterns,
burn incense for me.
Wherever I go or pass,
the myriad gods receive me in homage.

Striking the teeth thrice in succession,
he then opens his eyes,
withdraws his left hand,
and presses upon the Mountain Source—
whereupon the gates of the ghost-[spirit] well seal shut.
His hand brushes the Spirit Pool—
and the roots of evil scatter and disperse.
His hand hovers over the Terrace of Souls—
and Jade Perfected guard the passes.

Thus moved by the numinous root,
heavenly beasts come to stand guard.
A thousand spirits tremble and submit,
none daring to oppose me.
Such is the principle of spontaneity,
causing all to suddenly become thus.

Beneath the nose, the Mountain Source is the martial ford of the body,
the thoroughfare of the true and the perverse.
Those who are not true give rise to evil qi;
those who are true repel the myriad evils.
It lies in my power to direct and gather—
hence the omen of fortune or misfortune.

The Hall of Light, too, is the literary pool of the body,
the dwelling-place of life and death.
Preserve its spirit,
and one may peer into the inner vision.
Abandon its Way,
and one invites decay and putrefaction.
Thus, by my mastery and compliance with its art,
the determination of life, death, regret, and shame is fixed.

The above passage is drawn from the opening chapter of the *Highest Scripture of the Great Cavern*.
(This passage was transmitted by uncertain [oral instruction];
a certain Prefect copied it down.
This scripture has not yet been revealed to the world.)

When traveling by night, one should always strike the teeth—
there is no fixed number of times.
Ghosts and demons invariably fear the sound of striking teeth,
and thus dare not violate a person.
If one further rinses with saliva while reciting incantations, it is even better.

Those in the world who know the names of the gates of the Six Heavens’ Palaces—
no hundred ghosts dare bring harm.
When preparing to sleep...

Always first face north. Recite the invocation thrice, softly. The invocation reads:

"I am a disciple of the Most High,
governing below the Six Heavens.
The palaces of the Six Heavens are my domain—
not only my domain, but the dominion of the Most High.
I know the names of the Six Heavenly Gates,
and thus attain long life.
Should any dare to violate me,
the Most High shall sever your form."

The names of the Six Palaces are:
The first palace is named *Zhoujue Yintian Gong* (Palace of Yin’s Absolute End).
Proceeding eastward in order:
The second palace is named *Tailiang Shizong Tian Gong* (Palace of the Grand Clarity’s Ancestral Affairs).
The third palace is named *Mingchen Naifan Wucheng Tian Gong* (Palace of the Luminous Dawn’s Enduring Martial City).
The fourth palace is named *Tianzhao Zui Tian Gong* (Palace of Serene Illumination’s Transgressions).
The fifth palace is named *Zongling Qifei Tian Gong* (Palace of the Ancestral Numinous Seven Wrongs).
The sixth palace is named *Gansi Lianwan Lü Tian Gong* (Palace of the Daring Controller’s Linked Layers).

Then strike the teeth six times before lying down, to ward off all ghostly and demonic qi.
(This is done thrice in total. These two methods are recorded in the *Capital Register*, now transcribed for transmission.)

The method of the Northern Emperor’s Ghosts:
First strike the teeth thirty-six times, then recite:
"Tianpeng, Tianpeng,
Ninefold Primordial Youth,
Five Ding Generals,
High *Daochang* Northern Lord,
Seven Governors, Eight Numinous Ones,
the Most High expels all baleful forces."

Great-Long-Skulled Mighty Beast,
in hand the Emperor’s Bell.
White Owl of the Three Mornings,
sternly harnessed *{one version: "hoofed"}* Dragon.
Majestic Sword Spirit-King,
severing evil, erasing traces.
Purple qi ascends the heavens,
crimson clouds blaze forth.
Devouring demons, consuming ghosts,
body athwart, drinking the wind.
Azure tongue, green teeth,
Four-Eyed Old Man.
Heavenly Ding Warriors,
mightily suppressing southern inauspiciousness.
Heavenly *{Horse-Fodder}* rages fiercely,
mightily restraining northern blades.
Three hundred thousand soldiers,
guarding my ninefold layers.
Expelling corpses a thousand *li*,
dispelling all misfortune.
Should any petty ghost
dare approach to witness this,
seize the Heavenly Great Axe,
hewing the five forms of ghosts.
Flame Emperor rends their blood,
Northern Dipper burns their bones.
Four Brightnesses shatter their remains,
Heavenly Plan annihilates their kind.
One stroke of the divine blade—
ten thousand ghosts scatter and dissolve.

(End.)

Each four-line verse is followed by a single strike of the teeth as punctuation. Whether in darkest night or brightest day, this incantation may be recited as a constant invocation. Should any ghost be subjected to this incantation thrice, its eyes will fester and rot, and its body will perish at once. These supreme divine invocations are all names of the offices that slay ghosts. The Northern Emperor conceals their Way; should any mortal obtain this method...

To practice this constantly is the Dao of immortality. Men and women, old and young—all may perform it. This is what is called the Northern Emperor’s divine incantation, the ghost’s bane. Should any ghost be subjected to this method thrice, it shall perish of itself. They ever dread to hear these words. When illness strikes, perform this and recover at once. Strike the teeth while gazing inward, visualizing the five viscera. *(One character here—Yang’s edition is damaged beyond recognition; this copy also leaves it blank.)* The five spirits will naturally manifest.

*(This incantation is a secret of the capital; now it is covertly transmitted. It must not be leaked to the uninitiated—only those of the Dao may share its secrecy.)*

The five entries above were written by Yang and copied by this scribe. Yang’s transcription of the Northern Emperor’s incantation was recorded orally, with many *(people altering)* the text. The origin of wind-illness lies in damp burial mounds, yin moisture, and the stagnation of the Three Springs. Thus, the Earth Office stirs water and qi to clash, often producing wind-bi. In severe cases, the entire body becomes unresponsive; in mild cases, half the body or limbs lose function. If one frequently dreams of the northeast or northwest, wanders old dwellings, or sees places where spirit-platforms once stood...

When one’s dwelling nearly adjoins a burial mound—
the northeast of a grave is called *Zhengjueming*,
the northwest is called *Jiuhu Yi*—
these are both inauspicious sites for tomb litigation.
Should one encounter the deceased therein, the omen is all the more certain.
For those who frequently have such dreams:
upon waking, face upward, tap the teeth three times, and recite this incantation:

“Supreme Primordial, Lofty Mysterious,
Nine Capitals’ Purple Heaven,
Order the soul, guard the life,
Exalted Plain Perfected One.
I bear the supreme method,
receive the teaching of the Great Mysterious.
Long life and enduring vision,
spirit soars, body transcends.
Tombs and graves forever at peace,
ghostly litigation and treachery blocked.
Soul and spirit harmonious and joyful,
malign qi dispelled without trace.
Wandering specters and formless apparitions—
dare they offend my spirit?
The Northern Emperor rebukes and restrains,
draws the qi into the abyss.
Attaining the *Zhulu* of the Supreme August,
reverently memorializing Jade Dawn.”

Recite this twice, then tap the teeth three more times,
and one will no longer dream of burial mounds or the ghosts of deceased kin.
This is the Northern Emperor’s secret incantation.
Those of sincere and virtuous intent may all practice it.
For those who frequently suffer inauspicious or ominous dreams,
this method may be applied—
thus ghostly qi is extinguished,
and demonic spirits scatter and dissolve.

*(This should be transmitted only to those under the authority of the Chief Clerk. Only those of the Xu surname and the *yu* tone are permitted. Now it is said that the northeast is *Zhengjueming*—this differs from other accounts. Moreover, the name *Jiuhu Yi* does not appear in the *Tomb Manuals*.)*
For cases where the arms fail to respond...

When qi of deep-wind toxin lingers within the vessels,
binding and clinging to the bones, causing numbness—
acupuncture and moxibustion are fitting.
With acupuncture and moxibustion, the ailment is cured.
Moreover, one should apply the Northern Emperor’s Incantation of the Crooked and the Winding.
If performed a hundred times, the illness will also dissipate.

First, slowly press and massage the arm with one hand.
After a long while, when finished, close the eyes and turn the gaze inward.
Swallow saliva three times, tap the teeth three rounds,
and with a composed mind, softly intone:

“Supreme Four Mysteries,
Five Flowers and Six Courts,
Three Hun and Seven Po,
Celestial Pass and Earthly Essence,
Divine Talismans and Guarding Spirits,
Heavenly Embryo and Radiant Above,
Four Limbs and Hundred Spirits,
Nine Joints and Myriad Numina—
receive the register from Jade Dawn,
inscribe the scripture in Jade City.
Jade maidens attend the body,
Jade lads protect the life.
Forever aligned with the Two Luminaries,
ascend as a flying immortal to the Upper Clarity,
long abiding with the sun and moon,
years extending into the distant future.
Transcend and rise as an immortal,
attain the Great Peace.

Flowing wind congeals into sickness,
injected ghosts take flight in five directions.
Specters and tomb-qi,
yin-qi swirling about—
they encroach upon my four limbs,
disrupt my flourishing and decline.
Supreme Heavenly Ding,
Dragon and Tiger radiate might,
slaying inauspicious ghosts,
shattering wind and demonic influences.
Examine and expose hidden grievances,
concealed poisons and wrongs—
restore me to my ordinary state,
sun and moon shining together.
If examined and found in violation,
the Northern Asterism will seize and crush.
Should any dare to test,
they shall face the majesty of the Radiant Above.”

When finished—
*(This too should be reported to the Chief Clerk. The Chief Clerk suffers from frequent nightmares, constantly afflicted by tomb-injections, and also plagued by drinking accumulations and numbness in both hands. Thus, he is often taught these methods, with acupuncture and moxibustion to follow.)*

If a disciple is sincere,
press and massage the afflicted area,
applying this method.
However, do not require closing the eyes and turning the gaze inward—
only swallowing saliva and tapping the teeth.

In former times, a man named Tang Lan,
dwelling in the forests of Linlü Mountain,
was struck by a ghost,
his entire body unresponsive,
as if wrapped in cotton.
A Daoist taught him this method of pressing and massaging,
and the ailment was immediately dispelled.

This is the Northern Emperor’s Method of the Crooked and the Winding.
For all illnesses involving crookedness or winding,
applying this method is most effective—
not merely for wind-numbness or unresponsiveness.
*(Tang Lan’s origins are unknown; it is unclear from which era he came.)*

The Northern Emperor possesses these several methods, which are also employed among the schools of the lofty immortals. There is also the *Scripture of the Crooked and the Winding*, stored in the residence of the Western Duke of Clarity. (King Wen of Zhou was the Western Duke of Clarity.) Zheng Zizhen was the grandson of Zheng Kangcheng. He now resides on Yangzhuo Mountain. In his early studies, he suffered for years from unresponsiveness in both legs. In his later years, he used acupuncture and moxibustion, combined with the incantatory method of the Crooked and the Winding, and within a hundred days, all ailments were dispelled. (Zheng Xuan had only one son, who was killed by bandits. He had a posthumous son named Xiaotong. This son neither entered the mountains nor suffered from leg ailments. This Zizhen is also not the one from Gu Valley—his comings and goings are contradictory, and no reliable evidence can be discerned.)

Tang Lan now resides on Mount Hua. He obtained the *Method of the Rainbow Elixir* and, having compounded and ingested it, attained immortality. (From the preceding passage to this point, all should be considered *Instructions for Preserving Life*.) Thirteen rounds of acupuncture and three rounds of moxibustion cure all ailments. The left hand surpasses the right. The meridian of the Lesser Yang winds within the left elbow, so thirteen rounds of acupuncture are appropriate to regulate the internal meridian and enter the Lesser Yang. Moxibustion warms the qi, causing it to float upward; when the arm’s blood is afflicted by wind-numbness, three rounds of moxibustion are appropriate to supplement and stabilize the flow of fluids, ensuring the sinews remain unobstructed. Moxibustion should be applied to the *Youguan* of the hand, the *Fengxian*, and the *Five Streams*—these three locations are critically important. One must await an auspicious day.

I now part ways to instruct you separately, ensuring you do not lose this method. The Lady of the Right Central Watchtower once said: *(The three critical locations—Youguan of the hand, Fengxian, and Five Streams—are not recorded in the illustrated charts of acupuncture points or in any moxibustion classics.)* The progression of wind-examination always occurs during moments of weakened qi. When the body is deficient and contracted, illness invades. If you have now recovered, and can truly reduce strenuous activity, the wind-affliction will surely be dispelled. I shall now regulate the qi of the tomb’s inscriptions for you. Since you are slightly improved, you may also ascend the tomb to present your memorial. I will submit it through the proper channels, and thus the inscribed qi will be cut off.

In former times, a man from Dengyi Cloud Mountain had attained the Dao but suddenly refused to transmit his teachings to either hand. I sent someone to instruct him to apply moxibustion to the Fenghuai and Qujin points. Within six or seven days, he could perform the Five Animals’ Frolics. Once the effects of acupuncture are complete, you should focus on the moxibustion sites. Moreover, meditating on the practice of the Dao is no different from physically performing it.

Long ago, Lord Zhao Cheng’s legs were so debilitated he could not rise. Morning and night, he constantly meditated on bowing to the Supreme. After thirty years, a Perfected One of the Supreme bestowed upon him a dose of Flowing Brightness Sandalwood Elixir. Immediately, he could stand and walk, and later attained the Dao.

Here at the foot of Huming Mountain, when engaging in meditative bowing or mentally practicing the Dao, one should burn incense on both sides, as if preparing to perform a ritual. This is called "inwardly refining the Great Mystery," where the mind enacts numinous deeds, dwelling in the Three Palaces and reposing in the divine precincts. [These are the words of *Preserving Life*.]

*(The Fenghuai and Qujin points are not recorded in moxibustion classics. The "Tomb Litigation" chapter lacks a complete version. Neither Dengyi Cloud Mountain nor Lord Zhao Cheng appears elsewhere in extant texts.)*

When lying awake at night, visualize the solar image in your right hand, grasping it so that the sun’s crimson radiance ascends from your arm to the elbow and armpit. After a long while, the solar rays suddenly transform into fire, burning through the arm until it is completely permeated inside and out. Then silently recite:

*"O Radiant Upper Prime of the Four Luminaries,
At the division of solar and lunar qi,
Flowing light shines resplendent,
Infusing fluid to congeal the hun-soul.
Divine radiance scatters its brilliance,
Sweeping away filth and refining mist.
Pervading wind and qi,
All hundred evils are incinerated.
Grant me long life,
With limbs whole and sound.
Let the harm-inflicting dead be bound
And delivered to the Northern Asterism."*

After reciting, meditate for a long while, then release the body and forget the self. [This is *Preserving Life*’s account of the secret incantation for dispelling demons.]

*(This scripture has not yet been revealed to the world. If it is indeed part of the *Sevenfold Wisdom* restricted texts, it is unclear how the Young Lord could have seen it.)*

The above eight clauses were transcribed by the clerk. Yesterday, I presented the tomb matter to inquire of Lord Xun. Lord Xun said: *"When extreme yin congeals and stagnates, lingering long in the burial grounds, it causes the earth officials to surge and the tomb spirits to stagnate. Thus, wind and malevolent qi arise and persist. However, as the rushing qi disperses, the afflictions will gradually subside. Sealing and blocking may not be urgent. It would be better to unblock the well of the wife’s tomb to moisten the path of relocation, offering relief to thirsty travelers on the road—a hidden kindness flowing through the crossroads. Plant pear and cherry trees by the Dragon River, and scatter pine and poplar to stabilize the markers. This is what would be beneficial."*

(This Lord Xun is likely Xun Zhonghou. This was communicated after the death of the Chief Clerk’s wife.)

Fan Youchong, a Han-era Gentleman of the Imperial Secretariat, was skilled in geomancy. He devoted himself to the study of tombs and dwellings. By the end of the Wei dynasty, his teachings had spread to this region during the reign of Emperor Tong. He stated: *"My present tomb holds the Green Dragon’s commanding qi. Above, the Dark Mystery repels misfortune. The Black Warrior extends its form. Tigers roar across the eight directions. It is nearly a cave of immortals, a归宿 for those refining their forms—truly an auspicious tomb of the highest order."* Such were his words.

(This refers to the same Fan Jian who earlier adhered to the Three Qi. Though the Four Spirits share tomb principles, their configurations remain indistinguishable. His designation of the Vermilion Bird as the Dark Mystery is also unclear.)

Those who accumulate goodness, rest in virtue, maintain compassion for all things, and cultivate both the arts of longevity and the treasures within their forms—though they may possess such a tomb—do so merely to demonstrate the cycle of beginning and end. As for the fullness of spirit and the convergence of radiance, when the mysterious mirror opens, how could it ever become a decayed thing, never to emerge again? The occupant of this tomb may well be called one who aligns with the turning of the times.

(These passages all discuss the tomb of the Chief Clerk’s wife.)

The words of Xin Xuanzi (Xin Xuanzi’s affairs are recorded in Scroll Five).

The above three clauses were transcribed by Yang.

To establish the method of an auspicious tomb: after removing the clods, measure nine paces and nine feet backward—this is called *Dark Mystery Repelling Misfortune*. The Celestial Canopy Palace’s royal qi spirit (the *"Green Dragon Commanding Qi"*) is Zhao Zidu. The hundred taboos and harmful qi spirits of tombs all submit to it. It can control the essence of the Five Lands and transform misfortune into fortune. For the tombs of marquises and kings, the *Waving Banner* should be raised nine feet, using a stone three feet square (inscribed with *"Dark Mystery Repelling Misfortune"*) and buried three feet deep.

The foolish of the world may possess a thousand scrolls and ten thousand chapters, yet who can truly discern the four phases of fortune and misfortune? Tombs beneath *Repelling Misfortune* succeed by this method—and fail by it as well. Without divinity or sagacity, it is difficult to comprehend. Only those who master this art may do so.

The descendant (*"Black Tortoise Extending Its Body"*) Sun shall suffer no further afflictions of tombs. One who understands the methods of graves can suppress all the thousand taboos and ten thousand prohibitions, invariably transforming misfortune into fortune. Mastery of this method ensures a tomb remains eternally auspicious—its efficacy need not be proclaimed.

*(This sheet, written in an unusual hand on blue paper, may also be interpreted thus: the nine steps and nine feet are specified, rather than ten steps and three feet, because the nine feet enter the tomb’s interior, with the central point taken as the measure (*"Tiger’s Roar, Eightfold Descent"*). The *Repelling Misfortune* should correspond to the Vermilion Bird, yet it is placed at the tomb’s rear. For those of the *zheng* household with an *jia* orientation, the Vermilion Bird lies in the southwest; for the *yu* household with a *geng* orientation, it lies in the northeast—this is not debated here.)*

Carve a circle three feet in diameter and inscribe upon it:
*"The Celestial Thearch commands the royal qi beneath the earth, within the tomb. Spirits of the Five Directions, Zhao Gongming and all others: for the lord or marquis of such-and-such a state, born in the year of jia or yi, in such-and-such an era, who embodied the qi of purity and truth in life and returns to the divine palace in death, whose form now rests in the hidden realm, abiding in tranquil emptiness—repel and expel all taboos and harmful qi. They shall not dare to act recklessly or inflict harm. Let his descendants flourish, their literary arts attaining the Nine Merits, their martial prowess embodying the Seven Virtues. May they achieve nobility generation after generation, enduring as long as heaven and earth. This is by the decree of the Nine Heavens beneath the earth."*

*(A circle three feet in diameter—here, "three feet" is implied, though omitted. The method of squaring the circle requires all six edges to be equal, like the shape of a seal. Zhao Gongming.)*

Here now is the translation:

---

The *Twelve Hundred Official Rites* have mistakenly taken this as the name for "warm ghosts." The Nine Merits and Seven Virtues—these matters derive from the *Zuo Tradition*. When applying this method, one must take care that no one else learns of it. Should there be any insufficiency in the tomb’s royal qi, or mutual harm through clashing phases, inscribe a talisman to suppress it. There is nothing that will not be subdued and pacified, transforming misfortune into fortune. {…}

*(The line below has been cut off. Only one character remains, resembling this. It appears the next line was also trimmed, leaving the sentence incomplete. From the "three-foot circle" onward, this is written on a separate sheet, bearing the traces of a different hand—not the same as before. Both are written on blue paper, though the texture and hue differ slightly. It is doubtful that these were copied from an authentic source. As Fan Jian had already remarked thus, I fear later generations may have fabricated the method.)*

Oral instructions of the Shangqing Perfected, Feng Yanshou.

*(Earlier, he was described as a beggar from the market of Chu, the Perfected of Western Marchmount, Feng Yanshou. The title "Western Marchmount" does not preclude his designation as a Shangqing Perfected. The following fourteen entries, though not seen in authentic texts, align in substance with true methods. Thus, they are compiled separately and appended to the end of this scroll.)*

The Way of the student must begin with healing illness, ensuring the body is free of deficient evil, blood depletion, brain diminution, or stagnation of fluids and filth. Without first treating illness, even if one consumes elixirs and practices Daoist arts, it will bring no benefit to the body. There was once a Daoist named Wang Zhongfu, who from his youth was intent on such matters and devoted himself to the pursuit of divine transcendence.

He constantly practiced the method of absorbing and drawing in the twin luminaries and ingesting the auroral mists for over forty years, yet perceived no benefit. His son, however, followed the same practice for eighteen years and ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Later, the Perfected of Southern Marchmount suddenly descended to instruct Zhongfu, saying: "The reason you have not attained ascension is that your body harbors grave illness—your brain palace is depleted, sinews lack fluid infusion, and your numinous essences remain unsurging. Though you have connected with the luminaries and ingested auroral mists, it has brought no true benefit to your body." Thereupon, Zhongfu began to take medicinals to treat his illness while continuing his practice. After another eighteen years, he too ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Now he resides in the Dark Realm, where he received scriptures and was appointed Perfected of Central Marchmount, presiding over the Nine Mysteries’ bureau—a post he still holds today. (This account closely aligns with essential matters. No further distinction is made between Zhongfu and his son.)

The student of the Dao must first pacify the heart and nurture the spirit, ingesting medicinals to treat illness so that the brain palace is filled to fullness and the primordial essence remains unspilled. Only then may one preserve the spirit and ingest auroral mists, drawing in and exhaling the twin luminaries. If one frequently engages in sexual congress, leaking and discharging essence, then qi becomes foul, the spirit flees, and the numinous essence withers to exhaustion. Even if one were to receive the Dark Stave, Jade Records, Golden Scriptures, or the Great Ultimate, how could one escape the bonds of non-life?

Of old, former masters often warned of this matter, saying: "For the student of the Dao, one act of congress dissipates a year’s medicinal potency; two acts dissipate two years’ potency. Beyond three, the medicinal potency thus dissipated is entirely lost from the body." Therefore, the perfected and transcendent ones ever guard against this, regarding it as the greatest taboo against the continuity of life. (This principle accords perfectly with the purport of all scriptures.)

In the study of the Dao, one must silently nurture the spirit, drawing qi to its utmost and exhaling it gently. One must not speak loudly or shout, lest the spirit and qi become labored and impaired. Those who practice thus do not cultivate life.

For all who preserve the radiance of the spirit and engage in the affairs of the perfected transcendent ones, they must not lend their clothing to others, nor wear garments not their own. All items such as headscarves, coarse robes, shoes, and clogs should be kept fresh and abundant, for the three hun and seven po may dwell within them, and these are also taboos for the five spirits, as dampness and defilement must be avoided.

On the eight seasonal nodes, one should observe fasting and solemnity, devising good deeds to advance the methods of the Dao. Beware of anger, contention, joy, or wrath on these days, and do not impose punishments or authority, for these are grave transgressions and great taboos in the eyes of heaven and humanity. These three principles also accord with the purport of the scriptures.

For all who delve into the profound Dao or recite sacred scriptures, after every ten or twenty words, one should once or twice lick the lips and swallow the saliva. After every hundred or fifty words, one should two or three times click the teeth to summon the spirit and harmonize the blood and qi, allowing the numinous fluid to congeal and fill, so that the Imperial Unity may rejoice in its abode. This is what is meant by "the qi of the center is not labored, the blood’s inception is not leaked." (This is a separate method not found in the scriptures.)

The method for students: one must not weep tears or spit excessively, for these actions deplete the fluids and leak the essences, causing the throat and brain to become greatly exhausted. Thus, true persons and Daoists constantly practice exhalation and inhalation, swallowing and savoring, to harmonize the six fluids.

On the days of *jiǎ-yín* and *gēng-shēn*, when corpse-ghosts vie to disrupt the spirit and stir defilement, one must not share a mat with a spouse nor engage in face-to-face conversation. Instead, maintain purity, remain awake, and guard against these days, dispelling all desires.

On the days of the five *mǎo*, one should always enter the chamber in purification, bow with the heart toward the east, visualize the spirit, and meditate with the hope of perceiving the divine. Whatever one wishes to express may be set forth. Those who persist in this practice will have Jade Maidens descend to attend them. (These three principles also align with the scriptures’ teachings.)

On one’s own life-day, face the corresponding direction, click the teeth three times, and with the heart, bow twice while softly reciting the incantation:

*"Great Unity governs life,
Triple Union of the true.
Chamber of the womb, supreme radiance,
Mother mysterious, Father primordial.
You give birth to my five viscera,
Gather my spirit and essence.
Below, pour the jade fluid;
Above, ascend to the Muddy Pellet.
At dusk, refine the seven po;
At dawn, harmonize the three hun.
To the right, command the Jade Splendor;
To the left, summon the Golden Dawn.
Ordain me as an immortal,
Command spirits and employ the divine.
Ever preserve the flowing essences,
Soaring through the ten heavens."*

After the incantation, bow with the heart to the four directions, click the teeth three times, and swallow the saliva three times. This is called "The Supreme Way of the Hidden Morning Audience of the Womb’s Primordial," the Dao of fetal origin. Practicing this regularly will secure the hun and po, ensuring long life as a divine immortal. (This method is not found in the scriptures.)

Whenever entering the chamber to burn incense, one should always bow with the heart toward the mat, click the teeth, and silently pray, expressing whatever is in one’s mind with utmost focus and devotion.

Surely, one will attain spiritual resonance. (This also follows the example of morning stillness.)

Whenever one constantly visualizes and recognizes their own form, striving to make it vividly present before them, let there always be the radiance of the sun and moon upon the face, illuminating the entire form. Place the sun to the left and the moon to the right, nine inches from the face. Once visualized, click the teeth three times and softly pray:

*"Primordial Womb, Supreme Perfected,
Twin Effulgences, Two Mysteries,
To the right, embrace the seven po;
To the left, restrain the three hun.
May my spirit and luminosity
Ever abide with my form."*

After the prayer, click the teeth twenty-one times and swallow the saliva seven times. This is called "The Dao of the Celestial Lord’s Form-Refinement, Hun-Restraint, and Po-Control," which renders one spiritually radiant as an immortal, attaining long life without death. If the prayer is not recited, one may simply perform the visualization. (This method includes form-recognition but lacks the prayer in the transmitted teachings.)

Moreover, those who study the Dao must first regulate the hun and po, dispelling the corpse-demons. On the last day of the lunar month, the first day of the new moon, the days of *gengshen* and *jiayin*, one should purify oneself, enter the chamber, bathe to cleanse the dust, and sit upright upon the mat. It is best to remain sleepless. With a cinnabar brush, mark the left corner of the eye, and with a realgar brush, mark the right side beneath the nose, allowing a small portion to enter the valley (philtrum). Once marked, first click the teeth three times and softly pray:

*"Soaring Effulgence, Flying Coils,
Cinnabar and realgar scatter mist.
Seize the empty evil,
Sink the corpse-filth into oblivion.
Harmonize the hun, refine the po,
Unite with the Great Spirit.
Grant me deathlessness,
Eternal wholeness and longevity.
Clarity of perception, penetrating sight,
Ever-preserved flowing essences."*

After the prayer, click the teeth three more times and swallow the saliva three times. Simultaneously, press the second finger of the right hand beneath the right nostril and the second finger of the left hand beneath the left eye, each seven times. Maintain the pressure without lifting the hands. These two points are the gateways through which the seven po and wandering corpse-demons roam, the thoroughfares of thieving essence and malevolent qi. Thus, they receive the essence of cinnabar and realgar, sealing the path of corpse-demons, drawing the fragrance of the Twin Effulgences to suppress licentious chaos.

This is the Supreme Ultimate’s highest method. Practicing it regularly will harmonize the hun and po, disperse corpse-filth, and ensure long life as an immortal with penetrating vision. After three years of practice, lustful thoughts will entirely vanish. (This closely resembles the method of the Supreme Numinous Perfected and may be cultivated alongside it.)

All methods of Shangqing teeth-tapping and saliva-swallowing follow specific procedures, with a fixed sequence of prior and subsequent steps. They must not be practiced in a disordered or mixed manner, lest the true spirits become confused. Tapping the teeth summons the spirits of destiny; swallowing harmonizes the true. Harmony is received and circulated through the Six Fluids. To govern the spirits, one must sound the drum and proceed in orderly ranks.

In all practices of the above methods, when performing rituals and invocations, one should first tap the teeth—upper and lower striking each other, not left and right. With each inhalation and exhalation, three taps are ideal. Only after the ritual and invocation are complete should one tap the teeth again before swallowing the various fluids. This is called "summoning the spirits and harmonizing the true," a practice for those who seek ascension as immortals.

I have repeatedly observed that those of false and vulgar habits often misinterpret the scriptural texts, jumbling the sequence and disrupting the order. To employ such methods and expect benefit is truly lamentable. (This aligns with the intent of the *Five Spirits Scripture*.)

Here ends this scroll.
*Oral Instructions on Nurturing Nature and Prohibitions*
(Additional entries exist but have not been seen in authentic texts.)

These two or so transcribed matters—all being immortals’ practical instructions and minor techniques—aid in their application, and thus are compiled together.

**Oral Instructions of the Yellow Immortal Lord:**
When ingesting medicinal substances, avoid garlic and pomegranate seeds. Pork liver and dog’s-head meat are strictly forbidden; total abstention is best. Daoists must never consume pork or dog meat while engaging in chamber arts, lest the efficacy of the medicine be nullified. It is calculated that eating one *jin* [of such meat] wastes a hundred days’ [of cultivation]. Take heed of this warning.
*(Recorded by a disciple of Peng Zu.)*

**Oral Instructions of the Green Ox Daoist:**
At night, before sleep, visualize the sun above the forehead and the moon above the navel. This repels a thousand ghosts and ten thousand malevolent spirits, summoning jade maidens to descend. All misfortunes flee in submission. The method is profoundly efficacious.
*(This is Feng Junda, as recorded in the *Biographies of Divine Immortals* and the *Preface to the Five Marchmounts*.)*

**Oral Instructions of Shen Xi:**
When taking divine medicines, never face north—this is a great taboo. On *hai* and *zi* days, do not spit, for it depletes essence and dissipates *qi*, shortening one’s lifespan. The potency of the medicine becomes as inert as earth.
*(From the *Biographies of Divine Immortals*.)*

**Oral Instructions of Lü Gong:**
On the day of entering a mountain, before reaching a hundred paces from the mountain, first retreat a hundred paces backward, then turn and ascend with reversed steps. Mountain spirits will not harm you; all malevolent forces prostrate and flee, and every poison conceals itself.
*(From the *Biographies of Divine Immortals*.)*

**Oral Instructions of Luan Ba:**
When passing through mountains or the precincts of numinous shrines, visualize within your mouth the True Person, the Crimson Numinous Venerable, attended by two jade maidens. One maiden is named Hua Zheng, the other She Jing. The Venerable wears a crimson gauze robe, while the two maidens are clad in yellow above and below. Once the visualization is complete, cry out in a commanding voice.

**Oral Instructions of Luan Ba (Luan of Yuzhang):**
At the cry, the ghosts and spirits within the shrine swiftly arrive. Command all malevolent forces to prostrate before the Crimson Numinous Venerable and be slain. The myriad spirits retreat a thousand *li*. This is the method of the Envoys of the Three Heavens’ Frontiers for capturing ghosts.

**Oral Instructions of the Donghai Lesser Youth:**
Daoists seeking immortality must not lie with women. A single union dissipates a year’s medicinal potency. If one engages in the bedchamber without having ingested any elixir, one’s lifespan is reduced by thirty years.

**Oral Instructions of the Dongling Sagely Mother:**
Those who study the Dao must guard their speech. Excessive talk invites trials by mountain spirits and the hundred spirits. When lying down at night, close the eyes and visualize the Pupil Child within the *Niwan* Palace, directing the inner gaze toward the body’s spirits. Thus may one attain long life and ascend to heaven. Liu Jing likewise employed this method.

**Oral Instructions of the Wife of the Female Immortal Cheng Wei:**
When consuming elixirs, avoid all blood-tainted foods. Eating such things prevents the expulsion of the Three Corpses. Dried meats, however, are permissible.

**Oral Instructions of Feng Gang:**
When a Daoist falls ill, close the eyes and inwardly visualize the heart, generating fire to burn the body. Once the body is consumed, maintain the visualization until the essence appears faintly. The illness will then be cured. For pain in a specific area, concentrate the fire there. This method is profoundly efficacious.

**Oral Instructions of Chen Anshi:**
When a Daoist ties up their hair, arranges their topknot, eats, drinks, or handles their shoes, pillow, or bedding, they must not allow those who are not Daoists to witness their hair arrangement, dry their food, or disturb their shoes.

When using one’s pillow or bedding, the ghosts dwelling within the corporeal forms of the vulgar dead may come to invade one’s spirit. Thus, Daoists who dwell in mountain forests and seclude themselves do so to distance themselves from this clamor and defilement, severing all worldly affairs—fearing that external things, in all their variety, may violate their nature and life. Guard this well.

**Oral Instructions of Li Xiaojun:**
When a Daoist seeks immortality, they must not look upon corpses. Such sights severely harm the spirit and disrupt the qi. Even rulers, teachers, parents, or beloved ones may only be attended in death out of unavoidable duty. Thus, when a Daoist departs this world, they do not serve kings or lords—this is to be without a ruler. To exist alone and apart is to be without companions. Only for one’s parents, teachers, or masters may one attend their funerals, allowing the deepest grief to arise, though it risks injuring one’s nature and life. Yet if one is harmed for this reason, it is as though no harm were done. I guard this teaching closely, passing it on only by word of mouth.

**Oral Instructions of the Female Immortal, Wife of Liu Gang:**
Those who seek immortality must not lie with women on the ninth day of the third month or the second day of the sixth month.

On the sixth day of the ninth month and the third day of the twelfth month, one must enter seclusion and avoid all contact with women. If the Six Corpses stir in chaos, the stored blood will be disturbed, scattering and dispersing. The Three Hun will lose their guard, the spirit will wither, and the qi will depart—accumulating until death follows. The reason these days are taboo is not merely to restrain licentiousness or prevent the loss of fluids. It is to pacify the Female Palace, which resides in *shen* (the ninth earthly branch), while the Male Palace resides in *yin* (the third earthly branch). *Yin* and *shen* clash in mutual punishment, and this punitive force intensifies. On these days, the male and female Three Corpses emerge within the pupils of the eyes: the female corpse beckons the male, and the male corpse beckons the female. Disaster and harm flow between them, depleting the spirit and corrupting the true. Though one may not perceive it, the form is already damaged—blood flows from the *Niwan* Palace as the Three Corpses wage war within the eyes. On these days, even the most beloved of women or the most cherished of wives must not be faced. My former master attained immortality solely by cultivating this Dao, without extending exceptions even to the closest kin who might be without intent. You must be vigilant in this.

---

The *Zhen’gao*, Scroll Ten and Scroll Eleven, and *Jishen Shu*, First Chapter, state: The region of Jinling is the rich marrow of the Cavernous Void, the earthly lung of Juqu. Countless tread its soil, yet none comprehend it.

(These words were orally transmitted by the Lord of Life-Preservation. According to oral tradition, this should have been recorded before the sixth month of the *yichou* year, during the *jiazi* era, when the discussion of this mountain’s blessed origins began. Its soil is fertile, hence called "rich marrow." When waters rise, it floats, hence called "earthly lung." Though generations have traversed it, none have known its true location.)

The source of Mount Juqu curves to embrace, thus it is named the Village of Juqu. It lies one hundred and fifty *li* across the river; seek, and you shall find it.

(All passages hereafter in large purple script are records from the transmissions of the Three Lords Mao. As these transmissions are treasured and closely guarded, few have seen them. Here, I have carefully transcribed the accounts of the mountain to corroborate and clarify. The mountain’s form bends and winds, extending east and west in meandering turns, hence the name Juqu. The mountain’s ridgeline divides its boundaries: west and north belong to Jurong County, while east and south belong to Yanling. Since Jurong was established as a county, its village no longer exists. In ancient times, it likely lay near Shuxu. Today, the mountain is one hundred and fifty to sixty *li* from the Jiang River at Stone Gate, measured by footpath.)

East of the Jiang River, amidst the small marshes near Jinling, lies Mount Juqu.

(Here, Jinling refers to Moling, not the "earthly lung" of Jinling. The small marsh is today’s Chishan Lake. Gazing directly from the Jiang River toward the mountain, its position aligns east and west as described.)

This mountain is a cavernous void of inner vision, containing a numinous abode. Its grottoes open in four directions, its caves and peaks stretch long and unbroken. The ancients called it the Void Terrace of Jintan, the Convenient Chamber of the Queen Mother, the Eastern Window of Qingxu, and the Secluded Depths of Linwu. Its caverns interconnect, forming yin paths that lead in all directions—seven routes, nine springs, and four crossroads—making it a true cavern-palace of the immortals.

(This describes the interconnectedness of the grotto-heavens. The Queen Mother resides in the Linwu Grotto beneath Baoshan in Taihu, where the Dragon Might Elder entered and obtained the Five Talismans of Lingbao. Qingxu is the name of the Wangwu Grotto-heaven, indicating that Huayang is linked to this site.)

The mountain’s shape resembles the character *si* (巳), hence its name, Juqu.

(Today, standing atop the Xuan Ridge of Middle Mao Peak, one gazes before and behind at the ranges and ridges. They coil and curve, with Great Mao as the head, winding east, then north, then west, then north again, then east-northeast to the Great Transverse, folding back and forth like the leftward-written character *si*.)

Jinling cannot be harmed by war or flood, nor afflicted by plague or disaster. The *Yellow River Chart*, *Zhong Yao Yuan* Chapter, Scroll Forty-Four, states: "The altar of Jujin contains a mound where neither war nor sickness treads, nor do surging waves ascend." This is the very blessed land. If your heart awakens to this, it is your fortune. Yet know: from whom did this awakening arise?

(This *Yellow River Chart* was received by Shun and Yu, along with the *Luo Book* and its kind. Over forty scrolls remain today. These words were also meant for the Director of Records, signifying that his enlightenment came from the teachings of Lord Yang, which I have further expounded.)

Within Mount Juqu lies the land of Jinling, spanning thirty-seven or thirty-eight *qing*. This is the earthly lung of Jinling. Its soil is fertile, its well water sweet and pure. To dwell here is to attain transcendence and witness the Great Peace. The *Yellow River Chart’s Inner Yuan Classic* states: "The earthly lung has good soil and clear water. The mountain of Juqu, the mound of Jintan—here one may transcend the world and ascend to the Curved City." The *Yellow River Book’s Middle Chapter* also says: "The mountain of Jujin contains a mound where neither war nor sickness treads, nor do surging waves ascend." This is the meaning.

(The *Yellow River Chart* mentioned later refers to the same passage from the *Zhong Yao Yuan* Chapter. Though the characters for "mountain" and "altar" differ, the principle remains the same. This specifically describes the earthly lung of Jinling—a single tract of land with such properties. Other places may not share these exemptions.)

Jinling was anciently called the Land of Crouching Dragons. The *Yellow River Chart* examines the inverse, and thus records the turning of the kalpas.

The designation "to come" is but a provisional name. As for the name "Jinling," it has already endured for over two hundred years. (Tracing the name "Jinling," it originated in the time of Chu. When the First Emperor of Qin crossed the river, he found the qi oppressive and thus renamed it Moling. Since the Han dynasty, the old county seat lay at Small Danyang, still called the "former seat" today. In the third year of Taikang of Jin, the lands south of the Huai River were apportioned to it. In the ninth year of Yixi, the seat was moved to 〈missing〉 [Dou]chang. In the first year of Yuanxi, it was relocated to its present site. This is the Jinling east of the river. The "over two hundred years" mentioned in tradition refers to when Sun Quan of Wu sent men to mine gold and encamped at Crouching Dragon Mountain, whence the name "Jinling" arose—a natural resonance. Thus does one marvel at the *Yellow River Chart*’s inverse omen.)

In Qin times, Juqu Mountain was called the Altar of Jugold, for within its grotto-heaven stood a hundred-zhang gold altar, whence the name derived. Beyond it lay Mount Jijin, likewise named for its accumulated gold altars. In Zhou times, its source and marsh were called the Cavern of Curved Waters. Observing the mountain’s sinuous form, later generations combined the names into Juqu Mountain. In the Han, the Three Mao Lords came to govern there, and the elders renamed it the Mountain of the Mao Lords. The Three Lords once each rode a white crane, alighting in three separate places on the mountain, seen by many at the time. Thus arose folk songs, and the mountain was further divided into the Three Mountains of the Great, Middle, and Lesser Mao Lords, corresponding to the cranes’ perches. In sum, all remain but a single mountain—Juqu—without other names. The Three Mao Mountains, though linked in sequence, are but one name: Juqu. Later generations, interpreting events, have since branched it into dozens of alternate names, though originally it was not so. (Today, the southernmost and highest is called Great Mao Mountain, its central ridge crowned by three peaks standing like tripods. The next highest, nearer the rear, is Middle Mao Mountain. To the north, a solitary peak with clustered rocks is Lesser Mao Mountain. Between Great and Middle Mao lies Chang’e, its eastern extension reaching Yanling and Juqu, its western leading to Jurong Lake, forming a continuous stone ridge. Mount Jijin and Horse Ridge encircle it like a rampart. Between Middle and Lesser Mao lies Xiao’e, its eastern and western extensions mirroring the same pattern, linked by a small Horse Ridge. Beyond Lesser Mao Mountain’s rear stretch Lei Ping, Swallow’s Beak, Square Peak, Great Horizontal, and Constant Mountain, all connected in unbroken succession, descending to Po[net] Du. South of Great Mao lie Scallion Mountain, Bamboo Wu Mountain, Square Mountain, and beyond them, a chain of ridges reaching to the mountains of Wuxing, extending to Luofu and ending at the Southern Sea.)

The mountain yields gold. In the reign of Emperor Ling of Han, an imperial edict commanded the commanderies and counties [to mine it].

The gold from Juqu was mined to replenish the imperial armory. By the time of Sun Quan, he again dispatched palace guards to extract gold and regularly deliver it to the authorities. A military commander led a hundred households to garrison and settle the land of Fulong, which was then renamed Jinling. As the *Hetu* foretold in ancient times, it was indeed most wondrous.

(To this day, south of Great Mao Mountain, several deep pits and large hollows remain, traditionally called "Gold Wells," said to have been excavated during Sun Quan’s reign. In the eastern foothills of this mountain, fragments of stone often contain grains of gold. The military commander and his garrison once settled in Fulong, but no trace remains today. Only to the west lies Shuxu, formerly known as Zhuxu, now inhabited by common folk. Some ten *li* ahead of Shuxu, near Great Mao, stands Wu Xu Village, whose name appears similar but bears no connection to Jinling. Northwest of the Prefect’s residence, near the small ridge of Changyin, broken shards of pottery and scorched red earth are frequently found, suggesting former habitation. Though the land has been cultivated and no foundations remain, nor any wells, it may be that the Prefect’s well, too, has long since been buried. Neither Lesser Mao nor Great Horizontal show signs of gold mining, and the Gold Wells of Great Mao seem too distant to be the same site—these matters remain uncertain.)

The soil of Jinling resembles that of Bei[lost city] and the northern valley passes: firm and fertile, well-suited for growing grain. Digging wells here yields water akin to that outside Feng Gate in Chang’an—clear springs with deep, distant sources. The water is white in color.

Those who dwell on this land and drink its water, however, do not pursue the Dao.

The waters here also grant long life. Are they not nourished by the golden essence of Jin’s fluid? You must keep this secret. I possess a chronicle that records these matters in full—when the time comes, I shall show it to you.

*(This passage was composed by Lord Dinglu, who secretly instructed [you] to reveal it to the Marquis of Xu. The Northern {Lost City} Mountain lies several li north of Luoyang. The Northern Valley Pass is Mengjin Pass. Its soil is yellow-black and rich. Feng Gate is the northern gate of Chang’an. As for the present site identified as Jinling, there are no signs of cultivation, habitation, or well-digging—this is no mere oversight but rather the protection of true spirits, who prevent its careless occupation. My "chronicle" refers to the *Biographies of the Three Mao*. According to the Prefect’s records from the jiazi year, no written account existed at the time; thus, this text may have been orally transmitted around the guihai year. The chronicle also describes the land as fine and sweet, fit for dwelling and attaining transcendence—hence these explicit remarks.)*

The site is modest in scale, covering just over ten *qing* in seclusion, with six or seven *qing* of elevated, level ground. Now that its essence is known, its topography is plain to see.

The land thus appears clearly—hence it is not obscurely revealed. (The Lord of Life Preservation stated: According to the *Biographies*, the Jinling site spans thirty-seven or thirty-eight *qing*, though this likely refers to its outermost boundaries. As for the verified record, it is indeed just over ten *qing*, with the elevated, level ground being the suitable dwelling place. Ascending the site and surveying it as described, one may readily grasp its contours. One who roughly discerns the location—if they are an immortal transcending the world or one of the seed people—will naturally arrive without hindrance. Yet if unworthy, merely bringing hands to build a dwelling will surely see it fail to stand in any direction, swiftly compelled to relocate. The careless and vulgar multitude must not forcibly claim this place.)

To the left and right of Jinling lie valley streams and mountain sources. On the left of the ridge stands a mountain, while to the right flows a stream named *Muhuhu* Valley. West of the ridge lies a source called Yang Valley. The *Inner Classic of Named Mountains and Records of Blessed Lands* states: "The hidden dragon’s land lies west of Willow Valley and to the right of Jintan, fit for lofty dwelling"—this is precisely the blessed land of Jinling. (Now called Willow Valley, its source emerges from the spring behind the fields of Xiao Mao’s Tian Gong, flowing southwest to the head of Shuxu, where it enters the great [stream]. As for Yang Valley, its name has since vanished, though behind the ridges of Changyin Mountain lies a small [stream], flowing west before turning south, also converging at the head of Shuxu. Elders further say that the source of Yang Valley arises before Zhong Mao and behind Da Mao, where several streams merge into one, exiting the mountain to flow due west before turning north, likewise joining the great [stream]. Where the two [valleys] intersect lies this very site. Its general orientation is east-west, though not perfectly aligned—hence the reference to both left and right. Yet whether today’s two valleys are the same as those of old remains uncertain. Though passed down by elders, transmission has gradually eroded, and the sources’ courses have shifted.)

(Though no fixed landmark may serve as evidence,) in high antiquity this mountain was called Gang Mountain. The *Record of Confucius’ Blessed Lands* states: “Between the ridges of Gang Mountain lies the Hidden Dragon’s Domain, where one may escape floods, ward off illness, and attain long life.” The reason it was originally named Gang is that its substance resembles that of a golden altar. Thus, though a hundred generations have passed and its name has changed a hundred times, it is no longer called by its original name—truly lamentable.

(According to present accounts, a long and massive mountain northeast of Xiao Mao is called Da Heng Mountain, originally named Yu Gang Mountain. This mountain lies east of what is now called the Hidden Dragon’s Domain. The world also refers to the Hidden Dragon’s land as Dead Serpent Ridge, which roughly corresponds to the old name. Observing its length and increasing height, it is also called Chang Yin. *Yin* is pronounced *yu jin* [隱, rhyming with 觐], meaning “to conceal and shield.” Though this Gang Mountain is strewn with small stones, it remains habitable. From the southeast near the Prefect’s residence to the area around Lei Ping, there are many suitable dwelling places. Jiang Yuanchu of Yixing and others have now established fields and homesteads at the foot of the ridge, roughly four or five *li* from the Prefect’s residence.)

Beyond the Golden Court of Tongbai and the Golden Mound of Wu’s Juqu, it is a blessed realm for nurturing the true and a numinous site for attaining the divine. When the waters of the five Yaos poured eastward into the sea, all people fell ill and died—what could the soldiers of Wu’an do to me? For I dwell near the secluded gate of the Terrace of the Sun, relying on this to wander at ease.

(The above was orally transmitted by the Perfected Lord Wang of the Right Assistant [Wang Zhenren] and secretly shown to Lord Xu. This refers to the lands of Wu and Yue spoken of by the Divine Lord of the Dawn of Tongbai. Of these, only the two golden sites are the most blessed. *Wu’an* refers to the Qin general Bai Qi, who attacked Zhao at Changping and in a single day buried alive four hundred thousand men—no military slaughter in antiquity was more brutal. Hence, it is cited here as an analogy. This is what is meant by “soldiers and plague do not come, and great waves do not ascend.” Since it lies near a grotto-heaven and is guarded by divine immortals, it can ensure that the three disasters do not encroach. The preceding five entries are from Yang’s writings.)

Within the great heavens lie thirty-six grotto-heavens of the earth’s center, the eighth of which is the grotto of Juqu Mountain, encompassing one hundred fifty *li* in circumference. It is named the Golden Altar of Huayang Heaven.

(According to the transmitted records, up to the tenth heaven, these include the five sacred peaks, the eight seas of immortals, and the grottoes of distant barbarian lands. Since these fall outside the present scope, they are not enumerated here.)

The grotto’s walls are entirely of stone on all four sides, above and below. The flat upper area lies beneath the soil, emerging above ground only after thirteen or fourteen *li*.

(This refers to reaching the summit of the great mountain.) It spans forty-five *li* east to west and thirty-five *li* north to south, perfectly square and level. Its hollow interior measures one hundred seventy *zhang* in height, while the lower part is one hundred *zhang*. Below, the land still rises in mounds and recumbent *earth dragons*, while the upper vault is perfectly flat.

(As previously stated, the high part is one hundred seventy *zhang* and the lower part one hundred *zhang*, meaning the center is elevated and the four sides gradually slope downward. The present statement that “the upper vault is perfectly flat” refers to its surface being level without depressions or protrusions, not that it is flat like a board. It may also mean that when one ascends the mounds, one is lower relative to the heavens.)

Within its hollow, there shines a yin radiance by night and the root of the sun’s essence by day, illuminating this empty space so brightly that it rivals the sun and moon. The yin radiance governs the night, and the sun’s essence governs the day. Their forms are round like the sun and moon, flying within the dark void.

(According to the various grotto-heavens, each has these names, though with slight differences. They are likely the sun and moon of the great heavens, their essence divided to shine here. Since they govern day and night, they must rise and set, their brightness and dimness matching the sun and moon of our world. At the edge of the great heavens, one cannot reach the end, but the boundaries of this small heaven may nearly be touched and seen. The rising and setting of its sun and moon must therefore have limits, appearing and vanishing suddenly rather than through any aperture. Yet their size remains unknown.)

The Grotto-Palace of Juqu has five gates: two side gates to the south, side gates to the east and west, and the great northern side gate—altogether five auxiliary gates.

(Today, the great cavern on the mountain’s south side is the western side gate of the southern face. The eastern gate seems to lie within the ridge of cypress branches. The Liangchang Cavern is the great northern side gate, though the eastern and western gates are not apparent. The Middle Lord stated that the eastern side gate lies at the mouth of Small Mao, east of Middle Mao, and that entering here brings one closest to the grotto-heaven, though the outer opening is very small and blocked with stone. Details follow later. Thus, the western side gate should be similarly concealed, likely at the place now called the Stone Tomb. The cypress ridge has two or three cavern mouths, but the true gate may not open there. If one seeks these three gates with pure devotion, they may yet be found. Though the southern side gate’s outer entrance remains wide open, the inner passage is blocked, likely due to excessive defilement. The great northern cavern still sees the comings and goings of spirits and gods, but true immortals do not enter or exit through the five gates—they pass through all barriers as if they were nothing. These gates exist to show the structure of the mountain grotto or perhaps for those from outside who are meant to enter.)

Within the void, stone steps wind outward to meet the gateways, allowing passage up and down. Those who come and go in haste do not realize they are within the grotto-heaven and assume they tread an outer path. Since the light of the sun and moon is no different, and the plants, trees, waters, and marshes are indistinguishable from those outside, with birds flying to and fro and winds and clouds thickly gathering, there is nothing to arouse suspicion.

Such is the divine palace of the grotto-heaven—its numinous wonders boundless, beyond discussion or delusion.

(Those who gather herbs in the world often mistakenly enter such grottoes, where all appears thus, and so they do not find it strange.)

(Though none have yet heard of entering the Hua Yang grotto-heaven—such as the case of Zuo Yuanfang and his like, which need not be discussed—even those who do gain entry may fear to speak of it upon their return.)

The Grotto-Heaven of Ju Qu connects eastward to Lin Wu, northward to Mount Tai, westward to Mount Emei, and southward to Mount Luofu—all great thoroughfares of the Dao. Between them lie lesser paths and branching trails, crisscrossing in countless places. During the Jian’an era of the Han, Zuo Yuanfang heard from travelers that this divine mountain lay east of the Yangzi. Thus, he crossed the river in search of it. After three months of purification rites, he ascended the mountain and found its gate. Entering the hollow of the grotto, he reached the Yin Palace, where the Three Lords bestowed upon him three kinds of divine lingzhi. Yuanfang wandered the grotto’s halls for a year, its chambers and structures laid out with perfect order, filling him with awe. He never imagined the world could hold such wonders. Spirits came and went, adjudicating life and death as if they were earthly officials.

(Now, according to the regional records...)

Then, the Forest Chamber lies to the southeast, Mount Luofu to the southwest—only Mount Tai and Mount Emei stand in the true alignment. The straight path between them should span five or six thousand *li*. This route, passing through the region of Yingchuan, ought to lead directly to the Pure Void Heaven of Mount Wangwu. Yuanfang must have been pressed by the Wei Emperor Wu, and thus returned later. Later, he truly received [oral transmission] and said: "Only after five years of pure fasting was I able to deeply enter the inner and outer palaces." The three kinds of *lingzhi* were likely of the lowest grade.

At the northern edge of the Good-and-Constant Grotto Palace, where the mountain ridge extends, walk two hundred paces south to find two pairs of white jade discs buried by the First Emperor of Qin, seven *chi* deep in the earth. Above them lies a small slab of stone on the ridge, covering the [sunken] spot. Li Si inscribed the jade discs, their text reading: *"The sagely virtue of the First Emperor illuminates and pacifies the mountains and rivers. In his imperial tour of the azure rivers, he carved this inscription upon the plain jade. If excavated, they may yet be obtained."* Wherever the First Emperor trod upon mountains and rivers, he offered jade discs in sacrifice—not only at Mount Gouqu. (From here onward...)

The ink inscriptions were all definitively recorded by the Perfected Lord [orally transmitted] to inform the Chief Scribe. The preceding text copied by the clerk is missing its opening passage, which may not have been the original beginning. Upon investigation, the burial of the jade discs occurred prior to the Three Mao Brothers’ attainment of the Dao, yet the full account appears only afterward—thus the Perfected One is revealed to be without obstruction or concealment. According to the traditional account, this event coincided with the imperial ascent of the mountain, though it mentions only a single pair as extraordinary. Perhaps the character *ying* (應) was intended. The inscription on the paired jade discs was likely carved in seal script, similar to that on Mount Yu. Today, upon examining the site, the stone cover can still be seen, and it is by no means small.

At the northern edge of the Grotto Mouth of Mount Mao lies a mountain called Good-and-Constant Mountain, originally connected to Mount Gouqu—both sharing a single name. In the thirty-seventh year of his reign, on the *guichou* day of the tenth month, the First Emperor set out on his imperial tour. By the eleventh month, he arrived at Yunmeng, where he sacrificed to Emperor Shun at Mount Jiuyi. Sailing down the Yangtze, he observed the sacred catalpa tree, crossed Mei Shoal, passed through Danyang, and reached Qiantang, where he approached the Zhe River. The waves being treacherous, he diverted a hundred and twenty *li* westward, crossing through the gorge. Ascending Mount Kuaiji, he sacrificed to the Great Yu, gazed toward the Southern Sea, and erected a stone stele to extol the virtue of Qin upon Mount Kuaiji. Li Si petitioned to inscribe it, and the Emperor then returned. Passing through various mountains and rivers, he eventually ascended the northern ridge of Mount Gouqu and buried a pair of white jade discs. There, he assembled his officials, feasted with his retinue, and sighed, saying: *"The joy of imperial tours is unmatched by mountains and seas. From this day forward, let it be a constant practice."*

Then the assembled ministers all raised their cups in toasts, chanting: *"May it be a constant practice indeed!"* Again, great drums resounded, great bells were struck, and ten thousand voices joined in unison, shaking the mountains and marshes. The auspicious omens of praise and music were all deemed excellent. Thereupon, the northern ridge of Mount Gouqu was renamed Mount Liangchang, its name derived from this very meaning.

*(According to apocryphal records: In the thirty-seventh year of the First Emperor of Qin, in the first month, he set out on a tour to Yunmeng, Danyang, and the Zhe River, ascending Mount Kuaiji to sacrifice to the Great Yu and gaze toward the Southern Sea, where he inscribed a stele to record his achievements. On his return, he passed through Wu, crossed the river, and traveled along the northern sea until he reached Langye. In Pingyuan, he fell ill, and on the *bingyin* day of the seventh month, he died at Shaqiu. In the ninth month, he was buried at Mount Li. At that time, no defilement had yet occurred. Thus, he took the path from Yanling, ascending through Jurong, and the route along the river still passed through the place where the feast was held—it was not necessarily a deliberate visit to Mount Gouqu. The reason for halting and staying at the northern foothills was not to proceed further over the ridge. Moreover, at that time, the mountain’s numinous marvels had not yet been verified. The purpose of the jade disc offering was to establish a connection with the mountain in view, as the sovereign had declared: *"All mountains and rivers trodden shall be honored with jade discs."* Names follow their owners, and designations arise from the land itself. Later, during the reign of Emperor Cheng of Han, a minor sovereign received an oral transmission of a purple text stating: *"Governance shall be established in the northern cavern of Liangchang."* This likely became the basis for the name’s adoption.

Another examination of records shows that upon the First Emperor’s death, his passing was not announced. Instead, his carriage was laden with salted fish to conceal the truth. This corresponds to the sixth month of summer, aligning with apocryphal accounts. However, according to transmitted records, it was the tenth month of the thirty-sixth year, the *jianhai* month, which served as the first month of the Qin calendar. Thus, his tour extended only to Yunmeng. Otherwise, he would have died in the autumn of the thirty-eighth year. Since the Qin calendar was not yet established, it is impossible to definitively determine which month the *guichou* day fell in. This matter requires further detailed verification.)*

In the third year of Wang Mang’s *Dihuang* reign, on the *wushen* day of the seventh month (the twenty-fourth day of the month), he dispatched the emissary Zhang Chuan with a gift of one hundred *yi* of gold and five bronze bells to present to the Three Immortals of Mount Gouqu. *(Wang Mang’s system used gold as currency, calling it* yi*, shaped like coins but without holes, each weighing one tael and worth one thousand coins. One hundred* yi *thus equaled one hundred taels.)* In the seventh year of Emperor Guangwu’s *Jianwu* reign, on the *dingsi* day of the third month (the twenty-fourth day of the month), he sent the emissary Wu Lun with fifty *jin* of gold to offer to the Three Lords. These gifts were buried together at the highest point of Mount Xiaomao, where the earth had subsided, forming a mound of stones three to four *chi* deep. *(This refers to the present-day highest point of Mount Xiaomao, where the piled stones are located. These two events are not clearly recorded in oral transmissions.)*

In the second year of Emperor Ming’s *Yongping* reign, an imperial edict commanded prefectures and counties to repair and maintain the shrine of the Perfected Ones of Danyang’s Mount Gouqu. *(Upon the Three Lords’ initial attainment of the Dao, they rode white cranes and appeared on the mountain’s summit, witnessed by villagers who, moved by their efficacious responses to prayers, jointly established a shrine on the mountain’s eastern side, naming it the White Crane Shrine. During sacrifices, voices were sometimes heard, white cranes were seen within the curtains, or the sounds of music and performances were perceived. Thus, the people competed to serve and attend them.)*

This shrine still stands today in Ping’a Village on the mountain’s eastern side, where a woman surnamed Yin serves as its invocator. Subsequently, villages on the mountain’s western side each built their own shrines: the western slope of Great Mao became Wuwu Shrine, while the rear of Central Mao’s summit became Shuwu Shrine. All observe annual sacrificial rites with drums and dances, akin to blood sacrifices. By this time, they had fallen under the jurisdiction of the Western Brightness Bureau, no longer the retinue of true transcendents.)

At the uniquely lofty peak of Central Mao’s Black Ridge, the Lord of Life buried six thousand *jin* of cinnabar from the Western Hu’s Jade Gate in this mountain, at a depth of over two *zhang*. The sunken ground above is secured on all sides by small flat stones. On either side of the mountain, where spring water flows downward, the water takes on a faint reddish hue, and drinking it benefits one’s health. Below this mountain, on both sides, there are also small level areas suitable for quiet dwellings.

At the time, Zuo Yuanfang sought cinnabar from the Lord of Life and obtained only twelve *jin*. (Today, this ridge is strewn with many large stones, though no steady streams flow on either side of the summit. To the southeast, near the base, lies a long ravine, while to the southwest, near the base, there is also a small stream. South of the ridge, where recluses dwell, a gushing spring near the summit flows ceaselessly through winter and summer, though its water does not appear distinctly red. The level areas are habitable, with the best locations being near the ravine on either side. Zuo’s request for cinnabar likely occurred when he entered the grotto, intending to refine the Nine-Splendor Elixir in the furnace.)

Great Mao and Central Mao are connected, and within the long vale between them lies a continuous outcrop of stone, anciently called Mount Accumulated Gold. This mountain abounds in golden substances, and the area is well-suited for habitation. One should seek out watered sites to build quiet dwellings—this would be ideal. Any of these several locations will suffice for elixir refinement and the cultivation of the supreme Dao.

Before Central Mao and behind Great Mao, at the base where a long ravine extends east and west, the mountain opens outward to face a lodge. (This is the east-west ridge where recluses now dwell. The ridge is composed entirely of stone, its forms remarkably strange, with many perforations and side cavities winding deeply downward, some bottomless. When brittle objects are cast in, they produce prolonged, resonant echoes. The claim that the area abounds in golden substances likely refers to items cast here by transcendents over time. To the southwest stands a great stone cliff, split open to reveal a grotto within. After entering several *zhang*, the passage narrows until it no longer accommodates a person, though a rushing wind howls within. Just a few steps outside, a gushing spring flows year-round, providing water for those below. Near the ravine’s mouth, another spring surges upward, its waters swift—warm in winter and cool in summer. Directly opposite, slightly downslope, lies yet another aperture with an extraordinary gushing spring. Neither great floods nor severe droughts alter its flow; its water is slightly white, sweet, soft, and gentle, pouring forth endlessly. The only regret is its location on the mountain’s western side, preventing it from flowing east. One could, however, channel it separately to flow eastward for use.

Crossing this ridge to the southeast, a stone grotto’s water flows eastward, exceptionally fine. The site is secluded and sheltered, highly suitable for elixir refinement.)

Just beyond lies the aforementioned Jun Mountain. The sole concern is its proximity to the main path, where the rumble of carts and the voices of travelers are nearly audible.
