If this road were severed, cutting off the sounds of passing carts and footsteps, this would become the finest site. Careful consideration must be given to its suitability.

Of the dwellings on Mount Mao, only the present hermitage and the residence of the Chief Clerk at the southern cave mouth are truly excellent. The only regret is their remoteness and openness. Moreover, this entire mountain is free from venomous creatures—no centipedes, vipers, or scorpions. Though green snakes appear from time to time, they harm nothing. Tigers are also rare, and since ancient times, no one has been harmed here. Whether Daoist or lay, those who dwell in these mountains seldom suffer from febrile illnesses. The mountain’s virtue is vast and accommodating, never harsh or perilous. The only lament is the absence of verdant forests that remain lush year-round, as on the eastern ranges.

Since residing here in seclusion, the refining of qi has nurtured refinement, and in appearance, one appears far better. The mountain yields fine atractylodes and various medicinal herbs, and is most suited for pine and cypress—though none were planted by human hands, and they grow only where nature allows. Now, they are dispersed and cultivated annually.

At the Celestial Market Altar on Mount Mao, on all four sides lie treasures: gold and white jade, each weighing eight or nine thousand catties. They lie just over two *zhang* from the altar’s edges, buried nine *chi* beneath the earth.

Long ago, the Azure Lad of the Eastern Sea once rode a chariot drawn by solitary whirlwinds and flying wheels, traversing all the grotto-heavens’ mountains in his circuit. He once came to this mountain as well. On either side of the mountain flow springs, their waters infused with the essence of gold and jade. It would be most excellent to seek out a small, tranquil spot here for a hermitage. Drinking this water greatly benefits one’s essence, and it may be used to compound elixirs.

The stone altar of the Celestial Market lies precisely above the central Mystic Window of the grotto-heaven. This stone is from the Celestial Market Mountain of the kingdom of Anxi, and thus it is called the Celestial Market Altar Stone. During the reign of the Dark Emperor, he summoned the gods of the Four Seas to transport this altar stone and place it above the grotto-heaven. This was not done for Mount Juqu alone.

Beneath the immortals’ market altar lies the central window of the grotto-palace. The hollow within Mount Juqu’s belly is called the Grotto-Terrace Immortal Court. During the reign of the Dark Emperor, he summoned the gods of the Four Seas to transport the uncut jade and precious stones of Anxi’s Celestial Market Mountain, using them to fill the central Mystic Window above the grotto-heaven.

The Azure Lad of the Eastern Sea once rode his chariot of solitary whirlwinds and flying wheels, traversing all the mountains of the Grotto-Terraces. In each, he buried treasures of gold and white jade, eight or nine thousand catties each, around the market stone on all four sides, to suppress the yin-palace peaks. All grotto-heavens are thus—it is not only Mount Juqu.

The locals call the Celestial Market Altar Stone the "Immortals’ Market Altar," though their understanding is but a faint resemblance, unclear in detail. The traces of the Azure Lad’s whirlwind wheels remain distinct even now.

Of Mount Juqu’s features, all records now make clear distinctions—only the location of the Celestial Market Altar Stone remains uncertain when tracing its actual whereabouts.

The hidden dimensions should properly lie to the left and right of Great Mao Peak, yet no trace of their peculiarities is found in traversal. Perhaps they have been overgrown by earth and timber—this remains undiscussed. According to the *Baoming* records, whenever Master Zhao Cheng ascended the altar and let forth a long whistle, wind and clouds would instantly arrive. This suggests the site cannot be a minor location. Cloud vapors are often seen emerging and entering, always first rising from the northern yin side of Great Mao Peak—perhaps because the height and shade favor such manifestations. The Perfected One customarily drives a nine-dragon chariot, with the left flank steed named Whirlwind and the right flank steed named [ ]. Having trodden the mountain’s summit, he would point and mount the right steed. Today, atop Great Mao Ridge, a path extends eastward, its sides leveled as if by human labor, wide enough for wheel tracks. By common account, this is called the "Whirlwind Wheel Trace," and it remains unburied. Yet the stone altar is not manifest, and the buried gold treasures are equally difficult to locate. It is also doubtful that the grotto-heaven’s central Mysterious Window should lie so near the southern gate; perhaps it is instead among the Middle Mao peaks. The village elders, too, no longer know the location of the Immortals’ Market Altar. Since my seclusion here, seven or eight years have passed, and I have yet to exhaustively traverse the mountain—how much less could the careless masses ever fathom its depths? The reason I have not fully explored it lies in the lack of worthy companions to share the journey. The hardships of ascent are daunting, and traveling alone feels [ ]. Thus I have lingered here, a source of constant shame and regret.

Of late, I have wandered alone with my staff, hoping to record in detail all that I hear and see.

On the eastern side of Middle Mao Mountain lies a small cave. Its mouth is no larger than a dog’s burrow, barely wide enough for a person to enter. Yet the deeper one goes, the more spacious it becomes. A massive boulder seals the entrance, leaving only a small opening the size of a cup, guarded by the mountain spirits. This boulder occasionally opens of its own accord—it is called the Hidden Palace’s Lateral Gate. After purifying myself through fasting, I sought it out and found the way in, easier to traverse than the entrance to the Good-and-Constant Grotto. Within, many sandy paths twist and turn, and where they cross streams, passage is not always smooth. The route is also long, less convenient than the small Lateral Cave’s entrance. Descending straight down for three or four *li*, one arrives directly at the Hidden Palace’s Eastern Mysterious Side Gate. Two hundred paces inside the cave’s mouth, the darkness suddenly gives way to daylight. (This is the grotto-heaven’s eastern gate, its hidden dimensions now discernible. Yet I have not dared to venture in lightly. Earlier accounts claim the cavern palace extends thirteen or fourteen *li* beneath the earth, whereas this passage reaches the Side Gate in only three or four *li*. This is because the entrance lies near the mountain’s base, and the gate itself is not level. One must descend several more *li* after entering to reach the palace proper. The instant brightness within is due to inner light illuminating the space, not sunlight from outside.)

Great Mao Mountain also has a small cave on its southern face, nearly identical to the first. It is called the Southern Convenient Gate, its mouth likewise sealed by stone. However, if one purifies the heart with sincere fasting and directs devotion to the Controller of Fate, ascending the mountain on the second day of the month to extend invitations and offer prayers, one will naturally be granted an audience. If sincerity is absolute, how could the Hidden Palace remain unseen? What manner of man was Zuo Ci? (This is the eastern Convenient Gate on the southern face, likely located among the stone caves of the Cypress-Branch [Stone Dragon]. Many small caves dot this [Stone Dragon], making them difficult to distinguish at a glance. Only through profound spiritual resonance will the way open.)

Should one then consider entering, the "second day" refers to the second day of the twelfth month. According to tradition, there are two such days in a year—lest the eighteenth day of the third month, being clamorous and bustling, prove unsuitable for undivided devotion. Zuo Ci, having attained the immortal’s form, was readily perceived beyond the common. Thus, with a pure fast in the third month, he gained entry to the grotto. Though the Prefect’s bearing was noble and his advancement assured, his understanding remained obscured—how could he compare? These words were meant to encourage him. The Prefect’s later response echoed this sentiment, reciprocating in kind.

On the eighteenth day of the third month and the second day of the twelfth month, the Eastern Minister, the Lord Controller of Fate, ascends as the Supreme Perfected Sovereign of the Gathering Essence. The Perfected of the Great Void and the Azure Lad of the Eastern Sea convene upon Mount Gouqu, touring the grotto-chambers. Those who cherish the Dao and seek divine transcendence ought to purify themselves in advance and await these days, ascending the mountain to make their entreaties. Those of steadfast devotion and absolute sincerity will be met by the Three Lords, who shall draw them near, impart the essential Dao, and guide them through the grotto-gate. They shall avert the calamities of war and flood and behold the Sagely Lord of Great Peace. (As recorded in the *Book of the Central Lord...)

Those who regularly ascend the mountain on these two days to extend their welcome, offer prayers, and make entreaties—referring specifically to the second day of the twelfth month—rarely observe the eighteenth day of the third month, though it has occasionally fallen in the first month. Nowadays, the second day of the twelfth month is often bitterly cold with snow, and few from near or far come to the mountain. Only on the eighteenth day of the third month do officials and commoners gather in throngs, with hundreds of carriages and four or five thousand people, both Daoists and layfolk, men and women, their numbers resembling the crowds of a bustling city. They ascend the mountain together, chanting the *Lingbao* hymns of praise, and once the rites are concluded, they disperse. How can there be any among them with profound sincerity or secret communion, yearning to behold the divine Perfected? Even if one or two truly devoted souls appear, they are hindered by the clamor and defilement, unable to focus their minds or express their devotion. Thus, those who seek to be drawn near and receive favor have no means of attaining their goal.

Only in the secluded dwellings of the central cliffs, where access is restricted and the years kept pure, can one avoid the mingling of crowds. Since these places lie beyond the grotto and across the ridge, people naturally remain unaware of them.

On the southwestern slope of Mount Liangchang, there are habitable sites—former residences of the Lord Controller of Fate—where one may also compound the elixir. (When the Lord Controller first crossed the river, he established his dwelling here to purify himself and confront spirits and ghosts. Now, the term "slope" [*chui*] suggests a boundary or edge, and this location should properly lie west of the great ridge behind the Director’s residence. Yet elders today claim it is the flatland northwest of Great Mao Peak, among the pear trees of the low-lying area called *Xiabo*, said to be the former abode of the Lord Controller. The soil there is rich from cultivation, and many bricks, tiles, and relics of former habitation are unearthed, confirming it as a place once dwelt in. It is also said that the site is potent and must not be defiled. If this is indeed the case, then it should be described as west of Central Mao Peak, not so far as Liangchang. The statements of the Great Perfected are generally imprecise, mentioning whatever location they happen to cite.)

In former times, the forests and thickets at the foot of the mountain stretched far and wide, obscuring the sky and blocking the sun, offering countless secluded retreats. Now, with logging and farming, the land is open and accessible in all directions, and even the mountains have become exposed. Times change, and circumstances differ—no longer can the past serve as a standard. Yet these claims are not of long ago.

Such conditions have only grown more severe over the past fifty or sixty years. To the southeast of Liangchang, there is another habitable site. Among these places, one may find piled stones resembling the shape of a stove. Within the stove, there may grow a *jisheng* tree, its form curved like a canopy. This site is most excellent—only regrettable for its shallowness. Yet it remains sufficient in itself. (This place still exists today, though no longer with the *jisheng* tree of curved form. It lies near the northern cave’s flowing waters, and on either side, it borders the main road—hence its shallowness. Now, it has become desolate and remote, no longer a place one might go.)

To the northwest of the cave’s entrance lies a small, precarious plot of land, unstable and unsafe. Yet it would suffice for building a quiet retreat outside. (Today, much of this area has fallen into ruin, gradually encroached upon by villages. It is no longer fit for dwelling. In former times, it was said to be somewhat near the county, making travel convenient, and close to the cave’s entrance—hence its frequent mention. The outer quiet retreat was meant to accommodate traveling guests for rest, not as a place for one’s own cultivation. This further confirms the intent to keep proximity in mind. In the version compiled by Layman Gu, the section on the hermitage sites near this area was originally placed here. Yet in the copy reviewed by the scribe, it was not included in sequence but instead appeared separately before the residence built by the Prefect. In the Prefect’s later reply, he spoke of the northwest land’s instability and then proceeded to discuss Jun Mountain, ignoring the earlier mention of the Golden Village retreat—clearly indicating that the original was transmitted orally.)

Within the Juqu Mountains lies a place called Jun Mountain. This mountain is most fine. It also possesses gold.

One may go there to mine it, digging no more than one or two feet into the earth. When I was about to depart, I once buried gold here—those who wish to ingest gold may go and retrieve it. However, one must not use it to pursue private gain and thereby incur burdens.

(Nowadays, people no longer recognize the name Jun Mountain. Examining its form, it should resemble a mushroom standing alone, or perhaps it is [oral corruption of] "Cang" [苍], shaped like [ ]. According to the records, behind Great Mao Peak and east of Chang’a, in the valley where gold accumulates, there stands a solitary hill, towering like a Boshan incense burner. Moreover, it lies near the Gold-Accumulating Mountain, so this may well be the place. Today, it is overgrown with rocks and trees, and though the gold’s location is indicated in one or two spots, it would be difficult to search for. Only if you deign to grant permission would it be possible—no question of that. If one intends to undertake the work of refining and transmutation, this mountain’s front faces a long ravine where eastward-flowing water runs deep and secluded, possessing a favorable topography. Should construction ever be planned, I will provide further details.)

On Great Mao Mountain, there is a bronze tripod from the time of the Dark Emperor. (The ancient character for "tripod.") The tripod can hold four or five *hu* or so. Its casting and engravings are exceptionally fine, and it lies in the mountain’s highest spot, buried eight *chi* deep. A slab of stone covers it. The Dark Emperor commanded the Spirit of the Eastern Sea to conceal it here. (This was likely buried when the Anxi stone was relocated. Today, the highest point is strewn with rocks, and on auspicious days, Daoists from near and far all ascend to burn incense and pay homage. No grass or trees remain, only a small altar built of piled stones. There once stood a small tiled hut, but it collapsed in the wind. Since ancient times, emperors and kings have prized tripods because their two handles emulate the sun and moon, their three legs represent the Three Powers, and they can cook and transform the myriad things. Moreover, they possess the numinous ability to alter weight, concealment, and manifestation.)

The Middle Lord later replied, saying: "It was cast from the copper of Feather Mountain. (All mountains with grotto-heavens are like this.) Beneath Great Mount Mao there is also a spring. Below it, one may establish a quiet abode. The area near the water’s mouth is best—only slightly perilous and unstable. (Today, near the southern entrance of the great grotto, there is fine flowing water but many rocks. A short distance downslope, the land levels out. In recent generations, some have come to dwell here. Only in the early Song did the Daoist nun Xu Piaonü, supported by Prefect Lu Hui of Guangzhou, live for many years before her passing. Her disciple, surnamed Song, was a woman of lofty purity, untouched by worldly concerns. She grew old and died, and was buried south of the mountain. Song’s disciple, surnamed Pan, then inherited the residence and remains there to this day. During the Yuanhui era, several men also came to dwell in the area. By the early Qi dynasty, an edict was issued to Wang Wenqing of Jurong, who established a lodge here called Chongyuan. Halls, chambers, and side corridors were built, making it quite grand. Typically, seven or eight Daoists resided there, all supported by official stipends. For over twenty years, men and women from near and far have gathered here in succession, their dwellings stretching for several *li*, with over ten compounds. Yet those who truly study the supreme Dao are few—most merely perform Lingbao fasts and draft talismans. Recently, a woman has come to live at the grotto’s entrance, diligent in sweeping. She calls herself a ‘grotto officer’ and engages in shamanic divination, though much of it is frivolous and false. Such examples exist everywhere.)"

The eastern and western sides of Great Mao Mountain also have streams. During the late Jin dynasty, the place where the accomplished Daoist Ren Dun resided remains, with his alchemical furnace and earthen stove still extant. Today, several individuals such as Xue Biao live there. There is also Zhu Fayong, who dwells near a small hill, enjoying the expansive view but lacking water.

Liangchang Mountain faces the cave’s entrance to the east, gazing toward the ridge of the small hill. Atop it lie several thousand *jin* of buried bronze, sealed beneath massive stones. During the Han dynasty, the Qu family, who lived at the mountain’s base, were immensely wealthy, possessing hundreds of millions in wealth. They buried bronze vessels there, which remain to this day. There is also coinage, located on the western slope of the small northwestern hill.

(Today, this mountain still exists, though none know the exact location of the bronze or coins. Even if there are vague traces, they are unrecognizable. Upon examining the mountain’s terrain—its heights, depressions, and streams—it does not resemble a site of former villages or settlements. Perhaps due to the passage of ages, its features and traces have altered.)

Formerly, two letters were received, one on the first day of the third month and another on the eighth day of the eighth month. (These were received in a *yichou* year, while the previous year’s letter from the Prefect was dated to a *jiazi* year. The reply stated that the letter was simply placed in the quiet residence of the Zhu family in Shuxu, not delivered by Hua Qiao or Yang Jun.)

The letter of the first day of the third month states: *"Now we must reclaim the Red Stone Fields, making them a path for daily comings and goings."* Yet the task was never completed. It further says: *"How can one idly sit and watch the living and dead?"* This speaks to the sincerity of resolutely seeking the true Dao. *"If the heart is not in it, one cannot hold others accountable or ensure success."*

(The Red Stone Fields lie over ten *li* west of Central Mao Mountain, where a large pond once drew from the stream but fell into disrepair. The recluse has since restored it, cultivating over ten *qing* of fields. The Prefect once intended to conceal his traces by using the fields as a pretext, allowing him to gaze upon the sacred mountains from several vantage points. However, drought caused the pond to fail, and the plan was never realized.)

Thus, this letter gently mocks such claims.) Yet though he lacks a proper study, his letter speaks of fasting and purification. (There is also a reply to this: solemn words in memorials must not be lightly or recklessly composed—every movement and stillness must be thoroughly heard and understood.)

On the eighth day of the eighth month, the letter states: *"I humbly discipline myself and journey to the summit of Great Mao, beseeching to be specially received and recorded, that my eyes may behold your warm countenance and my ears hear your jade-like voice."* Such words reveal an urgent longing for the Dao. Having received your recent letter, I see your utmost sincerity and can thus diligently encourage your aspiration for the Way.

The Lord of Life resides in the Eastern Palace, and letters should not be combined indiscriminately, for virtue and rank differ in weight. (The Lord of Life permanently dwells in the Red City of Great Huo, while here there are only officials of the prefectural bureau. As positions vary in dignity, it is improper to compose a joint address. Though the two lords both reside in Huayang, their offices differ, and their letters must not share the same page. Generally, written memorials are not as effective as oral petitions—here one may clearly observe the proper forms and standards.)

We already know one another well, and the distinctions in our letters and correspondence are quite evident. How could I wish to cease them? (This refers to the Prefect’s careless habit of leaving letters in others’ quiet quarters before departing, fearing that if visitors arrive later, the contents may be leaked.)

The true numina guard their secrets closely, hence these words—to test the sincerity of one’s intentions. (The Prefect’s later reply to this was also rather artful and evasive.) These letters and documents must be kept from the sight of common folk. Should any layperson see them, they would dig up and desecrate the sacred mountain—your offense would be grave indeed. (This fears that cunning and avaricious men, learning of these golden treasures, would be capable of tunneling into the mountain, thus spreading the secret. Such a transgression would be no light matter. Not only would it violate the present admonition, but it would also leave an endless legacy of harm, burdening future generations. All must keep this strictly concealed.)

The above is the Lord of the Register’s reply to the Prefect’s earlier letter, concluding the discussion of matters concerning Mount Juqu. The Prefect’s original letter is no longer extant; only this later response remains, arranged here in order of the topics addressed.

The preceding twenty-one items were all transcribed by clerks. In my youth, around ten years of age, I heard elders in Xuxu (this should be written as *xu* [墟], though it has long been rendered as *xu* [虚]—the name of a village in present-day Shanxi) speak of how Mount Mao was once home to immortals and even had a marketplace, though they had long since departed. Later, when I asked Lord Bao about the mountain’s condition, he replied that the immortals still resided there and had not moved away. This mountain is the western gate of the Dongting Grotto-Heaven, connected to the Baoshan range within Lake Tai, which is why the immortals dwell within it. However, he mentioned only the Middle Immortal Lord by name, making no reference to his three brothers, nor distinguishing their seniority, nor noting that the Lord of Life holds a more exalted rank and governs separately in the Eastern Palace. As no records of this have been seen, it becomes clear that there are distinctions in rank and hierarchy.

I now respectfully heed your instruction and amend my understanding accordingly. (The Prefect later added in another reply: “I have drafts of this letter preserved, which is why I was able to compile and record it. However, the opening section of the previous page is missing, so this does not begin at the original start.” Lord Bao was Bao Qingjian of Jurong, universally called “Bao.” By the time of this reply, he had already received the transmitted records—this was the beginning of the *yichou* year.)

Proceeding three or four *li* directly down from the mouth of Little A, one arrives at the Eastern Dark Gate of the Yin Palace. Entering two hundred paces into this cavern mouth, the interior becomes as bright as daylight. I wonder whether this distinct radiance of the grotto-heaven is drawn from the sunlight to illuminate the cave, or if it possesses its own luminosity. Within this grotto-heaven, the palaces and bureaus are vast, said to comprise hundreds of halls and chambers. The officials here include the two Immortal Lords, brothers, as well as other immortals—how many men and women reside within? Are these merely stone chambers, or are there also halls of gold and jade? Are the palaces connected to Dongting and Baoshan? Did Lord Bao and his sister, Lady Zhu, ever enter this palace while in the mortal world? Were the two of them not yet deemed worthy of ascension and thus appointed as subterranean lords? Where is their seat of governance?

I, dull and ignorant, venture these questions, fearing they may give offense. (The matter of Lord Bao and his sister was not mentioned in the Middle Lord’s earlier letter; this must have been separately conveyed. Now, these questions are raised again, along with inquiries about the grotto, to which the *Dinglu* has provided further replies.)

The market’s stone platform and the name of the marketplace endure because of this. Today, I remain steadfast in my devotion to the Dao.

Gold and jade lie without intent—placed by the august spirit to further seal and secure. How could there be any violation of principle through excavation? This is merely a matter of insufficient scrutiny. To the left and right of the mountain flow spring waters, golden and jade-like essences, the very fluids of the land. Here, one may establish a quiet abode to conjoin the elixir. I shall bear this in mind. (The above responds to the question of residing by the Celestial Market’s springs, though no such abode has yet been established.)

I remain uncertain: from which generation does the Dark Emperor descend? A later-born, benighted and slow, I am unequal to much. I humbly seek instruction. (The Jade Pivot’s Yu Shui Wang was styled the Dark Emperor; external records confirm this. The Prefect’s doubt arose from oversight. This query is addressed further below.)

In your letter, you mentioned that east of Mount Mao lies a small cavern—the hidden palace’s side gate. Entering the Dao there is somewhat easier. Later, one should gradually cultivate through fasting and seek it out. The numinous lineage extends its regard, and thus one may forge golden gates and ascend jade chambers. The difference lies only in swiftness or delay; I dare not tarry in regret. (Such guidance has been given, yet if one does not swiftly seek the reclusive journey, how can it not be lamentable?)

So too do the host of spirits earnestly urge and encourage—it is told that Great Mount Mao likewise has a small cavern on its southern face, nearly identical to the first, called the Southern Convenient Gate. Joyful to receive your awakening counsel, I delight in this virtuous instruction. With single-minded devotion, I shall purify myself and renew my resolve. Having learned of the auspicious day, I shall arrive in secret at the appointed time. Though my sincerity be humble, the numinous powers will surely witness and reflect it. (This is the eastern gate of the southern face of the Dao, resembling the small eastern gate of the hidden palace.)

As for the identity of Zuo Ci—these words of praise and exhortation, this gracious regard extended even to one so lowly, only deepen my admiration. (Earlier records also mention this matter.)

It is told that at the southwestern edge of Mount Liangchang lies a place fit for dwelling, once the separate residence of the Lord of Life and Death, where elixirs may also be compounded. As I have seen in the transmitted records, my heart secretly aspires to seek out the former abode of the Lord of Life and Death, a lofty retreat. Yet I fear I cannot ascertain it with certainty. Now I shall quietly assess and seek a suitable place, and when the time comes, I shall seek further guidance. (Earlier records also mention this. The foundations have long since crumbled, making exact knowledge difficult—hence this request for further instruction.)

It is further told that to the southeast of Mount Liangchang lies another dwelling place, marked by piled stones like a stove and a *jisheng* tree resembling a curved canopy. One should seek out this site when the time comes.

It is told that northwest of the cavern’s mouth lies a small, precarious, and unstable plot of land, where an outer meditation hut may be built. My original intent was to establish both inner and outer meditation huts, and I shall now carefully consider what is most fitting. (I have never heard of such an outer meditation hut being built before, yet now there stands a clearly visible altar of piled stones. Tradition holds that an official once vanished here in a transformation—whenever I visit and pay my respects, I am moved to sigh with lingering emotion.)

It is told that Mount Jun is most excellent. Before departing, the Lord of Life and Death buried gold there, which those who wish to ingest gold may retrieve. I myself have long harbored the intention to compound golden liquid, though I dare not yet pursue this. If I dwell on the mountain for years, advancing daily in cultivation and learning, then in time, if the matter may be undertaken in secret, I shall seek your counsel at the proper moment. (The Writ of the Middle Lord states: "Before I departed, I buried gold," without specifying the Lord of Life and Death. The Prefect’s answer here is mistaken.)

It is told that on Great Mount Mao lies the bronze tripod of the Dark Emperor’s time, buried eight *chi* deep in the highest part of the mountain. This is an imperial site—□□□□□, the vessel left to instruct later generations. I am all the more grateful for this instruction, which may also serve as a place to establish a meditation hut. Deeply moved, I record this for my enlightenment.

As for the buried bronze and coins of the former Qu clan—this is entirely unrelated to our present purpose. Hearing of such distant matters, of eras long transformed, how can one not sigh with sorrow?

When I first received your letter on the eighth day of the eighth month, I set out at once to the empty village (this was still the former hamlet) and stayed briefly with my family. Then, upon receiving a letter from home, I learned that the response was that I should not yet ascend the mountain. Thus I returned, delivering this letter only to the quietude of Zhu’s household.

My heart is coarse and my nature base; as a mere mortal, I am defiled and impure. My devotion is not earnest, and I lack the power to ascend.

I have failed to grasp that my words have already reached your lofty attention. Having received your gracious examination, I find my words at odds with the matter—nothing remains but awe and trepidation.

*(Prefect Xuanting’s every movement is thoroughly known; even the slightest of my words, once uttered, is promptly reported and met with a response. Perhaps this was conveyed by Master Yang. Xu Fan’s household still remains. The later reference to "Xu Ou" should indeed be "Fan" afterward. Thus I know the location of the well and residence—his ancestor was once a disciple of the Prefect.)*

Long ago, I claimed the fields at Red Stone, which lie near the foot of the mountain and serve as a path for coming and going. This was my sincere intent. But soon after, a drought struck: the tenants reaped no harvest, and the embankment collapsed. Mu was then employed to oversee repairs, yet my strength has not sufficed to complete the task. It is not that I have abandoned the endeavor. Now that I dwell at the mountain’s base, I must resume cultivation. The distance from the cave’s entrance is the only reason I hesitate to settle there.

*(These fields lie west of the Greater and Lesser Mao peaks, close to the great mountain. The view for gazing upward and paying reverence is excellent. Yet when I say the cave’s entrance is distant, I mean specifically the northern entrance. Though the fields are fed by the ravine’s waters, in times of drought the flow dwindles, and building an embankment is difficult. I do not know whether I will ever reclaim them. The embankment still breaches; repairing it would require hundreds of laborers, but could then irrigate ten or so qing of land. The disciples at the hermitage also sow and plant here as the water allows. I have long wished to restore this embankment, both to honor the legacy of the past and to bestow a boon upon the people.)*

My letters are crude and artless—how could I cease writing them? Yet the purpose of letters is to convey one’s intent. Since you have deigned to favor me with your regard, and I have personally paid homage before the Celestial Lords and the two Immortal Ones, my wish is divinely inspired, bathed in sacred grace. Why then should I trouble you further with letters? This is what is meant by "having caught the fish, one forgets the trap."

*(This is merely a pretext to avoid ceasing correspondence, offered as a rhetorical deflection.)*

(In principle, this is most excellent.) I wonder where Lord Zuo is now. There is also Ge Xiaoxian, who likewise claimed to have attained the Dao. Where might he be now? Flesh-and-blood men are so fleeting—how I long to know.

*(Ge being a fellow townsman, I thus inquire. This passage also has a reply later in the text.)*

Here ends the reply from the Senior Archivist. All of this was written in his own hand, with many revisions, on white stationery, arranged in this order.

*(Year yichou. This line was originally written on the back of the paper.)*

*True Instructions, Scroll Eleven.*
*True Instructions, Scroll Twelve.*
*The Pivot of Spirit Verification, Second Chapter.*

Formerly, I received your letters and saw your intent, which deeply illuminated my own aims. In an earlier letter, you wrote of traveling west to Wu in your declining years, awaiting old age, and sighing in the depths of night without anyone to consult. Yet where sincerity is felt, attainment is never far off. In your next letter, you spoke of long-held aspirations and past affinities, expressing your constant wish that the Perfected One might transmit essential instructions, that you might gaze upon his radiant countenance and personally receive his sublime teachings. But if diligence has not yet reached the highest realms, and refinement has not been broadly cultivated, the swift pursuit of true essentials cannot yet be anticipated. As the *Book of Later Han* says...

Here begins the translation:

---

I was initiated into the mysteries at the Golden Portal of the Primal Register, transcending worldly traces and ascending to the pure void. How vast and distant is the mark of the mysterious! How deep and remote the abyss of wondrous caverns! To master oneself and purify the heart, to bathe in the fragrant streams—if one can achieve this, now is the time.

Your last letter spoke of glimpsing essential principles and cultivating the Five Spirits. You claimed to have journeyed west to the gardens of *Menliang* and roamed east to the Mystic Isles, yet without striving to the utmost, seeking only to be guided and drawn forth. Such words, if truly rooted in the heart, may indeed open the way. The cultivation of the Five Spirits, too, has reached my ears. Yet the Dao drifts upon external traces, untouched by inner truth. Thus, the cloud chariot’s sacred shafts, though aligned, remain distant.

In ancient times, Zeng Shen and Hua Qiao (from this, we know Hua Qiao was once graced by the descent of the Perfected) responded to the summons of the age, for matters may arise unexpectedly. The waters need not be still; if one has the destiny but lacks the resolve, it is the very affliction Shen Gong lamented—meeting the ultimate yet failing to act, like spilled water that cannot be gathered again. Thus, the ancients said: *"When the time comes, do not hesitate; the mountain recluse claps his hands in joy."*

Those who study the Dao must not rely solely on their innate endowment but must preserve and uphold purity and wholeness. Then may they gallop toward the age of the pine, turn the wheel through the Purple Clarity, where the numinous vision resounds on all sides, and the mysterious tones harmonize in chorus. Jade chimes resound through the clouds, attuned without deliberation—this may be called the hidden treasure of the secret Dao, the supreme flourishing of true radiance.

You, Jian, have long held such aspirations; now I merely return to your former intent. (This letter appears to be Master Pei’s words, while Yang’s letter does not connect to the later questions of the Mystic Emperor. It may not be Master Zhong’s reply. Moreover, the original four letters of the Chief Clerk no longer survive.)

As for the Mystic Emperor: of old, the Yellow Emperor’s son, Changyi, wed the daughter of Mount Shu, and she bore Gaoyang, whose virtue was titled *Ruipian* and *Yupian*. *Ruipian*’s father dwelled in the land of Weak Waters, while *Yupian* himself was tempered at the ford of the Seven Rivers—this is the Mystic Emperor. Wielding the trust of the myriad spirits and overseeing the host of gods to guide all things, he commands the hundred qi, summons thunder and lightning. Thus, riding his chariot of primordial union, he journeyed north to Youling, south to Jiaozhi, west across the Flowing Sands, and east to the Coiling Tree. All beings, great and small, all gods within the light of sun and moon, were subject to him. Traversing the four directions and circling the eight extremities, wherever there were mountains of jade terraces or mounds of yin palaces, he transported the stones of Anxi to seal and fill them. Casting bronze from Mount Yu into precious tripods, he offered one each to the divine peaks of the Grotto Mountains—not merely Mount Juqu alone. This is the so-called Mystic Emperor.

(What follows is Master Zhong’s reply to the four inquiries posed earlier, written on the blank space above. The account of *Ruipian* and *Yupian* aligns with the *Five Talismans*, except the latter omits the matter of burying the tripods.)

Bao *Qingjian* and his sister were both seventh-generation descendants of Li Zhan and Zhang Lü, originally from the northern village of Duling. They kept an inn at the Wei Bridge, accumulating hidden virtue and yearning for the Dao in hopes of transcending life.

---

Thus did their blessings extend to Bao *Qingjian* and his kin, enabling them to undergo transformation through kalpas, to alter their surnames and renew their lives—united as brothers. Though their ancestral roots differed, the shade of virtue they shared was the same, and so they were destined to be born into the same clan. Now, both serve as lords of the underworld, dwelling within the Grotto Palace.

What Bao *Qingjian* studied was originally shallow and meager, and his nature was further hindered by entanglements, so his attainment was not great. Should you wish to know the matter, it is thus: it resembles your own seventh-generation ancestor, Xu Zhao, styled Zi’a, who possessed the benevolence of rescuing the dying and the virtue of relieving the starving. Thus did his cloud-like shade flow to posterity, his hidden merit casting its blessing downward. It is for this reason that you now harbor a heart inclined toward the immortals and the perfected—this, too, arises from a cause. All things have their convergences of conditions; none attain them idly.

(At the time of this writing, the Master’s instructions had not yet been transmitted, which is why the discussion touches upon Zi’a’s meritorious shade. Bao was also versed in the numinous, yet he esteemed the arts of the bedchamber, hence the term “entanglements.” Later, he employed the *Tai Xuan Yin Sheng Fu* of the Yin Lord as the method of Great Purity’s corpse-liberation—this must be the highest rank among the lords of the underworld. Thus do the threads of fate and affairs intertwine in this manner. As for the good and evil of people today, how could they be said to arise without cause?)

As for Ge Xuan: he was skilled in transformations and illusions but clumsy in the mastery of his own body. Now, he has merely attained undying life, not true immortality. Initially dwelling on Mount Chang, he later entered Gai Zhu. He could ride tigers and command ghosts, accomplishing all things, yet he nearly failed to receive his celestial office.

They were often accompanied by Xie Zhijian, Huang Ziyang, and Guo Shengzi.

(Ge Xuan, styled Xiaoxian, was the paternal cousin of Ge Hong’s grandfather and the master of Zheng Siyuan. In his youth, he entered the mountains and attained immortality, yet none in his time could fathom his whereabouts. Legends spoke of an immortal in the Eastern Sea who sent him letters addressing him as "Immortal Lord," and thus Ge Hong likewise referred to him as such. This is why the Prefect posed the question, and the answer given here identifies him merely as an earthbound immortal. As for the *Lingbao* scriptures’ mention of the "Left Immortal Lord of the Great Ultimate," is this not an empty claim?)

Zu Ci now resides on Mount Xiao Kuo, frequently traveling to and fro and often appearing in these parts. He will soon receive his celestial office. Zu Ci’s complexion remains youthful, having gained the full benefit of the Nine-Splendor Elixir refined in the furnace.

(Zu Ci, styled Yuanfang, was a disciple of Li Zhongfu and the master of Ge Xuan. When the Wei Emperor Cao Cao and his son summoned all the masters of esoteric arts, Zu Ci was among them. At the end of the Jian’an era, he crossed the Yangtze in search of mountains, eventually entering a grotto. There, he begged for cinnabar to compound the Nine-Splendor Elixir—a method recorded in the *Central Scripture of Great Purity*. Mount Xiao Kuo is Mount Xiao Kuocang, located north of Qiaoxi in Yongjia. All these figures are of this kind.)

The arts he mastered were many, yet his disciples who sought immortality remained inferior—all for failing to encounter the teachings of the Three Supreme Grades. This is why Master Xu sighed with such feeling.

On Mount Juqu, there are five gates. If one sets the mind, resolves the will, and observes pure abstinence for three months, then ascends to seek these gates, all may be attained at once. Attainment allows entry, yet people themselves fail to undertake the fast and the search.

You now ask to know the dimensions of the palaces, the number of men and women, and the count of those who preside over them. I shall explain these matters to you in turn. Your letter, too, seems overly exhaustive—why such diligence? Why such diligence? Moreover, one must first present a petition to the Director of Destinies. Only when the Director commands an answer regarding the intricacies of the palaces may I dare speak of them. These are, after all, the Director’s separate precincts; even I cannot claim to be the rightful master of the Terrace of the Washbasin.

(Note: The subsequent discussion of various officials and figures is based on what was already presented to the Director of Destinies, thereby receiving full disclosure. Here ends the postscript to the *Record of the Determined*, answering the inquiries of the Prefectural Governor. Five additional entries follow, appended by Yang, sent from the Dark Emperor.)

The Eastern Minister-Director of Destinies oversees the host of perfected beings on Mount Tai, governing the myriad spirits of Wu and Yue. Truly, he may be called one whose Dao runs deep and whose virtue stands high, who subdues the multitudes of spirits. Jia Xuandao, Li Shusheng, Yan Chengsheng, and Chuan Daoliu—all once received the essential teachings of the Eastern Minister.

Xuandao was a native of Hedong, born at the close of King Wei of Zhou’s reign. Shusheng was a man of the commandery, born in the time of Emperor Yuan of Han. Daoliu was a native of Beidi and served as a general in the palace during Emperor Ling of Han’s reign. Chengsheng was a man of Wu who, in the time of Emperor Liu of Later Han, held the post of commandant of Wudang Commandery. All studied with utmost diligence and attained the true Dao. Now they reside in Xiaoyang Mountain, a branch of Mount Tai—these are what are called *earthly perfected*.

Those who come to make trial of this path do not follow a single route. Some, too, may have transgressed through the faults of others. This is most difficult to resolve. In such moments, can one afford not to be cautious?

These four are subordinate to the Directors of Destiny, charged with examining those who come to test their study of the Dao. Thus, the Senior Scribe sent a letter to Jia, and Jia presented it to the Directors of Destiny, who likewise replied—all of which is recorded in the preceding scroll. These individuals hold minor ranks and are not prominent in external records. King Wei of Zhou should properly be identified as King Weilie of the Warring States period. Though the commandery of Hedong had not yet been established at that time, the name of the place already existed. The Han court had no official post of *Palace General*; it should likely be *Palace Gentleman-Commandant*.

This was received by the Perfected of Purple Yang on the twentieth day of the sixth month. The following entry was copied by an assistant. On the night of the fifteenth day of the seventh month, the Lord of Mao Peak received the letter and delivered it to Minister Xu (that is, the Senior Scribe, who is later to become the Left Minister of Supreme Clarity).

Profound resonance gathers in stillness, and distant aspirations set the pattern. Their numinous radiance stands forth, twin principles naturally distinct. Surely they shall soar like the roc to the celestial vault, their cloud-fans stirring the spirit’s origin. Loftily they shall shake the jade towers, guiding the reins of the flowering perfected. Truly this may be called *advancing in marvelous form*, a sight beyond all comparison.

Here, in the Palace of Cavernous Yin, within the Lower Terrace of the Inner Observatory, the wind stirs no jade-chime drums, and the grasses flow with no streams of gold or silver. Though before, when the light of the true luminants pooled and the five symbols turned in motion, it was but a side pavilion of the Bending Stream, a floating reflection of the Lesser Heavens—how could it be worthy of the profound concern of Your Excellency’s office? Yet you have deigned to turn the orchid-perfumed truth toward this humble place. Still, the mirror reflects without fail, and the round of contemplation must penetrate. Thus, matters beyond the ordinary are celebrated in song, and traces are as one. If sincerity is what you seek, I shall conceal nothing.

Pondering the radiance of Shan Jian’s layered brilliance, one may hope to reach the destined gathering. Even the luminous clarity of the wise is but attained through shared study. Let it not be that we, your humble servants, stand abashed in the shadow of such wind.

This alone is to be magnified.

*(This reply was received a month after the previous letter, continuing the discussion of the desire to visit the Cavernous Palace. The phrase "the radiance of Shan Jian’s layered brilliance" seems to allude to the *Qinglu* text of the Wei tradition, though the names do not align. Since it was earlier stated that "bowing to the profound and gazing upon the pure before the jade blade," this passage must carry another significance.)*

---

The above passage is from Yang’s letter. Among the recorded officials of the Cavernous Palace, there are the Left and Right Administrators of Central Governance, corresponding to the modern offices of Chief Scribe and Marshal. There is also the Northern River Controller of Destiny, who oversees the Water Bureau’s assessments. This office typically oversees the Nine Palaces’ Forbidden Guardians. The Forbidden Guardians are responsible for guiding those destined to become the Seed People.

*(Within the Cavernous Palace, below the Two Lords, these three offices are considered the highest.)*

The Left Administrator of Central Governance corresponds to the Grand Treasury’s Chief Scribe. Formerly, Han Chong held this position. Chong, styled Changji, was a native of Biling in Wu Commandery. From youth, he was devoted to the Dao. The immortal Wang Yu of Linwu Mountain once transmitted to him the method of the Flowing Pearl Elixir. Chong practiced it diligently, and it proved highly efficacious.

Wang Yu told him, "By practicing this Way, you may also advance in official rank without hindering your ascent to immortality." Chong thus entered officialdom, gradually rising to become Magistrate of Wanling. He governed with benevolence and administered the people through the Dao. Tigers and wolves kept their distance, and locusts did not alight within his borders. Later, he was promoted to Governor of Runan, where he recognized the talent of his clerk, Yuan An. An would later rise to the position of Minister over the Masses. Contemporaries universally acknowledged Chong’s discernment in judging men.

When Empress Yin was interred, the wives of the two-thousand-*shi* officials from the capital and neighboring commanderies were to gather at the imperial mausoleum. Yet Chong alone maintained a life of purity and simplicity. His wife, resentful, wept before him. An imperial edict inquired into the matter, and Minister of Ceremonies Feng Xi replied, "Governor of Runan Han Chong embraces austerity and reveres the Dao, forgetting his own form. Though he holds high office, his wife spins and weaves herself. His governance is benevolent and unburdensome, treating the people as if they were wounded. Profound in wisdom and erudition, he possesses the discernment of a noble man—truly a luminary in the darkest night, a Zichan for Your Majesty. His wife, unaware of the virtue of frugality, weeps over her lack of fine clothing, which only serves to illuminate Chong’s illustrious power."

The Emperor marveled at this and increased Chong’s salary, raising his rank to that of a full two-thousand-*shi*. Later, when Emperor Xiaoming toured Runan, he visited Chong’s residence. Chong had sent his wife to stay with a solitary elderly woman. Upon hearing this, the Emperor sighed and said, "Han Chong is one who remains uncorrupted even after a hundred refinings." He bestowed upon Chong fifty bolts of fine silk. Chong governed the commandery for fourteen years, and his transformative governance became widely renowned.

Among the finest under heaven, at the age of seventy-four, Yuwei Xuan then transmitted to him the method of hidden liberation. Thus he departed and entered Great Mount Huo, where he received from Yuwei Xuan the art of "Disappearing Transformation through the Mud-Pill Purple Portal" to transcend the world. Now he resides in the grotto as the Left Director of the Central Bureau.

*(The* Han Shu *records similar events, though the wording differs slightly. Yuan An, styled Shaoping, initially served as a county functionary. Promoted through recommendation, he eventually rose to the rank of the Three Excellencies. He passed away during the reign of Emperor He and was the great-grandfather of Yuan Shao. In the Jin dynasty, there was also Feng Xi, who likewise served as Grand Master, their fame and rank being comparable. Since Han had achieved hidden liberation, this must have been a "recorded" [feigned] corpse. In present-day Jinling, atop the heights, stands Han’s tomb—a towering and immense mound, traditionally called "Han’s Tomb." It is suspected to resemble the tomb of Peach Lord, or perhaps this is merely an empty barrow, though the world calls it the tomb of Sun Ce’s general, Han Dang.)*

It is said that Wang Yuwei Xuan was a Gentleman-in-Attendance during the reign of King Zhuang of Chu. He received his arts from Jade Lord.

*(If this refers to King Zhuang of Chu from the Spring and Autumn period, the office of Gentleman-in-Attendance does not seem to match ancient posts, and the Han-era King of Chu did not bear the posthumous title "Zhuang.")*

The Left Director of the Central Bureau holds a position analogous to that of a Marshal. Now one Liu Yi, styled Zixiang, occupies the post. Originally from Yingchuan, Yi was devoted to the Dao and virtue from a young age, though his family was immensely wealthy. He often aided the destitute and troubled, delighting in performing hidden acts of virtue and secret kindness.

When Zhang Jizha of Chenliu was traveling to mourn his teacher’s passing, his carriage broke down and his oxen were exhausted. Yi encountered him at the border of Runan. They spoke, but Yi did not reveal his name. Instead, he pushed the carriage and offered his own oxen for Zhang to ride. His acts of aiding the dead and rescuing the destitute were not limited to this one occasion.

Later, Yi resided in Chang’an. He was recommended as a *jisuan* official and arrived at the capital. The Emperor, admiring his heart, appointed him Gentleman of the Palace and later promoted him to Governor of Chenliu. Within five hundred *li* of leaving Chang’an, he continued to gather the dead, succor the poor, diminish his own wealth to aid others, and practice virtue that reached even to Yangping. There, he encountered Master Ma Huang, who said to him: “Your benevolence has moved Heaven and Earth, and your hidden virtue has touched gods and spirits. The Supreme One is about to reward your sincerity. He has sent me to lead you to the Way of longevity. I am an immortal official. Are you willing to follow me?”

Yi then kowtowed and struck his breast, saying: “From my youth, I have cherished the pursuit of longevity. Now, by good fortune, I have met a divine immortal. I beg to serve and attend you.” Master Ma Huang then took Yi into Mount Tongbai, where he transmitted to him the Eight Arts of Concealing the Earth and the method of ingesting the essence of the Five Stars. He later bestowed upon him the name Donghua. Upon arriving in the grotto, Yi was appointed Left Director of the Central Bureau of the Register of Destiny.

*(The* Book of Han *states: Yi, styled Zixiang, was a native of Yingyin. His family was immensely wealthy, and he was always generous in his aid, though he did not regard it as a personal favor. Once, while traveling in Runan, he encountered Zhang Jizha of Chenliu, who was hastening to his teacher’s funeral. Zhang had met with cold and ice, and his carriage had broken down, leaving him stranded on the road. Yi saw him and said: “You are diligent in fulfilling your duty to the end and hastening to righteousness—your journey should not be delayed.” He then dismounted, gave Zhang his carriage, and departed without revealing his name, urging his horse onward. Zhang suspected it was Yi. Later, when he deliberately passed through Yingyin to return the borrowed carriage, Yi shut his gates and declined to see him, pleading illness. He remained steadfast in his resolve, refusing all official summons.)*

In Henan, he was appointed as *gongcao* in Linjun. Later, when the Yellow Turban rebels rose, Yi provided relief to those in dire need, sustaining hundreds with food. When clansmen or villagers perished, he arranged their burials; when widows or widowers lacked means to marry, he aided their unions. When Emperor Xian relocated the capital to the Western Capital, Yi was recommended as *shangji yuan*. At the time, the roads were beset by bandits, so he traveled by night and hid by day, eventually reaching Chang’an. The emperor commended his loyalty and diligence, appointing him *yilang* and later promoting him to Prefect of Chenliu. Yi then distributed his treasures, keeping only his carriage and horses. Loading these, he set out eastward. Hundreds of *li* beyond the pass, he encountered a scholar-official who had fallen ill and died by the roadside. He exchanged his horse for a coffin and removed his own robes to shroud the body. Soon after, he came upon an old acquaintance starving and stranded on the road. Unable to bear leaving him, he slaughtered the ox pulling his carriage to feed him. Others urged him to stop, but Yi said: “To see the dying and not save them is no act of a man of resolve.” Thus, they perished together from hunger. These accounts differ only in minor details, so both are recorded here in full.

Yi, styled Zixiang, follows the pattern of matching given names with their meanings, for *xiang* (相) also signifies "mutual." *Xiang* is pronounced *xiliang* (息亮切). It remains unclear which of the two is the correct interpretation.

The *Liexian Zhuan* by Ma Huang records that during the Yellow Emperor’s reign, there was a horse-master named Ma Shi. The *Dinglu Fu* lists a *Dianbing Zhifa Lang* (典柄执法郎), who was Chunyu Zhen, styled Shuxian, overseer of trials for those pursuing the Dao. Zhen was a native of Shangyu in Kuaiji. During the reign of Emperor Huan of Han, he served as magistrate of a county in Xuzhou, and later, during Emperor Ling’s reign, as an aide to the Grand General. From youth, he was devoted to the Dao, skilled in arts and calculations, and practiced the ingestion of sesame and *huangjing* (黄精). Later, he retreated into the Wu Mountains of Wumu to live in seclusion, where he encountered the immortal Huiche Zi, who transmitted to him the *Hongjing Dan Jing* (虹景丹经). Through cultivation, he attained the Dao and now resides in the grotto as *Dianbing Zhifa Lang*.

The *Zhouyi Cantong Qi* states: "During Emperor Huan’s reign, Chunyu Shutong of Shangyu received arts from Xu Congshi of Qingzhou. Observing the celestial patterns, he divined disasters and anomalies, often with accurate results. Due to his mastery of these arts, the commandery recommended him as a *fangshi* (方士), and he was later promoted to Market Director of Luoyang." These accounts differ only in minor details.

There is no Wumu Mountain in Wu; however, both Lou and Wuxing have Tianmu Mountains, which may be the same. Huiche Zi does not appear prominently elsewhere.

On the night of the first day of the twelfth month, the *Dinglu Jun* (定录君) spoke of the Northern River Controller of Destiny, whose post had long been vacant and was temporarily filled by Tao Jun. Jun appeared to be a native of Qiantang. In his youth, he served as an administrative aide in the commandery, later carrying his satchel to the Imperial Academy to study. He was well-versed in classical arts, disasters, and anomalies. In his later years, he became Governor of Jiaozhi. At the end of the Han, he abandoned worldly life and entered Zengcheng Mountain to study the Dao. There, he encountered Dongguo Youping, a man from the Qin era who had long lived in seclusion in Zengcheng and attained the Dao. Youping taught Jun the arts of ingesting the Nine Essences, refining *qi*, and aligning the heart with the auxiliary stars. Jun cultivated these practices and achieved the Dao, now residing in the grotto.

As Northern River Controller of Destiny, he oversees the examination and punishment of the Water Office. Though this post falls under the jurisdiction of the Court of Fixed Records, it ultimately receives its directives from the Eastern Flowery Palace. Tao Jun, styled Wengzhong—(The *Book of Han* makes no mention of this; his tomb now lies at Linping in Qiantang, its mound and altar still distinct, his descendants yet remaining. Locals have at times heard the sounds of drums and horns near the site, so none dare encroach or damage it, all knowing it as the Tomb of Tao, Controller of Destiny. The recluse Du of Qiantang, who served Jing Chan, once composed poems with a fellow hermit in praise of his virtue; these are collected separately.)—Dongguo Youping likewise left no other notable traces.

Zhang Jizi was to become the Immortal Marquis of the Supreme Ultimate. Jizi was Zhang Feng of Henei, styled Gongxian. In his youth, he was known simply as Jizi. This man, too, gained renown early. The Grand Tutor Yuan Wei admired his lofty conduct and married his daughter to him. The daughter’s attire was extravagant and splendid, yet Feng regarded her no more than a stranger. Only after she changed her garments did they become husband and wife. Later, he abandoned the world and entered the mountains, where he encountered the Mountain Chart Noble Scion, a transcendent and former grandee of King Ai of Zhou. The Noble Scion taught Jizi the method of refining the Nine Cloud Waters and strengthening the *qi* through osmanthus refinement. Jizi cultivated this practice and attained the Dao, now residing in the Eastern Flowery Palace as an appointee of the Supreme Ultimate. He sometimes serves as Minister of the Nine Palaces, jointly managing water governance and examinations with the Northern River Marquis. The Northern River Controller of Destiny may also hold the title of Marquis of Prohibitive Protection, as their duties overlap. The Northern River Controller of Destiny likewise governs within a grotto-heaven, presiding in tandem with Zhang Jizi. (The *Book of Wei* states...)

Zhang Fan, styled Gongyi, was a native of Xiuwu in Henei. His grandfather [pronounced *qian*] served as Minister over the Masses in the Han, and his father, Yan, as Grand Commandant. Yuan Wei sought to marry his daughter to Fan, but Fan declined. Naturally serene and devoted to the Dao, he declined all summons to office. Later, he served as Gentleman Consultant and participated in the military affairs of Chancellor Wei Wu, earning profound respect. Fond of relieving the destitute, he kept no surplus wealth at home. He died in the seventeenth year of Jian’an.

His younger brother, Cheng, styled Gongxian, was also renowned. Appointed Gentleman Consultant for his upright conduct, he later served as Remonstrating Censor, Grandee of Zhao Commandery, and Prefect. He followed Wei Wu on a western campaign, falling ill and dying in Chang’an. The accounts of their names and styles vary greatly. Cheng and Feng appear similar but were not Yuan’s sons-in-law. If this were Fan, his style would differ. Upon close examination of their deeds, it seems more likely he was the elder brother.

The *Book of Wei*’s biography of Wang Xiu further states: Xiu frequently traveled to Nanyang, often lodging at Zhang Feng’s residence. When Feng’s entire household fell ill, Xiu tended to them. However, the Zhang brothers had once taken refuge in Yangzhou under Yuan Shu—never under Liu Biao—and thus should not have been in Nanyang. These inconsistencies cast doubt.

The Mountain Chart Noble Scion appears in the *Arrayed Transcendents’ Biographies*, as recounted by the Lady of Zhonghou. (Investigating the grotto affairs, all were described by these two lords. Yet in these two entries, only the Lady of Zhonghou is mentioned—likely because she was originally an official in the Eastern Flowery Palace.)

I have heard from Dou Shi of the *Yi Qian*, who said: The Northern River Controller of Destiny, the Marquis of Prohibitive Protection, seems to have been modeled after someone. This must surely be the case.

(This Dou clan refers to Qiongying. The phrase "seems to have been modeled after someone" likely points to the Prefectural Administrator. Thus, the Central Lord’s account states: the verification of the Northern River Controller of Destiny is confirmed. It is feared that after receiving higher teachings, she would no longer hold this position. Yet overseeing the seed-people accords with the role.)

The Protector of Destiny’s Office employs many female officials. Among the Three Offices’ staff, there are seven—four women and three men. The Seven Attending Gentlemen of the Bright Dawn serve in a capacity akin to the present-day Imperial Censor and Deputy Censor, all subordinate to the Eastern Flowery and Square Cauldron Palaces. The Protector of Destiny oversees them collectively. One of the Attending Gentlemen of the Bright Dawn, Zhou Yuanzi, was the daughter of Zhou Chang, former Prefect of the Han River South Commandery. Chang, a native of Ancheng in Runan, was devoted to hidden virtue, his merits imperceptible. Once, during a severe drought while serving as Prefect of the River South, he gathered and buried over ten thousand unclaimed remains of travelers who had perished beside Luoyang, erecting a charitable burial mound and offering sacrifices. Rain fell abundantly in due time, yielding a bountiful harvest. Many of his deeds were of this kind. The Supreme One, recognizing Chang’s hidden conduct, commanded Yuanzi to receive transformation in the Southern Palace and attain transcendence. She now resides in the grotto. Yuanzi, too, had loved the Dao from youth, consuming *fuling* for thirty years. Later, she encountered Shi Changsheng, who instructed her in the art of transformation and escape—transformation and escape being the highest form of liberation from the corpse. (Chang was the younger cousin of Zhou Jia. Of a kind and sincere disposition, he served as Prefect of the River South during the reign of a certain emperor.)

In the second year of *Yongchu*, a summer drought persisted, and despite prolonged prayers, no response came. Thereupon, (Chang) gathered and buried over ten thousand corpses, and timely rain fell in abundance. His rank later reached *Guanglu Xun*.

*Mingchen Shilang* Zhang Taozhi was the mother of Zhu Yuyu, Han *Sili Xiaowei* of Pei. When Yuyu was executed alongside Chen Fan, his mother had long practiced hidden virtue. Upon hearing of her impending relocation, she was only then appointed *Shilang*.

(Ju Yuyu was a native of Pei. Among the "Eight Worthies" of the *Huan-Ling* era, he was later implicated in the case of the partisans. Li Ying and Du Mi were both imprisoned and died, though not contemporaneous with Chen Fan.)

*Mingchen Shilang* Xia Fu, styled Zizhi, was a native of Chenliu. From youth, he loved the Dao, consuming *zhu* and *er*, and harmonizing with *yunmu*. Later, he entered Mount Wu, where he received the *Lianhun* method from the Red-Bearded Master. He also encountered the True Man of Tongbai, who transmitted to him the *Huangshui Yunjiang* method. He attained the Dao and now resides in the grotto.

In his youth, Fu was summoned by the public office, yet he hung the summons on a mulberry tree and departed—such was his lofty and unyielding resolve.

(Later Han records and the *Biographies of Lofty Recluses* both state: Xia Fu was a native of Yu in Chenliu. In his youth, he was a scholar. During Emperor Huan’s reign, he was recommended for his forthright speech but declined the post. Of upright character, he did not align with the partisans. Resented by the eunuchs, he was ensnared in the proscription of the partisans. To evade disaster, he cut his beard and hair, altered his appearance, and fled to the forests of Mount Linlü, where he worked as a laborer. Later, he returned home, shut his door, and refused to see anyone. He died before the partisan ban was lifted. The Red-Bearded Master appears in the *Liexian Zhuan*; Tongbai refers to the Right Assistant King.)

The remaining individuals cannot be recounted in full, but all were chosen for their steadfast integrity and uprightness, their embodied seclusion and spiritual clarity—qualities befitting a *Shilang*, regardless of gender.

(As previously mentioned, there were seven in total; here, only two women and one man are described.)

The above was recounted by the Lord of Life-Preservation.

(This likely follows the *Jie Zhonghou*’s announcement.)

Commander of the *Tongchu* Prefecture, Liu Wenrao—Liu Kuan of Hongnong—loved the Dao from youth. He was once recommended as a *Han Fangzheng*.

Having been gradually promoted to Prefect of Nanyang, he regarded the people as his own children. His anger never showed on his face, nor did harsh words ever pass his lips. He practiced hidden virtue, rescuing the cold and destitute, so that the myriad people rejoiced and clung to him as to their own parents. Later, he became Minister of Education and Grand Commandant. The emperor bestowed wine upon him, but he prostrated himself and fell asleep. When the emperor inquired as to the reason, he replied: "Your servant bears heavy responsibilities and grave duties, and I am constantly anxious as if drunk. This morning, I sent my servant to the market to buy vegetables. The servant stole the money to drink wine, and when evening came, he returned without vegetables. I lay down in the chamber, still without food. When I awoke, I cursed him as a dead dog. After scolding him, I immediately tied my sash and came to court, fearing the servant might take his own life afterward. This is why I was so preoccupied that I unknowingly fell asleep. I humbly beg your mercy and forgiveness."

Liu Kuan’s heart was filled with benevolence and love, and all his actions were of this kind. At the age of seventy-three, he one day encountered the Master of Azure Valley, who descended into his bedchamber and bestowed upon him the method of the Staff of Release. He was about to depart for Mount Taihua, where he practiced the nine-breath qi-cultivation and was further instructed in the furnace-and-fire elixir formula. Through cultivation, he attained Daoist perfection.

Now, he resides in the grotto as the Youthful Prime Marshal of the Upper Marquisate, overseeing those who first embark upon the study of the Dao.

*(The* Hou Han Shu *states: Liu Kuan, styled Wenrao, was a native of Hongnong in Huayin. His father was named Qi. During the reign of Emperor Shun, he served as Minister over the Masses. Kuan was a man of prudence and sincerity. Once, a man who had lost his ox came to identify it among Kuan’s carriage oxen. Without a word, Kuan unyoked the ox and gave it to him, then walked home. Shortly after, a man of conscience found the ox and returned it. Overcome with shame and fear, the man was dismissed with thanks by Kuan. In the eighth year of Emperor Huan’s* Yanxi *era, he became Governor of Nanyang, where he consistently used whips of rushes. In the fifth year of Emperor Ling’s* Jiaping *era, he was appointed Grand Commandant. Once, while at the imperial seat, he dozed off drunk. The emperor asked, “Is the Grand Commandant drunk?” Kuan looked up and replied, “Your servant dares not be drunk. It is only that my burden is heavy and my responsibility great—my heart is as if drunk with worry.” Once, a guest arrived at Kuan’s home, and Kuan sent a servant to buy wine. The servant returned late and thoroughly drunk. The guest cursed him as a beast. Shortly after, Kuan sent someone to check on the servant, fearing he might take his own life. He said to his attendants, “This is a man, yet he was cursed as a beast—what greater humiliation could there be? This is why I feared for his life.” Later, he was enfeoffed as Marquis of Dai with six hundred households. He passed away in the second year of the* Zhongping *era at the age of sixty-six and was posthumously honored as General of Chariots and Cavalry. The court later bestowed the posthumous title* Zhaodao *Marquis. His son Song succeeded him. As this account differs in some respects, it is recorded here in detail. The Master of Azure Valley left no other notable traces. All these references to immortal teachings may belong to the lesser teachings’ limitations—otherwise, such discrepancies would not arise.)*

The affairs of Huayang will be further clarified later. Recorded on the twenty-third day of the first month.

The Venerable One of the Eastern Palace arrived to visit the grotto. At times, the Guest of Mount Gui would also gather with them—such were the days of grand assemblies of true immortals. How could one but briefly ascend to the realm of the Crouching Dragon to pay reverence at the sacred peak? It should be made known to Lord Xu. (This also appears to refer to the Middle Lord. As previously mentioned on the first day of the twelfth month, the Venerable One of the Eastern Palace is the Azure Lad, and the Guest of Mount Gui is the Queen Mother of the West. The earlier scroll also contains this instruction, bidding one to ascend the Crouching Dragon to gaze upon the mountain and perform obeisance—distinct from merely climbing a ridge. It is not necessarily a matter of proximity but rather what is fitting.)

In ancient times, there was a man who loved the Dao yet did not know the method of seeking it. He did nothing but bow and kneel morning and night before a withered tree, repeatedly pleading, "Grant me long life." For twenty-eight years, he never wearied. One day, the withered tree suddenly burst into bloom, its flowers exuding a sap as sweet as honey. Someone instructed him to consume it, so he ate both the flowers and the sap. Upon finishing, he became an immortal. Such was the utmost sincerity of his devotion. If even a withered tree can produce purple blossoms and yield sweet nectar, how much more should the numinous terrace of the Three Radiant Ones and the Five Mushrooms’ abode respond to frequent reverence of the mountain peaks, washing away impurities? Truly, it is lamentable.

The reason worldly people so often fail to attain their desires is no different from why you fail to attain yours—how could it be otherwise?

Once, there was a man named Liu Shaoweng who frequently entered Mount Taihua, bowing in reverence toward the mountain. After twenty years of this, he suddenly beheld the Elder of the Western Peak, who bestowed upon him the Dao of immortality. (The *Prohibited Mountain Talismans* mentions the Lord of the Western Peak; it is unclear whether this Elder is the same.)

Another man would go to the riverbank every dawn to bow to the river. After ten years of this, the River Earl and the River Lord appeared to him, presenting him with ten pairs of white jade disks and teaching him the method of walking on water without drowning. This man later attained the Dao on the Central Peak. (The River Earl and the River Lord are likely two distinct deities.)

When Zuo Ci first arrived, he too devoted himself to frequent bowing and reverence before the sacred mountains—for about five years.

Thus he attained profound advancement into the Eastern and Western Palaces of the Inner and Outer Courts. (Earlier it was said that entry came after three months—this differs greatly, perhaps because "profound advancement" is exceptional.)

In learning the Dao, one should be as steadfast as Shan Shiyuan, abandon worldly affairs like the Perfected One of Pristine Emptiness, and tread the deepest mysteries as did Zhou Ziyang. How could one fail to attain the Dao? (The *Traditions of Shiyuan* have not been transmitted. His renunciation of family to seek learning is recorded in the *Apocrypha*; he was a disciple of Yin Gongdu and later became the Perfected One of Mount Taihe. The Venerable Lord of Pristine Emptiness and Lord Zhou of Purple Yang each have their own hagiographies.)

One’s resolve must be steadfast and diligent; one’s spirit must be refined and empty. All actions and undertakings must be kept discreet. (The *Precepts of the Azure Lad* states: "In learning the Dao, one must be diligent and discreet. Diligence means unceasing effort day and night; discretion means that none but oneself knows." The Central Lord reiterates this—truly the ultimate admonition, a single phrase that encompasses all.)

The above was dictated by Lord Dinglu for transcription. (This passage also appears in Yang’s writings.)

As for gazing toward the forest—does one not yearn for those who have already transcended, as one admires that disciple bathing in radiance? It may be said: "Before your eyes, yet suddenly behind." (The wife of the Prefect has already transcended; now the Prefect gazes toward the forest peaks—how could he be without longing? Once he deemed himself her superior, yet now he lags behind. This is likely an exhortation meant to spur him on.)

The above was spoken by the Lady of the Southern Peak.

Zhang Jiangzi was a native of Xizhou and the younger sister of Zhang Ji. (Ji was a general under Dong Zhuo at the end of the Later Han, later a commander from Xiliang. He died in battle while attacking Rangcheng, struck by an arrow. He was the paternal uncle of Zhang Xiu. His sister’s marriage is not recorded in external sources, and it is unknown whether she was wed.)

Li Huigu was a native of Qi and the wife of Xiahou Xuan. (Xuan lived at the end of the Wei dynasty and was executed along with Li Feng by Sima Zhao. It is unknown whether his wife died before or after him. Li Feng was from Fengyi, not Qi—it is unclear whose daughter this Li was.)

Shi Shunü was a native of Shanyang and the daughter of Shi Ji. (Shi Ji was from Wuxing. During the reign of Sun Hao, he served as General of the Cavalry and defended Xiling. Here it is said he was from Shanyang.)

Fearing that the daughter might have married out and taken her husband’s commandery as her designation—or else this would be discordant—Zheng Tiansheng was the mother of {Dengyi} Zhi. ({Dengyi} Zhi, styled Bomiao, was a native of Xinye in Nanyang. In Shu, he served as Liu Shan’s General of Chariots and Cavalry. Later, while traveling, he saw a {chongyuan} carrying its young and shot it dead. Stricken with remorse, he died. It is unknown whose daughter his mother was.)

These several women of former times were famed for their benevolent conduct and virtuous reputation, and all now dwell within the grotto. In the grotto are the Hall of Yiqian and the Terrace of Han Zhen—both names of palaces. It is reckoned that they now reside in the eastern chamber of the Hall of Yiqian. In this hall there are altogether eighty-three persons. There is also the Lady of Xiechen, a daughter of the Nine Palaces. The Supreme One once sent her to teach these methods, all of which preserve life and confer scriptures. The Lady of Xiechen oversees their instruction.

The Lady was Huang Jinghua, daughter of Huang Qiong, the Han Minister over the Masses. Han Zhong transmitted to her the cinnabar of Mount Min, and by ingesting it she attained transcendence. (Huang Qiong was a native of Jiangxia, styled Shiying. During the reign of Emperor Shun of Han, he served as Minister over the Masses, Minister of Works, and Grand Commandant. He died at the age of seventy-nine. His father, Huang Xiang, during the reign of Emperor Zhang of Han, was President of the Imperial Secretariat and saved over a thousand lives.)

Lady Jinghua’s son, Yuwan, served as Minister of Education and Grand Commandant. He was killed by Li Quan. The Lady herself had not yet married at the time. Among these persons here, some are called "ladies," some "wives," and some "mothers"—each name is chosen for its clarity, not necessarily because it reflects the merit or blessings they confer.

The Hall of Embracing Perfection is where women who have already attained the Dao reside, subordinate to the Eastern Palace of the Supreme Primordium. There are nearly two hundred of them.
