(The eighty-three persons mentioned earlier reside only in the Lodge of Easy Transitions. Since the Hall of Embracing Perfection is of higher rank, one must be promoted and transferred to enter it.) These two palaces are exclusively for women. There are also the Palace of Youthful Beginnings and the Hall of Leisurely Repose, where male disciples study. (The names of these men and women will appear later; these are merely abbreviated designations of the most eminent.)

Among those with whom she resides, she is closest to the mother of Bo Miao. Others are also on good terms with her. (The mother of Bo Miao is the Lady of Zheng.) She prepares a bed to await the spirits, a longing most sincere in filial children. She has already brought benefit to her son.

Earlier, when I gave you that cup and cloth, it was merely a gift between us—something exchanged when I was about to enter the grotto-chamber. The regulations of the immortal officials forbid revealing the truth, so I had to disguise it as another favor. This, too, was an expression of deepest intent. She also wished to prepare a bed for repose, so that the refined *qi* of the departed might have a place to dwell. Keep this confidential. Her son’s Dao is like this.

*(This is an instruction to the clerk. The matters here all have their intended significance and cannot be openly annotated. From the Office of the Record-Keeper, there are thirty-one entries in total, accompanied by one volume of clerkly transcriptions and annotations.)*

*True Reports, Scroll Twelve. True Reports, Scroll Thirteen. The Pivot of Spirit Verification, Part Three.*

Among the rulers of the underworld, there are three ranks. Among the titles of ghostly marshals, there are likewise three ranks. All these are bestowed upon those who have accumulated merit and virtue in the world, whose deeds have ripened into fruition—whether through personal pursuit of longevity and attainment of the Dao, or through the virtuous conduct of descendants that glorifies their ancestors, or through recitation of the *Dongxuan* scriptures, whose transforming influence extends to ancestors seven generations prior. *(The *Dongxuan* refers to the *Great Dongxuan Scripture*; reciting it ten thousand times enables the refinement of the bodily essence in the Southern Palace for ancestors from the seventh generation onward, allowing them to receive the transformation into embryonic immortals. This is not the *Dongxuan Lingbao Scripture* as commonly referred to in the present age.)*

The paths to seeking transcendence are not singular, and the means of attaining it are manifold. In essence, it is through the accumulation of hidden deeds in the world that all may achieve this vast renewal of life. Ghostly marshals undergo martial liberation, while the rulers of the underworld undergo civil liberation—both are the beginnings of immortality. Their names are recorded in the Eastern Flower Registry, selected and inscribed by the Celestial Emperor, and they are not subject to the jurisdiction of the Three Officials’ bureaus.

*(It is also said elsewhere: Those who diligently attend to their duties while desiring to savor the purity of the Dao, or who outwardly conform to worldly customs while inwardly fearing death and yearning for immortality, may also become civil or martial liberation rulers.)*

The lowest rank of underworld rulers resides scattered in external dwellings, idly abiding without fixed duties.

Those not subject to the teachings and regulations of the Nine Palaces, nor engaged in the practices of refinement and transformation, though dwelling within the grotto-heavens, are the lowest among these rulers. According to the Siming method, this rank requires one hundred and forty years before qualifying for a single promotion. Only upon this first promotion may one ascend the immortal ranks and receive the commands of the immortals.

*(According to the Sword Classic, the rulers possess extensive hierarchies and promotion timelines, with vast disparities in duration. The numerical designations of these ranks differ from their titles, where greater numbers confer higher status—such is the structure of their hierarchy.)*

The second rank of underworld rulers may directly embark upon the immortal path, ascending through the immortal grades. After one hundred and forty years, they are promoted to fill the positions of the *Guanjin* (Custodial Offices), which resemble the scattered clerks of the mundane world. This rank represents the middle tier among underworld rulers. Li Dong and others currently belong to the first rank.

*(Li Dong, a native of Qu’e, served as a household leader and libationer. His original documents still exist, and he followed the methods of Bao of Nanhai. Though Li Dong’s talents were mediocre, his heart and conduct were pure and upright, thus qualifying him as the lowest among the rulers’ attendants—one whom the Xu family regularly employed. In the first year of Yongchang, when the Master was twenty-three, he received from Li Dong the Six Jia Yin-Yang Traveling Kitchen Talismans. Since this account is closely related, it is briefly mentioned here.)*

The third rank comprises the highest of the underworld rulers. They may freely enter and exit the halls and chambers of the immortals, roaming the lands of the divine provinces. They are assigned to the *Tongchu* and *Erfu* lodges, and upon entering, they attend the Eastern Flower Upper Terrace. There, they study the transformation of form, bathe in luminous essence, and refine their qi. After twelve years, their qi gathers the spirit-soul; after fifteen years, their spirit binds the corporeal soul. After thirty years, the bones within the coffin reattach to their spirit and qi. After forty years, they are restored as living beings and return to wander among mortals. After fifty years, they are appointed to immortal offices. After sixty years, they may roam the Broad Cold Palace. After a hundred years, they enter the Palace of Kunying—such are the highest of the rulers, the leisurely attendants of the immortals.

Zhang Jiangzi and others were previously among the second rank, having only just gained entry to the *Yiqian* lodge. The hierarchy of ghost marshals follows this same order. (The ranks of the *zhuzhe* are not restricted by gender. According to these time limits, once the bones within the coffin are obtained, they may be reborn into the world—such cases do occur, though their transformations and concealments are difficult to fathom, verging on the inconceivable.)

The *Yiqian* and *Tongchu* lodges are the halls and chambers for both men and women. Within them, all is tranquil. The Green Lad of the Eastern Sea visits twice a year to inspect these palaces and observe their inhabitants. (The phrase "twice a year" seems to follow the transmitted records, though earlier texts state: "On the twenty-third day of the first month, the Superior One of the Eastern Palace arrives"—suggesting no fixed schedule.)

Zhao Sutai has resided in the *Yiqian* lodge for four hundred years and refuses to leave, claiming there is no greater joy in the world than this place. Zhao Sutai was the daughter of Zhao Xi, a Han-dynasty Inspector of Youzhou. She rescued the destitute from the rivers, saved Wang Hui and others from extermination, and performed dozens of hidden acts of virtue. Thus, her person was permitted to ascend to the Vermilion Mound, and her children now dwell within the Grotto Heavens. Xi frequently enters and exits the Bureau of Fixed Records, while Sutai often roams the paths and lanes in plain attire, finding contentment in the mountains and marshes. (Zhao Xi is not prominently recorded in the *Book of Han*. Her "roaming in plain attire" likely refers to her sojourns within the Grotto Heavens, not the mortal world.)

Among those in the *Yiqian* lodge, some are of lofty attainment yet live in quiet obscurity, such as Dou Qiongying and Han Taihua.

Liu Chunlong, Wang Jinxian, Li Xizi, and Guo Shuxiang—these several individuals all possessed a celestial disposition of profound refinement, their bearing loftily transcendent, their talents rivaling the finest, their demeanor striking awe in the multitudes. These were the foremost among the masters, the most capable of the immortal officials. Next in rank were those such as Zhang Shanzi.

Lady Miaomu of Dengyi was renowned for her virtuous conduct, and thus later generations often revered and emulated her. Dou Qiongying was the younger sister of Dou Wu. Her seventh-generation ancestor, a man named Zhi, made it his vocation to gather abandoned bones and his calling to revive the dead. Thus, his blessings extended to Qiongying’s own lifetime.

(Dou Wu, styled Youping, was the great-grandson of Dou Rong. Zhi was likely Rong’s grandfather. Wu, too, was ever generous, scattering his wealth to relieve the destitute throughout the realm. During the reign of Emperor Ling, he served as Grand General but was executed alongside Chen Fan. When his mother gave birth to Wu, she also bore a serpent, which slithered away at once to the southern mountains. It returned only when she passed, and then vanished again.)

She wept bitterly for a long while before departing—this too was deemed extraordinary. Yet it remains unclear why both brother and sister should inherit the blessings of seven generations: the brother executed while the sister attained transcendence—this cannot be the Northern Dipper of the Daoist tradition. The ghostly bureaucracy has its own Northern Dipper Lord, who governs life and death.

(According to records, when Sun Hao’s defeated general Zhang Ti’s soldier Liu Rong fell ill and died, after three days he suddenly rose with a great cry, saying, “I reached the gates of the Northern Dipper, where I saw men binding Zhang Ti.” Startled, he faltered, and that very evening, Zhang Ti perished in battle. This would seem to correspond to the Fourth Palace. As for the Fifth and Sixth Palaces, whose domains remain obscure, they may well be the courts of judgment and retribution.)

The Supreme Emperor of the ghostly bureaucracy is the Northern Emperor, who presides over the First Heavenly Palace and governs all Six Heavenly Palaces. The remaining four palaces are each administered by the Four Luminous Lords.

(Though each is said to govern one palace, their specific locations are not clearly defined. Since the palaces stand in parallel, they cannot be assigned to the four cardinal directions. Examining the hierarchy of the Lords, the Second Palace is governed by the Western Luminous Lord, the Third by the Eastern Luminous Lord, and the Fourth by the Northern Dipper Lord, with the remaining two following in sequence.)

Each pair of heavenly palaces is overseen by one official, making three officials in total across the six heavens—akin to the offices of penal law, responsible for judgment and punishment. These are perpetually overseen by true immortals and the Lords of Life, who collectively govern them and share the duty of administering life and death. All major decrees and judgments issue from the immortal officials.

(This is the "Three Officials" often invoked in Daoist tradition. The *Scripture on Dispelling Demons* states that Mount Tai has a Left Fire Official, a Right Water Official, and a Female Official, also called the Three Officials, all charged with punishment. Today, the Three Mao Lords oversee them: the Great Lord as commander, and the Lord of Life as inspector. Their subordination to the immortal officials stems from the fact that not all people in the world die; some attain true transcendence and thus fall outside the Northern Emperor’s jurisdiction. Others, though dead, may undergo spiritual transformation and regain form—such cases are recorded by the true immortals. Hence, the office of the Lord of Life governs and unifies these matters.)

The Northern Dipper Lord of the ghostly bureaucracy serves as the examining official for the Daoist Seven Stars of the Northern Dipper. This ghostly office is also subordinate to the essence of the Nine Stars and ultimately answers to the Jade Lord of the Northern Dawn.

(The Northern Dipper in the heavens has its own overseers, so the ghostly bureaucracy mirrors this with a corresponding office. Its authority derives from the *Jade Rotating Square* and the *Jade Tablet*, thus it is ultimately subordinate to the courts of the Most High.)

... [text missing] ... need not all come directly. I shall reveal to you one or two secrets, fellow kindred spirits, so you may know the tranquility of the Dipper’s hidden realm. How can one not be diligent in the Dao?

(These three individuals are not mentioned in other texts. During the Later Han, there was a Li Yun, who also served as Magistrate of Baima and was executed for his forthright remonstrance. The phrase "fellow kindred spirits" refers to his address to the Chief Clerk and his subordinates.)

On the night of the twenty-fourth day of the seventh month, the Lord of Life delivered this message.

(According to earlier records, though the Chief Clerk, Sima, and others were informed by the Register of Destiny, this appears to be the initial account of the grotto’s affairs, dated the eleventh month of the *chou* year. The present mixed account, however, should follow later and is instead dated the seventh month, though it does not correspond to the *yin* year. The sequence is difficult to reconcile. Fourteen entries in total were received from the subterranean authorities, transcribed by clerks into a single scroll.)

The Cavern of Embracing Reality exists within all grotto-heavens, not only here. This particular cavern belongs to the Supreme Prime Prefecture and is subordinate to the Lord of Life. Within it, two female transcendents preside: one is Zhang Weizi, daughter of Zhang Qing, Grand Minister of Works during the reign of Emperor Zhao of Han. Weizi was devoted to the Dao and, having mastered the method of corpse-liberation, entered this realm.

She had previously resided in the Grotto of Easy Transitions. Weizi regularly ingested mist-qi, claiming that mist-qi is the essence of mountains, marshes, water, and fire—the fullness of qi within metals and stones. Prolonged ingestion allows one to disperse the form and enter emptiness, merging the body with cloud-qi. Weizi stated that she received this method from Chun Wenqi, the Jade Consort of Eastern Florescence in the Eastern Sea, who is the younger sister of the Azure Lad. Once, Weizi concentrated her thoughts in secluded stillness, her sincere heart moving the numinous, and so Wenqi descended to her. She transmitted the Dao of ingesting mist.

After receiving the Dao of mist-ingestion, Weizi also taught it at times to those studying in the Grotto of Embracing Reality and the Grotto of Easy Transitions. I once obtained this formula, which is excellent and practical. The method of ingesting mist is as follows:

At dawn, in a state of secluded stillness, sit or lie as you wish. First, close your eyes and inwardly visualize, faintly perceiving the five viscera. Then exhale through the mouth twenty-four times. Do this while keeping the eyes closed, so that you see the five-colored qi swirling and coiling around your face in a dense cloud. Next, inhale this five-colored qi through the mouth fifty times. Afterward, swallow saliva sixty times. Then softly recite the incantation:

*"Great radiance emits brilliance,
Numinous mist shifts on all sides.
Bound qi coils and bends,
Five colors fill the grotto-heaven.
Divine smoke converges and opens,
Metals and stones bloom with true essence.
Lush and deep, the purple void,
Refining form to preserve wholeness.
Emerging in light, hidden in seclusion,
Five spirits transform and divide.
United brightness fans the emptiness,
At times riding the six clouds.
Harmonizing and gathering my body,
Ascending to the nine heavens."*

After reciting, knock the teeth together seven times and swallow saliva seven times. Then open your eyes, and the practice is complete. This Dao is wondrously efficacious. Many in the Mystic Capital of the Divine Continent have mastered this art. Can you practice this method? With long practice, you will often ride clouds and mist in your travels.

*(This method of ingesting mist has been transcribed separately for use.)*

The account is found in the third scroll. (It remains here in doubt, accompanying the main text.) One such true woman was Fu Lihe. Lihe was the daughter of Attendant-in-Ordinary Fu Jian, nephew of Emperor Huan of the Han, from Beidi. Her family devoutly practiced Buddhism with diligence. The daughter daily swept and purified the space before the Buddha, offering earnest prayers and vows, her heart set on transcendence. The divine spirits, observing her resolve, allowed her to come here. After long residence and gradual transformation, she has only now become the Mistress of the Terrace of Embracing the True. She attained the Dao by ingesting the qi of the Five Stars. Lihe was skilled in song; when she sang, birds and beasts would gather in flight to listen.

The Lord of the Register of Destinies spoke thus. (The accounts of Zhang and Fu are not prominent in external records; they may each be found in their family genealogies.)

Another entry records Yang Shu, transcribed by Yuan. Wang Yan served as President of the Secretariat under Emperor Wu of Jin. His daughter, styled Jinxian, became the consort of Crown Prince Minhuai. When Luoyang fell into chaos, Liu Yao and Shi Le abducted Jinxian. As they crossed the Mengjin Ford of the Yellow River, intending to take her as wife, Jinxian cursed them, saying: “I am the wife of the imperial heir, daughter of the Minister of Education. How dare you, barbarian dogs, presume to violate me?” With these words, she leapt into the river.

The maidservant, named Liuchu, then declared, “If the great may do so, the small should do likewise,” and likewise leapt into the river.

At that moment, the female immortal Han Xihua of Mount Songgao was traveling and took pity on them. She embraced and rescued the two, allowing them to receive inner salvation while outwardly appearing dead. Their bodies were secretly preserved, and she then took them into Mount Songgao. Today, they reside within the Hua Yang Palace Grotto, in the realm of *Yi Qian*.

Liuchu was then about twenty-two or twenty-three years of age. Her form and countenance were also refined, and she possessed a steadfast heart and integrity. Her original surname was Tian, and she hailed from Yuyang. She was the granddaughter of Tian Feng, former magistrate of Junyi during the Wei dynasty. Feng had once performed deeds of hidden virtue, which extended to Liuchu.

(*The* Jin History *states: Wang Yan’s eldest daughter was named Jingfeng, of great beauty. Empress Jia arranged for her younger brother, Mi, to marry her. His youngest daughter, named Huifeng, was wed to Crown Prince Minhuai, whom he resented. Thus, she was not particularly favored. In the sixth month of the fifth year of Yongjia, Wang Mi, Liu Yao, and Shi Le sacked Luoyang. When the bandits sought to force themselves upon the consort, she drew a blade and declared, “I am the daughter of the Grand Commandant and consort of the imperial heir. I would rather die than suffer humiliation at the hands of traitorous barbarians.” She was then killed. Her family later interred her northwest of Luoyang, north of the Luo River, and she was posthumously enfeoffed as Consort Zhen Ding. This account differs slightly from the above.*)

Fan Youchong was a native of Liaoxi. Having conceived through transformation and altered his form, he now dwells here.

He constantly ingests the Three Qi. The method of the Three Qi involves visualizing the blue qi, white qi, and red qi, each like {a thread extending}, descending from the sun in the east and entering directly into the mouth. {The hands press} ninety times. Once satiated, one stops. Practicing this for ten years, the three-colored qi naturally reside within the body, and one thereby attains transcendence as a divine immortal. This is the High Lord Yuan’s *Method of the Primal Inner Radiance* from the *Great Simplicity*. Perform it daily at dawn, conducting it with eyes closed. Gazing at the sun is also beneficial. Though the method is rarely transmitted, its effects are profoundly verified. Lord Xu may practice it. (This method was also transcribed in a duplicate text, found in the third chapter under "Cultivation Practices.")

Fan the Overseer is this very person. Formerly, he served as the Youthful Overseer of the Beginning; now he resides in Huayang. (Elsewhere it is said he once served as a Han dynasty Palace Attendant and was skilled in geomancy, taking grave sites and dwellings as his focus. This is also recorded in the third chapter.)

The three entries above are all from Yang’s writings.

Li Zheng of Henei formerly received the *Method of Guarding the One* and the *Cavern Chamber* and attained the Dao. Initially dwelling on Mount Luoyang, he has recently entered Huayang. He also oversees matters of examining and exorcising malevolent influences among the people. Zheng once served as tutor to the Duke of Changdao Township. When he received the Dao and entered the mountains, he was already sixty years of age. (It is unclear what office Li held; he likely served in an overseer’s post. The Duke of Changdao Township was Emperor Yuan of Wei’s original enfeoffment.)

To the northeast of {Wangzheng} Mountain lies a cave that connects to the southern cave of Fang Mountain in the great Juqu region. Jiang Bozhen frequently ascended this mountain to gather *shinao*. The *shinao* lies beneath the northern cave of Fang Mountain. Master Fanyang also once gathered and ingested it. ({Wangzheng} Mountain is still part of the Great Horizontal Mountain. As later stated, “The aide constantly traveled with five individuals from Fang Mountain.” However, it is unknown whether a path leads into the grotto-heaven.)

Master Fanyang is He Miao of the Deer Track Cavern. This substance is also found in the northern Zhushan. (The location of Zhushan is unclear. North of Juqu, west of Deer Track Mountain, there is a place called Zhujiao Mountain, which is likely the site.)

*Shinao* resembles stone but is slightly speckled in color and {coarse and large}. It is found in many places. One may ingest it.

At times, it causes one to feel heat. It also prevents thirst. Li Zheng, before entering the mountains, suffered from wind-bi illness, which took a long time to heal. This man had previously indulged excessively in bedroom affairs, so his illness then was not as mild as it is now. (Nowadays, *shinao* is also found east of Great Mao Peak. Its form is round and small, resembling *zengqing* but with a texture like stalactite beds. Its lower part is bright white, occasionally marked with black specks, and it is {coarse and large}. Ingesting it generates heat, and its therapeutic effects resemble those of stalactites.)

In the Great Huo Mountains of Luojiang, there is a Terrace of Flowers, where five-colored concealed *zhi* grow. (This is the domain governed by the Southern Perfected and the Deity of Fate.) The Huayang Cavern also harbors five kinds of night-glowing *zhi*. (These are the *zhi* that the Deity of Fate requested to cultivate within and beyond Juqu.)

Liangchang Mountain bears {tomb-fire} fire *zhi*. This substance appears on the ground like {tomb-fire}, though its essence resembles grass rather than being so. It is as large as a bean, with purple blossoms, and emits light when viewed at night. Consuming one piece brightens one aperture in the heart; eating seven pieces brightens seven apertures, enabling one to write at night. It is calculated that consuming forty-seven pieces grants a lifespan of ten thousand years. (To date, no one has reportedly encountered it.)

(For this is merely a matter of failing to seek with utmost sincerity.) Within Baoshan Mountain grows white *zhi*. There is also the water of Hidden Spring, a true purple in hue. (This is Mount Linwu, situated in the great Wu lake.)

On Huayang’s Mount Leiping lies the Field Lord’s Spring. Drinking its waters expels the three worms from the abdomen, and its flavor matches that of Hidden Spring. It is said to be the flowing essence of jade-sand. Used to launder clothes, it requires no lye—this being its singular marvel. (This water now wells up from the earth, resembling boiling water in appearance, yet its taste is surpassingly fine. When used to wash soiled garments, they become clean at once. This is the place now called Willow Vale, located roughly one *li* southeast of the Prefect’s residence.)

In the time of Gao Xin, there was an immortal named Master Zhan of the Upper Rank who planted plums upon the Earth of Crouching Dragons, filling the land with them. Master Zhan now serves as the Right Guardian of the Nine Palaces. He often tells others: “In former days, while dwelling beneath Huayang, I ate white plums of extraordinary sweetness. The memory lingers still, yet in an instant three thousand years have passed.” (Various chronologies record that from the first year of Yao’s reign—*wuxu*—to the *jimao* year of the Qi dynasty, 2,803 years elapsed. Gao Xin was Yao’s father, so this statement must date to the Jin era. To speak of three thousand years when only 2,800 have passed since Yao’s time suggests that external calendars may be imprecise. For instance, the *dinghai* count would already have been exceeded. The *Bamboo Annals* record a precise span of 2,643 years—an even greater discrepancy.)

Later, Guo Sichao planted the Five Fruits in that same place. The land is also suitable for cultivating *mu-shi*—the *mu-shi* of Blessed Land, used to dispel calamities and the myriad ills. During the Qin, the Daoist Zhou Taibin and Jiang Shumao, the Marquis of Baling, came to reside beneath Mount Juqu. They also planted the Five Fruits and the Five Pungent Vegetables. Shumao was enfeoffed as marquis during the reign of King Xiao of Qin. The place is now named Jiangba after him, for the land took its name from Shumao. (The name remains to this day. A great road runs from behind Small Mao Peak to Yanling, and is called Jiangba Road. However, during the reign of Duke Xiao of Qin, the state had not yet annexed Chu to establish commanderies, and Baling County was only founded in the early Jin. It is unclear how there could have been a marquisate of Baling—likely it refers to the Ba of Bashu.)

Both men attained immortality and now serve as Left Ministers on Penglai. The various Jiangs of Nanzheng are descendants of Shumao. Shumao once wrote to the officials of the Supreme Ultimate Palace, saying: “Formerly, I studied the Dao in Guigu, attained it in Shaoshi, nurtured my *ge-yu* in Huayang, and awaited ascension in the Realm of Leisure. At times, I rode the whirlwind-chariot, feasting in Juqu. I awoke to the swift passage of time, how things endure while people perish—what toil is this?” Taibin was also a man of talent and skill, adept at playing the zither. He once taught Mi Changsheng and Sun Guangtian. Guangtian was none other than Sun Deng, who could play eight notes on a single string—a truly marvelous feat. (Sun Deng is the same “long-whistler” mentioned by Xi Kang of Mount Kuaiji, who also spoke of seeing him play the one-stringed zither. These words are not empty.)

Shumao cultivated the Five Pungent Vegetables and often sold them to purchase cinnabar for his use. Even now, among the mountains, leeks and scallions remain—perhaps his lingering seeds. (The place is now called Leek Mountain, west of Great Mao Peak, where large leeks grow in abundance. Garlic and scallions also appear elsewhere, not solely in Jiangba.)

Before the present dwelling lies a pond, constructed by Guo Sichao. He raised its embankments so the water would be deep. Yet over the long ages, the pond walls have crumbled. (The present dwelling seems to refer to the Prefect’s residence. Before it lies a pond, with a dike to the west—this dams the waters of Willow Brook. Yet it is far from Guoqian, which lies northwest of the Northern Grotto. There is a large reservoir there, where Sichao likely once resided. The meaning of “before the dwelling” is unclear; perhaps the Prefect established fields there. Further details may follow.)

Sichao often sailed a small boat upon the pond, striking the hull and singing:
*“A clear pool girds the numinous peak,*
*Long forests lush with verdant green.*
*The dark bird hides in secluded wilds—*
*In leisurely words, I speak my mind.*
*I ply my oars upon the sacred waves,*
*Bowing my head to seek the Morning Wind.*
*Yet the hour of liberation eludes me,*
*And I wander free among these hills and groves.”*

*(The “Morning Wind” refers to the wind of the Jade Dawn of Supreme Purity, not the bird of that name in the *Mao Odes*.)*

*“Beyond the Nine Abysses, the waves surge with gods,*
*I refine the Dao and attain the Golden Perfected.*
*This is the feather of the numinous phoenix.”*

“Conceal my radiant dragon scales,
Lift high this inch of heart-matter.
Ten thousand breaths are but dust and grime—
Pity the morning-born mayfly’s grace.
Who can exhaust your chariot wheels?
*(Feminine favor does not outlast the mat,
Masculine love does not wear out the wheels—
The mayfly lives but a morning,
An image of man’s fleeting world,
So easily dissolved.)*

Roaming the void, I shed the whirlwind,
My spirit steps trace no fixed form.
The spherical light blazes with bright auroras,
Nine phoenixes sing to the morning sun,
Their radiant plumes fan the River of Heaven.
Auspicious clouds soar in billowing flight,
And I arrive at the Great Tenuity’s halls,
*(I sip this golden nectar,)*
Wandering beyond the Dark Abyss.
Neither existing nor ceasing to exist.
*(The Dark Abyss and Nine Abysses lie beyond the Eight Extremities,
Atop the peaks of the Nine Auroras.
Ascending the Wood Star,
Also called the Dark Radiance’s Eastern Sun Abyss—
Thus Ruoshi told Lu Ao:
‘I and Hanman have our tryst above the Nine Abysses.’)*

I ride the fiery chariot, dancing through Divine Empyrean,
Clad in auroras, girded with the nine suns,
Peerless as the High Emperor’s dragon wheels.”

Thus I arrive at the Northern Florescence Chamber,
Where divine tigers roam through jade groves.
Wind and clouds merge as one,
Opening and closing the gates of dark mystery.
Spirit-transformations erase all traces of the profound.
*(In the Four Courts, he served as Jade Terrace Canopy-Bearer,
Hence the saying: ‘Peerless as the High Emperor’s dragon wheels.’)*

The *Dinglu* states:
*(The preceding twelve clauses were transcribed as one scroll.)*

Of the Four Courts, he was a man of Yan State.
Four brothers all attained the Dao—
Four Courts was the eldest.
In truth, his dharma governed the Three Offices,
Six hundred years without transgression.
Seated, he transcended and ascended.
When Four Courts’ term ended,
He was promoted to Left Immortal Duke of the Nine Palaces,
Overseeing the Jade Terrace Canopy-Bearers.
For long the post lay vacant, unfilled,
Until Sihe succeeded Four Courts.

The villagers below the mountain
Still call a level marshland “Guo Qian,”
Preserving Four Courts’ surname in common speech.
Four Courts once sent men to cultivate this land,
And year after year, whenever he traveled,
He would pass by this mountain
To visit Sihe,
Wandering and gazing over the plains and hills.
*(This is recorded in the Mao Tradition.
According to such accounts...)*

The place called Guo Qian was merely where cultivation took place—it was not his residence. His dwelling must have been the estate of the Prefectural Scribe. Now that the embankments and walls have collapsed and water no longer pools deeply, people treat the land as fields. Yet the terrain remains low and expansive; even slight damming still forms a pond, allowing one to row a boat and sing. But none can trace the footsteps of that distant age—how lamentable!

Later accounts say that Four Courts passed by each year to wander and gaze, for even after his promotion, he still cherished his former home, often feasting in the Eternal Realm and gazing fondly upon his old companions.

Zhang Xuanbin, a man of Dingxiang, was nominated as a Filial and Incorrupt Candidate during the reign of Emperor Wu of Wei. Returning to his hometown, he served as disciple to Ji of Xihe, consuming medicinal herbs and practicing the arts of the Cavern Chamber and White Prime. Later, he encountered the Perfected One Fan Ziming on Shaoshi Mountain, who transmitted to him the Dao of vanishing transformation and concealed radiance. Formerly on Mount Tianzhu, he now resides in the Hua Yang Grotto as the Warden of Prohibitions. The Warden of Prohibitions oversees the offices of waters and rains.

This man excels in discoursing on emptiness and nothingness. Scholars of discourse often uphold the principle of fundamental nonbeing, saying: Nonbeing is the dwelling of Great Being; from it, Lesser Being arises. Accumulating Lesser Being nourishes Lesser Nonbeing; perceiving Great Being reveals the root of Great Nonbeing. Being contains Nonbeing, and Nonbeing contains Being.

Thus, my eyes see no things, and things likewise do not see the absence. By lodging in Being, Nonbeing is accomplished; by lodging in Nonbeing, Nonbeing is attained. Here, Nonbeing has no dwelling—even the Great Void is but the dwelling of Nonbeing. Before I was born, the world knew no Nonbeing. Such is the foundation of his discourse.

The numinous ones of Tongbai could not refute him. He himself claimed: "Formerly, I visited Song Chensheng of Penglai. Chensheng was the Left Duke of Penglai. When I discoursed with him on Nonbeing, he roughly grasped my meaning. Beyond this, he still could not fathom the root of Being—how then could he fathom the root of Nonbeing? When I discuss emptiness and nothingness with others, none in the world can match me."

Such is the loftiness of his qi and the firmness of his principle that even Dongqing Jun, Ziwei Xuanqing, could not contend with him. The Warden of Prohibitions also serves as the Regent of Life-Preservation. (This discourse on the principle of emptiness and nothingness uniquely attains the school of Nonbeing, yet its profound subtlety resists exhaustive scrutiny. Even among Perfected Ones, talent and understanding vary greatly. Dongqing of Tongbai and Ziwei Xuanqing are esteemed as the most eloquent debaters—hence they are cited as standards. According to the *Zuozhuan*, the heir apparent of a lord is called *fujun* when accompanying him and *jianguo* when acting as regent. The role of *jianguo* is that of a deputy; thus, this "Regent" may derive from *jiansi*.)

Zhao Weibo was a native of Dongjun. In his youth, he studied under Master Zhang of Handan, who was a man who had attained the Dao.

In his later years, he resided at Mount Song, where he transmitted the *Jade Pendant and Golden Bell Scripture* to Fan Qiulin. Qiulin was the wife of Wei Xingdao, the Han dynasty General of the Tower Ships. Having studied the Dao and attained immortality, she then imparted to Xingdao the Way of the Sun and Moon. She also ingested the *Ninefold Radiant Mirror Flower*, thereby achieving transcendence. Formerly, she had come to reside within Huayang, serving as Vice-Minister of Life-Preservation. The *River Chart* states: "In Wu and Chu, many have witnessed the Great Peace." She would often tell others: "These words are not empty; this verification will soon come to pass." (How can such scholars not redouble their diligence and effort?)

The preservation of the *Luminous Mirror* is no ordinary worldly method. According to the oral instructions received from Fan Qiulin: "Master the art of whistling. Let your whistling resemble the mingled cries of a hundred birds, or the wind surging through a forest, or the sound of drums being struck." Once, at the Altar of the Celestial Market, she faced north with fervor and let forth a long whistle to summon the wind. In an instant, clouds gathered above, and surging qi stirred the forest. At times, dark mists converged in a whirlwind; at others, a fine rain descended in a mist.

The Ministry of Life-Preservation has four Vice-Ministers. This woman oversees torrential rains and waters, as well as the governance of the *Five Mushrooms of Gold and Jade* and the *Herbs of Metal and Stone*. Should one wish to summon a great deluge, one may present a petition to her. (The Warden of Prohibitions also governs rain and water; if seeking rain, one should petition both together.)

One of the Vice-Ministers was Le Changzhi of Xianyang, a fellow townsman of the Eastern Minister of Destiny. He was recommended for office by the Lesser Lord and served as Gentleman of the Palace during the reign of Emperor Huan of Han. In his later years, he followed Master Li of Mount Song in studying the Dao, practicing the *Sevenfold Primordial Method* and attaining immortality. (Though more than two hundred years had passed, he still enjoyed the grace of the Minister’s favor.)

Another was Zheng Zhizheng, an inner-chamber disciple of Master Meng. He was appointed by Master Meng. (Master Meng was a native of Jingzhao.)

One may also address him as Master Meng, though his name and rank remain unknown. Another was Tang Fang of the Western Mountains. (This is the figure recorded in *Accounts of Divine Transcendents*—a native of Shu who served Li Babai.) Le Changzhi oversaw calamities and disasters; Zheng Zhizheng was in charge of examinations and annotations; Lord Tang Fang governed life and death; and Zhao Weibo kept the registers of immortals. Together, they recorded the names of Dao cultivators and oversaw the manifestation of torrential rains, numinous mushrooms, and sacred herbs. (The officials of the Cavern Palace supervise and inspect the people of Wu and Yue, though no more than these four Vice-Ministers hold office. Below them are the Four Masters, whose affairs are detailed in the third scroll.)

Here the *Register of Destinies* concludes. (To the right of this passage, a note in brush and ink refers to the preceding section, though a gap appears here—likely due to a later excision. It is feared that other matters may have been lost as well, a cause for deep regret.)

Du 〈*qi*〉 [Guangping] was a native of Duling in Jingzhao. At the beginning of the Jian’an era, he crossed the Yangtze to the east and entered Kuaiji under Sun Ce’s patronage, accompanying him for a time. Later, he served Sun Quan as Commandant of Establishing Trust. In the second year of Huangwu, he gradually turned to the study of the Dao.

He encountered Master Jie {Yuyan}, who transmitted to him the arts of *xuan* and *bai*. Thereafter, he withdrew to the eastern slopes of Great Mount Mao, where he dwelt in seclusion. Those who mastered the *xuan* and *bai* techniques could conceal their forms, and he was often seen appearing in the markets and villages. Du 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}] and Xu Zongdu, along with Yan Xiansheng, formed a trio who together resided within Mount Mao. At times, they entered its grottoes, or gathered herbs and firewood, trading them in Xuqu for clothing and provisions—unnoticed by others.

Now, {Yuyan} was a disciple of the White Ram Master and presently dwells in Mount Fang in Jian’an. (According to the *Talisman of the Forbidden Mountain*, {Yuyan} was slain by Sun Quan but transformed his form and departed, journeying to Mount Fang in Jian’an to seek the White Ram Master. It was likely at this time that Du received the Dao.)

The pronunciation of 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}] is *Xue*, identical to that of Shun’s minister. The character 〈*qi*〉 [{}] consists of four strokes, while *qi* (契) has three, differing in their regional variants.

Xu Zongdu was a native of Jinling and served as Left Chief Military Clerk under Sun Hao, with Lü Ti as his commander. He received the *qi*-restraining arts from Master Fenggu, which is how he came to associate with 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}]. Yan Xiansheng was the nephew of Bu Zhi and a disciple of Zongdu. 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}] had two disciples: one was Hanhua, the granddaughter of Sun Ben. In her youth, she secretly formed an attachment with 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}], later studying the Dao and receiving the methods of Jie {Yuyan}, which she in turn transmitted to Hanhua.

When Hanhua first departed, she initially fled to Jian’an and took refuge with Zhang Yi, the magistrate of Shaowu. Yi was a close relative of 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}], which allowed her to escape unharmed. Once the situation stabilized, she returned to Mount Mao.

Hanhua practiced the Mysterious-White Method and maintained a measure of restraint. Even now, they sometimes lived together. The Mysterious-White Dao forbids sexual relations, yet from the time she received the Dao from 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}], she was unable to engage in such conduct. (Sun Ben, Governor of Yuzhang under Wu, had a son, and Wangsun Xi of Shanyin had a son named Hanhua. Investigating these two, one finds they initially indulged in licentiousness without restraint, but upon entering the Dao and applying their resolve, they were able to restrain and sever such impulses. This is why they were deemed worthy of transmission. Ben was a cousin of Sun Quan through the same paternal line and had four sons, each named Lin, An, Xi, and Shu, though none were named Xi—perhaps this was a childhood name, or Xi may have been a royal surname.) Her younger brother’s only son was Chen Shijing. During the reign of Sun Xiu, Shijing served as Gentleman of the Palace and from youth was fond of the Dao. He frequently visited Buddhist temples and was a townsman of 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}], which is why in later years he too received the methods. When 〈*qi*〉 [{Guidaida}] first brought Hanhua to Jian’an, Shijing accompanied them on the journey, and in truth, it was he who facilitated their secret plans.

These several individuals now dwell beyond Mount Mao, not as regular cave-dwelling guests. They occasionally enter [the mountain] and often visit the residences of the Prefectural Administrator for leisure. Yet they mostly remain among the Great Mao peaks. (From the beginning of Jian’an to the enthronement of Sun Xiu spans sixty-two years. When Du first followed Sun Ce, he was scarcely twenty; thus, by the time of his flight, he would have been over eighty. This cannot be so.) Shijing now ingests *zhu* and *zexie*, while Hanhua consumes nothing. (Mount Mao has no stone chambers, so they must have built huts. Since they take medicinal preparations, they require drug implements and still rely on clothing and provisions. They cannot be entirely secluded; rather, at that time, the forests were dense and secluded, with no one tracing their footsteps.)

They practiced the Way of Guarding the Mysterious and White, often sitting or lying at will. Within the *niwan* [mud-pill palace] was black *qi*; within the heart-mind was white *qi*; within the navel was yellow *qi*. The three *qi* together arose like clouds, covering the body. Thereupon, they transformed into fire, which encircled the body. The body became pervasively luminous, internally and externally as one. They cultivated this from dawn until midday, then ceased.

Thus, they performed the qi-ingestion one hundred and twenty times in total, completing the practice. The Dao is precisely thus—enabling one to attain longevity and immortality, warding off all harm. This is called "knowing the white and guarding the black," whereby death cannot be sought; "knowing the black and guarding the white," whereby all evil dissipates. They strictly avoided the meat of the six livestock and the five pungent vegetables. One should sleep separately and meditate in stillness, with particular aversion to sexual intercourse—engaging in it leads to death. This Way resembles the practice of Guarding the One, though it is a more direct and essential method, with even greater strictness regarding sexual abstinence. The prohibition in Guarding the One merely requires moderation. Initially, the stored qi emerges like a small bean, gradually expanding to pierce the heavens. The three qi intertwine like smoke, coiling around the body, merging into a single primordial chaos. Suddenly, fire arises within the threefold smoke, combining with the radiance to refine the entire body. Within the body, the five viscera become luminously transparent. This, too, is an essential Way.

These several individuals have already lived over three hundred years, owing precisely to the power of the Mysterious and White. They are all practitioners of the undying arts, though they have yet to attain the full Dao of immortality. *(The matters of the Mysterious and White have already been transcribed in detail in the third chapter on cultivation practices. Calculating from Du’s age of around twenty during the early Jian’an era to the third year of Xingning in the Jin dynasty, he has reached one hundred and ninety years.)*

These individuals began their studies late in life, yet this states they have all lived over three hundred years—perhaps the character "three" is an exaggeration, and "two" would be more plausible. For those who wish to practice the Mysterious and White, they should follow its scripture, which is but a brief text. It can be practiced alongside other methods to dispel the ten thousand evils and repel the thousand harms. After thirty years of practice, one may conceal the body and hide the form, traveling five hundred *li* in a day. (This Way is also called the *Fetal Essence Central Radiance Mysterious and White Method*.)

On the night of the fourteenth day of the eighth month, the Immortal Lord of Preserving Life spoke. (This transmission was surely meant for Ya, though it may have been a test to tease the Prefect.) Ya, in your practice of *guarding the One*, have you not yet risen from your separate chamber? (This discourse concerns the matters of the Mysterious and White and *guarding the One*. Suddenly recalling the *yin* beast, for the *yin* beast has yet to abstain from the bedchamber—hence this mention.)

Still radiance veils the vast forest,
Dim dawn ascends the eastern clouds.
Morning winds dance through sixfold smoke,
Lush and dense, the eightfold Dao surges.
Why must the Five Peaks alone be fair?
Named mountains, too, suffice to climb.
With outstretched hand, I grasp the cavern mounds,
My heart nests in hidden revival.
Exhaling and inhaling fetal essence,
Who can surpass the Mysterious and White?

The above was often chanted in song by Du Guangping. Now recorded here for clarity.
The above was transmitted by the Perfected Lord of the *Dinglu*.

(This should likewise correspond to the fourteenth-night discourse. From Du came nine passages, all transcribed by clerks, forming a single composition.)

Towering, towering—Mount Cen’s peaks,
Fragrant the secluded crags and ridges.
Lofty, lofty—the Master,
Riding harmony, comes soaring.
Hair unbound, brow at ease,
He tills the slopes himself.
Three meals suffice,
No need for packed provisions.
Jade traces gleam in the east,
Phoenix echoes resound in the west.
Dukes and marquises summon him—
He rides the wind, robes billowing.
Dwelling, he makes no boast;
Emerging, he seeks no fame.
Clad in coarse cloth, he moves with leisure,
Staff in hand, he strides freely.

(This composition bears a different hand’s script, appended after the preceding poem, followed again by the matter of the secluded *bib* gate. Since the authentic text concludes with the prior piece—already marked as complete—this poem cannot be Du’s work, nor is its author known.)

The recently designated quiet abode lies here—
This is the innermost chamber of Jinxiang.
Were it not for Magistrate Xu and his sons,
Who could dwell here?
In later generations, only a worthy child of virtue
Shall reside in this land.
You, his descendants, keep this secret—
Do not lightly disclose it.

Here must be the very site where Magistrate Xu later built his dwelling. The land of Jinling is vast, yet this is its most excellent tract—only true immortals may reside here, and thus only the magistrate’s clerk could dwell in it. The "worthy child of virtue" remains unfathomable: he may bear the surname Chi, or be a great man, or one of the heroic worthies destined to rise in future times, who shall again come to live here. Since this concerns the affairs of later descendants, it is not fitting to speak of it prematurely. Moreover, this land bears profound fortune, and one must not broadcast it widely, lest others recklessly occupy it and bring defilement.

The dwelling Magistrate Xu now builds faces east toward a small mountain called Mount Leiping. In the Zhou era, the Lei clan raised dragons here. Later, Jiang Shumao the Field Elder also lived on this mountain. To its north flows Willow Water, also called Elder Tian’s Spring, for the man once dwelt on this mountain and drew from these waters.

(Mount Leiping lies north of Little Mao Peak, its foundations adjoining. Elder Tian’s Spring still exists today. Willow trees grow thickly on either side, hence the name Willow Water. This spring is the very one earlier described as "washing clothes without ashes." Since the magistrate’s residence fell into ruin, no one has known its location.)

By the early Song dynasty, Lady Tan, the Princess Dowager of Jing of Changsha, sponsored a Daoist surnamed Chen and established a Daoist residence northwest of Mount Leiping—this is the present Northern Residence. Later, there was Mount Jurong, and Wang Wenqing, as recorded in the annals, became its master. Learning that Magistrate Xu had once built his dwelling here, he extensively consulted the village elders. In the seventh year of the Daming reign, an elderly adept named Xu Ou mentioned that his ancestor had served Magistrate Xu and passed down knowledge that the magistrate’s residence lay just before the present residence, where the tallow trees stood, and that the well before the refectory hall likely remained. At the time, the site was overgrown with weeds and brambles. Wang then cleared the vegetation and searched, indeed discovering a brick well, its mouth nearly filled with earth. He excavated and repaired it, reinforcing it with tiles. Now it holds fine water, slightly white in hue, perhaps the very water earlier described as resembling that outside Phoenix Gate.

Thus confirming it as the former residence, which had long lain abandoned, with none daring to dwell there. Since it was said to be the "golden chamber to the inner room," it must have been the most fertile ground for crouching dragons. To its northwest stretches a long ridge, called Mount Long Concealment.

Northeast of Mount Leiping lies another mountain, commonly called Great Horizontal Mountain, though its true name is Mount Yu Ridge. The *Records of Famous Mountains* refers to it as Ridge Mountain. At its foot flows a spring where Li Ming once compounded the elixir of immortality and ascended to the Mystic Continent. The site by the water remains to this day. Due east of this mountain stands the ancient tomb of King Yeyi of Yue. (Originally written as *墓*, later generations changed it to *冢*. The mountain now extends far and wide. The later reference to an ancient immortal compounding the elixir likely refers to this place. Li Ming merely stated it lay in a certain region, so it may have been slightly south of here. No foundation traces remain by the water, perhaps gradually reclaimed by overgrowth.)

King Yeyi was the fourth-generation descendant of Goujian. Initially refusing to ascend the throne, he fled into a cave on Mount Jing. The people of Yue lured him out, and later, during the Wu dynasty, he was relocated back to Kuaiji. In the eleventh year of King Xuan of Zhou, he was killed by Sun Zhujiu, who was in turn slain by the people of Yue. How his tomb came to be so far away remains unknown—perhaps there was some numinous quality to this place. Though no definitive burial mound has been found, there appears to be a designated funerary precinct.

The above was spoken by the Lord of the *Dinglu*.

(The following three entries were transcribed by an assistant.)

The jade inscription of Huayang lies west of Tongchu Prefecture, facing east—or, as some say, on all four sides. Its text reads:

Loosening the sash, donning coarse cloth,
One seeks the principles of life and livelihood.
Nurturing and preserving the Three Also,
Penetrating my jade scripture.
Governing the Eight Elders,
And the Twenty-Four Perfected.
Sleepless, gazing inward,
Subtle qi flows ceaselessly—grasping and recording the Great Simplicity.
Mysterious, yet again mysterious,
The spirit Dao exists in the present.
When the disciple arrives, then shall it be attained.

*Yi Qian* says: The Lady of *Dengyi* spoke these words to him. To unravel this is to attain immortality—these are the essential words of transcendence. *Yi Qian* did not comprehend them; perhaps the Marquis of Xu can elucidate them.

(*Yi Qian* was the wife of the Prefectural Clerk. The Lady of *Dengyi* was the mother of *Dengyi* Zhi. This inscription is only roughly comprehensible—all pertains to the higher Dao. Yet the later lines are obscure and cannot be fully understood, hence the request for the Clerk’s interpretation. Or perhaps it was merely a playful test.)

The above entry was transcribed by So-and-so.

The recluse now resides in peace before the *Zhaoling* Terrace. He wishes to erect a small stone stele and inscribe upon it the *Fifteen Eulogies of Huayang*, all in praise of the events within and beyond this mountain grotto, so as to manifest sincerity to the numinous precinct and perpetuate it for distant ages.

Yet the stele has not been carved with *distinctive force*. For now, its text is recorded here as follows:

**The River Chapter** traces the records of the past;
**The Annals of Kong** illumine the names of old.
Thrice lodged, they adorn the celestial order;
Twice metallous, they mark the earth’s glory.
*Right: Pivot of the Realm.*

The dwelling arises from non-being to being;
In being, it returns once more to emptiness.
The numinous structure needs no craftsman—
Its void-form achieves success of itself.
*Right: Essence and Image.*

The spirits gather in the Three Treasuries;
Their paths diverge yet join the Five Conveniences.
Yin’s radiance greets the night’s brightness;
Dawn’s essence gazes upon the morning’s hanging.
*Right: Form and Position.*

The southern peak towers like a dark tripod;
The northern ridge stretches like Qin’s jade disk.
Within and without, the Jade-Sand Ford;
All around, hidden wheel-tracks.
*Right: Mark and Permeation.*

To the left, it is girdled by Willow Water;
To the right, it is scoured by the Yangvalley Stream.
The soil bears the hue of *Beiyang*;
The well is pure with Phoenix-Gate Spring.
*Right: Regional Distinctions.*

Guo’s thousand peaks stand as flowing banks;
Jiang and Ba stretch across distant traces.
Temple visages may at times receive offerings;
Separate dwellings ever maintain reverence.
*Right: Traces and Designations.*

Wu’s dwellers knew not this land;
Yue’s clansmen, *great in speech*, concealed their migrations.
Tree canopies cast only fleeting shade;
Stone hearths have never known smoke.
*Right: Analogous Attachments.*

Fruit groves luxuriate in dense *mumu* trees;
Vegetable gardens trail lingering bitterness.
*Ying* mushrooms may illumine the night;
Field springs ever wash away dust.
*Right: Patterns of Things.*

Dismounting reins at Tortoise Mountain’s guest;
Unbridling steeds before the Azure-Flower Lad.
In repose and feasting, the Hall of Embracing Reality;
In lofty gatherings, the Palace of Leisurely Ease.
*Right: Wanderings and Assemblies.*

Clear songs soar with feathered flocks;
Long whistles return with tumbling clouds.
A lone string holds transcendent melodies;
Empty discourse admits no contention.
*Right: Talents and Heroes.*

The marked abode lies below Thunder Flat;
The standing stillness joins Stone Shade.
The higher Dao has already stilled thought;
Soaring blossoms align with the heart’s axle.
*Right: Learning’s Endowment.*

Roaming the jade blade of Square Corner;
Dwelling in reclusion at Huayang.
If the Double Brilliance should chance to resemble,
The Seven Origins will then support one another.
*Right: Profound Correspondence.*

The numbered cycle is nearing completion;
The *Hai* count has not yet ended in *Ding*.
Thus inheriting the Tang era,
They shall attend as guests at the Sage’s court.
*Right: Germination of Opportunity.*

Having crossed the spiritual, there is a place to be;
Going or staying follows what is fitting.
How could numinous traces be manifest or hidden?
The dark paths know one another of themselves.
*Right: Course of Karmic Destiny.*

Carving stone above the Dark Window;
Displaying sincerity at the Winding Stair Gate.
In movement and stillness, one attends with solemn record—
Not betraying the grace of protection and recommendation.
*Right: Sincere Aspiration.*

*Right: These fifteen poems each bear two characters below, encapsulating the intent of their respective chapters. Every character within the poems carries meaning and purpose. Later readers may seek their significance within the text itself.*

*Declarations of Perfected, Scroll Thirteen.*
*Declarations of Perfected, Scroll Fourteen.*
*Investigating the Pivot of the Numinous, Chapter Four.*

To the southwest of Great Maoshan lies Four-Flat Mountain, commonly known as Square Mountain. At its base is a grotto chamber called Square Terrace. The grotto has two entrances visible from outside the mountain, connecting with Huayang and designated as a secluded lodge of separate chambers. Those who attain the Dao dwell here.

*(This mountain lies some twenty *li* from Great Maoshan. Six or seven *li* to the southwest, one grotto mouth is visible from outside. Recently, someone entered and saw a green serpent within, hence calling it Green Dragon Grotto. Near the summit and on the northern and western faces, there are also grotto cavities—none can determine which are these two entrances. The mountain also has springs that never fail in winter or summer. The mountain’s [text missing] is level, hence the names Four-Flat and Square Mountain. It abounds in southern candlewood; today, it is heaped east of Accumulated Gold Mountain. [Text missing] these trees all grow tall. The Daoists in the lodge rely on them for medicinal use.)*

Among those who first resided here were Zhang Zuchang and Liu Ping’a.

Lu Zihua, Cai Tiansheng, and Long Bogao—all dwelled upon Square Terrace.

Zhang Zuchang was a man of Pengcheng. During the Wu dynasty, he came from the north and gained entry to this chamber. Zuchang feigned death by falling from a carriage, thus concealing his form in this secluded lodge to cultivate the practice of *guarding the One*. He took as his master Bao Cha of Shangdang, fifth-generation descendant of Bao Xuan, the Han dynasty Minister over the Masses. Cha received the Dao from Master Wang. (Bao Xuan, Han dynasty Colonel-Director of the Imperial Secretariat, was slain by Wang Mang. Xuan’s son was Yong; Yong’s son was {Rili}; {Rili}’s son was [name missing].)

Liu Ping’a bore no given name or surname, refusing to reveal them to others. At the end of the Han, he served as Chief of Ping’a in Jiujiang, hence his sobriquet. Skilled in the healing arts, he performed acts of virtue, curing others’ illnesses as if they were his own. While traveling, he encountered the immortal Zhou Zhengshi, who taught him the Dao of *concealment and preservation*. Assuming the guise of a hat-wearing traveler, he came to dwell in this chamber. He regularly ingested the *solar and lunar essences*, his complexion like jade, appearing no older than thirty. (How could the two masters not know his true name? Having once revealed it, they chose never to speak of it again. The origins of Dai Meng’s clan are recounted later.)

Lu Zihua was a man of Shanyang and a disciple of Master Yin. He had already consumed the *rainbow elixir* but had not yet studied the *Inner Canon*. He came to study under the Eastern Minister, receiving and reciting the *Hidden Writ of the Grand Empyrean*. He took delight in the seclusion of Square Terrace and had no desire to ascend to the rank of immortal.

Cai Tiansheng was a man of Shanggu. In his youth, he served as a whistling attendant to the Master of Whistles, selling assorted incense in the wilds to sustain himself. His nature was kind and sincere.

Cai Tiansheng never spoke ill of others. Once, while traveling to purchase incense, he encountered the River Earl’s daughter. Recognizing her as an extraordinary being, he bowed twice and offered her a full eave’s worth of incense. Moved by his sincerity, she taught him the method of paying homage to the Heavenly Emperor and the Jade Sovereign. Thereby he attained immortality, assuming the form of a staff-bearing recluse, dwelling in seclusion upon Square Terrace. The maiden still visits him to this day, and Tiansheng regards her as his master.

Long Bogao was a man of the Later Han. When the Han general Ma Yuan, the Pacifier of the South, admonished his brother’s son, he praised this man as exemplary and worthy of emulation—this was none other than Bogao. Later, Bogao followed the immortal Daolin of the Knife and received the method of fetal ingestion. He also regularly consumed the *Azure Nourishment* formula. Assuming the guise of a drunken death, he concealed himself upon Square Terrace, becoming a disciple of the Master of the Fixed Record. (Bogao’s given name was Shu. A native of the capital district, he served as Magistrate of Shandu during the *Jianwu* era of the Han, rising to the post of Governor of Lingling. When Ma Yuan campaigned in the south, he wrote to his nephew Yan, saying: “Long Bogao is sincere, circumspect, and prudent, his speech beyond reproach. Modest, frugal, and upright, he is both incorruptible and commanding. I cherish and esteem him; may you and your peers take him as your model. Even if you fail to match Bogao, you will still be a man of caution and restraint—like one who carves a swan but falls short, yet still resembles a duck.”)

To the northeast of Thunderflat Mountain and the southeast of Constantgood Mountain lies Swallowmouth Mountain, so named for its three small peaks standing in close proximity. It is also called Squarecorner Mountain. In ancient times, men once compounded the Nine-Cauldron Elixir here. The Recluse delighted in dwelling here at that time, and still frequents the place. Beneath Squarecorner Mountain lies a grotto chamber called the Square Source Lodge, which has two entrances often visible from without. These five men now sojourn here together. (This mountain lies southwest of the Great Horizontal. Two earthen mounds, resembling a shield and a dragon, once stood nearby, though their names are no longer known. The earlier mention of Li Ming compounding the elixir refers to this place. The Recluse—after Yuan the Assistant departed the world and wished to conceal his name—took this title. Here, before receiving his official appointment, he paused to cultivate his practice in the grotto lodge. The mountain still has two small entrances, and the five men, including the four from Fourflat Mountain, come and go freely, often gathering together. Thus, when Master Yang dreamed of Yuan the Assistant, he said, “I have just come from Fourflat Mountain.” The six entries above were newly transcribed by hand, having been conveyed by the Master of Life Preservation.)

Within Deer Track Mountain lies an impassable ravine. The “impassable ravine” is a space of barely one or two *mu*, with no passage leading in or out—hence its name. The grotto chamber is surrounded on all sides by blue-white stone, which naturally radiates light like a silken canopy unfurled. The floor is perfectly level, with stone beds and stone couches of varying lengths, their partitions and divisions as though carved with precision, yet all in perfect order. To the northeast is a small opening, barely wide enough for a person to enter. Once inside, one may proceed two or three hundred paces.

Upon reaching the grotto chamber, the entrance is initially narrow, but grows ever more spacious the deeper one proceeds. Outside the entrance to the south lie three piles of stone. Beneath these piles, a rope may be found. Alternatively, a small stone may be used to cover the cave’s opening. The size of the opening resembles the Three Convenient Gates of Huayang. These gates, too, are sealed with small stones. Without prolonged purification and fasting, the rope cannot be found. The Deer Track Grotto is likewise—it does not admit impure *qi*.

(This mountain now lies within the borders of Southern Xuzhou, directly opposite Maoshan. From the north, it can be seen. Daoist adepts also dwell here. The deer tracks are upon the stone, hence the name. The entrance to the grotto is discernible, yet it is kept solemn and pristine, with none daring to profane it. As this text likens it to the Three Convenient Gates of Huayang, the southern and northern grottoes were originally open, while the remaining eastern, western, and southeastern passages were sealed.)

Within the Deer Track Mountain and its grottoes reside Xie Zhijian and Wang Boliao, also known as Fanyangzi. They are the younger brothers of He Miaoshuda, the Han-era Commandant of Yue Cavalry, born of the same mother, who from youth were fond of the Dao.

They once dwelled south of Fanshan in Hedong, practicing *fu-shi* (ingesting medicinal substances), hence their self-styled name, Fanyangzi.

*(Zhong Jun, in response to Prefectural Scribe’s inquiry regarding Ge Xuan, stated: "He resided on Gaizhu Mountain, constantly accompanied by Xie Zhijian. Now Zhijian is here—whether this is the same person coming and going, or if there are two individuals sharing the same name, remains unknown. The *Book of Later Han* records that He Miao was He Jin’s half-brother, serving as General of Chariots and Cavalry. He curried favor with the eunuch faction and, when Jin was assassinated, Miao was slain in battle at the Vermilion Bird Gate by Wu Kuang, one of Jin’s generals, and beheaded. Dong Zhuo later exhumed his corpse and dismembered it. Since he did not deliberately employ *bingjie* (liberation through a violent death) to depart, it is unclear how he came to reside here. Their mother was also executed. Moreover, Miao was not born of the same mother as Jin, and their official ranks differed. The record states his given name was Miao and his courtesy name was Shuda, which does not align with propriety. It is likely he was another brother, not necessarily the Miao who died in battle.")*

There was also Feng Liang. Feng Liang, a soldier from Guanjun in Nanyang, began as a county clerk in his youth. At thirty, he became a commandant. While escorting an inspector, he felt ashamed of his lack of ambition. He destroyed his carriage and oxen, tore his robes and headscarf, and departed to study under a master, receiving instruction in the *Book of Songs*, the *Commentaries on the Classics*, the *Book of Rites*, and the *Book of Changes*.

He further studied Daoist arts and astrological divination. His family believed him dead, yet after fifteen years he returned. He refined his resolve and integrity, maintaining a stern and reverent demeanor. Though the province and commandery extended invitations with honors, he declined. An imperial edict specially summoned him as a worthy and eminent candidate, yet mid-journey he abandoned the post and returned home. At the time, the Three Dukes vied to yield their positions to him, but he would not descend to accept. At sixty-seven, he departed the world, crossing eastward into the mountains. Today he resides in the Deer Track Grotto.

*(A man of the Later Han Emperor An’s reign; the* Han History

Shanxing was skilled in the arts of wind-angle divination, mastering the six-day, seven-division method. He could observe qi and foretell fortune or misfortune. He often earned his livelihood through divination. Emperor An summoned him for a policy debate, where he stood out among the scholars. Later, he was appointed magistrate of Wu County. One day, a sudden gale arose, and [Rang] Zong divined that the capital would suffer a great fire. He recorded the date and sent someone to verify—his prediction proved true. The nobles, upon hearing this, memorialized the throne, and he was summoned as a Academician. Zong, ashamed to be known for his divinations, upon receiving the summons, hung his official seal and cord in the county courtyard that very night and fled. He never served again.

His son, [Qi]ye, styled Zhiyuan, inherited his father’s arts and refined them further. His disciples often numbered in the hundreds. In the second year of Emperor Shun’s *Yangjia* reign, he was summoned to the palace. He submitted a memorial on eleven matters and was appointed Gentleman of the Palace. After returning home, he was murdered by a fellow townsman, Sun Li.

Among the others were Wang Shuming, Bao Yuanzhi, and Yin Gai’s wife, along with over twenty more. All dwelled in the Northern Mountains, though their names and deeds cannot be fully recorded here. These few were the masters of the secluded grottoes and mountains. The immortals of these secluded grottoes long for those who seek the Dao, wishing to share their cavern abodes, yet in this benighted age, such people are rare.

(These grottoes, being entirely cut off from the world, serve as the dwelling places of earthbound immortals—far beyond the bounds of the thirty-six heavens.)

The Dao is lost to the adorned. How lamentable! To dwell in a household is to debase oneself.

(*Adorned* refers to those who covet official rank and courtly attire, thus neglecting the Dao. The household is but a temporary lodging.)

Within these grottoes lies an eternal abode—yet to cling to it with such attachment is truly to debase and ruin oneself. (This, too, serves as a subtle admonition to the Chief Clerk.)

The Lord of Life-Preservation spoke thus. (The above three entries are Yang’s writings.)

Fan Shuai said: The Three Offices have prison officials, not called *Tingwei*, but *Dali*. Li Feng now serves as *Dali*. I still hold one vacant post, intending it for Wang Fuzhi, not to bestow upon Xu Hu. Holding office is like the Three Officials of the prison today. (Li Feng, styled Anguo, later changed to Xuanguo, was a native of Fengyi. Li Yi’s son, originally of humble birth, possessed talent and ambition. He served the Wei as Palace Attendant and President of the Board of Civil Office. Conspiring with Xiahou Xuan to depose the Prince of Jin, Jing Wang, their plot was exposed. Summoned to court, he was struck in the waist with a sword hilt by an attendant. *Dali* should be rendered as *Dali*—the ancient prison official. During the Former Han and Wei dynasties, the *Tingwei* was also called *Dali*. This office is, in fact, a celestial post. Wang Fuzhi is Wang’s childhood name; Xu Hu is Xu Yuya.)

Bao {Qingjian} remarked: Since I am now Chief Clerk to your esteemed office, and those rat-like underlings behave as they do, you may inform the Prefect.

Having thus returned to office, Ying is also under my charge. His family has declined in recent times—what can one do but preserve what remains of them as best one may? (It is likely that *Shuzi* was Bao {Qingjian}’s childhood name. Bao was of Nanhai Prefecture and later achieved liberation through transformation. His sons had not yet returned to the capital, which is why he entrusted them to me. Bao was, in fact, Master Xu’s teacher.)

The two preceding entries bear Yang’s handwriting.

The Daoist of Wudang Mountain, Dai Meng, was originally surnamed Yan, named Ji, styled Zhongwei, and lived at the close of Emperor Ming of Han’s reign. Those who cultivate life-concealment all hide their names and birth times, hence he changed his surname to Dai and claimed official appointment under Emperor Wu. Yet this man, from youth, cherished the Dao and virtue, never serving in the world. Orphaned young, he supported his mother; when she died, he observed mourning rites and buried her. Completing his mourning period, he entered Huayang Mountain, consuming *zhu* (atractylodes), rhubarb, Solomon’s seal, mica, realgar, cinnabar, and *zhi* grasses. He received the method from Perfected One Qingling—disciple of Pei of Jizhou—and attained the Way of deathlessness. The Perfected One Pei bestowed upon him a jade pendant, a golden cauldron, scriptures, and the Stone Essence Gold Radiance Talisman. Thereafter, he could move with a light body and vigorous step, traversing the famous mountains.

Daily he could traverse seven hundred *li*, passing through many places. Yet he had still not attained the rank of immortal. (Dai then practiced with the jade pendant and golden cauldron, achieving only deathlessness, not transcendence as a divine immortal—this discrepancy in principle is somewhat difficult to clarify. Later accounts state that *Xuan Zhen* also contains its essential excerpts, and those who practice it become divine immortals who do not die, which again differs from the original scripture. As for the *Stone Essence Gold Radiance Talisman*, since it is not used as a sword, it serves only for liberation and transformation. Those who ingest a single talisman—this talisman governs concealment and reclusion, not vigorous travel. Cultivating the five grades of *zhi*—there are also methods for this in the world.)

The immortals Guo Zihua, Zhang Jilian, and Zhao Shuda, and later one named Shan Shiyuan, would come and go, journeying with him. Formerly dwelling on Mount Wudang, he now arrives at Great Mount Huo, desiring to receive texts from the Lord of Life-Command. Thus, he has not yet been permitted. (Shan had already attained the rank of Perfected One of Grand Harmony, so he should reside on Mount Taihe in Nanyang. The other three do not appear to have any other notable manifestations.)

Master Dai patted his belly, wherein lay over ten scrolls of scripture—these were the *Yellow Writ of the Grand Tenuity*. This man was the master of Xie Yun. (According to the oral transmission of the *Golden Register*, the eighth fascicle of the *Yellow Writ of the Grand Tenuity* has a catalog stating there are eight scrolls in total, yet only this one scroll has appeared in the world. Now Master Dai possesses over ten fascicles, which is also inconsistent. The meaning of "patting the belly" refers to constantly binding them at the waist. Other transmitted accounts are similar to this. Xie Yun, styled Daotong, was a man of Liyang. In his youth, he was abducted by others.)

He was sold to Dongyang. Later, he reported the matter to the authorities but was falsely accused and imprisoned in Wushang. The case nearly resulted in his execution. One night, an old man bestowed upon him a talisman, and a youth in yellow robes came and went. Thus, he was spared. During the Xiankang era, he arrived in Xiangyang and entered Mount Wudang, where he met Dai Meng—this was the same man who had previously been in prison with him. Thereupon, he received the Dao. He later took office as magistrate of three counties: Liyang, Xinfeng, and Xidao. Wherever he served, his spiritual efficacy was abundant. Though he lived past seventy, he showed no signs of aging. Eventually, he announced his departure from the world.

The youth in yellow robes was none other than the official of the Jade Pendant and Golden Bell. It is said that there was always one person seated beside him—*fu er* (likely *rou fu*, though its form is unclear)—who was the Perfected One of the Grand Ultimate, coming and going at times.

(According to this account, it appears to answer a question about the youth in yellow. The meaning may also refer to the hour of *wu*. Since this concerns Xie, it likely stems from a face-to-face inquiry into the matter.)

Having received and practiced the *Scripture of the Jade Pendant and Golden Bell*, one naturally summons the Perfected One of the Grand Ultimate. As the saying goes: *"Ingesting the Ninefold Radiance of the sun and moon, one is welcomed into the household of the Grand Ultimate."* This is what it means. Such is the method of the Mysterious Perfection.

This also constitutes its essential excerpts. Those who practice it attain immortality and do not die.

The Perfected Master Pei had thirty-four disciples: eighteen studied the Buddhist Dao, while the remainder pursued the Dao of immortality (*Beixu*—likely a miswritten form of *Pianhuyong*). (His disciples included Liu Xianlin, Xin Zhongfu, and Zhao Zichang.)

The Perfected Master Zhou had fifteen disciples, four of whom mastered Buddhist teachings. (His foremost disciples were Wang *Yuwei* Da, Li Jiandao, and Quan Fajian.)

At Mount Tongbai, there were twenty-five disciples, eight of whom studied Buddhism. (The foremost among them were Yu Hongzhi, Zhu Faling, Zheng Wencheng, and Chen Yuanzi. This list merely highlights the most eminent. Both Xin and Quan appear to bear foreign surnames, likely indicating Buddhist disciples.)

The above eight entries were transcribed by a clerk and compiled into a single connected record.

In the mountains of Huo, there were Daoist practitioners Deng Yibo and Wang Xuanfu, who received and practiced the methods of consuming *qingjing* stone-cooked rice and absorbing the solar cinnabar radiance of *danjing*. Through concentrated meditation in the Grotto-Chamber, after thirty-four years, they attained inner vision of the five viscera and the ability to write in darkness at midnight. On the fifth day of the first month of this year, the Supreme Emperor dispatched a feathered chariot to welcome Yibo and Xuanfu. On that very day, they ascended to heaven riding clouds and dragons in broad daylight. Now they reside in the Northern *Xuanpu* Terrace, where they have been appointed as Perfected Ones of the Central Marchmount. (Yibo was from Wu; Xuanfu was from Pei.)

In the mountains of Huayin, there were Daoist practitioners Yin Qianzi, Zhang Shisheng, and Li Fanghui.

These were all contemporaries of Emperor Wu of Jin. They received from the immortal Guan Chengzi the method of steaming cinnabar and consuming *Atractylodes*, and by partaking of these, gained longevity and vigorous movement. They further received from Su Men’s Zhou Shouling the Dao of consuming the radiance of *danxia*. Having practiced this for fifty years, they concentrated their minds on inner vision, no longer requiring food or drink. Their bodies became light and strong, their complexions like those of youths. On the twelfth day of the second month of this year, the Supreme Unity dispatched a summons to welcome them. On that very day, they ascended to heaven riding clouds. Now they reside in the Dark Realm, where they have been appointed as Perfected Ones of the Lofty Immortals. Zhang Shisheng was named Earl of the Eastern Source. In Mount Heng, there were Daoist practitioners Zhang Lizheng and Ye Mingqi. Lizheng, having entered the mountains during the late Han, consumed *huangjing*, his complexion remaining robust and youthful, perpetually like that of a man of forty. Mingqi entered the mountains at the end of the Wei and consumed pills of *zexie* and cypress seeds. Together they dwelled in a cave, later both receiving from the Western City Lord Wang the *hongjing* cinnabar formula. They had been consuming this elixir for forty-three years, though they suffered from the scarcity of cinnabar. Both emerged in Guangzhou, becoming Buddhist monks during the tenure of Teng Han as Prefect. Thereupon they attained inner and outer clarity, their eyes bright and bodies light, able to travel five hundred *li* in a day. They also practiced *shouyi*, having maintained this for thirty years.
