On the first day of the third month, the Eastern Florescence dispatched a summons to welcome them. On that very day, they ascended to heaven riding clouds. Now they reside in the Wind Chamber of the Square Cauldron, both having become Upper Immortals. (Teng Han served as Prefect of Guangzhou in the *jiayin* year of the tenth *Yonghe* reign [354 CE]. Their transcendence occurred in the *yichou* year, twelve years later, meaning they had already been consuming the elixir for thirty-two years before emerging once more.)

In Mount Qian of Lujiang, there were Daoist practitioners Zheng Jingshi and Zhang Chonghua. Both received oral instructions from the immortal Meng Deran at the beginning of the Jin and entered the mountains to practice the method of guarding the five viscera and containing the solar essence, while also consuming sesame. They further consumed the Dark Elixir. After a long time, they no longer required food or drink, their bodies becoming light and strong, their original forms transformed.

On the nineteenth day of the fourth month this year, the Northern Mystery Elder Taiyi welcomed him with clouds, and he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. He now resides in the Dark Realm.

In Mount Guacang, there was a Daoist practitioner named Ping Zhongjie, a man from Hezhong. During the great Hu turmoil in the Central Plains, he crossed the Yangtze and entered Mount Guacang. There, he received from his master Song Jun the *Way of the Mind-Mirror as a Repository*, mastering the hundred spirits and practicing the *Cavern Chamber* arts. After forty-five years of such cultivation, his meditative essence deepened, his form grew younger, and his body attained genuineness. On the first day of the fifth month this year, the Central Yellow Elder dispatched an envoy to welcome him. That very day, he rode clouds and mounted a dragon, ascending to heaven in broad daylight. He now dwells in the Cloud Terrace of Canglang.

(The "great Hu turmoil" refers to the time of Liu Yuan and Liu Cong. Shi Le was the "lesser Hu.")

In Mount Xiaobai, there was a Daoist practitioner named Zhao Guangxin, a man from Yangcheng. At the end of the Wei dynasty, he crossed the Yangtze and entered these mountains. He received from Li Facheng the method of ingestion and from his master Zuo Jun the *Way of Guarding the Dark Center*, through which he perceived the five viscera and mastered the art of penetrating vision. After seventy or eighty years of such practice, he traveled among commanderies and states.

At times, he would sell medicines and move among the world of men, yet none recognized him. Often, he came to the capital’s markets to purchase cinnabar, from which he refined the *Nine-Flowered Elixir*. Upon completing the elixir, a single dose was taken, and the Supreme Unity Dao Lord dispatched an envoy on the seventeenth day of the sixth month this year to welcome him. After lingering three days to bid farewell to his fellow practitioners in the mountains, he then rode clouds and mounted a dragon, ascending to heaven in broad daylight. He now dwells in the Eastern Flower.

In the Wolf Five Mountains of the Eastern Sea, there was a Daoist practitioner named Yu Wengsheng, a man from Kuaiji. Formerly, he received from the immortal Jie Jun the method of ingesting solar essence. During the Wu dynasty, he came to conceal himself in these mountains, where he also practiced the *Way of Cloud-Turning and Form-Returning*. Through prolonged and concentrated refinement, his body grew ever younger, like that of a child. On the twenty-third day of the seventh month this year, the Eastern Supreme Emperor dispatched an envoy to welcome him. That very day, he rode clouds and ascended to heaven. He now resides in Mount Yanggu. (The Wolf Five Mountains lie in the sea, opposite the White Chapter Shore, and are now simply called Wolf Mountain.)

In Red Water Mountain, there was a Daoist practitioner named Zhu Ruzi. At the end of the Wu dynasty, he entered the mountains, where he ingested chrysanthemum and atractylodes. Later, he encountered Xi Gui Zi, from whom he begged the method of transcending the world. Xi Gui Zi transmitted to him essential instructions and the *Method of Visualizing the Mud-Pill Palace*. After thirty years of practice, he could summon clouds and rain within his grotto chamber. On the fifth day of the eighth month this year, the Queen Mother of the West dispatched an envoy to welcome him. That very day, he ascended to heaven in a five-colored cloud chariot. He now dwells on the Terrace of Accumulated Stones. (It is said that Red Water Mountain lies ten *li* south of {Jin Yi} County, accessible by entering three hundred *li* from Nanxi Mouth. The mountain is entirely red, fifty *li* in circumference, and over a thousand *zhang* in height. This should correspond to the Red Rock in the northeast of Yongjia in Linhai. The Red Mountain where Master Xu resided, also called Burning Mountain, is this same place.)

In the Five Sacred Peaks and other renowned mountains, Daoist practitioners number in the hundreds of thousands. This year alone, those who attained the Dao and ascended to heaven are too numerous to record by name. Every year, every month, there are those who depart. Such accounts cannot be exhaustively chronicled; here, only those who attained the Dao in a single year are briefly noted. Some, though unwilling to ascend as immortals, remain for long ages in the Five Peaks and famous mountains—their number is likewise beyond reckoning. Others serve as immortal officials, charged with overseeing the sacred mountains, and these, too, number in the thousands.

On the twentieth night of the ninth month, the *Record of the Pure Numinous* was issued. (The preceding eight entries were transcribed by an assistant and compiled into a single scroll. *Pure Numinous* here refers to Pei Qingling. This ninth month should correspond to the *yichou* year, recording those who attained the Dao within that year.)

Wu Mu was a man of Chang’an. In his youth, he served as a county clerk, abusing his office to extort the people. When the people brought suit against him, the law demanded his execution. Mu fled in panic, escaping deep into the mountains and forests. After days without food, he came upon a stone chamber where he found Master Sun engaged in reclusive study. Around the chamber, millet and sesame were planted, and the room was always filled with food. Mu arrived and begged for sustenance, remaining for over a month without leaving.

Sun the Master knew he was a fugitive but initially made no inquiry. He gave him food, tended to his needs, and taught him scriptures and the Dao, expounding on fortune and misfortune. Upon hearing this, Mu’s heart opened and his mind awakened. He knocked his head to the ground, striking his chest in remorse, confessing the origins of his deeds, the conduct of his life, and the facts of his crimes. He begged for a chance to reform. Thereupon, he remained in the stone chamber, sweeping and serving the Master. After forty years, the Master transmitted the Dao to him. Together they gathered herbs and consumed sesame, diligently cultivating the scriptural teachings. After three hundred and twenty years, he ingested the elixir and ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Zhu {Quantu} was a man of Chenliu. He was lawless, devoting himself to robbery and banditry. Later, when his crimes were discovered and men came to seize him, {Quantu} escaped and fled far to another region. He arrived at Shaoshi Mountain in Runan, where he encountered Master Feng engaged in reclusive study. The Master said that after three years, he would transmit to him the Way of the Perfected Immortals. He remained in the mountains, consuming herbs and cultivating the Dao for thirty-eight years. Later, he entered the Eastern Mountains, living to the age of one hundred and forty-seven. An immortal descended and led him into the cavern of Dayou Mountain, where he became a Perfected One.

Guo Jing was a man of Yingchuan. Orphaned in youth, he had no parents, and his brothers lived in poverty and hardship.

With nowhere to settle, he wandered without refuge. At sixteen, the county summoned him to serve as an official. Later, he committed an offense and fled into hiding, remaining concealed for two months without emerging. He encountered Master Zheng, who saved and delivered all beings, exhorting him with the Dharma. Jing then followed Zheng, carrying his eaves and tending to his needs for seven years without敢懈怠. He received the essentials of Daoist guiding and pulling, consuming mountain yam and poria. He attained a lifespan of three hundred years. Later, on Tianwei Mountain, Master Red Pine descended to transmit the true Dao to both of them. Now, he resides in the cavern of Dayou as a Perfected One.

Fan Boci was a man of Guiyang. His family originally followed worldly customs, but he suddenly fell into madness and evil influences, contracting a consumptive illness that confined him to his bed for over a year. His family invited physicians and expended their wealth, yet his condition remained incurable. Hearing that the Great Dao was pure and required no offerings, his resolve shifted. Learning of Shen Jing’s diligent cultivation as a Daoist and his efficacy in healing, he abandoned worldly affairs to follow him. After fifty days, all his ailments were cured. Later, he sought out Lu Wanzhi to receive the true inner Dao. Wanzhi could not enter the mountains, but Boci, discontent with the world, bid farewell and entered Tianmu Mountain. There, he consumed sesame and cultivated with focused intent for seventeen years. The Perfected Lord of the Great Cavern and Controller of Destiny descended to transmit the thirty-six-scroll scripture. Later, he ingested the returning elixir and ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Now, he is the Perfected One of Mysterious Unity.

*(The passages marked "云云" were omitted by the copyist, not due to any lacuna in the original. There are four entries, transcribed by someone in the eastern region who claimed them to be authentic texts, though their authorship and transmission remain unknown. As their content is meaningful, they are recorded here in continuation.)*

These four individuals each had their own circumstances: the first was a harsh official, the second a violent bandit, the third orphaned and destitute, and the fourth bound to worldly affairs—all enduring the meager fortunes of the mortal realm. Yet they attained the Dao, demonstrating that its realization depends on the mind’s creation, not on good or evil.

Sima Jizhu later entered the stone chamber of the Weiyu Mountain’s Dayou Palace, where he received the *Method of Transforming Form through Stone Essence and Golden Radiance* from Xiling Zidu. Xiling Zidu was the Celestial Maiden of Supreme Mystery. Among those who now reside in the Dayou chamber with him is Bao Shuyang of Guangning.

The men of Taiyuan, Wang Yangbo, and of Yingchuan, Liu Yuwei, as well as Duan Jizheng of Daijun, all received the Dao from their master, Xiling Zidu. As Jizhu prepared to depart, he left behind a simulacrum of himself upon his pillow and mat to serve as his likeness. His tomb lies south of Shengpan Mountain in Chengdu of Shu Commandery. In former times, Marquis Wu of the Zhuge clan erected a stele before Jizhu’s tomb to inscribe his virtue. At the end of the stele’s eulogy, it reads:

*"In the vast and silent mystery,
Yin and Yang merge as one.
Heaven and Earth unite in balance,
And the ten thousand beings flourish in clarity.
The Master’s principles are manifest—
Discerning softness and hardness.
Spirits and gods bear witness,
While the Six Perfections shine forth."*

(*The* Zhen Gao *states that Jizhu swallowed rainbow essence, yet his head and feet came to rest in separate places. The commentary to the* Sword Classic *says, ‘He ingested the elixir blade, and worms flowed from his body.’ Now, according to the account of the Eastern Minister, he left behind a simulacrum upon his pillow and mat to serve as his likeness—this likely refers to his departure through the ‘spirit-pill transmigration’ method. Since the Han histories do not clearly record his end, there is no way to further discern the details of the matter.*)

The Guangning Master Bao Shuyang was a grandee under King Zhang Er and King Zhang Ao of Zhao during the reign of Emperor Gao of Han. From youth, he cultivated longevity practices. After ingesting cassia powder, he died suddenly in a privy. His tomb now lies on the northern mountain of Jicheng in Liaodong. (Emperor Gao established the Yan Commandery, with Ji as its seat—this likely refers to the time before its division.)

The Taiyuan Master Wang Yangbo was originally Wang Tan, a Palace Attendant and Zhonglang of Langye during the regency of Empress Lü under Emperor Gao of Han. From youth, he consumed *ze xie*. Together with the Marquis of Liu, Zhang Liang, he gathered herbs on Mount Zhongnan, but Wang Yangbo did not keep pace. He then became a disciple of Jizhu. (In the Former Han, Palace Attendants were not eunuchs, and Zhonglang was not an office of the Gentlemen of the Palace—this position may have been established later.)

The Yingchuan Master Liu Hui was originally Liu Feng, a Master of Public Carriages under Emperor Jing of Han. Later, he studied under Jizhu. In his later years, he ingested the essences of the sun and moon, becoming an inner-chamber disciple. After attaining the Dao, he returned to his hometown in his later years. Leaving behind his form in staff and sandals, he died beneath a mulberry tree. His tomb now lies on the western mountain of Ancheng County in Runan.

The Daijun Master Duan Jizheng was originally a recluse. No records exist of his ingestions. In his later years, he studied the Dao under Jizhu.

Crossing the Qinzhou Reedy Waters, he was detained and drowned, his body recovered and buried by the riverbank. This is present-day Qinchuan in Nanzheng. This man was also an inner-chamber disciple of Jizhu. (Upon examining these four individuals, all employed the arts of the Spirit Pellet and miscellaneous liberations.)

Jizhu had one son and one daughter, both of whom attained the Dao. The son was named Fayu, and the daughter was named Jihua. Both now reside within Mount Weiyu. Jihua is presently reciting the Thirty-Nine Chapters, though she has yet to complete them. (This principle is also difficult to expound in detail.)

Jizhu recited the Jade Scripture and ingested the essence of Bright Cinnabar. His hands cradled the radiance of dawn, and his complexion today remains that of a twenty-year-old maiden. His beard measures three feet in length, black as ink. Yesterday, the Eastern Minister recounted this, just as I have set it down—truly a wondrous affair. Yet I know not how it may truly be. (This line was recorded by Master Yang in a letter to the Prefectural Governor. The phrase of uncertainty may indicate another meaning.)

Jizhu relinquished his form and concealed his light, hiding his traces in Mount Weiyu. The *Purple Yang Tradition* fully records these matters. Last night, the Eastern Minister arrived, and I ventured to inquire about Jizhu’s origins and later years. The Eastern Minister responded, instructing me to set it down separately, as presented above. I hope you will not take offense. Once read, consign it to the flames. (This is Master Yang’s letter to the Prefectural Governor. The *Biographies of Perfected Transcendents* compiled by Zhou Jun of present times records Jizhu’s affairs only briefly. No other authentic handwritten account has been seen. Judging by these words, it appears to be incorrect. It seems there was a preceding annotation, but one line is now missing.)

What follows is the record of the Sagely Master Li. In general, it aligns with the foregoing, though the Eastern Minister has additionally provided annotations. The annotations approach ten thousand characters—a most extraordinary work. Recently, we have discussed many matters of divine transformation, and Jizhu’s case was briefly mentioned. Last month, I was further entrusted with the *Commentary on the Scripture of the Divine Tiger*. The commentary is unlike anything heard in the world, and I myself do not yet grasp its purport. Should I learn of others like Jizhu, I shall certainly report it in confidence. (This is also Master Yang’s letter to the Prefectural Governor. Since it concerns Jizhu’s affairs, I have placed it in sequence here rather than appending it to the lower scroll. The Prefectural Governor, compiling the *Biographies of Perfected Transcendents*, wished to place Jizhu first. Thus, Master Yang sought to inquire about his origins and later years. The two scriptures annotated by the Director of Destinies have yet to be revealed to the world.)

The above ten entries contain Yang’s writings. Fan Anyuan recently remarked, “Zhanzi did not serve the Qi.”

The Qi forces attacked him. The *Commentary on the Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "Zhan was without propriety." (This refers to an event in the *Zuozhuan*. The character {言甚} is written as 谭. Its pronunciation is *tan*. It is the name of a state. In the thirteenth year of Duke Zhuang’s reign, it was destroyed by Duke Huan of Qi. The reason for recounting this here is unclear, though it seems to allude to something.)

Zhuangzi studied under Master Changsang, who transmitted to him esoteric teachings, whence he was called Zhuangzi. He concealed himself on Mount Baodu, ingested the Northern Nurturing Fire Elixir, and ascended to heaven in broad daylight, whereupon he was appointed to the rank of Attendant Gentleman of the Gate of the Supreme Ultimate. (Master Changsang was the teacher of Bian Que, as recorded in the *Wei Chronicles* and the *Records of the Grand Historian*. If the world truly knew Zhuangzi to have been such a figure, his writings would be all the more esteemed.)

Shi Cun was a native of Qi who styled himself the "Gentle Basin Master." He attained the Dao of escape, transformation, and luminous manifestation, and now resides on Mount Song. Some say that in the past, there was a Master Gourd on Mount Shaoshi—this was none other than he. However, he has not yet received the scriptures of the Most High, and thus has not yet attained Perfection. His practice of the Jade Axe and the Military Fire Talismans represents but a branch of what he has received. Shi Cun was among the three thousand disciples of Confucius. (That one of the three thousand should be such a figure, yet not numbered among the seventy-two, makes clear that the Master did not teach the Dao of immortality. Master Gourd was the teacher of Fei Changfang. The Military Fire Talisman remains extant in the world. The above three entries contain Yang’s writings.)

Han Weiyuan, the Perfected One of Mount Jiuyi, once received teachings from Song Dexuan of Mount Song. Dexuan was a man of the Zhou King Xuan era.

Those who ingest this *Lingfei Liujia* attain the Dao, gaining the ability to travel three thousand *li* in a single day and repeatedly transform their forms into birds and beasts. Having obtained the Way of the Dark and Numinous, they now dwell on Mount Songgao. Weiyuan followed them for a long time before receiving the method, and through its practice, he achieved the completion of the Way. He now resides on Mount Jiuyi. Among his female disciples were Guo Shaoyao, Zhao Aier, Wang Lulian, and others—several dozen in all—who likewise received this method and attained the Dao. Some roam the Dark Continent, while others dwell on the Eastern Florescence Terrace or the Square Cauldron Terrace; all remain present today.

The Lady of the Southern Marchmount spoke of this, saying: Guo Shaoyao was the daughter of Guo Jian, the Han general who crossed the Liao River and Duke of Dongping. From youth, she was deeply devoted to the Dao. The Perfected One thus transmitted to her the *Liujia*. Zhao Aier was the elder sister of Zhao Gai, the Prefect of Youzhou and Chief Clerk to Liu Yu, the Inspector of Yuyang. Fond of the Dao, she achieved liberation through the corpse and later received this talisman. Wang Lulian was the daughter of Wang Bogang, the City Gate Commandant of Fanyang during the reign of Emperor Ming of Wei. She too studied the Dao, and one day suddenly abandoned her husband, Li Ziqi.

Entering the mountains of Luhun, the Perfected One transmitted this method to her. Ziqi was a man of Wei in Sizhou and tutor to the Prince of Qinghe. He often spoke of this woman’s mad flight, saying that one day she vanished without a trace. (This account appears in the *Scripture of the Numinous Flight and Six Jia*; the Chief Clerk copied it out.)

The Han Grand General Huo Guang had a slave in charge of his wardrobe named Huanche, who once observed two stars and thereby attained an age of six hundred years; he remains there still. (This account appears in the *Scripture of the Square Cauldron and Cavern Chamber*; the later Chief Clerk copied it out. According to the *Book of Wei*, in the first year of the Qinglong era, the Inspector of Bingzhou, Bi Gui, sent a Xian slave of the Han General Who Crossed the Liao, Fan Mingyou, aged three hundred and fifty years, who ate and drank like an ordinary person. The slave said: "Huo Xian was the lesser wife of the Director of the Palace Banquet. Hu You’s wife was the former wife of the Director of the Palace Banquet." If one follows this wife, it is certainly not false.)

Those who ingested the blossoms of langgan jade and then tended their graves at Yingqiu were Yanmenzi, Gaoqiuzi, and Master Hongya. Yanmenzi’s grave lies in Yuyang’s Shuilu County. (In Yuyang of Youzhou there is a Shuilu County; today in Shangdang there is also a Shuilu County. Yanmen is the same as Xianmen.) Gaoqiuzi’s grave lies in Wenxi County of Zhongshan. (In Zhongshan there is Anxi County; Wenxi belongs to Hedong.) Master Hongya’s grave lies in Guzang County of Wuwei. (The *Records of Liangzhou* call it Guzang County.) The people of these three commanderies all speak of this.

The ancient tombs of the dead are now empty. Yet it was not known that in Gaoqiuzi’s time, he achieved liberation through the corpse and entered the Six Radiance Mountains. Later, he ingested the powder of golden liquor and further received the blossoms of langgan jade in Zhongshan. Only then did he feign death, entering the Dark Realm, where he received scriptures and became the Perfected One of the Central Marchmount—and he remains there to this day. Yanmenzi now resides in the Golden Court of the Great Cavern of Mount Meng, where he received scriptures and became the Immortal Minister of the Middle Prime. Master Hongya is now the Perfected One of Qingcheng. Those who rinsed with dragon embryo and parted through death, or drank jade essence and knocked upon their coffins, were the former masters Wang Xicheng, Zhao Boxuan, and Liu Zixian. (Master Wang once used the sword for liberation, not the dragon embryo or various elixirs; perhaps "jade essence" refers to quchen.) Those who ingested golden elixirs and met their end were Zang Yanfu, Zhang Zifang, and Mo Dizi. Those who took nine cycles of the pill and left their corpses behind, or swallowed the *naogui* and had insects flow from their bodies, were Sima Jizhu, Ning Zhongjun, King Yan of Zhao, and Prince Jin. (Tongbo also used the sword for liberation; this should refer to those who swallowed the *naogui*, not the nine cycles. Sima Jizhu also employed divine pills for martial liberation, as the *Right Radiance* states: "Head and feet in separate places.")

King Yan of Zhao pursued immortality yet left no distinct traces. (Jingchun remarked: "Had there been no efficacy, he would have investigated the matter earlier.") King Mu of Zhou journeyed north to the slopes of Kunlun, where he personally drank the crimson marrow of Mount Jiang and partook of the fruit of the jade trees, yet his tomb lies in Jijun. (This account is recorded in the *Chronicles of King Mu*, from the time he met the Queen Mother of the West.) Great Yu visited Mount Zhong, where he consumed purple *mushi*, became intoxicated on golden wine, ingested the *Lingbao*, and practiced the Nine Perfections, yet he was still buried in Kuaiji. (This matter also appears in the *Five Talismans*; the *Teachings of Master Mao* further states he received and practiced the Way of Profound Perfection.)

The Great King of the Northern Rong, Changhu, presented Emperor Shun with the frost of white *lang* and the ten-cycle Purple Flower, elixirs that grant long life and flight to transcendence, aligning one with Heaven and Earth. Yet Shun consumed them and soon died, buried in the wilds of Cangwu. These lords had already ascended the numinous slopes with dragon hymns and stirred the cloud pools with phoenix drums, yet they still employed corpse-liberation and feigned death—seeking to sever the bonds of life and death, and to show the people the limits of beginning and end. How could this be the same as the decaying corpses in the Great Yin, flesh offered to ants? Their intent was simply to restrain those who defy the world and to block the aspirations of the vulgar.

(Though among the sagely kings of antiquity, none have shown clear signs of Yao or Tang attaining the Dao, nor traces of their becoming spirit-officials—)

As for Master Qingjing, Peng Keng, Feng Gang, the Four Elders of Mount South, and the Eight Lords of Huainan, all consumed the supreme elixirs, yet not a single dose. They themselves chose their words and deeds, retiring in seclusion to the mountains and forests, delighting in roaming as immortals, grieving only at ascending the void. It was not that they could not ascend to Heaven—they simply chose not to. These lords wandered freely among the Five Peaks, altering their names and appearances, no longer resorting to the ultimate art of corpse-liberation.

(Peng Keng is the name of Peng Zu. Qingjing also appears in Peng’s *Traditions* and the *Transmissions of Lord Wang* among the methods for consuming *shi-xun* rice. Feng Gang and the other immortals each have their own distinct manifestations.)

The Yellow Emperor himself mined copper from Mount Shou to cast the tripod, while tigers, leopards, and a hundred beasts tended the fires and assisted at the furnace. Once the tripod was completed, the Yellow Emperor suddenly passed away and was buried on Mount Qiao. Five hundred years later, the mountain collapsed, revealing an empty chamber with no corpse—only a treasured sword with a vermilion scabbard remained. One day, even that vanished without a trace.

(The *Traditions of the Immortals* records: riding dragons while grasping their whiskers, and Prince Jin driving a swan—all differing accounts.)

(This may also be a case of transformation followed by reappearance.) Master Jade was the father of Emperor Yao. He once journeyed to Mount Zhong, where he obtained the *Scripture of Nine Transformations and Ten Changes*, enabling him to conceal the sun and moon and wander among the stars. Later, he suddenly fell ill and passed away, his tomb established on Mount Bohai. During the decline of the Xia dynasty, someone opened the prince’s tomb, finding the chamber entirely empty—only a sword remained on the northern couch, emitting the roars of dragons and the howls of tigers. No one dared approach it, and afterward, it too vanished without a trace. (The father of Yao is written as 帝{人告} in some texts, while external records use the character {学告}.)

The tomb of Prince Wang Qiao lies in Jingling. During the Warring States period, it was opened again, revealing only a sword in the chamber. Just as someone attempted to examine it, the sword suddenly flew into the heavens. Luan Ba once achieved liberation through martial transformation, departing to reside in Mount Linlü. After thirteen years, he returned home and now dwells in Mount Chimei Redstone. (*The Book of Han* states that Luan Ba served as governor of Guiyang and Yuzhang before being imprisoned and executed—this was likely a case of using the *Lingwan* elixir for liberation, as also recorded in the *Transmissions of the Immortals*.)

These three accounts are all excerpted from the hand-copied *Sword Scripture*, a text not easily encountered. Since they describe the deeds of various immortals, I have carefully transcribed them here to supplement the categories. Where do the perfected ones reside? Radiant as the southern star, they embrace the myriad ages, ascending on light to revolve above. Transforming into the Three Ways, with the sun and moon as neighbors, they are truly profound, truly masters—known as the Primal Ones. Changing into the Three Elders, they befriend the ancestors of emperors. How could one know the perfected? They do not come to wander, observing the signs of transformation among mortals, blending with the world as companions. Casting off their feathers and sinking their bells, they rest in the southern lands—how could they delight in omens, lingering long among the俗?

From the *Mysterious Records of the Seven Sages*: When the Red Lord descended to teach, he altered his traces and took the form of a śramaṇa, revealing the names of himself and his six disciples. Bound by the world’s defilements, he did not conform to the age’s destiny. The Red Omen lurked in terror, and the Three Pillars brought calamity. The worthy ones journeyed south, making for the Three Peaks. The Dark Lord came forth—who among men could know him?

(The Red Omen is the delusive star of {tomb-fire}; the Three Pillars are three stars within the Five Chariots. *Step* is read *lǚ*, returning.)

In the Primal Realm, he is the Primal Lord; in the Dark Palace, the Dark Master; at the Southern Star, the Old Man of the Southern Culmen; in the Great Void, the Perfected of the Great Void; on the Southern Peak, Red Pine Master. He is the teacher of the Celestial Thearch’s Four Perfected Ones and the companion of the Great One.

(These four passages were transcribed by the Senior Archivist. It is unclear from which scripture they originally derive, but all expound the teachings of the Red Lord of the Southern Peak—his titles as master and companion—with slight variations from other texts.)

Mount Tongbai rises eighteen thousand *zhang* high. Its eightfold ridges encircle over eight hundred *li*, appearing identical from all four sides. It stands at the eastern sea’s edge in Kuaiji, one end submerged in the ocean. Within Tongbai lies the Land of Immortality of Golden Court, forty *li* in circumference, veiled by yellow clouds. Its trees bear jade-teeth and sapphire fruits; its springs flow with stone marrow and golden essence. The mountain is wholly composed of five-colored gold. Journeying south along the Cinnabar River, one finds a cavern where paths converge—passing through it for over thirty *li* leads to the destination.

(This mountain now lies within the borders of several counties, including Linhai. The submerged end is today called Kuocang, situated north of Ninghai County and south of {Jin Yi}. Golden Court is the place referred to by the former Right Assistant Minister. Though this site lies beyond the mountain, it resembles Golden Numen, yet its numinous wonders surpass it. Few today know its true location, though those gathering vines occasionally encounter and enter it.)

The {Tuwu} and {Fu'ao} abound here—naturally, one may seek them out. Yet since I have already settled in Wu, how could I again search for them in Yue? Thus, I set my heart at rest.

(The office of the Perfected One of Tongbai is, after all, within the grotto-heaven.)

The Lady of Purple Tenuity said: The following passage is from a certain text:

The Eight Stillnesses Mountain rises five thousand *li* high and spans {seven thousand} *li* in circumference. It adjoins the Square Mountain of Canglang, and beneath it lies the Azure Sea. Upon the mountain stands the Dark Pool Mystic Palace of the Forest-Riding Perfected One, the domain governed by the Eastern King Father. This mountain is composed of numinous jades and jasper, bearing blue-green blossoms and crimson fruits, while flying gold issues forth from its crevices. It lies northeast of Canglang Mountain and southeast of Penglai Mountain.

(This is the dwelling of the Great Thearch of Fusang. The Square Mountain is Mount Fangzhang, a renowned island in the sea, frequently mentioned in the *Preface to the Five Marchmounts*.)

To the northwest of Fangzhang stands the great Yin Completeness Mountain, and to the southwest of Canglang lies the great Yang Perpetuity Mountain. Each encircles fourteen hundred *li* and rises seven hundred *li* in height.

This mountain is the dwelling place of many perfected transcendents and immortals. These two mountains serve as the markers of the Yang Nine and Hundred Six kalpas. When the Hundred Six kalpa approaches, the waters of Yang Perpetuity Mountain recede, while those of Yin Completeness Mountain rise. When the Yang Nine kalpa approaches, the waters of Yin Completeness recede, while those of Yang Perpetuity rise. Recently, it has been the turn of Yin Completeness, and its waters have already risen nine thousand *zhang* above the mountain’s base. The arrival of the Hundred Six kalpa is not far off. (When the water’s edge of Yin Completeness emerges so high above the mountain, this should correspond to the Yang Nine kalpa, yet it is spoken of as the Hundred Six—this may be a misstatement. Alternatively, it could mean the water’s rise has reached such a height, not that the water first receded. Yet water seeks its own level and is not swift or turbulent. The two mountains are not far apart; how could one stand so abruptly isolated?)

The following two entries were transcribed by the Senior Scribe: Before reaching the first high mountain west of the temple, at the northern end of Dragon’s Tail, there is a great spring. It bursts forth from the earth, over six *zhang* in length and five *zhang* in width, penetrating six *chi* into the ground. Its flow strikes the earth for over two hundred *bu* before diverging three *li* from the road. Opposite the second high mountain west of the temple’s rear, there is another great spring. It emerges from the earth, over four *zhang* in length and three *chi* in width, penetrating four *chi* into the ground. Its force scours the earth for over three hundred *bu* before diverging two *li* from the road. Near the temple’s rear flank, there is yet another great spring. It issues from the earth, over five *zhang* in length and four *zhang* in width, penetrating slightly over two *chi* into the ground. Its flow converges inward, one hundred fifty *bu* from the temple.

North of Jiang Mountain, there are three such erupting springs, their currents flowing northwest. These three entries record strange phenomena, yet as no eyewitness account exists, their veracity remains uncertain. It is also unknown when these eruptions occurred. To the south of the mountain, there are documented sites of such outflows, now blocked with piled stones and called "Jiang Hou’s Watering Place." No such traces are found behind the mountain—perhaps these mark the appointed time of a future kalpa.

*True Reports*, Scroll Fourteen.
*True Reports*, Scroll Fifteen.
*Revealing the Subtle*, First Chapter.

Mount Luo {lies in the northern *gui* region. (This *gui* region need not refer to the six directions but likely indicates a bearing from the Central Kingdom, directly facing the north of Youzhou and Liaodong, in the midst of the Northern Sea—its distance from shore is unknown, measured in tens of thousands of *li*.)} The mountain rises two thousand six hundred *li* in height and spans thirty thousand *li* in circumference. Beneath it lies a grotto-heaven, fifteen thousand *li* in circumference. Above and below, there are palaces of spirits and gods. On the mountain stand six palaces, and within the grotto are six more, each spanning a thousand *li* in circumference. These are the palaces of the Six Heavens’ spirits and gods. (If each palace were fifteen thousand five hundred *li* in circumference, there would be three hundred twenty-five such sites. Here, only six palaces are mentioned, leaving three hundred nineteen unaccounted for. Such a vast expanse seems implausible; perhaps there are errors or omissions in the text.) The palaces above are the outer courts, while those within the grotto are the inner courts, their systems being equivalent. (Since this mountain lies beyond human reach, its summit may accommodate such structures—though how many grotto-gates exist remains unknown.)

The first palace is named the Palace of Zhou’s Absolute Yin Heaven. Proceeding eastward in sequence...

Within the grotto, a straight passage extends three thousand seven hundred fifty *li* from east to west. Now, if one palace spans two thousand *li* in circumference, its diameter would be two hundred fifty *li*. Were the six palaces arranged side by side, they would occupy but one thousand five hundred *li*—leaving over twenty *li* to spare on either side. The distance to the south and north is far greater, for all must serve as protective barriers; otherwise, their purpose would remain unfathomable.

The second palace is named the Palace of Tai Liang’s Ancestral Heaven of Affairs. The third palace is named the Palace of Ming Chen’s Enduring Martial City Heaven. The fourth palace is named the Palace of Tian Zhao’s Sinful Qi Heaven. The fifth palace is named the Palace of Zong Ling’s Sevenfold Transgressions Heaven. The sixth palace is named the Palace of Gan Si’s Linked and Repeated Heaven.

(These six heavenly palaces each must bear some significance, though their meanings can only be roughly discerned and ought not be lightly expounded.)

These six heavenly palaces constitute the governance of the spirits and gods of the Six Heavens. The six palaces within the grotto share the same names and are identical in form.

(This should correspond to the dwelling where the Ghost King adjudicates the sins of the dead—a place the scriptures refer to as the abode of King Yama. This king is now the Northern Great Emperor. Yet it remains unclear which deities preside over the Five Paths. All living beings, upon death, fall under their jurisdiction. As for the locations of hells, they are not confined to a single place; Mount Tai, rivers, and seas each have their own. The outer palaces of this mountain likely serve as administrative offices, overseeing records and documents, while the inner palaces within the grotto are where the dead reside and undergo judgment. Accounts from scribes tell of those who died and revived, all reporting that they first journeyed northward to the palaces and courts for examination.)

At times, it resembles passing through city gates where documents are inspected—likely all occurring within the outer palaces atop this mountain. As with Humu Ban’s journey to the Lord of Mount Tai’s abode, one may unknowingly enter the grotto. Perhaps ghosts and spirits are illusory, preventing mortals from perceiving the truth.

Those among the living who know the names of the Six Heavenly Palace Gates need only recite them thrice while facing north before sleep, in a low voice, and the hundred ghosts will not dare to harm them. (Earlier, they were called palace names; now, gate names. The gates derive their names from the palaces. The palaces are but empty titles, while the gates bear inscribed placards visible to all ghosts—and now to mortals as well, which is why they submit in awe.)

The incantation goes:
*"I am a disciple of the Most High, governing the Six Heavens below. The Six Heavenly Palaces are under my command—not only under my command, but under the authority of the Most High. I know the names of the Six Heavenly Palaces, and thus I attain longevity. Should any dare to transgress, the Most High shall sever your form."*

(Here, "governing the Six Heavens below" means they are not governed by the Six Heavens. Not only do I command them myself, but the Most High has ordered me to oversee them, thus reinforcing my authority—much like officials in commanderies and counties who receive imperial appointments, not mere blank commissions.)

The first palace is named the *Zhoujue Yintian Palace*. Proceeding eastward in order, the second palace is named— (These two characters in Yang Jun’s manuscript appear at the bottom of the page, but the copyist, unfamiliar with them, rendered them as seven characters. All contemporary versions mistakenly list this as the seventh palace. This is an error, for there are only six palaces—how could there be seven? The incantation’s phrase "proceeding eastward in order" is meant to astonish the ghosts by demonstrating knowledge of their sequential positions.)

Continuing in this manner through the sixth palace, one then taps the teeth six times before lying down, to ward off the qi of malevolent ghosts. (After reciting the incantation once and tapping the teeth six times, the process is repeated twice more before sleep.)

(This method has already been transcribed in detail in the third chapter on cultivation practices.)

When a person first dies, they all proceed to the *Zhoujue Yintian Palace* to receive their fate. Some may first go to famous mountains, Mount Tai, or rivers, but they do not necessarily go directly to the first heaven. Only on the day of their judgment—when their sins are examined and their fortune determined—must they come to this first heavenly palace. (This palace is governed by the Northern Emperor, and all matters must ultimately pass through it—like prefectural and county prisons, which, though initially under local jurisdiction, must eventually be sent to the capital for final sentencing.)

The *Tailiang Shizong Tian Palace* and its attendant ghosts constitute the second heaven. Those who die suddenly or violently also pass through here.

(This palace is specifically responsible for their collection. Those who die suddenly or violently may have unresolved records or share names with others, so they first come here for verification and inquiry.)

When virtuous persons and sages pass away, they first proceed to the *Mingchen Third Heaven Palace* to receive their fate. (Later texts mention the *Four Bright Lords*, each governing one palace—it is unclear which lord resides over these two palaces, *Tailiang* and *Mingchen*. Though those who die violently and the virtuous and sagely may temporarily pass through here first, they must still ultimately proceed to the *Zhoujue* for final judgment.)

Fortune and misfortune, blessings and calamities, the extension of life or the imposition of guilt—all are determined in the *Tianzhao Fourth Heaven Palace*, governed by the *Ghost Official* and the *Lord of Earthly Dipper*. (Here, the merits and transgressions of one’s former life are once more recorded. Yet Wu, who died for his utmost loyalty, must surely be ranked among the immortals.)

*Han Taihua* was the sister of *Han Anguo* and the wife of *Li Guangli*, the Han general of the Two Armies. *Guangli* possessed accumulated merit from past lives, and now resides in the *Southern Palace* undergoing transformation. (Though *Guangli*, as a renowned Han general, killed many in his campaign against *Dayuan*, his merits from former lives enabled him to transcend this.)

Han An Guo, styled An Guo, was of such blessed fortune that it should not have been tied to the Li family’s support. Yet as husband and wife share the same fate, there arose concern that others might suspect otherwise—hence the separate notation here. (This may also have arisen from karmic conditions.)

Liu Chunlong was the daughter of Liu Fengxian, Han’s *Zongzheng* (Minister of the Imperial Clan). (Fengxian served as *Zongzheng* during the reign of a certain Han emperor.)

Li Xizi was the grandmother of Li Zhong. (Zhong was *Taishou* of Dongping in the early Jin dynasty. His grandfather was a commoner, a farmer. Yet he practiced many hidden virtues: during heavy snow and bitter cold, he would not cover his stored grain, leaving it exposed in the courtyard, ever fearful that birds and sparrows might starve. Such was the depth of his care.)

(Though Li Zhong does not appear in the *Book of Jin*, this account suggests that his wife’s transcendence may have arisen from her husband’s merit, while his own reward remains unseen—a matter difficult to explain in detail.)

Wang Jinxian was the daughter of Wang Yan. (Details follow below.)

Guo Shuxiang was the mother of Wang Xiu. (Wang Xiu, styled Shuzhi, was a native of Beihai.)

Wang Xiu served as *Langzhong Ling* under Emperor Wu of Wei. At the age of seven, he lost his mother. She died on a *du* day, but her surname and family remain unknown.

In his youth, the prefecture office had three notable figures: Wang Shaodao, Fan Shusheng, and Li Boshan—all paragons of the prefecture in their time. Shaodao was fond of the Dao and was the same Wang Dun’er who had been *Taishou* of Shanyang during the Han. Fan Shusheng was a native of Beidi and later served as *Huangmen Lang* under Emperor Wen of Wei. Li Boshan was the father of Li Chong, who had been *Ling* of Baima during the Han and practiced hidden virtues. Some inherit the Dao across generations, their virtue accumulating through the ages—such was the case with these two prefectures’ transcendents, who advanced step by step to enter this rank.

Xiang Liangcheng composed the *Palace Chant*, which reads:
*"Zhou’s end severed the imperial dawn,
In truth, affairs entangled in layered deceit.
Flaming as smoke in the vault of heaven,
Bursting like radiant brilliance.
Wuyang girds the divine blade,
Serene and luminous, swallowing the Azure River.
The gates of Chang swing open to the cinnabar well,
Cloud gates rise in towering majesty.
Seven wrongs traverse the wondrous canopy,
Linked bends spread their enchantments.
Six heavens bar the northern path—
This is the domain of ghosts and spirits."*

The chant comprises twenty thousand words; here, only the names of the Six Heavenly Palaces are excerpted. It may be recited softly at night to repel malevolent spirits.

(Formerly, the third palace was named *Wucheng*; here it is called *Wu[BeiBei]*. Perhaps it bears two names. The *Biography of Su Shao* states that among the sagely was Xiang Liang the Righteous, and among the worthy, the virtuous Master Ji. Yet it remains unclear to which era Xiang belonged—some speculate he was Xiang Yu’s uncle, Xiang Liang, though he should not be ranked above Master Ji.)

The grain *zhongsi* has kernels like pomegranate seeds—exceptionally large and distinct, their color and flavor resembling water caltrop. It was also offered to the transcendents above.

(There is a later account of *zhongsi*; since it appears in a different record, repetition is not a concern. The "pomegranate seeds" refer to the common pomegranate.)

Yan Qingjia was the ancient Flame Emperor, now the Northern Supreme Emperor and lord of all the world’s ghosts and spirits.

(The Flame Emperor, Shennong, invented agriculture and tasted the hundred herbs—his sagely merit rivaled that of the Yellow Emperor. Yet he should not be the Ghost Emperor. Another *Flame Emperor* was the *Dating* clan defeated by the Yellow Emperor; this is likely the one intended, not Shennong. Moreover, external texts describe Shennong as ox-headed, and in Buddhist depictions, the lord of hell is also ox-headed—this further compounds the uncertainty. The *Four Illuminations Duke* was promoted.)

Since the term of office has its set duration, the ranks and titles of the Supreme Emperor should also be elevated to their utmost. (Though this has already been over three thousand years, perhaps it is like the earthly emperors who remain unchanged while their ministers and assistants are frequently replaced.)

King Wu of Zhou now serves as the Ghost Official and Lord of the Northern Dipper. (King Wen’s son, King Wu of Zhou, surnamed Ji, named Fa, overthrew King Zhou of Yin and became the Son of Heaven. He reigned for two years before passing. The *Rites* state he lived to ninety-three; the *Bamboo Annals* say forty-five. According to later records, the Four Illuminations Duke was promoted to immortal rank, yet no mention is made of the Lord of the Northern Dipper. Since he is subordinate to the Jade Dawn, he should likewise have been included in the same rank of promotion.)

Xia Qi serves as the Eastern Illuminations Duke, leading the Lord of the Dipper’s teachers. (Son of Yu, surnamed *Si*. The *Bamboo Annals* state he reigned for thirty-nine years before his death at seventy-eight. From his collapse until the *jimao* year, it has been exactly two thousand four hundred and twenty-five years. An *An* Controller of Fate states that after two thousand four hundred years in office, he was promoted to fill the Nine Palaces above. By this reckoning, the fourth year of Yuanhui of the Song has already passed.)

King Wen serves as the Western Illuminations Duke, leading the Northern Emperor’s teachers. (King Wen’s name was Chang. The *Rites* state...)

King Wen died at ninety-seven. Though both father and son were esteemed for their sagely virtue, they could not escape the ranks of the *guan*-ghosts. While this may be due to the fault of their execution, it must also be because they did not cultivate the Dao of immortality.)

Duke Shao serves as the Southern Illuminations Duke. (Duke Shao’s name was *Jia Baibai*. A bedchamber son of King Wen, he was enfeoffed at Shao and later established as lord of the state of Yan. According to records, the Duke of Zhou, Duke Shao, and the Grand Duke all assisted in the mandate to overthrow Zhou. Yet while the Duke of Zhou’s contributions were unparalleled, among the ranks of immortals and ghosts, none of his deeds stand prominently recorded. The Grand Duke, wielding the battle-axe and staff, enforced punishments with the greatest severity, yet he appears in the *Arrayed Transcendents*. Duke Shao’s virtue flowed like the sweet pear, yet he was instead appointed to a chief ghostly office—such matters remain difficult to fathom. All must stem from the karmic roots of their respective destinies. Among the Six Palaces, King Wen and his sons suddenly occupy three, demonstrating the profound eminence of Zhou’s virtue.)

Wu Jizha serves as the Northern Illuminations Duke. (Youngest son of King Shoumeng of Wu and uncle to Helü, he descended from Taibo and also bore the Ji surname. He yielded the throne and dwelt in Yanling, where the present Temple of Jizi stands. Though possessed of benevolent and worthy virtue, he was nonetheless secondary to the earlier sages, yet his case was exceptional. Examining these lords, their promotions have been irregular, for the Dao shifts with the times in its employment of persons. From the era of Xia Qi onward, over two thousand years passed before their ranks were altered—ten times the number of those who serve as lords below the earth. This clarifies the gradations of the immortal hierarchy, hence their exalted and wondrous status.)

The **Four Illuminations Duke** also had four distinguished guests. Yet these four lords would later all ascend to the immortal ranks.

The **Four Illuminations Master** commands the ghosts of the four quarters.

*(The four guests—
their deeds appear later.
Moreover, according to the later *Record of Fixed Announcements*,*
**Xiao [夹百百]** is the **Eastern Illuminations Duke**, advancing to the **Right Guardian of the Nine Palaces** in the **Upper Complement**. This is proof of the immortal hierarchy, differing from what came before. Since **Qi** has not yet departed, **Xiao** cannot yet attain immortality—likely an error in the text. If it is indeed the **Eastern Illuminations Duke**, then it should still refer to **Qi**. The matter of doubt is addressed later.)*

The **Western Illuminations Officer** has sixteen attendants, overseeing the blood and grain offerings for the ghosts of the state’s ancestral temples and halls.
*(These officers should also be subordinate to the **Western Illuminations Duke**. The blood and grain offerings are for those who have received their mandates and hold their posts—not for malevolent spirits clinging to the living.)*

**Zhou [岂页]** served as the **Ghost Official and Commander of the Mandate**. Now, **Dengyi [登邑] Yue** and **Cheng Xia** have succeeded him due to his many misdeeds.
*(**Zhou [岂页]**, styled **Boren**, was from Ancheng, Ruzhou. After serving Jin and crossing the river, he rose to the rank of **Master of Writing and Attendant**. In the first year of Emperor Yuan’s **Yongchang era**, when Wang Dun marched south, Zhou was seized at the **Stone Head Gate** and killed at age fifty-four. He was posthumously honored as **Light-bearing Marquis** and granted the title **Lord of the Light Office**.
**Dengyi Yue**, styled **Boshan**, was from Chen Commandery. He distinguished himself in the suppression of Guo Mo.)*

「In the early years of the **Xianning era**, he was appointed as **General Pacifying the South** and **Governor of Guangzhou**. He died of illness in Yuzhou.

Later, **Xin Yuanzi** said that **Dengyi Yue** served as **Chief Military Advisor** to **Xie Youyu**. This must be after his transfer.

**Cheng Xia** succeeded the **Governor of Chen Commandery**, acting as a strategist for **Shi Le**. His sister became Le’s wife. He rose to the rank of **Right Master of Writing and Attendant**, holding the title **Governor of Chen Commandery**. Le was killed by **Shi Hu**.

**West Capital’s** **Court Attendant** **Jia Yi** once failed to properly manage the **Fengshu** affair and was dismissed to guard **Mount Tai**. The **Lord of Mount Tai** recently appointed him as **Chief Military Advisor**, and this was accepted.

**The Gate Keeper of South Gate**. Now, **Zhou Fu** succeeds **Xi Yue** as **Gate Keeper**. One household has two **Gate Keepers**, and there are four **Gate Attendants**. In total, there are eight **Gate Attendants** under the **Heavenly Gate**.

The **Gate Attendants** are subordinate officials to the **Heavenly Gate Gate Keepers**. This must refer to the **Northern Emperor’s Gate**.

*(Later Han records state: "The keys to the Southern and Northern Gates belong to one palace, meaning there are two **Heavenly Gates** in total. The **Su Shao** biography mentions there are eight **Gate Attendants**, referring to the divination of **Yan Yuan** and **Bu Shang**. Now we see them holding office, so perhaps this is not the case.")*

**Zhou Fu**, courtesy name **Dao He**, was from Chai Sang in Xunyang. He was the son of Zhou Fang. Initially, he served as a general under Wang Dun. When Dun marched east to attack the capital, the campaign failed, and Zhou Fu fled with **Xi Yue** into the lands of the Western Yang barbarians. After Dun’s execution, he was again appointed as a general to suppress Su Jun. Later, he conquered Shu and pacified Li Shi, receiving the title **Marquis of Jiancheng**. He was then made **General Pacifying the West** and **Governor of Yizhou**. This was when he was about thirty years old. He died of illness in the third year of Xingning. He was posthumously honored as **General Pacifying the West**, and his posthumous title was **Xiang Gong**.

**Xi Yue**, courtesy name **Dao Wei**, was from Gaoping. He was the son of **Xin Yin**. In the first year of Yongchang, he led the various displaced peoples to cross the Yangtze into the eastern lands. Later, he helped suppress and defeat Wang Dun, receiving the title **Marquis of Gaoping**. He was then appointed **Grand General of Chariots and Cavalry** and **Governor of Dui Zhou**, garrisoning Guangling. He later garrisoned Xuzhou. After Su Jun was pacified, he was made **Sifang** and his title changed to **Marquis of Nanchang**. He still garrisoned Jingzhao City. He died of illness in the fifth year of Xiankang, at the age of seventy-one. He was posthumously honored as **Grand Master**, and his posthumous title was **Wencheng Gong**.

**The Northern Dipper** governs the **Heavenly Gate Gate Keeper**. Now, this refers to **Zang Hong**, who succeeded **Wei Qiao**. Another person is **Wang Bo**, newly appointed. *(This is also the South Gate. The remaining four are the Four Bright Gate Keepers, who should also correspond to the Four Bright Gates. None of them are prominent.)*

Zang Hong, courtesy name Zǐyuán, was a man of broad-minded virtue and moral integrity from Sheyang, Guangling. In the late Han dynasty, he raised an army to overthrow Dong Zhuo. Later, he served as the **Taishou (太守)** of Qingzhou and Dong Commandery. He betrayed Yuan Shao, who besieged and surrounded him; when his supplies ran out, he was captured and executed.

Wei Qiao, courtesy name Jìmèng, was a native of Tianshui. He possessed both talent and virtue, and people naturally gathered around him. In the late Eastern Han dynasty, he seized control of Longxi and declared himself king. In the first year of Jianwu (建武), Emperor Guangwu launched an expedition against him. Wei Qiao, driven by resentment and starvation, died of illness.

Wang Bo was a man from Bohai. Jin Shangshu Jin Shi had talent and ability. He defected to Shi Hu and became **Zhongshu Sheng (中書監)**. *(He was later killed.)*

Ji Shàn originally served as **Fuhé Jiāngjūn (抚河将军)** and **Sima (司马)**. Now, he holds the position of **North Heavenly Gate Gate Keeper (北天修门郎)** and succeeds Tian Lù. Ji Shàn and Yu Tan take turns guarding the **Heavenly Gate (天门)** for one day each. *(The North Heavenly Gate corresponds to the North Emperor’s Gate.)* Ji Shàn, courtesy name Sīyuǎn, was from Jùróng, Danyang. He first served in Wu as **Zhōngláng Jiāngjūn (中郎将)**. After Wu’s fall, he returned to Luoyang and was recommended as a **Xiùcái (秀才)**. He gradually rose in rank to become **Taishou (太守) of Kuàijì (会稽)**, then **Shìzhōng (侍中)**, Shangshu (尚書), Púyè (仆射), and **Piàojì Jiāngjūn (骠骑将军)**. He died of illness in the third year of Tàiníng, at the age of seventy-two. He was posthumously honored as **Kāifú (开府)**.

Tian Lù was posthumously honored with the title **Mù Hóu (穆侯)**. During the reign of **Arcu Wu (魏武帝)**, he served as **Cāngūn (参军)** under **Chéng Rìlì (程日立)**. Later, he became **Taishou (太守) of Héjiān (河间)**. He rebelled and was defeated by **Yán Ròu (阎柔)**.

Yu Tan, courtesy name **Sī'ǎo (思奥)**, was from **Yúyáo, Kuàijì (会稽余姚)**. He was the grandson of **Yu (虞) [Mào] [Fān]**. His rank rose to **Yì Jiāngjūn (卫将军)**, **Yòu Guānglù (右光禄)**, **Kāifú (开府)**, and **Wǔchāng Hóu (武昌侯)**. In the eighth year of **Xiányáng (咸阳)**, he died of illness at the age of seventy. He was posthumously honored as **Guānglù (光禄)** and given the title **Xiàoliè Hóu (孝列侯)**.

**Jīndāo (金刀)**, who led the title of **Lúshān Hóu (庐山侯)**, was courtesy named **Jūn Sī (君思)** and hailed from **Kuàijì (会稽)**. He served under **Jìn Chéng Mù Gōng (晋成穆公)** as **Zǔ Chángshì (左长史)** and **Dānyáng Yīn (丹阳尹)**. He later became **Zuǒ Mín Shàngshū (左民尚书)** and **Píngshòu Hóu (平寿侯)**. In the seventh year of **Yǒnghé (永和)**, he died of illness.

Gù Hé, after returning from garrison duty in **Liáodōng (辽东)**, was dismissed from service. The **North Emperor (北帝)** then appointed him as **Zhí Gài Làng (执盖郎)**. **Fàn Míng (范明)**, who had been **Zhí Gài Làng**, was promoted to **Diǎn Bǐng Hóu (典柄侯)**.

Gù Hé, courtesy name **Jūn Xiào (君孝)**, was from **Wú Jùn (吴郡)**. Orphaned young, he possessed resolve and integrity. He served the **Jìn (晋)** as **Shìbù Shìlàng (吏部侍郎)**, **Yùshǐ Zhōngchéng (御史中丞)**, **Shìbù Shàngshū (吏部尚书)**, **Lǐngjūn (领军)**, **Shàngshū Bùyè (尚书仆射)**, and **Shàngshū Lìng (尚书令)**.

In the seventh year of **Yǒnghé (永和)**, he died of illness, at the age of sixty-four. He was posthumously honored as **Zhìzhōng Shìtú (赠侍中司徒)** and granted the title **Mù Gōng (谥穆公)**. *[The text in brackets is unclear.]*

**Fàn Míng (范明)**, whose name was recorded in the *Han* as **Fàn Míng Yǒu (范明友)**, may not have been the same person. *[The text in brackets is unclear.]*

When the **North Emperor (北帝)** summoned **Xǔ Shìxiānsheng (许先生)** for a test, he was referred to as **Diǎn Bǐng Hóu Zhōu Páng (典柄侯周鲂)**. *[The text in brackets is unclear.]*
**Zhōu Páng (周鲂)**, courtesy name **Zǐyú (字子鱼)**, was from **Yángxián (阳羡)** in **Wú Jùn (吴郡)**. He was the father of **Zhōu Chù (周处)**. Serving under **Wú (吴)**, he held the post of **Tàishǒu (太守)** of **Fǎn Yì (番邑)**—later known as **Yáng (阳)**—and was renowned for his **wēihuì (威惠)**. **Yán Báihǔ (严白虎)**, also from **Wú Jùn (吴郡)**, had gathered crowds in the mountains during **Sūn Cè’s (孙策)** time. When **Cè (策)** pursued him, he fled and died in **Yúháng (余杭)**. His younger brother was named **Yú (舆)**, equally brave and strong. **Cè (策)** feigned a meeting with him, and he was killed by a spear thrust.

**Yīn Hào (殷浩)**, courtesy name **Yuānyuán (渊源)**, was from **Chángpíng (长平)** in **Chénliú (陈留)**. In the early years of **Kāng Dì’s (康帝)** reign, he was appointed **Shìshì (刺史)** of **Yángzhōu (扬州)**. In the sixth year of **Yǒnghé (永和)**, he was promoted to **Zhōngjūn Jiāngjūn (中军将军)** and **Dūdū Wǔzhōu (都督五州)**, leading a northern expedition against **Yáo Xiāng (姚襄)**. Though defeated, he was later removed by **Guàn Wēn (桓温)**, exiled to **Dōngyáng (东阳)**, and perished in the twelfth year of **Yǒnghé (永和)** from distress, at the age of sixty-four. Skilled in **tántǔn (谭论)**, he later engaged in debate with **Hé Yàn (何晏)**. **Yàn (晏)**, courtesy name **Píngshū (平叔)**, was the grandson of **Hé Jìn (何进)**. Proficient in **xiānlǐ (玄理)**, he rose to the rank of **Zhìzhōng Shàngshū (侍中尚书)** but was implicated in the **Cáo Shuǎng (曹爽)** faction and executed by **Sīmǎ Xuānwáng (司马宣王)**.

**Wēn Táizhēn (温太真)**, courtesy name **Shānjiāo (山乔)**, was appointed **Jiāng Hǎi Guó Bó (监海开国伯)** and governed **Dōnghǎi (东海)**. He later recruited **Dù Yù (杜预)** as his **Chángshì (长史)**, granting him a rank comparable to that of a **Dàjiāngjūn Chángshì (大将军长史)**. **Wēn (温)**, from **Qí (祁)** in **Tàiyuān (太原)**, served under **Jìn (晋)** as **Píngnán Jiāngjūn (平南将军)** and **Shìshì (刺史)** of **Jiāngzhōu (江州)**, suppressing **Sū Jǔn (苏峻)**. He attained the position of **Piāoqí Jiāngjūn Kāifú (骠骑将军开府)** and was ennobled as **Shǐwǔ Gōng (始五公)**. He died of illness in the fourth year of **Xiānhé (咸和)**, at the age of forty-two, and was posthumously honored as **Zhìzhōng Dàjiāngjūn (赠太将军)** and granted the title **Zhōngwǔ Gōng (中武公)**.

**Dù Yù (杜预)**, courtesy name **Fú Kǎi (符凯)**, was from **Dùlíng (杜陵)** in **Jīngzào (京兆)**. He was well-versed in knowledge and strategy, having annotated the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. Entering service under **Jìn (晋)**, he began as **Shàngshū Làng (尚书郎)** and eventually became **Dūdū Jīngzhōu (都督荆州)** and **Zhènyáng (镇襄阳)**. He contributed to the conquest of **Wú (吴)** and was ennobled as **Dāngyáng Hóu (当阳侯)**. In the fifth year of **Tàikāng (太康)**, he returned to **Luò (洛)** and died of illness in **Dēng Yì (登邑)** County, at the age of sixty-three. He was buried in **Luòyáng (洛阳)** and posthumously honored as **Zhìzhōng Zhēngnán Dàjiāngjūn (征南大将军)**. His title was **Chéng Hóu (谥成侯)**.

**Hé Cìdào (何次道)**, whose given name was **Chōng (充)**, was from **Jiāngqián (庐江潜)**. He held the rank of **Shàngshū Lìng Piāoqí Jiāngjūn (尚书令骠骑将军)** and was later appointed **Yángzhōu Shìshì (扬州刺史)** and **Lù Shàngshū (录尚书)**, assisting in the governance of the **Zhèngshì (正世业)**. Devoted to **Fó (佛)**, he frequently practiced **shīhuì (施惠)** and established **gōngdé (功德)**. He was always engaged in **shànshì (善事)**. *[The text in brackets is unclear.]*
*(Later, **Xīn Xiāozǐ (辛玄子)** also spoke of such matters.)

In the second year of **Yǒnghé (永和)**, on the **wùyín (戊寅)** day of the first month, he died of illness at the age of fifty-five. He was posthumously honored as **Sīgōng (司空)**, with the title **Wénmù Gōng (文穆公)**. According to this decree, he held a **guǐzhí zázhì (鬼职杂位)**—though not the **Sìmíng Gōng (四明公)**, he still received the transformation in the **Zhūfú Gōng (朱火宫)** and ascended to the rank of **xiān pǐn (仙品)**. This must be the result of profound **virtue/power (功德)**.

**Wèi Wǔdì (魏武帝)**, as **Běijūn Tàifǔ (北君太傅)**, served under the **Běijūn (北君)**—the **Běidǒu Jūn (北斗君)**, who is also **Zhōu Wǔwáng (周武王)**. Since each **Sìmíng (四明)** has its own **bīnyǒu (宾友)**, there was fear that the **Běidǒu Jūn** might not establish this office. Thus, it was decreed that **Wèi Wǔdì Cáo Cāo (魏武帝曹操)**, a native of **Péi Guó (沛国)**, from the **Yánjiāo (言焦)** clan, would be appointed to this role. A hero who **bá luàn (拨乱)** and **kuāngdìng (匡定)** the world, he was封封 as **Wèi Wáng (魏王)** and granted the **jiǔxí (九锡)**. He died of illness in the first month of **Jiàn’ān 25 (建安二十五年)**, at the age of sixty-six. That same year, in the tenth month, **Wèi Wén (魏文)** received the **shàn (禅)**, and he was posthumously honored as **Tàizǔ Wǔ Huángdì (太祖武皇帝)**.

The rest cannot be fully recounted. After all, the affairs of **guǐshén (鬼神)** are not meant to be revealed to the world.

This is what **Xuān Gōng (荀公)**—that is, **Xuàn Zhōnghóu (荀中侯)**—said. Having been enrolled under **Sīmìng (司命)** and overseeing all **guǐguān (鬼官)**, he knew these matters thoroughly. However, his account is disjointed, with events interspersed and seemingly answered as questions arise, rather than presented in a natural sequence. Since most people doubt the existence of the **yōumíng guǐshén (幽冥鬼神)**, he warned against revealing these matters, lest they invite **suspicion and slander**, thereby causing further loss.

The preceding section does not record the year or month. When **Wáng Yìshǎo (王逸少)** later examined the records of **Wáng Xī (王羲)**, it was determined that this likely occurred in the year **yǐchǒu (乙丑)**.

When a person lies in bed, the head must be raised high.
If raised high, the earthly *qi* will not reach upward,
and the ghosts will not blow upon them.
The *qi* of ghosts invading humans
always ascends from the earth below.

Ghosts—being yin in nature—
often rely on objects to disperse their *qi*.
They may attach to humans or beasts,
cling to vessels or utensils,
or lodge in food and drink,
only then unleashing their malevolence.
Once, a sick man lying on the ground
saw a ghost at the foot of his bed,
using its hand as a pipe to blow upon him—
this is the act of ghostly blowing.

When lying in a chamber,
one must keep it clean and well-ordered.
If clean and well-ordered, it receives pure *qi*;
if not, it receives impure *qi*.
Impure *qi* disrupting a chamber
will cause undertakings to fail
and actions to collapse.
Even one’s own body—
must be washed and bathed in purity.
Otherwise, there is no hope.

For ghosts and spirits are dust and turbidity incarnate,
and these things follow human wickedness.
Since they are restless and lack mastery,
how could the Dao be discussed?

Do not speak of the Dao while studying it.
If one speaks of the Dao while studying it,
ghosts will assail them,
and their undertakings will fail,
causing illness—
for their essence is not yet true.

(*The True Instructments* often warn against this:
Every word and deed, if revealed, diminishes one’s *ling* [灵].
How much more so for sickness?
What is called "transcendent" is achieved through sincere practice—
why seek to speak of it?
Demons lurk, ever watchful,
like echoes and shadows.)

Shān Shìyěn, after receiving the teachings of Master Mèng,
would lie down in the evening,
first reciting the *Huángtíng Nèijǐng Jīng* once before sleeping,
so that his soul and spirit might refine themselves.
If one practices this Dao for twenty-one years, they will become transcendent—
this is the fulfillment of ten thousand offenses.
Even three or four recitations are beneficial.
Lady Jiāng of Běiyuè said:
Reading this scripture also keeps one free of illness—
this is the Dao of immortality.

(These four principles are all worthy of adoption.
Their details are separately transcribed in the Third Section.)

Master Mèng—this refers to the gentleman Mèng from Jīngzhào.
When it comes to practice, it is best to follow Zhèng Chéng’s method.
In the previous section, there was mention of Lady Jiāng of Xīyue.
Now, the *Huǒtiān Xià* [Scripture] speaks again of Běiyue.
I am uncertain whether these are two separate individuals,
or if this is an error in transcription.

At night, one should habitually grind their teeth.
There is no fixed limit to how often this should be done.
Ghostly evils and malevolent spirits always fear the sound of grinding teeth—
thus, they cannot harm a person.
If one combines this with rinsing the mouth and reciting incantations,
the result is even better.
(For those who strike their teeth [in ritual], their spirit is preserved—
thus, ghostly evils cannot interfere.
Now, those who practice the higher Dao
constantly strike their teeth day and night—
this is why they can achieve longevity.)

In the past, there was a man named Báozhù from Jìběi.
(His name is obscure; it does not appear in external records.)
He never studied the Dao.
Nor did he know magical techniques.
At the age of over forty,
he suddenly contracted *miànfēng* [a wind-related facial disorder].
His mouth and eyes became misaligned.
When he opened his mouth, his upper and lower teeth would repeatedly strike against each other,
producing a loud sound.
This condition persisted day and night without cessation.

He lived to the age of one hundred and twenty-seven.
Later, he encountered an extreme cold snap and fell into the River of Longevity, drowning.
The Northern Sovereign’s officials also dispatched ghosts and calamity-demons of the sun to seize him,
yet these demons never dared approach him.
The Ghostly Bureau inquired as to why.
The Celestial replied:
*"This man was skilled in many magical arts,
by which he constrained us.
He habitually struck his teeth,
mimicking the thunderous beat of heavenly drums,
to awaken the spirits within his body.
The spirits dared not scatter,
and the ghostly *qi* could not penetrate.
Thus, he had no fateful connection to be taken."*

By this reasoning, had Báozhù not crossed the icy river,
he might well have lived beyond a thousand years.
Instead, during the severe cold, he walked upon the ice,
his mouth closed and unable to strike his teeth again—
thus, the ghosts seized him while he drowned in the river.

Even those afflicted with *miànfēng* whose teeth chatter uncontrollably
still repel death and ward off ghosts—
how much more so for a Daoist adept who deliberately strikes his teeth,
sounds the heavenly drums,
and fully invigorates his bodily spirits!

(The Daoist alchemical text states:
*"Constantly swallowing saliva and striking the teeth
shortens one’s lifespan."*
Yet by this very account...)

Human life has no fixed limit—
all that exists is premature death.
If one cultivates the Dao with diligence,
like Báozhù striking his teeth,
why should one not attain eternal years?
The true peril lies in occasional slackness,
when one is then seized by ghosts—
perishing like one drowned in the river.

As for all ghosts, they lack true wisdom;
they rely on wind-illness as their craft—
how could they overcome such folly?

---
In the **Doushan** [都山] realm,
trees, waters, and marshes resemble those of the mortal world.
Yet its rice grains are no larger than millet,
tasting like water chestnuts.
The other four grains are not so.
Only rice is called *Chóngsī* [重思]—
a name of great significance.

Du Qióng composed the *Ode to Chóngsī* [重思赋]:
*"Spring’s soft mist unfurls—
lush and verdant, the sacred grain.
The divine canopy spreads,
timely blessings for the people’s feast.
The four seasons’ harvests rejoice,
offerings made to the gods of grain.
The holy ears rise in abundance,
their vast stalks bending my dark hall.
Here, auspicious signs appear—
the Emperor’s radiance shines forth.
This is what is spoken of."*

(Here, it further speaks of **Doushan’s** affairs,
yet again returning to *Chóngsī*.
It says that those who worship the *Zhuren* [祝人]
must not partake in sacrificial feasting—
for this is the very act of offering to the Daoist officials, as previously stated.
Moreover, when a ghost’s lifespan is fulfilled,
it receives the remaining years of its host,
and upon death, sacrificial rites are held in its honor.)

Du Qióng, styled **Bóyú** [伯瑜],
a scholar from Shu [蜀],
was well-versed in learning and possessed literary talent.
He annotated the *Book of Songs* and mastered divination.
He could recall the events of the Wei dynasty as if they were contemporary,
and served under Liu Bei’s regime as **Honglu [鸿胪]** and **Taichang [太常]**.
He died in the **Yanxi [延熙] era’s** thirteenth year,
having lived past eighty.

Eight attendants served the Emperor at dawn:
Xu Shu, **Guanglong [广龙]**, Yuán Yú, Li Guang,
Wang Jia, Hé Yàn, Jié Jié, and Yīn Hào—
all like the **Shizhong [侍中]** of the mortal world.

(Li Guang, the **Xiáqí General [骁骑将军]** under Emperor Wu,
was accused by officials during his campaign against the Xiongnu.)

Xīn Guì [心貴] sighed and slit his own throat to die.
Wang Jia, a man of Shu Commandery,
served as a **Lángzhōng [郎中]** under Emperor Píng.
By the time of Wang Máng [王莽],
he abandoned his post and returned to his homeland,
refusing to submit to Sun Shù [孙述].
He died, sword in hand.

Xu Shu, styled **Fúzhí [符直]**
of Yǐngchuān [颖川],
recommended Zhuge Liang to Liu Bei.
Later, when Wei Wu [魏武] captured his mother,
he surrendered to the Wei.
He rose to the rank of **Zhōngchéng [中丞]**.
In the **Dàhé [大和]** era of Emperor Ming,
he died of illness.

Guanglong [廣龍] Dé, styled **Lìngmíng [令明]**
of Nán’ān [南安],
followed Zhang Zhen [張鎮] in surrendering to Wei Wu.
He was appointed **Lìyì Jiāngjūn [立義将军]**
and stationed at Fánchéng [樊城],
but was slain by Guan Yu.
He was posthumously honored as **Dú Hóu [杜侯]**
and buried in **Yèyì [業邑]**
with his head and body intact, as if alive.

Yuán Yú, styled **Shìdū [世都]**
of Píyáng [濮陽],
was eloquent and skilled in arts and techniques.
He served under Jin Wu [晉武],
was summoned to the **Sītú [司徒]** Wei Shu’s [魏舒] household,
and reached the position of **Shìzhōng [侍中] Zhōngshū Shìng [中書令]**.
Jié Jié, styled **Zìlián [稚連]**
of Jǐnán [濟南],
was his younger brother.
He once served under Jin as **Huángmén Shìláng [黃門侍郎] Zhōngchéng [中丞] Jīngyù Zhōuzhìshǐ [荊豫州刺史] Shàngshū [尚書]**.
During the reign of Zhao Wang Lún [趙王倫],
he was killed by Sun Xiù [孙秀].

Hé and Yīn [何、殷], the two mentioned earlier,
were of the eight who served before—
only Dào [道] and Dé [德] remain.
Now I must ask further:
Who are these four who serve at dawn?

Among them are **Sìmíng Gōng [四明公]** and **Běidǒu Jūn [北斗君]**.
They are like the five who serve at dawn—
the eight from before were merely officials under the Northern Great Emperor.
Their selection and use were also the same.

(The title *sìdì chén* [侍帝晨] also applies to the *xiān guān* [仙官].
Both hold the rank of *shìzhōng* [侍中].
This passage states their selection and use were identical—
whether it means only their titles and positions,
or evaluating their talent and insight while also considering their merits and virtue,
is unclear.
These individuals’ talents and stations
were forever unlike one another.
Perhaps in the **Yōutú** [幽途] they are distinguished differently.)

There are also four **Zhōngláng Zhíshì [中郎直事]**, akin to the **Shàngshū [尚書]** of the mortal world:
Dài Yuān, Gōngsūn Dù [公孙度],
Liú Fēng, and Guō Jiā.
Now they are seen in office—
Liú Fēng was the adopted son of Xuán Dé [玄德].
(This office should be the **Tàidì Lǐngliáo [太帝領僚]**, now equivalent to the *sàncáo shàngshū* [散曹尚書].
Dài Yuān, styled **Ruòyú [若愚]**
of Guǎnglíng [廣陵],
served under Jin, advancing through the ranks to **Hùjūn Shàngshū [护军尚书]**, **Pūshè [仆射]**, and **Piáojì Jiāngjūn [骠骑将军]**.)

Guō Jiā.

With Zhou [Yè] together perished at the hands of Wang Dun.
He was posthumously honored as *Guānglù* [光禄], and his posthumous title was *Jiǎn Hóu* [简侯].

Public Sun Du, styled **Shūjì** [叔济], was of Liaodong.
He was the ancestor of Yuān.
Initially, he served as Governor of Liaodong.
During the *Jiàn’ān* era, he usurped the throne and proclaimed himself king,
establishing imperial insignia and passing the *zhuànguó* [传国] heir to his son, Kāng.
His grandson, Yuān, was captured by Sīmǎ Xuānwáng [司马宣王].

**Liú Fēng**, originally of the Kòu clan of Luóhóu [罗侯],
was the adopted son of Liu Bei when the latter had no heir.
By nature fierce and bold, he possessed strength, martial skill, and qi.
Later, he was granted a command and executed.
This was a case of an outsider inheriting the surname—
yet he still managed to assume the lineage.

**Guō Jiā**, styled **Fèngxiào** [奉孝],
was from Yǐngchuān Yǔ [颖川阳翟].
A strategist for Wéiwǔ, he served as *jūn móu jìjiào* [军谋祭酒].
He died of illness at thirty-eight.
His posthumous title was *Zhēn Hóu* [真侯].

**Xuán Dé** now holds the title *Běihé Hóu* [北河侯],
ruling alongside Hàn Suì.
He is now under the *Xiān Guān* [仙官].
(The *Xiān Guān* also includes the *Běihé Sīmìng Jìn Bǎo Hóu* [北河司命禁保侯],
who oversees matters of the Three Officials [Sān Guān],
belonging to the *Dōnghuá Guān* [东华官], led by the *Bǎomìng Jūn* [保命君].
This must mean the *Běihé Hóu* is subordinate to them.)

**Liú Bèi**, styled **Xuán Dé**, was of his native commandery.
He first raised an army in rebellion,
later seizing Shu and assuming imperial titles.
After three years, he died of illness at sixty-three.
His posthumous title was *Zhāoliè Huángdì* [昭烈皇帝].
At the time, he was one of the Three Sovereigns alongside Wéiwǔ Sūn Céng.
Though his rank was low, being under the *Xiān Guān*,
his merit may yet compare favorably.

**Hàn Suì**, styled **Wén Jiǎo** [文纣],
was from [omitted].
At the end of the Han, he led armies and sowed chaos in the western lands.
In the twenty-fifth year of *Jiàn’ān*, Wéiwǔ launched a campaign against him.
Suì fled to Jīnchéng, where he was killed by his own generals, including **Móu Yǎn** [麴演].
Though bold and fierce, he could not escape calamity—
yet he was granted the honor of serving alongside Liu Bei in the *Xiān Guān*,
further elevated in rank.

There are also two *Dà Jìn Chén* [大禁晨], equivalent to the *Shàngshū Lìng* [尚书令].
These were held by Hàn Guāngwǔ and Sūn Wéntái.
(Hàn Guāngwǔ, Liu Xiù, styled **Wénshū** [文叔],
was the eighth-generation descendant of the High Ancestor.
He raised armies to overthrow Wang Mang and the Chìmǐ,
pacifying the realm.)

The emperor reigned for thirty-three years before dying of illness at the age of sixty-three.

Sūn Jiān, styled **Wén Tái** [文台], was a native of Wú Commandery. He was the father of Sūn Quán. Clǎn Shù recommended him as the *Pò Lǔ Jiāngjūn Yùzhōu Shìshǐ* [破虏将军豫州刺史], leading the campaign against Dòng Zhuō. Later, he attacked Liú Biǎo. In the second year of *Chūpíng*, he was killed by an archer under the command of Liú Biǎo’s general, Huáng Zǔ, at the age of thirty-seven. Though loyal and valiant, his station was humble, yet he now shares rank with the Son of Heaven—much like Hàn Suì’s parallel with Guāndì [玄德].

There were also two *Zhōng Jìn Chén* [中禁晨], equivalent to today’s *Zhōngshū Lìng* [中书令] and overseer. These were held by **Yán Huái** and **Yáng Biāo**. Huái’s style was **Sī Jì** [思季], and Biāo’s was **Wén Xiān** [文先].

(Yán Huái, styled **Sī Jì**, was a man whose worth was yet to be fully realized. Yáng Biāo, styled **Wén Xiān**, hailed from Hóngnóng. He was the father of Yáng Xíu, the Han *Sīkōng* [司空]. During the chaos of Dòng Zhuō’s rebellion, he supported and protected the Emperor Xiàndì. In times of peril, east and west, he endured three terms of office until his death in the sixth year of *Wéiwén Huángchū* [魏文黄初], at the age of eighty-four. The *Xù Zhǎngshì* [许长史]’s son now holds the title of *Dànfāng Hóu* [弹方侯]. There were two *Dànfāng Hóu*—one overseeing the south, the other the north.
