The *Xù Zhǎngshì* served as the *Nán Dànfāng Hóu*, with Liú Zàn as his *Sīmǎ* [司马]. Bào Yuànlì [鲍员力] was the *Běi Dànfāng Hóu*, with Wéi Zhūn as his *Sīmǎ*. Each commanded the keys to the southern and northern gates, with Xù leading a thousand soldiers of the southern command and Bào commanding a thousand of the northern. Their primary duty was to guard the gates and apprehend offenders. This resembles today’s *Yǔlín Jiān* [羽林监], with southern and northern forces akin to the Daoist *Tiāndīng Lìshì* [天丁力士] and armored troops.)

(The two *Zhōng Jìn Chén* and the southern and northern *Dànfāng Hóu* were likely also officials under the *Běidì* [北帝]. At the time of this record, it may have been customary to invoke the name *Xù* [许], though it is possible Yáng [杨] himself was not dismissive of him. The *Běidì* incantation’s reference to "threatening the south and north" pertains to these troops, who were surely valiant warriors.)

The *Xù* lineage’s deeds are recorded in another chapter.

**Liǔ Zàn**, courtesy name *Zhèngmíng*, hailed from Chángshān in Kuàijī. As a youth, he served as a county clerk, devoured military texts, and harbored grand ambitions. When he struck down Yellow Turban rebels, he injured his foot—so severely that one leg became permanently bent. He then severed his own tendon to restore its function. Later, he became Left Protector-General. While campaigning with Sūn Jǔn against Huáinán, he fell ill before reaching the front and was slain by Wèi’s general Jiǎng Bān. He was seventy-three years old.

**Bào** *Chūyè* (courtesy name *Shùyè*), descendant of the ninth generation of Bào Xuān, was the son of Bào Xìn. Renowned for his integrity and noble conduct, he served as Middle Regulator and Yellow Gate Attendant during the *Jiàn’ān* era of the Han. Under Wén Dì of Wèi, he held the post of Grand Historian. Though he repeatedly remonstrated against imperial decrees, he was demoted to Chief of Documents and Law. Later, he was executed.

**Wèi Zūn**, courtesy name *Gōngyì*, was from Wù. He was the grandson of Wèi Zhāo, a man of vast learning and literary talent, skilled in calligraphy. During the reign of Jìn’s *Chéng Mù*, he served as Left Minister of Civil Affairs, Yellow Gate Attendant, and Secretary of State. He succeeded Wáng Yìshào as Governor of Línchuān Prefecture, but died in mourning for his mother at the age of sixty-four.

「孙策 was the guest and friend of the Eastern Command Prince. (The eldest son of Sun Jian, courtesy name Bó Fú, he inherited his father’s forces at the end of the Han and first pacified the Jiangdong region. Then he sought to stabilize the central lands of China, was appointed to lead an expedition against the rebel general, and was ennobled as the Marquis of Wú. While crossing the Yangtze River with a light cavalcade, he was struck by an arrow from an enemy. His wound festered and he died at the age of twenty-six. His younger brother Quán succeeded him in his duties. Later, he was posthumously honored as the Duke of Chángshā. Initially, when Sun Jian set out from the east, he saw a lucky omen of seven thousand soldiers. Later, when he looked in a mirror, he saw this same omen again, which enraged him. Thus, his wound broke out and he died. His martial prowess surpassed that of Xiàng Yǔ’s descendants, yet none could claim the title of king—only Sun Jian remained unparalleled in obscurity. Hence, through the ages, it was said that he was the King of the Ten Mountains of Wúxīng, forever dwelling in the prefectural hall. So the prefects dared not ascend, and those who did invariably died. A separate temple was also erected in his honor, where he was invoked as the **Conqueror King** [霸王].)

**True Edicts** [《真诰》] Volume Fifteen. **True Edicts** Volume Sixteen. **Elucidating the Profound and Mysterious** [《阐幽微》] Second Section. The **High Ancestor of the Han** [汉高祖] was the guest and friend of the Southern Command Prince. (Líu Bāng, courtesy name Jì, a native of Fēng in the commandery of Péi.)」

The rise from humble origins,
Conquering Qin and pacifying Xiàng.
Founding the Han’s foundation.
Reigned twelve years before dying of illness at sixty-two.

The Jin Emperor Xuān for the Western Bright Prince’s guest and friend.
(Sīmǎ Wěi, courtesy name Zhòngdá, of Héinèng. In the Wei, he served as Grand General and Grand Tutor. In Jiāpíng’s third year, he died of illness at seventy-two. Posthumously honored as Prince of Xiàngguó, with the temple name Xuānwén Hóu. Emperor Wǔ of Jin received the Mandate of Heaven and posthumously conferred the title of High Ancestor Xuānhuángdì.)

Xún [or variant] as the Northern Bright Prince’s guest and friend.
(Xún [or variant], courtesy name Wénruò, of Yǐngchuān. In the late Han of Wǔ, he served as Grand Secretary. Possessing noble bearing and discernment, he initially became a strategist for Wèi Wǔ, intending to stabilize the Han dynasty. Suspected and fearful, he took poison and died at fifty. Posthumously honored as Jìng Hóu, with the posthumous title of Tàiyǔ. Among the guests and friends, his standing was akin to that of the Yánlíng match. Though his Four Bright rank was not inferior, his virtue and prestige were indeed superior.)

Among them, those whose ancestral fate had accumulated hidden virtue and mercy—
At their time, they were directly appointed to celestial offices,
Or entered the Southern Palace to receive transformation,
Without regard to their earthly rank.

The extent of one’s sins and merits in life
Is weighed and judged accordingly.
Generally, those who practiced hidden virtue,
Who showed great compassion for the destitute and afflicted,
Were swiftly summoned to the Southern Palace to become immortals.

(Those who in life performed hidden deeds of virtue and profound merit,
Who cherished the Dao and believed in immortality—
Their rewards varied in depth and weight,
Thus their retributions were not all the same.
Some attained earthly immortality and did not die.
Some departed through corpse dissolution, leaving their forms behind.
Some, after death, entered the Cavern Palaces to study.
Some first went to the Vermilion Fire Palace to refine their forms.
Some first became lords of the underworld before ascending in rank.
Some first passed through the Ghostly Bureau before being transformed.
Some could not depart in life, but their merit extended to their descendants.)

Those who in life were granted the Dao and thus plucked away to transcendence—
All such cases, with ranks spanning high and low, tens of levels apart,
Cannot be uniformly sought or reckoned.

**Guang Yu** (元规), surnamed **Yuan** (元), served as the General of the Northern Marshal’s Guard.
He appointed **Guo Changxiang** (长翔) as his Chief Clerk,
And **Hua** (欠, *lit.* "missing sound"*) as his Military Advisor.
These were the so-called "Marshal Officials."
They commanded thousands of ghostly soldiers.

(*This differs greatly from what Xin Xuanzi said—
Perhaps due to differing accounts or the possibility of later alterations.
**Guang Yu**, styled **Yuan** (元规), was from Jingchuan.
During the Xianhe era, he was appointed as the General of the Western Campaign,
Governor of Jiang, Jing, and Yue, and stationed in Wuchang.
In the sixth year of Xiankang, he died of illness at his post,
At the age of fifty-two.
He was posthumously honored as a Grand Marshal,
And his posthumous title was *Wen Kang Gong* (文康公).
Before his illness, he alone saw Tao Qian riding in his carriage to summon him.
Thus, he fell ill and departed.

**Guo Fan** (翻), styled **Changxiang** (长翔), was from Wuchang.
From his youth, he held lofty aspirations.
**Guang Yu** wished to recruit him as a senior aide,
But he refused to comply.
After his death, he spoke to his son, Ling (灵):
*"Guang Yu serves as the Grand Marshal of the Eastern Pacification.
His domain lies east of the Donghai Sea,
And he commands a hundred thousand troops.
He has appointed me as his Military Advisor.
Originally, he intended to recruit Xie Renzu for this role,
But deemed his standing insufficient.
Jiang Dashou then took him as a Cavalry Commander,
So I was forced to press Rong Changyu into the position of Chief Clerk,
And entrusted him with military affairs.
He was highly esteemed.
Now I say..."*

King Wang served as the **Shàngshū Lìng** (Grand Secretary).
He wielded great authority, deciding all major affairs of state.
According to this account, it aligns with what **Xuánzǐ** (玄子) said.
If this is the case, then **Guo Fan** should have been appointed **Changshi** (長史) later.
However, since **Xie Renzu** was still alive as **Púshè** (Master of Writing) and **Zhēnxī Jiāngjūn** (General Pacifying the West),
his claim that his standing was insufficient becomes particularly difficult to reconcile.

The **Chénxiàng** (王丞相), **Wang Dao** (王导),
was the father of **Rong Changyu** (王长豫).
Changyu died young.
**Huà** (華), styled **Zǐyú** (子鱼),
a native of Píngyuán (平原),
served as **Táishǒu** (Governor) of **Yùzhāng** (豫章).
He was a contemporary of **Sūn Cé** (孙策).
After Cé’s death, he joined **Cáo Kāng** (魏武帝, Cao Cao).
He later held high office as **Sītú** (Grand Tutor) and **Tàiyuè** (Grand Marshal),
and was ennobled as **Bóping Hóu** (博平侯).
He passed away in the fifth year of **Tàihé** (太和),
at the age of seventy-three.
He was posthumously honored as **Jìng Hóu** (敬侯).

**Kǒng Wénjǔ** (孔文举) was appointed **Hòuzhōngwèi Dàjiāngjūn** (General-in-Chief of the Rear Guard).
He took **Zhāng Qiào** (张绣) as his **Sīmǎ** (Military Advisor)
and **Táng Gù** (唐固) as his **Changshi** (長史).

**Kǒng Róng** (孔融), styled **Wénjǔ** (文举),
a descendant of **Kǒngzi** (Confucius) in the twenty-first generation,
was a renowned scholar of the late Han.
He served as **Táishǒu** of **Běihǎi** (北海),
but was later executed by **Cáo Gōng** (曹公, Cao Cao).

**Zhāng Qiào** (张绣), a native of **Wǔwēi** (武威),
was a descendant of **Jì** (济) through his maternal line.
During the late Han chaos, he raised an army.
Later, he surrendered to **Cáo Kāng** (魏武帝, Cao Cao),
holding the title of **Pòqiāng Jiāngjūn** (General Pacifying the Qiang).
He followed Cao Cao in the campaign against the **Wūmán** (乌丸).
Before reaching **Liǔchéng** (柳城), he died.
He was posthumously honored as **Dìng Hóu** (定侯).

**Táng Gù** (唐固), styled **Zǐzhèng** (子正),
a native of **Jùróng** (句容) in **Dānyáng** (丹阳),
was known for his disciplined conduct and profound scholarship in Confucian studies.

「Commented on the *Guoyu* with the *Gongyang* and *Liuliang* commentaries.
During the fourth year of Emperor Wu of Han, **Sun Quan** was appointed **Shangshu Púyè** (Grand Secretary and Minister of Works).
At over seventy years of age, he died of illness.

**Táo Kàn** (陶侃), styled **Shìhéng** (士衡),
originally from **Dānyáng** (丹阳), later relocated to **Fānyì** (番邑) in **Yáng** (阳),
and then settled in **Lújiāng** (庐江), belonging to **Zhēnyáng Chái Sāng** (浔阳柴桑).
Throughout the Jin dynasty, he participated in numerous military campaigns,
earning great merit. He rose to the rank of **Shìzhōng Tàiyè** (Chief Counselor and Grand Tutor),
**Dūdū Shíbā Zhōu Jīngjiāng Èr Zhōu Shìshǐ** (Superintendent of Eight Commands, Upriver and Downriver Regions),
and **Chángshā Gōng** (Duke of Changsha).
In the fourth year of the *Xiánhé* era, he returned to Changsha
and died at **Fánxī** (樊溪) at the age of seventy-six.
He was posthumously honored as **Dà Sīmǎ** (Grand Commander-in-Chief),
with the posthumous title **Huán Gōng** (Marquis Huán).

**Guǎngyú Liàng** (广臾亮) succeeded him.
Yet, **Guō Chángxiáng** (郭长翔) in his *Língyǔ* (灵语) stated:
*"Táo Gōng* (Lord Tao) was indeed exiled for his crimes and had not yet been reinstated."
Another record states:
*"After Táo Gōng’s death, a short time later, his spirit sent a messenger to deliver a message,
passed down to his son, who said:
'The Duke of Changsha and I once conversed in heaven about matters of fate.
That is why you now know.
At that time, Guǎngyú was still alive.
He died three or four years later.'*

**Téng Hán** (滕含), son of **Téng Bìng** (滕并), was from **Nányáng Xī È** (南阳西鄂).
During the *Yǒnghé* era, he served as **Píngnán Jiāngjūn** (General Pacifying the South) and **Guǎngzhōu Shìshǐ** (Governor of Guangzhou).
He died of illness in the province and was posthumously honored as **Dài Hóu** (Marquis Dai).

Táo Kàn, due to his own merits and status, was further elevated.
**Xú Níng** (徐宁), styled **Ānqī** (安期),
was from **Dōnghǎi** (东海), a descendant of **Xiàn Zhī** (羡之).
Initially recommended by **Huán Yí** (桓彝),
he served alongside **Guǎngyú Liàng** (广臾亮) as **Hùfū Gōngcáo** (Functionary of Military Affairs).
They were renowned as *"the clear scholars of the Eastern Seas and Tai Mountains."*
Later, he rose to the rank of **Zhèngyuán Lìbù Láng** (Regular Official of the Civil Office Department),
**Guànjūn Jiāngzhōu Shùnyáng Jiǎnhòu** (General of the Vanguard, Marquis of Shunyang in Jiangzhou).
In his youth, **Xiàn Zhī** (羡之) once encountered his spirit in person.»

「He claimed, "I am your ancestor." He warned him of good fortune and misfortune.
Later, events unfolded exactly as he had spoken.

Cài Mó, styled **Dàomíng** (道明),
was from **Kǎochéng** (考城) in **Chénliú** (陈留).
He was the son of **Kè** (克).
He rose to the rank of **Governor of Yángzhōu** (杨州).
He was also appointed **Sìtú** (司徒), but declined.
In the twelfth year of **Yǒnghé** (永和), he died of illness at age seventy-six.
He was posthumously honored as **Sìkōng** (司空) and **Wénmù Gōng** (文穆公).

The reason this [unidentified person] was not captured by the rebellious general
was also due to ghosts—
ghosts cannot control one another.
It was because he was human.
No one is naturally subject to supernatural domination.
Even the **xiānzhēn** (仙真, transcendent beings) still wield the reins and bells
to discipline these [mortals].
How much more so for those of lesser rank?

The **Sì Zhèn** (四镇, Four Vanguards) each command a ghostly army of ten thousand.
The **Sì Guān** (四官, Four Officials) lead troops numbering no more than a few thousand.
Among the **Sì Zhèn**, there are:
**Tàishān Jūn** (泰山君, Lord of Mount Tai),
**Lúlóng Gōng** (卢龙公, Marquis of Lulong),
and the **Dōngyuè Dà Jiāngjūn** (东越大将军, Grand General of Dongyue),
the **Nánbā Hóu** (南巴侯, Marquis of Nanba).
Each of the **Sì Guān** leads ten thousand men.

(The **Sì Zhèn** are not the Four Quarters [of the cosmos],
but rather the four cardinal directions within China.
Their return journeys span no more than a few thousand *lǐ* (里).
Elsewhere, there should be even greater numbers—
that is why, later, I spoke of hundreds of such places.)」

The **Nánbā Hóu** (南巴侯, Marquis of Nanba) was **Hé Céng** (何曾), whose style name was *Yǐngkǎo* (颖考). A native of **Yángxià** (阳夏) in **Chénjùn** (陈郡), he was the son of **Hé Zǐ** (何子). By nature, he was extravagant yet well-versed in learning, and practiced filial piety and fraternal respect. Initially serving under the **Wèi** (魏), he later rose to the positions of **Shàngshū** (尚书), **Zhēngběi Jiāngjūn** (征北将军), and **Sì Tú** (司徒). He was ennobled as **Lánglíng Hóu** (朗陵侯). In the **Jìn** (晋) dynasty, he held the titles of **Tàishǐ** (太尉), **Tàibǎo** (太保), and **Tàizǎi** (太宰) as **Lánglíng Gōng** (朗陵公). He died in the **Tàishǐ Sìnián** (太始四年) era, at over eighty years of age, and was posthumously honored with the title **Yuángōng** (元公).

**Cáo Rén** (曹仁), whose style name was *Zǐxiào* (子孝), was **Wèi Wǔ** (魏武) Emperor **Cáo Cāo**’s (曹操) younger brother. Renowned for his valor and archery, he often accompanied campaigns with distinction. His rank eventually reached **Chēqí Jiāngjūn** (车骑将军) and **Dūdū Yángyǐng Yìzhōu Zhūjūn Shì Dà Jiāngjūn** (都督荆扬益州诸军事大将军). He was ennobled as **Chén Hóu** (陈侯). He died of illness in the **Huángchū Sìnián** (黄初四年) era, at fifty-six years of age, and was posthumously honored as **Zhōnghóu** (忠侯).

As for **Liú Táo** (刘陶) serving as **Dōngyuè Dà Jiāngjūn** (东越大将军), there were three such figures across the **Hàn** (汉), **Wèi** (魏), and **Jìn** (晋) dynasties. The **Hòuhàn** (后汉) one bore the style name *Zǐqí* (子奇) and was from **Yǐngchuān** (颖川). He served as **Língdì** (灵帝) Emperor’s **Shìzhōng** (侍中) and **Shàngshū Lìng** (尚书令). Later, he was imprisoned and died in confinement. The **Wèi** (魏) one bore the style name *Jìyǎo* (季冶) and was from **Huáinán** (淮南), the son of **Liú Yè** (刘晔). Though skilled in debate, he lacked moral conduct. **Cáo Shǎo** (曹爽) appointed him as **Xuǎnbù Láng** (选部郎), but he was later executed as **Píngyuán Tàishǒu** (平源太守) by **Jìngwáng** (景王). The **Jìn** (晋) one bore the style name *Zhèngyú* (正舆) and was from **Pìguó** (沛国). During the **Yǒngjiā** (永嘉) era, he served as **Yángzhōu Shìshǐ** (杨州刺史). It remains unclear which of these three was the **Dōngyuè Dà Jiāngjūn**, but by inference, it was most likely **Zhèngyú** (正舆).

Xún **Zǐqí** (荀｛岂页｝) served as **Tàishān Jūn** (太山君).

(Xún **Zǐqí**, style name *Jǐngqiàn* (景倩), was the fourth son of **Xún Yú** (荀丕). He was well-versed in learning and possessed eloquence and logical reasoning. He assisted the **Jìn** (晋) dynasty’s establishment. Beginning from a lowly position, he became a **Huángmén Láng** (黄门郎), then successively held the offices of **Shàngshū Púshè** (尚书仆射), **Sìkōng** (司空), **Tàiyè** (太尉), and **Tàifù** (太傅). He passed away in the tenth year of the **Tàishǐ** (太始) era, at the age of seventy, and was posthumously honored as **Kāng Gōng** (康公). According to **Sū Yáo** (苏韶)'s *Transmission* (传云), **Liú Kǒngcái** (刘孔才) served as **Tàishān Gōng** (太山公) and plotted to rebel against the **Northern Dì** (北帝). He was later executed and eradicated. **Kǒngcái** (孔才) was none other than **Liú Shào** (刘邵).

Additionally, **Méi Yí** (梅颐) held the post of **Yùzhāng Tàishǒu** (豫章太守). In a dream, he was summoned to serve as **Tàishān Fǔjūn** (太山府君). Upon waking, he died shortly thereafter. It remains unclear whether these two positions were distinct from that of **Tàishān Jūn** (太山君).

It is also said that there existed a **Tàishān Lìng** (太山令), commanding a force of ten thousand soldiers to garrison a location—such garrisons numbered several hundred in total. Some commanded garrisons of several thousand soldiers, all subordinate to one another. Each had a **Chángshǐ** (长史) and **Sīmǎ** (司马).

**Wáng Wéndù** (王文度) was stationed in **Guǎnglíng** (广陵). Suddenly, he saw a soldier appear and summon him, saying, *"You are to be appointed as **Píngběi Jiāngjūn** (平北将军) and **Sìshǐ** (刺史) of **Xú** (徐) and **Dài** (兖) states."* **Wáng** (王) replied, *"I have already been appointed to this office."* The soldier said, *"This is a celestial appointment."* In a short while, the soldier vanished, and soon afterward, **Wáng** was led away and died.

The **Dao/Way** (天地间事理) of existence transcends human understanding and cannot be fathomed by mere conjecture. Even in the unseen and visible realms, there are three categories: **xiān** (仙, the celestial), **rén** (人, the human), and **guǐ** (鬼, the spectral). Those who cultivate virtue may ascend to become **xiān** (celestial beings). Those who are exiled from the celestial realm may reincarnate as humans. The wicked among humans may descend to become **guǐ** (spectral beings). The fortunate among **guǐ** may return to human form. **Guǐ** (spectral beings) emulate humans, while humans emulate **xiān** (celestial beings). This cyclical transformation follows patterns, with each category mirroring the others in kind. It is merely a subtle distinction between the hidden and the manifest.

The enlightened observe this **Dao/Way** (达者监之) and thus find no further cause for concern.

Xún took **Gù Zhòng** (顾众, *Gù Zhòng*, lit. "Gù the Crowd") as his **Tàishān** (太山, *Tàishān*) **jiāngjūn** (将军, *General*). He appointed **Cáo Hóng** (曹洪, *Cáo Hóng*) as his **sīmǎ** (司马, *Military Secretary*). **Huán Fàn** (桓范, *Huán Fàn*) served as his **chángshǐ** (长史, *Chief Clerk*).

**Gù Zhòng** (顾众, *Gù Zhòng*, *Gù Zhòng*), courtesy name **Chángshǐ** (长始, *Long Begin*), was from **Wújìng** (吴郡, *Wu Commandery*). He was **Gù Xīn** (顾心, *Gù Xīn*)’s son, and **Gù Bì** (顾秘, *Gù Bì*)’s grandson. He held offices under the **Jìn** (晋, *Jin Dynasty*) as **Dānyáng** (丹阳, *Dan Yang Prefect*), **Lǐngjūn** (领军, *Commander-in-Chief of the Army*), **Shàngshū** (尚书, *Secretary*), and **Púshè** (仆射, *Attendant-in-Chief*). He died in the second year of **Yǒnghé** (永和, *Eternal Harmony*), at the age of seventy-three. He was posthumously honored as **Tèjìn** (特进, *Extraordinary Progress*), and his posthumous title was **Jìng Bó** (靖伯, *Pacifying Marquis*).

**Cáo Hóng** (曹洪, *Cáo Hóng*), courtesy name **Zǐ Lián** (子廉, *Child of Integrity*), was a younger cousin of **Wèiwǔ** (魏武, *Wei the Martial*). Though his family was wealthy, he was stingy and frugal. He participated in many military campaigns and was appointed **Piāoqí** (骠骑, *Light Cavalry General*), granted the title **Lèchéng Hóu** (乐成侯, *Marquis of Joyous Achievement*). He died of illness in the sixth year of **Tàihé** (太和, *Great Harmony*).

**Huán Fàn** (桓范, *Huán Fàn*), courtesy name **Fú Zé** (符则, *Symbol of Justice*), was from **Píguó** (沛国, *Pei Commandery*). He was skilled in strategy and administration. He served under the **Wèi** (魏, *Wei Dynasty*) and rose to the position of **Tàisīnóng** (太司农, *Grand Minister of Agriculture*). He was implicated in the **Cáo Shuǎng** (曹爽, *Cao Shuang*) case and executed.

When **Wáng Yìshǎo** (王逸少, *Wang the Leisurely*) had some matter to attend to, he was imprisoned in the **jìnzhōng** (禁中, *Forbidden City*) for five years. He said, "The matter has already been resolved." (This is **Wáng Yòujūn** (王右军, *Wang of the Right Army*), who, at the time, did not wish to call **Yáng Jūn** (杨君, *Lord Yang*) by his name, hence he referred to him by his courtesy name. **Yìshǎo** (逸少) was **Wáng Kuàng** (王旷, *Wang the Broad*)’s son, who was **Wáng Yòujūn**’s younger brother. He had a refined manner and was skilled in calligraphy. Later, he served as **Kuàijì** (会稽, *Huai Commandery*) **Tàiguān** (太守, *Prefect*).)

The Prefect of Kuàijì departed in the eleventh year of **Yǒnghé** (永和). He declared, *"My spirit shall not return to office."* Earlier, he had frequented **Xǔ Xiānshēng** (許先生, *Master Xu*), and though he had some admiration for the Dao, by the fifth year of **Shēngpíng** (升平), in the year **Xīnyǒu** (辛酉), he died at the age of fifty-nine.

In the year **Yǐchǒu** (乙丑), it was said that five years had passed since his death. Yet, he was still imprisoned—though no record of his offense could be found. It was feared that his imprisonment was due to lingering resentment in his heart, and that his spirit had been exiled.

**Jiǎng Jì** (蒋济), ennobled as **Nánshānbó** (南山伯, *Marquis of Nan Mountain*), led two thousand troops. **Jiǎng Jì**, courtesy name **Zǐtōng** (子通), was from Píng'é (平阿) in **Chǔ** (楚). Serving under the **Hàn** and **Wèi**, he rose to the position of **Tàiyè** (太尉, *Grand Tutor*). He followed **Xuānwáng** (宣王, *Prince Xuan*) in executing **Cáo Shuǎng** (曹爽), and died that same year, posthumously honored as **Jǐnghóu** (景侯, *Marquis Jing*).

When he served as **Lǐngjūn** (领军, *Commander-in-Chief of the Army*), his wife dreamed that their deceased son appeared as **Tàishān Wǔbó** (太山五伯, *the Fifth Marquis of Tai Mountain*), coming to welcome the western grandson at the ancestral temple. The grandson was then the **Lǐng** (令, *Magistrate) of Tàishān**, and the dreamer begged him to intercede for a favorable transfer. **Jiǎng Jì** granted his request, and the grandson soon died as well. Later, another dream revealed that the transfer had already been recorded.

Such cases demonstrate that, though ghostly offices and ranks may roughly correspond to one’s earthly status, they are often wildly inconsistent. This disparity arises from the varying degrees of **virtue/power** (德) and **achievements** (业), as well as the weight of one’s merits and faults. Thus, their posthumous rankings are reassigned, no longer strictly adhering to their original foundations.

The King **Guǎng Yì** (广异) served as a **Ghostly General of a Department** (部鬼将军). **Guǎng Yì**, with the courtesy name **Shìjiāng** (世将), was from **Lángyǔ** (琅玉), the son of **Móuchéng** (修龄), and the father of the dreamer. He excelled in talent, arts, governance, and literature, skilled in composition and music. He rose to the rank of **General of Píngnán** (平南将军) and **Inspector of Jīngzhōu** (荆州刺史). At the age of forty-seven, he fell ill and died. He was posthumously honored as **Kāng Hóu** (康侯) and granted the title of **Chuàoqí** (骠骑).

Such cases reveal those with **posthumous recognition** (有识位者). They are roughly identifiable, but those without rank are countless and beyond full comprehension.

These are all matters discussed in later sections. For instance, **Xún Zhōnghóu** (荀中侯) was mentioned without a surname, yet later it was clarified—perhaps to distinguish him from others. The individuals discussed are mostly from recent times, likely due to the **transformation and variation** (代谢参差) of their **karmic records** (亿识), as well as the ease of recognizing those from the **Three Dynasties** (三代). Yet, the **Two Hán**, **Wéi**, and **Jìn** dynasties indeed had many talented and renowned figures, such as **Liú Xiàng** (刘向), **Dòng Zhòngshū** (董仲舒), **Yáng Xióng** (扬雄), **Zhāng Héng** (张衡), **Cài Xuān** (蔡川), **Zhèng Xuán** (郑玄), **Wáng Bì** (王弼), and **Ruǎn Qǐshān** (阮稽山) and their peers. They should not be **scattered without rank** (空散), nor should their **karmic calculations** (数术) be like those of **Guǎn Gōu** (管郭), who lack clear traces. Thus, they likely do not belong to the **Three Officials** (三官) but may instead be **assigned to Daoist/Immortal duties** (仙家驱任).

In the earlier discussion of **emperors and kings** (帝王), their posthumous status was also uneven. **Wéi Wén** (魏文) and **Jìn Wǔ** (晋武), though **Mandated Sovereigns** (受命之主), did not attain prominence. Conversely, **Wéi Wǔ** (魏武) and **Jìn Xuān** (晋宣) were celebrated, while **Sūn Quán** (孙权) should be ranked alongside **Liú Bèi** (刘备), yet neither was recorded in the **Celestial Strategies** (道策). These discrepancies arise from the **foundations of their earthly kingdoms** (启国之基) and the **joyous acknowledgment of their great achievements** (功高乐推). As for those who merely **inherited the throne and upheld the traditions** (继体守文之君), none were recognized.

The following two sections were recorded as per the **emperor’s decree** (受旨), all by **Yáng Jūn** (杨君). The texts were meticulously compiled, and later officials further transcribed them. Both versions are identical, each forming a separate volume, detailing the beginning and end of these matters.

**Xǔ Zhào** (许肇), now the **Right Commander of Dōngmíng Gōng** (东明公右帅), holds a position akin to the **Chief Secretary** (世间中书监) of the mortal realm. Though **Xǔ Zhào** (许肇), with the courtesy name **Zǐ’ē** (子阿), is the seventh-generation ancestor of **Chángshǐ** (长史) and **Sìtú Jìng** (司徒敬), his **meritorious deeds** (赈救之功) were not of the **hidden virtue** (阴德) kind.

「Hence, he had not yet received the **transformation of the Dao** (受化). Yet his blessings spread to later generations (福流后叶). Only then did the **Celestial Court** (上) elevate him, making him a **Nine Palaces Immortal** (九宫之仙) thereafter. Such was the office of **Right Commander** (帅晨), and **Sìmíng** (四明) also held this title.

**Shào** (邵) served as **Dōngmíng Gōng** (东明公). **Yún Xíng** (云行) then became **Right Protector of the Nine Palaces** (九宫右保公) as a **Celestial Supplement** (上补). (Earlier, **Shào** was recorded as **Nánmíng Gōng** (南明公); now it is **Dōng** (东). If this is not an error in the title, then the difference between **Dōng** (东) and **Nán** (南) must be due to his **ascension and promotion** (迁擢). Thus, he must be the **Central Lord of the Nine Palaces** (启中君) who later took the name **Yún Shào** (云邵). He also possessed the **virtue of the Sweet Osmanthus** (甘棠之德), so his **lifespan and celestial tenure** (年月) were not restricted.

On the night of the seventeenth day of the seventh month, the **Celestial Records** (定录) were finalized as per the **Central Lord’s testimony** (君所告). These two entries were separately recorded (别受), unrelated to the **Ministry of Archives** (记部). **Xīn Yuànzǐ** (辛玄子), in his own account and verse (自叙并诗), states: (The following line is partially missing; the exact characters remain unknown.) Yuànzǐ, courtesy name **Yán Qī** (延期), was from **Dìnggǔ, Língxī** (陇西定谷人), a native of **Lǒuyáng** (上洛) in **Yúnzhōng** (云中) during the reign of **Emperor Míng of Hàn** (汉明帝). He was the son of **Sīn Yǐn** (辛隐), the **Prefect of Three Commanderies** (三郡太守) of **Zhào** (赵国) in **Lǒuyáng** (上洛).

(Sīn Yǐn’s courtesy name is unclear; the **external records** (外书) do not provide this information. According to the **ancestral lineage of the Sīn clan** (诸辛旧开陇豪族), the **Former Hàn** (前汉) had **Sīn Qìngjì** (辛庆忌), and the **Later Hàn** (后汉) had **Sīn Lián** (辛缮), both men of **lofty integrity and uprightness** (高直之士). **Sīn Yǐn** (辛隐) was the **eighth-generation ancestor** (八世祖) of **Sīn Yuànzǐ** (辛玄子), with **Sīn Yǐn** (辛隐) being the **seventh-generation descendant** (七世孙) of **Sīn Tiánbǐ** (辛田比). This spans merely **one hundred and forty to fifty years** (一百四五十年中), making the eight generations seem too compressed.)

From his youth, **Yuànzǐ** (玄子) was devoted to the **Dao** (道), diligently observing **precepts and vows** (法戒) and practicing **sincere and arduous cultivation** (至心苦行).

From midday on, he subsisted on simple vegetables, refining his form and preserving his essence, untouched by external influences. Though the prefectures and governorships sought to recruit him, he never lowered himself to accept. He wandered the mountains and forests, abandoning worldly affairs and dust, his aspirations aligned with those of **Yú Jìn** (子晋) under **Sīhóu** (丝侯) and **Zēn** (岑), and his companionship with **Língyáng** (陵阳) in **Bù Xuán** (步玄). Thus, he changed his name to **Yuànzǐ** (玄子) and took **Yánqī** (延期) as his courtesy name.

He did not foresee that his ancestors had committed many transgressions, bringing calamity upon their descendants. He was ensnared by imperial edicts, his fate sealed by later generations, and his life did not last long—he perished at the **Chángliáng Ferry** (长梁之津). The **Western Queen Mother** (西王母) observed his arduous cultivation; the **Heavenly Sovereign of the Northern Dipper** (都北帝) pitied his **Dao-heart** (道心) and instructed the **Office of Fate** (司命) to send decrees to the **Three Officials** (三官), commanding them to seize his corporeal form, return his soul to its true nature, and grant him rebirth. Thus, he was endowed with **spiritual essence** (胎神), attaining the rank of **Língshén** (灵神). Over two hundred years have passed since then.

(His death by drowning mirrors that of **Wang Yǎn’s daughter** (王衍之女), and it is feared that this corporeal form may never again be reborn, as it is but a **retrograde essence** (反质) and **spiritual embryo** (胎神). Though he possessed a Dao-heart, he lacked Dao-achievement, so he could not immediately ascend to the ranks of the immortals.)

Recently, he received the **initiation name** (度名) **Nángōng** (南宫) and the **destined strategy** (定策) **Zhūlíng** (朱陵). Now he conceals his essence, awaiting the proper time, before officially ranking among the immortals. Meanwhile, the **Great Emperor** (大帝) has already seen fit to appoint him as the **Marquis of the Eastern Sea** (东海侯), replacing **Guǎngyú** (广臾). He is also now charged with the role of **Central Officer of the Imperial Guards** (禁元中郎将), overseeing the **ghosts and spirits of Wúyuè** (吴越). His duties in the **royal affairs** (王事) are endless—this is indeed laborious.

The **Morning Crown and Azure Sash**—
the rustling sleeves of feathered grace,
the chime of bells upon celestial steps,
the turning of jade halls—

Though the heart remains pure yet the sun sets,
I lament not yet ascending to join the **Mysterious True**,
to rank alongside **Yùkūn** and **Tǔyōng** in serene repose.
I hear the lingering strains of the **Divine Music**,
plucking **spirit-mushrooms** from the hidden peaks,
shaking my emerald robes in the Nine Heavens,
dancing the **Dark Feathers** of the Ten Directions.

Now I must govern the host of ghosts,
judge right from wrong,
dwelling amid the filth of mortals,
my voice mingling with the clamor of demons.
My deeds stray from **Dao and Virtue**,
my gaze falters before the True.
Yet I compare my stillness to the **Silent Void**,
soaring free in the **Primordial Beginning**.

For harmony unites like sounds,
and kindred things share their nature—
thus I am called to the **Upper Clarity**,
my name inscribed in the **Cinnabar Register** of the **Mysterious Seed**.
The **Qi of the Dao** merges with the **Virtue of the Spirit**,
my luminous form ascends in splendor—
that is why they come to follow.

Now I present three poems,
to express the depth of my esteem.
The verses read:

In the Nine Heavens, the **Dark Feathers** of the Ten Directions resound—
now I must govern the host of ghosts,
command the spirits of the **Booming Register**,
and preside over the **Silent Void**’s appointed duties.
Thus I first compose these verses to express my deepest reverence.

The **Ghost Script** differs in its mysteries,
not penned by my own hand—
I must have received its words through the **Oral Transmission** of the **Cinnabar Register**.

In days past, I entered the **Land of Darkness**,
my carriage responding to the **Divine Summons** of the **Spirit Way**.
The **Ghostly Qi** dispersed with the **Void of Space**,
and vanished like the **Celebratory Clouds**.
My form was not of **Luminous Jade**’s essence—
how could the **Dark Artisan** carve such perfection?
Yet my **feet like butterflies** drift in whispered verse,
my gaze uplifted to admire the **Resounding Bamboo**.
The **Forest Chamber** holds its own delight,
cutting off all ties with the **Lofty Exchange** beyond.
The lingering **Effulgence** clings to the **Round Radiance**,
but the **Auspicious Sounds** are few and far between.

*(This poem recounts the origins of my deeds.)*

The **Silent Void**’s **Dao** is a vessel for inspiration,
its **Dark Art** a conduit for the **Moving Sounds** of the **Spirit Way**.
Though the **High Wheels** may seem uneven,
all things seek the **Ten Thousand Fathoms**’ path.
The **Playful Wind** attunes to the **Dao’s Child**,
my **Spirit** in perfect harmony with the **Divine Essence**.
I submit to the **Waves of the World’s Transformation**,
my heart set upon the **Lofty and Elusive Forest**.
With **Kindred Souls**, I wander beyond the **External Reason**,
and together we rest in the **Stillness of the Eastern [Garment] [Peaks]**.

*(This poem explains the reasons for my esteemed arrival.)*

My **Spirit Way**’s **Virtue** extends far—
my **Playful Tracks** transcend the **Land of Darkness**.
In the **Void of Space**, there are things that exist,
yet within them, there is no permanence.
The **Awakened Speech** bridges the **Boundaries of Having and Not Having**,
and we meet again at the **Hao Bridge**.
With **Divine Vision**, I grasp the **Dark Art’s** revelation—
the **Ghosts and Spirits** forget their own **Dao’s Reason**.
*(This poem explores the profound mysteries of human and ghostly interaction.)*

The **Dao’s Child** speaks thus:

「In the time of the **Wei [state]**, there was **Sin [田比]**, courtesy name **Zu [佐]**. He was the grandson of the seventh generation. In the first year of Emperor **Jianwu [建武]** of the Han, he followed the relocation from **Longxi [陇西]** to settle in **Yingchuan [颖川]**, **Yang [阳]** County. **Sin [田比]** served under the **Wei [state]** and was appointed as the **Commander-in-Chief of the Great General [司马宣王]’s army**, **Superintendent of the Armory [衛尉]**, and granted the title of **Hou [封侯]**. His son was named **Chang [敞]**, who became the **Governor of Hebei [河內太守]** and **Minister of the Imperial Sacrifices [太常卿]**. (*All as said aligns with the records in the **Wei Book [魏書]**.*)

The **Dao’s Child [玄子]** says: **Guang [廣臾]** was born as the **Grand General of the Jin [晉]**. The **Northern Emperor [北帝]** once appointed him as the **Commander of the Pacification of the East [抚东将军]**. Later, he was transferred to become the **Eastern Sea Hou [东海侯]**. Now, he is again appointed as the **Left Attendant of the Emperor’s Morning Court [台侍帝晨右禁监]**—a position of great weight. Recently, **Feng [馮] Huai [怀]** was taken as **Commander of the Armory [司馬]**. The **Emperor’s Morning Court Attendant [侍帝晨]** is now like the **Chief of the Right Division [世右衛將軍]**, but with even greater importance. (*This differs greatly from previous accounts. It seems he was later promoted to **Chief Attendant [侍中]** while also leading the **Right Division [衛]**. This would make him the **Commander of the Right Division [衛]**, as there was no **Commander of the Armory [司馬]** at that time. **Feng [馮] Huai [怀]**’s courtesy name was **Zu [祖]**, and he was from **Changle [長樂]**. During the reign of Emperor **Cheng [成]** of the Jin, he served as **Scattered Cavalry of the Imperial Sacrifices [太常散騎]** and **Constant Companion [常侍]**. Upon his death, he was posthumously honored with the **Golden and Purple Light of the Imperial Sacrifices [金紫光禄階]**.*)

The **Left Attendant of the Emperor’s Morning Court [左禁监]** was **Xie [謝] Youyu [幼舆]**. He appointed **Deng [登] Yi [邑] Yu [岳]** as **Commander of the Armory [司馬]**. (*This is equivalent to the **Left Division General [左衛將軍]**. **Youyu [幼舆]** was named **Kun [鲲]**, the father of **Xie An [謝安]**’s elder brother **Xie Shang [謝尚]**. He served as **Chief of Staff for Wang Dun [王敦長史]** and **Governor of Yuzhang [豫章郡太守]**. At the age of fifty-three, he fell ill and died. He was posthumously honored as **Minister of the Imperial Sacrifices [太常]**, and his posthumous title was **Kang Hou [康侯]**. **Deng [登] Yi [邑] Yu [岳]** has already been mentioned earlier, but here it states that he was the one who replaced **Zhou [周] Gai [岂]** as **Commander of the Armory and Chief of the Guards [司馬帥]**.*)

**Xi [希] Yi [邑]**, **Nanchang Gong [南昌公]**, first served as **Southern Zhuyang [南朱陽]** **Great Gate [大門]** **Spirit Pass Guardian [靈關侯]** for the **Northern Emperor [北帝]**. Later, he was transferred to become **High Official of Gaoming [高明司直]**. Once, he was seated and involved in a dispute with **Liu Qing Sun [劉慶孫]** over an official pardon.»

Now he was about to resume his office once more. The **High Official of Gaoming [高明司直]** was akin to the **Worldly Grand Secretary [世尚书]** or **Grand Chamberlain [仆射]**.

(Previously, it was stated that **Xi [希邑]** served as **Southern Gate Warden [南门亭长]**. The **Southern Gate Warden** was likely the same as the **Spirit Pass Guardian [灵关侯]** position. After **Zhou [周] Gai [岂]** took over his duties, he was then transferred to the **High Official of Gaoming [司直]**. The words of **Guo Changxiang [郭长翔]** also confirm this: *"**Xi [希邑]** was greatly humbled when appointed as **Southern Gate Warden [天门亭长]**. In olden times, the selection often used **State Officers of Two Thousand Bushels [州征二千石]**—there were none who were **Three Dukes [三公]** in rank. This is why he was quickly promoted."*)

**He Qidao [何次道]**, currently residing within the **Nanchang Palace [南宫]** at the **Cheng Hua Terrace [承华台]**, has already received the **Scripture [书]**. Upon reaching the **Southern Peak [南岳]**, this person was still alive. His deeds of benevolence were numerous, so he was able to regain his form early. (Previously, **Xun Gong [荀公]** spoke of **He [何]** first obtaining the **return of his red vitality [还朱火]**. Now it is said that he had already received the **Scripture**, meaning **He Xiangzi [玄子]**’s restoration came later.)

Zhou Bó-rén has recently been appointed as the **Central Protector of Ximing Gong [西明公中都护]**. The **Central Protector** is an office akin to that of the **Grand Tutor of the Realm [世太傅]**. However, **Yōu Bùpíng [攸不平]** was seated in selection—his promotion was unjust. He was demoted to **Central Guardian [中护]**, equivalent to the rank of **Minor Tutor [少傅]**.

(Zhou originally held the post of **Marshal of Destiny [司命帅]**. He should have been succeeded by **Chéng Xí [程遐]** before being transferred to this office. **Yōu Bùpíng**, styled **Bódào [伯道]**, was from **Xiangling, Pingyang [平阳襄陵]**. He served under the Jin as **Master of the Imperial Heir’s Attendants [太子洗马]**, **Minister of Personnel [吏部郎]**, and **Governor of Hedong [河东太守]**. He was captured by **Shí Lè [石勒]** but later returned to the east, becoming **Governor of Wujun [吴郡太守]** and **Minister of Personnel [吏部尚书]**. He died of illness in the first year of **Xiánhé [咸和]**, aged forty-seven, and was posthumously honored as **Lord of Light [光禄]**. When **Yōu Bùpíng** returned from rebellion with the Hu, he abandoned his own son and brought only his younger brother’s child across the river. Thus, he had no descendants, leaving no heirs. **Xiè Àn [谢安]** sighed, *"Heaven’s ways are inscrutable—why did **Yōu Bódào [攸伯道]** have no son?"*

The above is what **Xīn Xuánzǐ [辛玄子]** spoke of—his accounts of the **Netherworld [冥中]** are numerous. Here, I have only roughly recorded the **fragmentary [鹿鹿鹿]** portions, without elaborating further. (Though this record is from **Xuánzǐ’s** teachings, it also includes words from **Yang Jun [杨君]**. Yang’s original text is lost, and only a copyist’s draft remains. The manuscript was later partially cut away, so the accounts may not be entirely complete.)

Those of **utter loyalty and filial piety**, upon death, receive the **Scripture [书]** to become **Netherworld Rulers [地下主]**. After **one hundred and forty years**, they are granted instruction in **Lower Dao [下仙之教]** and taught the **Great Way [大道]**. From then on, they gradually ascend, eventually **supplementing the ranks of the Immortals [补仙官]**. After another **one hundred and forty years**, they are permitted a **trial advancement [试进]**. (These **Netherworld Rulers** are the same as those recorded in the **Cavern Texts [洞中所记]**—such as **Lǐ [李] (East)**—not separate ghostly officials appointed anew. Each **one hundred and forty years** marks a promotion to the next rank, where they serve as **Immortal Envoys [仙人使]**, only then beginning to receive Daoist teachings.)

The **filially pious** can move **ghosts and spirits**, causing **birds and beasts of the mountains** to guard their tombs **[巡其坟土延]**. The **utterly loyal** act with **unwavering rectitude**, their **essence piercing the sun [精贯白日]**, or even **severing their own bodies** to inspire their rulers. **Bǐ Gān [比干]** now resides at **Róngshān [戎山]**, while **Lǐ Shàn [李善]** is at **Shàoshì [少室]**. Many have undergone such **transmutations [变鍊]**, but these two serve as examples. (Bǐ Gān’s **severed heart** exemplifies **utter loyalty**, while accounts of filial sons moving spirits are also numerous. Yet here, only **Lǐ Shàn** is mentioned—his case seems misplaced, as his deeds align more with **loyalty than filial piety**. Perhaps it is because he preserved **Lǐ Yuán’s [李元] lineage**, ensuring succession, thus fulfilling filial duty. The merits of such acts need not be debated further. Figures like **Chéng Yīng [程婴]** and **Qí Sūn Qiújiù [齐孙杵臼]** should also be counted among the **filially pious**.)

**Lǐ Shàn**, styled **Cìxùn [次逊]**, was originally a slave of **Lǐ Yuán [李元]** from **Yùyáng, Nanyang [南阳育阳]**. During the **Han Jiànwǔ [建武]** era, when **Lǐ Yuán’s** household perished and amassed great wealth, only one orphan, **Xùzǔ [续祖]**, remained—a baby in arms. The slaves conspired to divide the estate among themselves, but **Shàn** secretly carried **Xùzǔ** away, fleeing to **Xiáqiūshān [瑕丘山]**, where he nursed and raised him. When **Xùzǔ** was over ten years old, **Shàn** revealed him to **County Magistrate Zhōnglíyì [锺离意]**, who then recommended him, taking all the slaves under his care and establishing **Xùzǔ** as the heir. The **Emperor Guangwǔ [光武]** appointed **Shàn** as **Attendant of the Imperial Heir [太子舍人]**, later promoting him to **Governor of Jīnnán and Jiāngxi [日南九江太守]**. His deeds were as described, though **Zhōnglí’s** account differs slightly in places.)

Those who possess **supreme sagely virtue [上圣之德]**, upon death...

「All receive the **Three Officials’** scrolls as rulers of the underworld.
After a thousand years,
they are then assigned to supplement the **Five Emperors** of the **Three Officials**.
Some become the **Lords of the East, West, South, and North**,
to govern the spirits and deities.
After another thousand four hundred years,
they may attain the **Great Clarity** and roam freely,
becoming the **Middle Immortal** of the **Nine Palaces**.

*(In terms of years, even this **sagely virtue** falls short of loyalty and filial piety.
Calculating from death, a total of two thousand four hundred years
before one may enter the ranks of the **Immortal Stage**.
This makes it clear that the earlier passage must refer to **Xià Qǐ [夏启]**, not **Míng Gōng [召公明]**.
After **Jìzǐ’s** death, until the **Jìn** era of **Xīngníng [兴宁]**, it was only about eight hundred and eighty years—
far short of a thousand.
I wonder if he had already become a **Lord**?
*[The records from the capital mention none of the **Five Emperors** in this context.
Perhaps this follows the precedent of the **Northern Emperor**, who also has five.
Thus, later mentions of **Heroes** as the **Five Emperors’** supreme ministers,
while the **Northern Emperor** has **Qín Huáng [秦皇]**.
The **Sū Zhāo** transmission also states that **Yáng Xióng [扬雄]**, **Zhāng Hēng [张衡]**, and others are among the **Five Emperors**—
but **Yáng Xióng** and **Zhāng Hēng** are not **supreme sages**;
their ranks are humble,
and they should not be equated with **Yán Dì [炎帝]**.
There may also be lesser **Five Emperors** not listed here.
The accounts of **Yáng Xióng** and **Zhāng Hēng** may not be accurate either.)*

Those who possess **boundless magnanimity** and **unrivaled prospects**,
nurturing their **浩然** *Great Qi*,
yet do not seek honor and wealth,
upon death
receive the **Three Officials’** scrolls as the **spirit of benevolent clarity**.
After four hundred years,
they may attain the rank of **ruler of the underworld**.
From there, they advance in stages,
with three hundred years marking each **ascension to Immortal Officials**.

*(This refers to the rank of **recluse-sage** and **eremitic virtuous ones**.
From the **ruler of the underworld** onward,
this is entering the **Immortal Stage**,
no longer serving as a **spirit-official**.)*

Those with **uttermost integrity and utmost frugality**,
upon death
receive the scrolls as **pure spirits** of the **Three Officials**.
After two hundred and eighty years,
they may attain the rank of **ruler of the underworld**.
From there, they gradually ascend
to become **Immortal Officials**,
with two hundred and eighty years as one stage.

*(This rank is slightly inferior to that of the **recluse-sage**,
yet the years required are double those for loyalty and filial piety—
thus, it is clear that **loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and frugality** are the most esteemed virtues in practice.)*

As for the **utterly frugal**,
they do not eat food not earned by themselves,
nor wear silk or cloth not acquired by their own labor—
**Wáng Yáng [王阳]** was such a man.
*(This category should be measured against **Yí Qí [夷齐]** as the standard.
There are many examples among **recluse-sages**,
yet today only **Wáng Yáng** is cited—
his natural purity was innate,
not a deliberate act of pristine virtue.
**Wáng Yáng** was a person of the **Former Han** era.)*

As for the **utterly chaste**,
luxury and splendor cannot sway their uprightness,
nor can the might of a great kingdom shake their reputation and conduct.
For men, **Gù Guāng [务光]**’s deeds are similar.
For women, **Sòng Jīn Piāo’s** daughter is the example.
*(The **chaste** are not merely those who avoid licentiousness in deed—
they also resist the **inflamed heart** of coveting honor and profit.
**Gù Guāng** declined the **Tāng** dynasty’s offer of office,
yet he carried stones and cast himself into the river.)*

Sòng Jīn Piāo’s daughter—likely the one **Zǐxú [子胥]** encountered
while washing silk by the **Fèi Shā [浣沙]** ford on the banks of the **Piāo Shǔi [漂水]**.
Later, when she returned his gift of gold,
she was thus called **Jīn Piāo [金漂]**.
(The character *piāo* may also be written as *liáo* [漂], meaning "water chestnut.")

Her ancestors had meritorious deeds before the **Three Officials [Sān Guān]**.
Though their virtue extended through generations,
some descendants were reborn in altered forms—
changed surnames, new lives—
this was the **seven-generational hidden virtue**,
its roots and branches intertwined.

When death came,
she left behind **one bone** to return to the **Three Officials**,
while the rest followed her body’s journey.
Men’s remains were buried on the **left**,
women’s on the **right**—
each received a **scroll** to serve as **underground sovereigns**.
After **280 years**,
they could ascend to the **Dao of Earthly Immortals**.

At the moment of death,
her flesh remained supple as the living,
and when she shed mortality,
her corpse did not stiffen—
her fingers stayed unblue,
her limbs unwrinkled—
this was **preordained virtue**,
a natural **emaciation-death [shíjiě]**.

(This was from **ancestral hidden deeds and profound virtue**,
not constrained by outward actions—
not something she herself could **argue or force**.)

Thus, she left **one bone** to repay the **Three Officials**—
this bone was likely the **corporeal bone**,
not the **spiritual bone**.
Once abandoned, it would again be reborn—
the *Great Capital* [Dàdū] discusses among the **Daoists and Ghosts** [xiān guǐ] those who in life practiced **self-mutilation** (kūfù) or **self-beheading** (fùjǐng),
whose bodies were split and shattered by death—
these were forever severed from their later forms.
Their **spirit** had already departed beforehand,
so now their **flesh** could be killed, maimed, or mutilated—
initially, their **spirit** was not cut off.
Yet some in the world see **ghostly bodies** not fully intact—
these are merely **corporeal shells** [shǐpò] sheltering **spirits**,
not the **Great Spirit** [Dàshén] that governs their **true essence** [běnjīng].
The theory of **emaciation-death** [shíjiě] has many variations,
already recorded in the **Third Volume**.

All five of these cases
were those who cultivated **virtue/power** [dé] and attained **Daoist Immortality** [xiān],
without study or effort—
only the difficulty of their **ranked stages** [jiējí] required time.
Once they attained the **name of self-liberation** [zìdù], they no longer followed the **Three Officials'** commands**.

(Though these five cases encompass seven distinct **matters** [shì],
each varying in **lightness and weight** [qīngzhòng],
only those of **unwavering resolve** [zhì] could achieve such examples.
Now, even in the **grades of Daoist learning** [xué dào zhī pǐn],
mere trifling **minor practices** [wēiwēi xiǎoyè] suffice to match them—
how much more so for true **mysteries** [zhēnmiào]?
From this perspective,
one should not underestimate oneself!)

Among those with **heroic talents** [yīngxióng zhī cái],
who pacified the **four seas** [sìhǎi],
slayed tyrants and ended chaos,
expanded the **Nine Regions** [jiǔzhōu],
and established the **title of Emperor** [dìwáng],
ruling over the **four quarters** [sìhài] as **subjects and servants** [chénqiè]—
after death,
they received **scrolls** from the **Three Officials** and **Four Assistants** [sìfǔ],
some becoming **Prime Ministers of the Five Emperors** [Wǔdì shàngxiàng],
others **Guests of the Four Ming Lords** [Sìmíng gōng bīnyǒu],
to aid in governing **a hundred ghosts** [bǎiguǐ],
and overseeing the **administration of life and death** [zōnglǐ sǐshēng].
These ranks are forever bound to the **Three Officials**,
with no hope of ascending to **Daoist Immortality**.
They are **condemned** [zuò] for their **violent deeds** [fá] and **cruelty** [jǔ],
which harm the **cycles of life and death** [sǐshēng duōgù].

(Their **official duties** [gōngzhōng zhū rén zhí] are all accounted for—
I suspect **Xun** [Xún] may have **omitted** [shì] one.)

The refined and virtuous—
not the domineering and cruel—
only those who devise sagacious schemes and strategies to assist **Wei Wu** [魏武]
are granted the rank of **Guests** and **Friends**,
and placed alongside **Han Gao** [汉高] in station.
Perhaps there are deeper intentions here.

All who in this world possess talent, insight, or skill
and are acclaimed in their time,
after death,
are assigned their positions according to their merit—
no need to dwell on their petty crimes in life
before they are consigned to the **examinations of the dead** [zhū rén zhū zhí].
If their transgressions are extreme and unjust,
they are beyond reckoning.
If they are idle and frivolous,
they are not distinguished from ordinary souls—
**no matter the size of their guilt, all are settled in their fate.**

**Qin Shi Huang** [秦始皇] now serves as the **Prime Minister of the Northern Emperor** [北帝上相],
while **Liu Bang** [刘季] is a **Guest of the Four Ming Lords** [南明公宾友].
Such figures are many—
here, I merely indicate their general ranks.
(This is the principle for those who established imperial titles—
the **Northern Emperor** [北帝] having a **Prime Minister**, just as the **Four Ming Lords** [四明] have **Guests**.)

**Qi Huan Gong** [齐桓公] now holds the post of **Chief Prohibition Officer under the Three Officials** [三官都禁郎],
overseeing the **registers of life and death** [主生死之简录].
**Jin Wen Gong** [晋文公] now serves as the **Water Official of the Three Officials** [水官司命].
The followers of **Chu Yan Gong** [楚严公] and **Zhao Jianzi** [赵简子], numbering hundreds,
still linger as **scattered spirits** [散息] within the **Three Officials’ Bureau** [三官府],
yet none have been appointed to duty.
These ranks are merely the **offices of the Three Officials** [三官之寮]—
they have no connection to the **true affairs of the Daoist Immortals** [真仙家事].

(The **Five Hegemons** [五霸] were also heroes of their age—
**Qi Huan** and **Jin Wen** share key positions,
while **Chu Yan Gong** [楚严公] was the **Zhuang King** [庄王].
Though **Zhao Jianzi** [简子] did not reach the status of a **Hegemon** [霸限],
he wielded absolute authority over life and death.
The legend of his dream where **the Heavenly Emperor** [天帝] commanded him to shoot a bear
must be the **Northern Emperor’s Bureau** [北帝之府].
The **Sword Canon’s Preface** [剑经序] states that **Yan Zhao** [燕昭] also attained **Daoist Immortality** [得仙].
As a **sixth-century sovereign** [六国时英主], he thus escaped the **Three Officials’ jurisdiction**.
This shows that **pursuing alchemy alone** [鍊丹独往]
is also an extraordinary distinction.

From the discussion of **loyalty and filial piety** [zhōngxiào] onward, all these points
are drawn from the **Scribe’s writings in the Sword Canon** [掾写剑经].
The words of **Lord Dongqing** [东卿司命] on **ghostly and divine matters** [鬼神事]
are recorded below for verification.

Since the **Three Dynasties** [三代],
the **virtuous sages** and **heroes** [贤圣及英雄] have ascended to **Daoist Immortality** [为仙],
yet in the **ghostly realm** [鬼中], none are seen—
**no Yi Tang** [殷汤], **no Zhou Gong** [周公], **no Confucius** [孔子].

「King Fuchai—Hòu Lǘ—along with the ministers of the Spring and Autumn period, such as Gōu Jiàn, Wǔ Zǐxù, Sūn Wǔ, Bái Qǐ, and Wáng Jiǎn, and extending down to figures like Hán Xìn and Xiàng Yǔ, some attained the ranks of Daoist Immortality [仙品], yet the Immortal Way [仙家] does not acknowledge them.

Take the tyrants Jié and Jiǔ, or the usurpers Wáng Màng and Dǒng Zhuó—those whose cruelty and depravity were extreme, lest they be denied even the lowest celestial offices [补职僚]. Even the fierce warriors of foreign lands, such as Mòdún, Shí Kuài, and Shí Lè, are omitted from mention [不预及言之].

The *Zhēn Gào* [真诰]—specifically its sixteenth and seventeenth scrolls—along with the *Wò Zhēn Fǔ* [握真辅], First Chapter of *Xiāo Jì* [萧寂门]—discusses **preserving the spirit and safeguarding the form** [研神保形], **harmonizing the soul and subduing the vital forces** [和魂夷], and **guarding the divine gate of life-nourishment** [守养神关].

How can such practices be compared to those who dwell in ornate halls, strike gongs and drums, feast upon five great cauldrons, and indulge in fine silks and embroidered garments?

The **Great Unity** [大罗] and the **common mortal path** [垄樊] are but one and the same.

Thus, the **ancient masters of the Dao** [古之高人] all abandoned the latter in favor of the former.」

The Master Lao remains in tranquil obscurity, unobservant of the clamorous and the keen.
What then of we, the vulgar and unenlightened?
*(Unknown whence this passage originates, yet containing Daoist speech—
thus transcribed for reference alone, written by hand.)*

**Xuān Xuān** then expounded, annotating:
*"Thus Xuān Xuān took the Eight Winds as the key to the [sacred] bag of stone and wood,
the heavens and earth as the [fortified] ramparts,
the Four Seas as the [ceremonial] vessel,
and the Nine Isles as the millet of the [cosmic] field.
Accumulating them in myriad forms,
steaming them with yin and yang—
their molding and casting:
expansive, resonant, blowing and piling,
hard and soft, clear and turbid—
their forms and kinds diverging.
Inhaling and exhaling, containing and releasing,
[the Dao] reveres and honors [life’s] splendor."*

He annotated further:
*"The Nine Absolutes—beasts, spirits, birds—
arise from this, yet remain unfathomable.
Their brilliance dazzles the eye,
their marvels at their height—
a year insufficient to exhaust their transformations,
myriad forms insufficient to contain their depths,
day and moon insufficient to illuminate their brilliance,
the Eight Marshes insufficient to traverse their paths.
The Azure Clouds are deemed lowly,
the Nine Expanse shallow,
the Eight Encompassings small,
the Four Poles near.
Thus, in their ceaseless change,
they perpetually enter the mustard seed’s interior—
the jade morn’s precious gem,
the Taiwei’s mighty spirit."*

*(Xuān Xuān then expounded further,
tuning the *gōngbǎi* and *róng* [musical instruments],
lines drawn from the *Shén Hǔ Yǐnwén Huīshén Shī* [神虎隐文挥神诗].
Now re-annotated:
These words are taken from Yang Xiong’s *Xuānwéi Lùn*,
with slight additions and omissions—
thus aligning with principle and meaning,
worthy of acceptance.)*

The heart and governance harmonize within the Great Unity—
only the Tang and Yu could fulfill this duty.
The spirit and transformation sway beyond all bounds—
only Fuxi could inherit this lineage.

Thus, the twenty-five-stringed instrument’s craft
requires Yajuan’s skill to wield its divine resonance.
The bow and arrow’s plain implements
require [Houyi’s] Yǔ [or] Fēngmeng’s mastery to perfect their art.
*(This passage too is from the *Xuānwéi Lùn*—
yet unclear what metaphor it illustrates.)*

Like the *Yǐn Bǎopǔ Wàipiān* in its broad metaphors,
all divergent passages are marked in red for distinction—thus.

As for the wondrous and mysterious,
the strange and the cunning—
boundless in their guile—
nurtured by yin and yang,
concealed in rivers and marshes—
then there is **Hē** and **Hé** bathing the sun in the sweet springs,
the crow **Wū Fēi** overseeing the shadow at **Fú Sāng**.
The **Jiāng Nǚ** ascends the **Shuǐ Méi** and loosens her jade belt,
while the two **Rù** sisters ride the wind over **Xiāo Xiāng**.
The hidden dragon and serpent wage war in the **Xuán Quán**,
while **Fān Qiū** loses his horse in **Huái Yáng**.
The **Líng Zhōu** shifts the seas at the southern pole,
and the **Dōng Shān** gathers afar in the **Dì Xiāng**.
The **Mǎ Huá** and **Mǎ Liú** hold the reins against the giant tortoise,
while the **Jiāng Shǐ** is moved by dreams from the **Sòng King**.

Thus, though **Dòngtíng** be vast,
**Jì** cannot contain it with a blade.
Though **Lú Lóng** be steep,
**Yuè** cannot scale it in a morning.

The **Shān Mián** mountain hangs suspended,
its chasm a thousand *rèn* wide.
The **Shù Mǎ** and **Mián Zhú**—
then **Ānlè** returns to **Jìn**,
while the **Liáo Hǎi** stretches wide,
spanning the celestial channels.
**Gōngsūn** does not compete—
thus his downfall comes swift.

As for the flying **fēihú** and white horses,
the **Jímò** and **Tiānshān**,
the sources of the **Sān Jiāng** and **Jiǔ Hé**—
what words suffice to speak of them?

The Master’s chariot flies with four steeds harnessed,
its gaze sweeping through groves and thickets.
Yet only the treasures of mountain and river are heard—
why has the realm of Wei not yet been fully explored?

When Wu Qi spoke but once,
the heart of Lord Wu was stirred.

*(These two lines are from the expanded *Yǔ* section of the *Capital Ode*.
All four lines are from different hands.
The small script on green paper is ancient and crude,
yet since they connect with the true script,
they are recorded together and carried along.
—Yang Jun)*

The First Emperor of Qin built the Long Bridge across the Wei River,
six *zhàng* wide, three hundred eighty *bù* long—sixty-eight spans.
In the Han, a Water Commissioner was stationed north of the bridge,
with fifteen hundred laborers under his command,
administered by the Jingzhao prefecture.
Dong Zhuo destroyed it.
Emperor Wu of Wei rebuilt it,
three *zhàng* wide—this is the bridge of today.

*(The *zhōng* is an auspicious omen.
In the Jin Dynasty, there were six *zhōng*—
their appearance was certain.
The Five Hegemons of the Jin era surpassed them in virtue,
so the six *zhōng* were merely fortunate omens,
not a sign of decline for future generations.
Was there not a warning of lost territories?
This is a note of forty-eight characters added by Huang Min.)*

This was the twelfth year of Emperor Yi of Song.
The Ho Mountain collapsed, yielding six *zhōng*.
Thus, one sought to associate it with the Song ancestors,
and hastily composed this passage—
yet the events diverged so greatly as to invite ridicule.

The Qin built the Alang Palace,
twenty *lǐ* southwest of Chang’an.
The palace stretched a thousand *bù* east to west,
three hundred *bù* north to south.
Its upper halls seated ten thousand,
while its courtyard could accommodate a hundred thousand.
The Second Qin Emperor was slain by Zhao Gao in the Yìchūn Palace,
located three *lǐ* south of the city.
He was buried nearby—
as Sima Xiangru described:
*"The tomb lies overgrown and unkempt."*

The Qin confiscated weapons nationwide,
melting them to cast twelve bronze statues.
They were placed in the palaces—
*(these fourteen characters occupy a single line;
the preceding fish-rotten residue leaves ten missing;
now the order is completed as such)*
—and remained in Chang’an during the Han.
Dong Zhuo melted them down for coinage.
Two statues remained in the Eastern Palace before Qingmen.
Emperor Ming of Wei sought to relocate them to Luoyang,
but the effort failed at Ba City.
They now stand on Ba City’s main road to the south,
with an inscription on their chest:
*"In the twenty-sixth year of the Emperor,
he unified all the feudal lords under his rule,
establishing commanderies and counties,
rectifying the laws,
and standardizing measures and weights."*

The Great One came to take up residence beside the **Water Omen**.

His stature was five zhang in height.
His footprints measured six chi.
These were inscribed by Qin Chancellor Meng Tian and Li Si.
*(The Qin character is missing.)*

Four bronze bells hung in the temple.
All were in the Han High Ancestor’s Temple.
Emperor Ming of Wei relocated two to Luoyang.
Thus, the remaining two stand in the Southern Copper **Masheng** Lane—
the **Northern Zhu** bell.

In Han Zhao’s tomb at Pingling,
and in Han Xuan’s tomb at Duling,
two bronze bells stood in Chang’an.
General Xiahou Zhengxi sought to relocate them to Luoyang,
but their weight proved insurmountable.
Now they rest on the southern road of Qingmen.
The western one is the Pingling bell,
the eastern one the Duling bell.
*(Here follows the matter of the First Emperor’s tomb.)*

Hongmen lies ten-odd li north of the First Emperor’s tomb.
The *Han Book* records:
Zhang Liang there resolved his crisis.
As Qin King (should be **Chu King**; a mistake, calling him Qin King),
Xiang Yu appointed Stipe’s Duke as **Han King**,
establishing his capital at Hanzhong.
Yet he divided Guanzhong into three:
Qin Zhāng Hán as **Yong King**, capital at Daqiu—
now Huáilǐ is its site.
Sīmǎ Xīn as **Sài King**, capital at Lìyáng—
now Wànyuán County is its site.
Dǒng Yì as the **Yù King**,
capital at Gāonú.
Gāonú County lay northwest of Xianyang—
now it is **omitted** (or **abbreviated**).
The High Ancestor, emerging from Hanzhong to the north,
raided all three fronts and subdued them.
The *Han Book* records this.

Exploiting the opportunity to act,
he swept through the Three Qin—
this was his deed.
These three counties today still bear the sites of their ancient capitals.
*(Hereafter, fifteen or sixteen items are missing—
clearly lost or omitted.)*

**Du Ling**—the Mausoleum of Emperor Xuan.
Emperor Xuan, in his youth, took refuge with the Xu family in Du County.
He was buried in the Southern Mound,
and a temple was erected to him north of Qu Pool,
named the **Leyou Temple**—
taking its name from the nearby **Wanyuan** estate.
Forty-five noble clans from east of the Pass were relocated here
to accompany the Du Ling Mausoleum.

Sima Xiangru, in his elegy for the Second Emperor, spoke of
standing on the **Qujiang’s** [mistaken character] isle—
meaning Qu Pool itself.
*(This entry has been expanded and slightly altered.
It is unclear how it came to be thus.)*

The ten items preceding this
are all taken from Yang Jun’s transcription of Pan Anren’s *Record of Guanzhong*,
written in fine white paper with elegant cursive script.
It seems he merely copied these matters at random.

Within the Eastern Palace’s precincts,
there was a verse recited:
*"The small fish not yet cooked in the cauldron—*
*I weep beneath this rock."*
*(This is a song from the Donghua Palace.)*

*"The winds obey, the white road opens—*
*The River Spirit leads the way ahead."*
*(These two lines are from the *Huishen* poem.)*

*"The Wind Lord does not stir the branches—*
*The Divine Tiger holds them fast."*

On the twenty-fourth day of the eleventh month,
in the blink of an eye,
a voice was heard from the **Dongfang Chamber**:
*"Within the crimson canopy,*
*there sounds like a human voice,*
*reading aloud like this."*
*(This records the Three True Ones of the Dongfang Chamber,
along with the preceding entry—
all from Yang Jun’s own recollections and experiences.)*

Upon receiving the letter,
he knew his heart had been cleansed of guilt,
and the words expressed deep humility.
In reply, he came again,
Zhang Sheng bowed his head.
The note was signed: *"To Master Xu.*
*I recently learned of your northern journey.*
*I regret we could not meet in person.*
*Now I send a single thread of Huang Zhang’s longevity.*
*Will you truly not come again?"*
Zhang Sheng bowed his head.
The note was signed: *"To Master Yang.*
*I dreamed of a woman-like figure,*
*wearing a feathered robe,*
*who brought these two folded letters.*
*Upon opening and reading them,*
*I awoke—*
*I recall having heard these words before,*
*yet much remains forgotten.*
*Later, I dreamed of burning incense*
*and entering the inner chamber."*
*(All this records the dream of Zhang Tian Shi’s letter.
Zhang Sheng’s name is thus concealed—
now revealed to the Chancellor,
so it is not openly displayed.
It also appears in the *Systematic Commentary on the Laozi*,
where all refer to "this humble servant bowing in reverence."
Perhaps this too is from Master Xi’s letter.)*

On the twenty-seventh day of the fourth month, **Xingning** year,
Yang Jun dreamed of a man
wearing red robes and a peaked cap,
holding two tablets in his hands,
and carrying two more in his arms.
He summoned Xu Yufu,
who stepped forward and presented the tablets—
all inscribed in green characters.
Yang was then summoned to serve as **Chancellor**.
In a moment, Xu Yufu emerged.
Yang pointed and said, *"This is Master Xu."*
Xu Yufu spoke for himself:
*"I am to be appointed in the thirteenth year.
Now I am summoned early—
I do not yet know the proper etiquette to address others.*
*If you say so, I can act as a messenger."*
Xu Yufu then bowed in submission,
still uncertain of the proper rites.
He was studying the **Ten Thousand Words** [mistaken character],
and it would take thirteen years before he could properly serve.
He then bowed to Yang and departed.
*(This half-sheet is a transcription,
written down as Yang Jun recounted his dream.
Thus, it remains within the Yang records.
The Chancellor’s position—
the one who attends the Emperor at dawn—
the tablets inscribed in green—
these are the **green bamboo slips and white tablets**.)*

On the twenty-ninth day of the fourth month, at midnight,
he dreamed of sitting beside Xu Yufu,
though he knew not where they were.
After a long while...

I saw the **Nanyue** Lady and the **Ziyang** True Person Zhou Jun together arrive.
They sat upon a single bed.
Then I saw Xu Yufu.
He spoke to the True Person Zhou Jun, saying,
*"I have long heard of Master’s **Guard-One** method.
I beg to request its transmission."*

Zhou Jun replied,
*"My former teacher, Master Su, once said to me,
‘To seek the True through the Dao—is this not overly roundabout?
Let me raise this point with you now.’"*

Xu Yufu said,
*"My aspirations are shallow and limited.
I covet the **Dao** and **virtue**,
so I wish to beg for the **Guard-One** method."*

Before he finished speaking,
Zhou Jun again said,
*"What was once not transmitted through the **Dao**
was merely because of the **bounded exchanges** of mortal men.
*(Zhou was from Ruyin. A seventh-generation descendant of Han Grand Commandant Bo. Hence, he is called a **bounded man**.)*
You are a **True Person**—
and already possess great transmission.
Why would you seek to **Guard-One** further?"*

Having spoken, he suddenly awoke,
yet did not know where he was.
This dream was exceedingly clear,
so I recorded it.
**Fourth month, ninth day, on the night of the fourth watch,**
I dreamed of traveling north and ascending a high mountain.
Lost and disoriented, I did not awaken.
Only when the sun rose four or five *zhang* did I wake.
Upon waking, I recalled climbing the mountain for half a day,
reaching the summit, where there were thousands of palaces,
so vast they defied description.
The mountain’s four sides were encircled by great waters,
yet I did not know where I was.

I then looked upward,
and in the sky saw a white dragon,
its body tens of *zhang* long,
flying eastward through the heavens,
its radiance dazzling the sky.
Then I saw, to the east, a fair woman in white robes,
also moving through the air,
facing west toward the white dragon.
She entered its mouth,
emerged again,
and did so three times before stopping.
She then returned to my right,
facing me.
To my left stood an old man,
wearing embroidered robes and a lotus-crowned cap,
leaning on a nine-jointed red staff.
Both gazed at the white dragon.

I asked the old man,
*"What kind of woman enters the dragon’s mouth?"*
He replied,
*"This is the **Taisu** Jade Maiden, Xiao Zifu.
She takes the dragon to refine her form.
This person appears to be **Fangxiang**, an official."*

I asked again,
*"Who is this old man?
Why has he come to this place?"*
He answered,
*"I am the **Penglai** Celestial Lord Luo Guangxiu.
This is **Penglai Mountain**.
I govern its heights.
The **Fu Lord** came to visit,
and thus we were able to meet."*

I asked the old man again,
*"Can this dragon be ridden?"*
He replied,
*"This dragon awaits the **True Person** Zhang Youshi,
Shi Qing’an,
and Xu Yufu."*

I asked again,
*"Is it one dragon that the four are riding together?"*
The old man replied,
*"This is a dragon that serves the Morning Emperor.
It is like the world’s **Zhāochē** chariot drawn by the Vermilion Bird—
each day, one rides it to ascend directly.
In a moment, the old man called these four worthies.
Before they arrived, I spread out a mat with you, the old man, and this maiden,
and we sat together on the mountain, all facing north,
gazing at the sea and the white dragon.
There was also wine and food set before us.
The wine was like pomegranate seeds,
and we ate them together.
The **mùbàn** was also like the world’s **mùbàn**,
and within the **mùbàn** was salmon.
After a long while, the four arrived from the east,
riding a fresh **niúcē** chariot,
with green oxen and green oil—
a grand carriage that came before us.
They bowed to the old man and to me,
and spoke with us.
After their words concluded,
the old man said, ‘The four men I spoke of earlier—
these are they.’
I saw that Zhang Youshi was about fifty years old,
Shi Qing’an was very youthful, around thirteen or fourteen,
Xu Yufu was the same age as I saw him today,
and Ding **Yùwéi** Ning was about thirty-four or five.
All wore fine single robes,
with **jīnzé** hanging down and **bān** sandals.
Only Qing’an wore an empty **jīnzé**."*

I said, "Not so."
The old man replied, "Zhang Youshi is a man of Changshan.
He is my disciple.
Shi Qing’an is a man of Ji Province.
He is the disciple of the Lady Gouyi.
Their talents are equal, their virtue matched—
both are esteemed among men of letters.
The old man then addressed the four, saying,
‘You may each compose a poem
to display your skill before the Master.
I too wish to hear your refined words of literary beauty.’

At this, the old man gave each a sheet of green paper,
and a brush to each as well,
to present to the four.
The four took them and said,
‘We fear our haste may betray us.’

Then Shi Qing’an composed his poem first.
Its words were:
*"The Ling Mountain crafts the dawn’s radiance,
The upright cliffs pierce the celestial peaks.
Purple mists drift across the land of gods,
Riding the gale, harnessed to the white dragon.
Hand in hand, we four guests arrive,
To the eastern hall of the Master of Sanglin.
Though my years are few, my heart is vast—
What wisdom do I bring?
I have not yet descended to yield the path.
Only in mutual reverence do we meet."*

Next, Zhang Youshi composed his poem.
Its words were:
*"To the north, I wandered beyond the Great Desert,
To climb the gates of Penglai.
Purple clouds met the Ling Palace’s halls—
Fragrant mists, how thick they rise!
Ah, splendid is the Master’s virtue!
Long have I dwelt in his Dao’s embrace.
The True Ones gather in their ranks,
Together observing the white dragon’s stride.
When shall we four loosen our belts and part?
I have a friend in whom I take delight—
Joyful mornings we share in his company."*

Then Xu Yufu composed his poem.
Its words were:
*"I wander through mountains of marvelous height,
Rinsing in the clear currents of the Stygian Sea.
From afar, I gaze upon Penglai’s realm—
Towering peaks pierce the heavens’ depths.
Purple mushrooms cloak the vermilion cliffs,
Four steps planted with fragrant *linyu* [citrus].
Swirling mists of divine splendor drift,
Gleaming radiance fills the halls of gods.
Beyond the Seven Awakenings’ bounds,
I freely grasp the mysteries of Heaven’s birth.
Sufficient lies within my heart’s measure—
Why seek the white dragon’s glory?"*

Last, Ding Yùwéi Ning composed his poem.
Its words were:
*"The Black Mountain frames the Stygian waves,
The golden halls reflect the Ling Chamber’s light.
The Duke of Luo upholds his singular virtue—
Graceful ease in all he does.
His form rests in the northern cold,
Three divine ones dwell in the Nine Heavens.
Together we lodge, then journey forth,
Praising your years of highest attainment.
Though I was but youthful in my twenties,
I did not yet revere the worthy.
Mocking the Master of Penglai,
I called him ‘Broad Rest’ before.
Now, Master, what shall you do?
To dismiss this youth’s brushstrokes?
The four finished their poems,
and each presented his to the old man.
After reading them, he laughed and said,
‘Each poem reveals their unique talent and nature.
Shi Qing’an has an unconventional brilliance yet moves lightly.
Zhang Youshi’s verse is harmonious but hard to fathom.
Xu Yufu is cautious and full of doubt.
Ding Yùwéi Ning is naturally withdrawn but slow to express himself.
To move lightly is to lack depth.
To be hard to fathom is to not grasp the Dao.
To doubt excessively is to think without divine insight.
To express oneself slowly is to miss the soul’s brilliance—
These are the paths of difference.
If each could return from their confusion,
And awaken to what they truly know,
They would not fall short.
The Master’s disciples—when they request to discuss doubts,
Doubt is what blinds them from divine insight.’

After the poems concluded,
each took up their staff and descended the mountain.
As they descended...

The Master then saw this woman riding a white dragon depart to the north.
I and the others walked southward.
Upon reaching the mountain’s foot, each parted ways.
The Master said, *"In twenty years’ time,
we shall meet again at the Seven Arts Hall—
to wander this place once more."*
At these words, I suddenly awoke to understanding,
my brow beaded with sweat.
For the rest of the day, I could neither eat nor drink.

Halfway down the mountain,
I saw Xu the Clerk ascending.
We met between the cliffs.
The Master spoke to the Clerk, *"Why do you come so late?
I have set aside four pints of wine
at the place where I sat on the mountain.
Go drink it, then return to follow me."*
The Clerk immediately went up the mountain.
In a short while, he returned,
moving swiftly.
Before he reached the foot, the Master met him again.
The Master said, *"Did you not drink the fine wine?"*
The Clerk replied, *"I still regret its sourness."*
The Master said, *"This is wine from the House of Great Peace—
it soothes the human intestines."*
The Clerk exclaimed, *"If one seeks longevity,
why should they drink the House of Great Peace?
What sourness could there be?"*
Thus, he is but a rustic child.
Hold fast to your single resolve,
take no heed of trifles.
Later, I shall rely on you
to assist and shelter me.*

The Clerk and I then went east together.
The Master returned to the mountain.
The other three departed westward fifty paces.
The Master sent a messenger to inform me:
*"Xu the Clerk will serve as my toothpick."*
I replied, *"I have no objection."*

To the tenth night.
I first composed a memorial on this dream.
The Master reported it to the sages.
Upon receiving this strange dream,
they were as clear as if they had not slept.
They did not discern what wish it might convey.

Only **Zǐwēi** Lady replied:
*"Your true nature is inwardly stirred—
spirit seeks far and wide,
divinity descends from the Eight Mysteries,
your form soars with your soul.
This is indeed the omen of supreme attainment.
The matter reveals the hidden depths—
not a vain fabrication.
As the **Luó** Master spoke,
you may discreetly inform **Yùfǔzi** and the others.
Do not widely proclaim the sacred intent within.
This is no small matter.
Take great care.

The other sages laughed.
**Qīnglíng** said:
*"It is through the **míng** of the **míng** that you perceive this.
Your heart resonates with piercing clarity—
the power of **Nányuè** Lord."*

(The matter of this dream was later written down in the eastern region.
Since I did not see the original myself,
I do not know who composed it.
The place mentioned is where **Yáng** Lord was to write this and present it to the **Chángshǐ**.
Thus, it is said to be *mine*.
This was in the fourth month, possibly the year **Yǐchǒu**,
or it could also be the year **Yín**.)

On the twenty-third night of the tenth month.

The dream took place on a vast mountain.
Someone appeared and told me,
*"This is the Great Cave of Mount Móng.
The chamber is square, with seats facing each other.
All are made of cypress beds with dragon-scaled mats.
