The walls are inscribed with countless texts, yet they cannot be deciphered.
The Chángshǐ is dressed in a coarse hemp robe and white undergarment.
He sits facing west, his back to the east.
There are three others, all in embroidered robes with flat caps.
One of them declares:
‘I am the father of Zhào Shǔtái—
you once saw me in Wù.’
(As recorded in the *Dìnglù*: ‘In the past, Zhào Shǔtái, known as Wáng Shìqīng, was said to be deeply learned.
Yet he never understood human intentions,
and was summoned to serve the Northern Míng Lord’s court.
This seems to be his son,
but I do not know which era he belongs to.’)
I sit facing south, my back to the north.
The Chángshǐ kneels before me.
Taking up the brush, I begin to write,
then pause in deep contemplation.
After a long while, I finish.
He immediately asks:
‘May this writing be passed on?’
I reply:
*"The Dao of the sun and moon—
with reverence, I bow twice.
Now, I offer a fine painting, a wine cup, and a tray of food from the south.
Next year’s sixth month,
you may enter the city."*

The matter of dispatching—
how excellent, how excellent!
The appointed time of water and fire
seeks me on the day of the great tree.

*(Shèng is still the sound of Chéng.
In the Han, there was also a man named this.)*

There are those who study yet fail to grasp it—
never has there been one who attained it without study.
Truly, this saying is true!

The Chángshǐ then wrote it upon green paper
and showed it to me.
*(Within the text, it says:
‘This writing is made
to carve one’s name.’)*

Dēng Nán asked: ‘What is meant by *guō*?’
The Chángshǐ replied: ‘It is like a *guō* within the cave—
not the *guō* of a tomb.’

Again he asked: ‘Why *qián*?’
The reply came: ‘*Qián* is the beginning of reverence.
Below, there are characters—
the written form of reverence.’

Again he asked: ‘Why *shèng*?’
The answer was: ‘*Shèng* is the servant beneath the sun—
the sun completes, and the moon attains.’

The three men in brocade robes together exclaimed:
‘Fortunate! Fortunate!
Study cannot deceive the coming and going of the Dao’s appointed time.’

*(This entire passage is from what Yang Zì recorded in a dream—
unknown which year it was.
It speaks of entering the *guō* in the sixth month,
but the omen remains unmeasured.
The upper half of this line was clipped away—
it should be the naming of one’s own name.)*

Master Xù, in days past, concealed his brilliance and withdrew from the world,
his light hidden in the ninth heaven.
In the dark, he observed the divine—
his radiance illuminated the spirit’s hall.

Fortunate! Fortunate!
In days gone by, our intimate exchanges,
our entwined oaths,
transcended the crowd—
a lone awakener, forging his own path beyond.

Master was then thirty years old when Great Yang was thirty.
When Master first entered Dongshan, Yang was but sixteen.
When he vanished from the world, Yang was nineteen—
thus did Yang’s devotion to the Dao begin so young.

Since parting from the radiant dust of the world,
today I gaze eastward across the Milky Way,
tears falling before words.
I imagine the Mysterious Palace in harmonious fusion,
its auspicious governance now complete.
At this moment, riding the Eight Winds,
it sweeps away all dross and obscurity.
The Six Heavens exert their might,
dispelling demonic *qi*.
May the true and authentic faith
spread through the Three Epochs.
The boundless sentiment of the Void
transforms endlessly.

Before your departure, you often expressed concern,
thinking of dreams you had seen.
You entrusted me with your handwritten traces as proof.
Since then, I have felt the hidden communion—
after receiving the inscription,
time seemed to vanish in an instant,
as if a thousand years had passed,
yet I was able to obtain it.
How strange and inexplicable!
Truly, how subtle and wondrous!
Truly, how subtle and wondrous!

This is what I recently dreamed, as recorded in my sparse notes.
I now reveal it in secret.
*(This was written after Master was tested—
Yang sent it to him in reply.
On fine hemp paper, exquisitely preserved—
this is the original manuscript, hence its survival.)*

Xī respectfully bows, bows again.
In the cold darkness,
I humbly inform you:
Your body remains in harmony, bringing comfort.
I have yet to pay my respects in person.
Respectfully, I write no more.
Yang Xī respectfully bows, bows again.
Xī reports:
Your third daughter came yesterday to offer a *jìngsài* rite.
Today, she is slightly better,
though still not fully recovered.

The multitude’s mood is turbulent and restless.
Their movements and stillness alike bring news of distress.
The recent plague and pestilence are most dreadful,
Yet still they do not cease.
This only adds to my profound anxiety.

To the *Giving Officer* Xu, Lord of the Prefectural Household, *(these six characters written on the back of a folded paper)*
Xī respectfully reports:
The two minor official matters were recently settled due to the dismissal of the Chief Clerk, who was transferred to Zheng’s Western Chamber.
Zheng’s Western Chamber also happened to be where he was stationed.
But the affairs were not yet fully completed.
Once finished, they would be submitted to the superior.
I now consider this a matter of personal sentiment.
This passage was composed for the descendants of Chen Zouwang and Rong Yi—
Indeed, it was a well-crafted work.

Respectfully, *(this letter lost its upper sheet)*
Xī bows, bows again.
I humbly inform you:
Your body remains in harmony, bringing comfort.
Last night, a messenger from the Liu family departed.
Their concern seems like that of the bereaved in mourning.
Tomorrow morning, Xī will accompany the Chief Clerk to the tomb to pay respects.
Perhaps later I may visit again.
Yang Xī bows, bows again.

The other day, I also received a carriage inquiry.
I suspect it was not properly examined.
At least, it was sent with *heartfelt* urgency.
This is not the doing of one who delves into the Dao’s profound treasures,
But rather the natural course of things.
To the *Longshì* Xu, Lord of the Prefectural Household, *(these six characters written on the back of the paper as well)*

Xī respectfully reports:
I received silk fabric,
Which I will use to make summer clothing for my aged mother.
Truly, this is a gracious favor akin to the Western Marquis’s care for the elderly.
Yet Xī’s reception of kindness is excessive and beyond measure.
The honor flows far beyond what I deserve.
I can only bear the debt of gratitude,
With nothing to offer in return.
As for the gift of silk and cloth,
It is not what I should accept.
Even a modest offering of filial piety
Would suffice for my own needs.
Respectfully, I return it.
I hope you will deeply understand.

Xī respectfully reports.
Xī respectfully reports.
This place was once pure and serene,
Now free of dust and filth.
Moreover, the residence of the young clerk is fine.
But Xī will soon depart,
Unable to stay long and share company.

I will report again later.
Xī respectfully reports.
*(These two entries were written on the same sheet, and it seems the upper sheet was lost.)*

Xī bows, bows again.
In the cold hours of the night,
I humbly inform you:
Your body remains in harmony, bringing comfort.
This is my final report after returning from the visit.
Yang Xī bows, bows again.
Xī respectfully reports.

"Strange affairs in the wilderness.
The *Xīyì* letter was answered separately.
I paid respects on the 2nd day of the 2nd month.
Respectfully reported."

Xī bows, bows again.
Today’s report again fails to dispel the *wind-dryness*.
I humbly inform you:
Your body remains in harmony, bringing comfort.

I do not presume to elaborate further.
Yang Xī bows, bows again.
Xī respectfully reports.

The *Cloud Zhi* Dao is not to be entrusted with this letter to be delivered.
Xī will personally attend to it.
Respectfully reported.
To the Honorable Chief Administrator Xu, Sir.

*(Attendant reports: These nine characters are inscribed on the back of a folded sheet. The letter from the Master and Yang Xī: The upper sheet bears repeated bows, while the lower sheet includes the single petition and the respectful report. Moreover, the self-reference to "Your body" is used, which may be explained by the irregularity of the ritual. It is neither the intent of a subordinate nor the proper conduct of a teacher and disciple. This is simply the expression of deference to one's superiors in age or rank, and the attendant's title reflects their duty. It is not a fixed rule that one must always adhere to—it should be adapted according to circumstances and cannot be strictly taken as [standard] practice.)*

Xī bows, bows again.
On a fortunate day, my wishes are fulfilled.
Though I have not yet paid my respects, I am deeply moved.
I humbly inform you:
Your body remains in harmony, bringing comfort.
Xī has now finished burning incense.
You should temporarily return home to rest in stillness.
In the evening, I will personally open [the letter].
Respectfully reported, without further elaboration.
Yang Xī bows, bows again.
Xī respectfully reports.

There has been no recent inquiry about the wilderness.
Yet the new wife of Hua is indeed well.
I still hang my heart on the 2nd day of the 2nd month for paying respects.
Xī respectfully reports.

Yesterday, I already dispatched Chen [with provisions] for food and drink.

Long History Master, Lord Xu:

*(These six characters inscribed on the folded paper’s reverse—
to be answered while in the mountain retreat. The fifth day of the tenth month.)*

Xī respectfully reports:
The official reply has been completed, yet I forgot to send it.
Just as I was about to dispatch a messenger,
I received word and now seal it for delivery.
A proper copy will also be transcribed and submitted separately.

I am uncertain whether the prescribed *chóngwán* (虫丸) remedy has been taken as directed.
If it remains unfinished, should the course begin anew upon the coming year?
As for what I previously obtained,
I divided and took it daily as usual—no irregularities were observed.
Only at the outset, for six or seven days,
I felt a warmth in the head and a surging heat in the abdomen.
Beyond that, nothing else.
Perhaps the effects are gradually taking hold.
Respectfully reported.

Xī respectfully reports:
The Chief Clerk, the virtuous scholar-official,

must surely feel deep longing.

my yearning beyond words.
The household affairs are, for now, manageable.

Xī respectfully reports:

Xī respectfully reports.

Xī respectfully reports.

It is a matter of several days.

Xī respectfully reports.

Xī respectfully reports.

I am truly moved.

Xī respectfully reports.

Prefect Xu, Lord of the Allowance (长史许府君侯).

This letter of nine characters (written on folded paper, backside):
Xī respectfully reports.

Master Jì excels in profound learning,

The collection is now roughly complete.
It should soon be ready for viewing.

The collection should ideally be completed for seventy-two individuals.

Xī respectfully reports.

Kǒng Ānguó compiled the seventy-two disciples of Confucius.

I report.

Thus, I no longer speak of them.

The appointed time for this matter shall indeed be the *jiashen* year.

All the grievances—
My pen cannot fully express them.

*(Since the minor official departed,*

Since the minor official’s passing,
*(cough)*[omitted]—
not a month passes without dreams.
I have dreamed of him ten times.
Even in sleep,
his form appears with grievances.
His words and deeds
are as if he were still alive.
Yet, since I no longer believe,
near and far mock me.
I dare not speak of it again.

Now, I fully recount the dream,
merely to inform you again.
I hope you will not take offense.
If you believe this to be mere delusion,

*(This letter has no title.
It was also sealed in an envelope.
The minor official would often come in person to record it with me,
using sleep and dreams as a pretext.
At the time, among the noble guests,
some heard that Yang could commune with spirits.
Believers often sought his answers,
while skeptics ridiculed him.)*

Thus, this account is recorded in *Zhenyue* Scroll Seventeen, *Zhenyue* Scroll Eighteen, and *Wozhenfu* Chapter Two. On the nineteenth night of the third month, I dreamed that a junior clerk came and sat quietly here. After a long while, he spoke of the Three Meetings at the waters of Xiaomao Mountain—how splendid the performances were. He said he had come from the Four Flat Mountains, and along the way, he saw his uncle holding a torch in his hand, intending to create mischief. He told me, *"Master, you should instruct Alang: such ghostly flames make people’s mouths shut tight, unable to speak. This thing transforms into wind. Do you know this?"*

The junior clerk further said, *"The grass on Fang Mountain is truly excellent—unusually fine. Its leaves are vast. I took much of it yesterday, but recently lost the cloth wrapping. I wish to beg this cloth from you, Master."* (The junior clerk and his younger brother both died early. I do not know who this might be. Fang Mountain is the Four Flat Mountain, where the leisurely retreat of Fangyuan was once shared with Long Bohao and others. After gathering the southern torchgrass, he also begged for the cloth wrapping—those in the caves still require clothing and food, hence the saying.)

Du Guangping likewise cut firewood to trade for grain. How much more so the scholars dwelling above the caves? Truly, they would not fail to know the practice of fasting to abstain from grains—it is simply that they ought not to do so.

The junior clerk further said, *"The burial site is now unauspicious. Cutting off the tomb’s *mai* brings many ill omens."* (This was dreamed on the nineteenth evening. Thus, what was recorded earlier in the text—what was written in white—was as follows: the clerk also returned to bury his remains in the old tomb, though it was an empty grave, yet still requiring a propitious site.)

This is the end of the letter to the *changshi* (Master of Records). What remains of the true handwritten text concludes here. (One line is missing before this, and the two preceding characters are also lost.)

My heartfelt sentiments cannot be fully expressed. Upon reflection, I received word and roughly agreed. I soon went to visit, and our reunion is near. Recently, I have been in a state of anxious anticipation—truly beyond words.

The *jishì* (Assistant Minister) An He (who is the *changshi*) will depart from Nanzhou on the nineteenth day and return on the twenty-second. The *gongcao* (Functionary) has already entered the capital. Yesterday, we met and comforted each other’s hearts. (The *gongcao* is the clerk’s elder brother, whose childhood name was Kui.)

At that moment, my mind was wandering—my return had not yet been completed. Shall I invoke the *yin* virtue of the zither strings? Let me offer a lighthearted smile instead. (The zither strings matter is from the *Peng Su Jing*—a text on the *fangzhong* arts.)

This is all for today. Tomorrow, the *gong* (my lord) will again depart for Nanzhou to bid farewell to the *dà sīmǎ* (Grand Marshal). The *dà sīmǎ* will set out on the twenty-sixth. The seventh seems unlikely to join the campaign. (The *gong* is Simwen, serving as *sītú* (Grand Tutor). The *dà sīmǎ* is Huan Wen, stationed in Juxu, preparing to launch a northern expedition against the Murong. The seventh is likely the clerk’s younger brother, whose childhood name was Sìbó, then serving as *shàngshū láng* (Secretary of State). This was in the third month of the fourth year of *Tàihé*—the year *jǐsì*.)

「Thus he sends me far away with the rice.
Is it to prepare for the *dòngzhāi* (Great Cavern Ritual)?
If the Master were to refine the *xiánjìng* (Mystic Mirror) in distant lands,
to purify the voice and ascend the lofty tones,
dwelling in the depths of the forest,
far from worldly affairs,
then this matter would never come to pass.
But now I hear it—truly, the *dharma* is complete.

（The *dòngzhāi* refers to the *Dà Dòngzhāi Fǎ*—the Great Cavern Ritual. The *xiánjìng* is likely a metaphor for the *Tàihé* or *Tàihé* Ritual texts, which the clerk now possesses in full as the *shàngpǐn qījuàn* (Seven Volumes of the Supreme Grade).）

I believe my writing is finished.
Let me polish it further.
By autumn and winter,
I shall again reveal the *sūtra*.
（Unknown which *sūtra* this refers to. Next year, the clerk will depart into seclusion.）

Hence, I do not take the *shíxùn* (Stone-and-Rice).
When spring grass grows,
this matter is easy to find.
I imagine often wandering at the *xiánshuǐ* (Mystic Waters).
This month, I must go.
Speak of our brief reunion.
（The *Nánzhú* [Southern Incense] is not carved in winter,
but its flavor and hue are finest in spring.
Since it is called *shíxùn*,
it must be the clerk’s method of combining stone and rice.
As recorded: “The next dose is a meal of rice,
with grains unobstructed—
just one prescription for a thousand years of life.
The method differs, that’s all.”
Thus, I still don’t know where the *xiánshuǐ* is.）

I also do not trouble the *Lǐ* (Lee) family.
The *Lǐ* are ill and cannot attend to matters.
I’ve already burdened *Yì* (Yi) with labor.
The *shàngshū* (Secretary) has ordered me—
if it’s like this, there will surely be an autumnal gathering.
This is truly trifling—
not worth your slightest concern.
But the *Gěishì* (Palace Attendant) says:
Upon returning to *Nánzhōu* (Southern Prefecture),
I must also hasten.
By mid-April,
I wish to reach *Dōngshān* (Eastern Mountain).
I hope there will be no mistake.

（The clerk later notes:
*"Chá yī bù"* (Tea, one scroll)—
though the meaning isn’t clear, it’s likely a gift for the clerk.
*Chá* here refers to *míngchá* (green tea),
which the clerk needs for his *dàn yǐn* (dietary regimen of abstinence),
and also to aid in light sleep.）

A day without seeing you,
I constantly fear my vulgar and stingy heart has already arisen.
Could you still emerge from the dust of *fēngchén* (worldly affairs)
with such carefree ease?

（The following eight entries are from *Yángshū* (Yang’s Records),
all concerning the Master’s return to the mountains and the clerk’s affairs.
The original paper is lost—
these are letters between teacher and disciple,
clearly framed as Master and servant,
written in ancient style.）

I follow the *Gěishì*’s manner and spirit as before,
yet he is still so deferential.
In my haste to leave the *shìchéng* (Palace Secretariat),
I tremble with anxiety.
Burning incense and entering stillness,
I compose a nocturnal petition to lay bare my concerns,
so that the full *dànkǔ* (Elixir of Great Bitterness) may be revealed—
its principles distinct in motion and repose.
I await further inquiry.
（This is a brief note,
likely written from the county.
It concerns the illness of the *Hǔyǎ Dàoshì* (Tiger’s Claw Commandant).）

The *Gěishì*’s letters before and after,
I present to the *Shénmǔ* (Divine Mother).
Through them, I address the *xiǎoyuàn* (Junior Clerk).
I also submit my replies to the *Shénmǔ* before and after.
The *xiǎoyuàn* intercepted the *Gěishì*’s letters,
leaving only this one to be returned.
（This also pertains to the *Hǔyǎ*—
events after the clerk’s departure.
The *Shénmǔ* is likely *Nánzhēn Fùrén* (Southern True Lady).
The following are Yang’s external letters and his own recorded dreams,
along with his replies to the Commandant and other clerks,
as well as preserved notes.
There are also separate records of exchanges with the *Zhēnrén* (True Person),
already mentioned in earlier volumes.）

The *Dàoshì*’s letter states:
*"In the year of *Yǒu* (the Ox), I studied.
In the year of *Xū* (the Dog), I shall return.
In the year of *Xū*, the Dao will traverse the world.
It says that from the year of *Xū*, I shall receive the law."*
（This entry is from the clerk’s later records.
He notes that in the year *gēngwǔ* (Regnal Year *Gēngwǔ*),
he departed—
this *Xū* (Dog) refers to the years *guǐyǒu* (Year of the Ox) and *jiǎxū* (Year of the Dog),
when he received the *fǎ* (law)—
that is, the *Dàoshì*’s teachings on the *sūtra*’s two *bèn* (leaps/phases),
and his attainment of *Zhēn* (Perfection) in the eleventh year.
Thus, the records say:
*"After sixteen years, I shall see you at *Dōnghuá* (Eastern Flower)."*）

The *sūtra* says:
*"The Master of all *guān* (passages) begins with the *jìng* (Mirror) of clarity."*

（This is from the *Huángtíng Jīng*—the Yellow Court Classic.）

The dates are as follows:
September 17th—190 days passed.
October 21st—200 days passed.
October 26th, November 1st, 8th, 11th, 15th, 17th, 20th, 23rd, 30th.
November 2nd, 6th.
（This should be November 26th.）
November 13th, 20th, 21st.

Four hundred days passed.
December 25th.
January 13th.
January 26th.
February—four hundred days.
The second day, thirty days passed.
Four hundred forty days passed.
All the above are marked in red ink and written in red.

From the twenty-sixth night of the ninth month onward, *(all the fine annotations between this and the previous section are in the original hand.)*
Since another sheet was added with two entries for the ninth month,
this marks the events of a single year.
*(Yet, there may be many missing entries that no longer exist.)*

The twenty-eighth day.
The twenty-seventh day of the ninth month—
thirteen nights passed.
*Chánggǔ* (Long Valley) *Huángníng* (Yellow Calm).

At midday in the tenth month,
the sun rose, the moon set, and light shone upon the *Yángmén* (Yang Gate).
On the sixth day of the eleventh month at dawn.
On the twenty-first and twenty-fourth days of the twelfth month.
All the above are marked in red ink and written in red.

This is the *Chángshǐ* (Long History) recounting his recitation of the *Huángtíng* (Yellow Court) Classic.

The passages marked with both red and black ink
were inscribed when, during practice, he encountered a pen—
thus, he recorded them on the spot.

The phrase *"Chánggǔ out-day"* and so on
are lines from the *sūtra*.
This must be where, while reading this passage,
he suddenly had an urgent matter arise,
so he noted it in the margins.

*Dàdòng* (Great Cavern) *Zhēn xuán* (True Mysterious).
*Zhāng liàn* (Zhang’s Refining) *sān hún* (Three Souls).
*(From the "Evil Dream Incantation")*

*Tàishàng gāojīng* (Supreme High-Purity).
*Sān dì* (Three Emperors) *dānlíng* (Dan-Essence).
*(From the "Good Dream Incantation"—these two entries originally belonged to the *sūtra* and should appear in the Great Elixir section. They have been excerpted here and also appear in the Fifth Chapter.)*

*Tàidū tiānlù* (Great Capital Celestial Records).
Manifested in the *Xuángōng* (Mysterious Palace).
*(From the "Purple Text" of the Immortal’s Physiognomy)*

*Zuǒ mù tóngzǐ* (Left-Eyed Boy).
*(From the "Five Spirit Classic")*

*Xiānzhě xīnxué* (The Immortal’s Heart-Study).
*(From the "Twenty-Four Spirit Classic")*

First, close the twenty-four breaths.
*(From the "Purple Text" of the *Xuánquè* (Mysterious Gap) Ritual)*

Practiced for eighteen years.
*(Also from the *Xuánquè* Ritual)*

To walk the Dao without knowing the spirit-names,
to return the essence without knowing how to ingest this talisman.
*(Also from the *Xuánquè* Ritual)*

The *pò* (corporeal soul) drinks only.
*(The "Human Return" Water-Moon-Essence.)*
*(From the "Purple Text" *Jūhún* (Summoning Souls) Incantation)*

*"I am the Heavenly Eye."*
*(From the *Fēibù* (Flying Steps) Classic Incantation)*

Three pecks of *tǐ* (teeth) *Tàiyuán* (Great Origin) *Shàng xuán* (Upper Mysterious).
*(From the "Tomb Dream Incantation"—now in the Third Chapter.)*

The *pò* listens only to drink.
Moon-yellow, sun-red.
*(From the "Purple Text" *Zhìpò* (Subduing the Corporeal Soul) Incantation)*

*Mùyù zhù* (Bathing Incantation).
*Tàishàng gāozhēn* (Supreme High-Truth).
*(From the "Nine Truths Classic" Transmission Incantation)*

*Zhìchóng wán* (Subduing Insect Pill).
*(From the *Sūjūn* (Master Su) Transmission)*

*Jìdào sīhé* (Seasoned Daoist Thoughts).

「(This seems to be a recollection of the names of the Two Master Teos—likely a mistake in writing *Dao* instead.)

*(The "Jade Slips, Green Records")*
*(The "High Gallery Carved Stone")*
*(From the *Kōngcháng* (Empty and Constant) Incantation)*
*Shí jīng yù mǎ* (Stone-precision, Jade-horse).
*(To discern the forms of ghosts.)*
*(Also from the *Kōngcháng* Incantation)*

Below *Bāoshān* (Embracing Mountain) lies a stone chamber with a silver door,
its dimensions a hundred *lǐ* (leagues) in span.
Below *Kūnlúnshān* (Kunlun Mountain) flows the Yellow Water,
named *Rìyuè shuǐ* (Sun-Moon Water).
*(Those who drink it attain immortality.)*
*(These two entries are of unknown origin; their accounts have not been verified.)*

*(Instruct Master Rì to transmit knowledge of the True Dao.)*
*(Instruct the Green Boy to transmit the attainment of the True Dao.)*
The *zhīzhēn* (Knowledge of the True) refers to knowing the True and thereby obtaining it;
The *chéngzhēn* (Attainment of the True) refers to diligent pursuit and thereby acquiring the True Dao.
*(From the *Xiāomó* (Dispelling Illusion) Sutra’s Preface—these are merely abbreviated records of their intent,
meant as reminders for oneself.)*

The **4th day of the 1st month,**
the **8th day of the 2nd month,**
the **11th day of the 3rd month,**
the **16th day of the 4th month,**
the **20th day of the 5th month,**
the **24th day of the 6th month,**
the **28th day of the 7th month,**
the **19th day of the 8th month,**
the **16th day of the 9th month,**
the **13th day of the 10th month,**
the **10th day of the 11th month,**
the **7th day of the 12th month.**
*(These are the days of the *Lǎozi Bái* (Laozi’s White) Ritual.)*
*(This appears to be an external technique of the *Tàiqīng* (Supreme Clarity) Dao—likely copied by the Grand Historian for personal use.)*

The **4th day of the 1st month, Gēngshēn**,
the **8th day of the 2nd month, Xīnyǒu**,
the **11th day of the 3rd month, Gēngxū**,
the **16th day of the 4th month, Guǐhài**,
the **20th day of the 5th month, Rénzǐ**,
the **24th day of the 6th month, Guǐchǒu**,
the **28th day of the 7th month, Jiǎyín**,
the **19th day of the 8th month, Yǐmào**,
the **16th day of the 9th month, Jiǎchén**,
the **13th day of the 10th month, Dīngsì**,
the **10th day of the 11th month, Bǐngwǔ**,
the **7th day of the 12th month, Dīngwèi**.
*(These are the days of the **Shàngdì Hài** (Supreme Deity’s Harmful) Ritual.)*
*(These days are all calculated according to the lunar stem-branch sequence, breaking through inauspicious days. One may infer their purpose similarly—though they may not be entirely authentic. Even though all affairs are deemed unpropitious, they are often days for customary practice, and one may still encounter auspiciousness. Thus, they should not be omitted.)*

As for the **quiet chamber (jìngshì)**:
1. **Thatched hut (máowū)**,
2. **Square room (fāngliù shì)**,
3. **Enclosed wall (huándòu)**.
The method for constructing the chamber:
Use **four pillars (sìzhù), three crossbeams (sāngé), and two main beams (èrliáng)**,
all of the same kind of material.
The chamber’s **east-west length (tóudōngxūshǒu)** is **one zhang nine chi (yīzhāngjiǔchǐ)**,
its **central width (zhōngcháng)** is **one zhang two chi (yīzhāngèrchǐ)**,
with **three chi (sānchǐ)** remaining at each end.
The **rear north eave (hòuliù)** extends **three chi five cùn (sānchǐwǔcùn)**,
while the **front south eave (qiánnánliù)** extends **three chi (sānchǐ)**.
The **ridgepole (dòng)** rises **nine chi six cùn (jiǔchǐliùcùn)** from the ground,
and the **side crossbeams (èrbiān gé)** are **seven chi two cùn (qīchǐèrcùn)** from the ground.
Open the door in the **southeast (dōngnán)**,
with a height of **six chi five cùn (liùchǐwǔcùn)** and width of **two chi four cùn (èrchǐsìcùn)**.
Use **solid materials (màomì)** for the door panels,
ensuring they are **dense and thick (màomì)**—
let there be **no gaps or cracks (wúshǐyǒuxì)**.

The **south-facing** door—**a single-leaf panel (piànhùyōng)**—
is called **"Passage of Light" (Tōngguāng)**.
Its length is **one chi seven cùn (yīchǐqīcùn)**,
its height **one chi five cùn (yīchǐwǔcùn)**.
When seated within the chamber, it must stand **level**.
A board is placed at the **center of the brow (méizhōng)**.
The bed rises **one chi two cùn (yīchǐèrcùn)** in height,
stretching **nine chi six cùn (jiǔchǐliùcùn)** in length,
and **six chi five cùn (liùchǐwǔcùn)** in width.
Mats are arranged according to the **seasons and months**,
turning with the **lunar phases (yuèjiàn)**.
The **walls are plastered thick—one chi (yīchǐ)**—
and smoothed meticulously.
This method suits **remote mountains, great marshes, or uninhabited wilds**,
unfitting for human dwellings.

Upon entering the chamber,
**each of the four seasons has its own method**.
Yet this is but **inherited from the Dao/Way’s tradition**—
a single affair of the **Daoist school**,
nothing extraordinary.

**Essentially, it is known thus:**
*"This, and so forth—*
*(The Daoist **static chamber method (jìngshìfǎ)** differs.
Perhaps it was passed down separately,
yet without careful verification.
Now, when visualizing entry into the chamber,
one may follow it as well.
Some say there should be a **sutra** as well.)"*

On the **fifteenth day of the first month**,
while **on duty in the Secretariat (Shàngshūshěng)**,
I prayed for dreams of extraordinary **numen (língxiān zhēnxìang)**—
many revealed blessings for my descendants.
On the **second day of the intercalary month at dusk**,
I again dreamed of **celestial assemblies (xiānlíng gònghuì)**,
and begged for auspicious responses.
I also saw **(a missing character) of extraordinary splendor**.
At the **midpoint of the month**,
suddenly, I encountered **Nine Elders (Jiǔlǎo xiānsheng)**,
who summoned me in **chariots (chēzhào)** and led me along.
We met with joy, and they said:
*"We have been in Xuānchéng for forty days and shall return."*
They inquired after my affairs and said:
*"We shall reach Wúhú by the twenty-third day,
then return to visit you.
We shall be delighted to see you again."*
*(This was when I was **Magistrate of Yùyáo**,
later serving as **Secretary of the Secretariat**.)*

On the **fourth day of the intercalary month at dusk**,
I dreamed of **colorful banners (cǎiwù)** in the shape of **flags (yángxíng)**,
all spread out upon the mats—
some like **paintings (huà)**, some like **embroidered brocade (jǐnxiù)**,
their **characters bright and radiant (wénzì huànbǐng)**,
as if **speaking yet unspeakable**.
The more they unfolded, the more wondrous they became.
They said it was **Yang’s household treasures (Yángshè wùwù)**.
Yet I did not see **Master Yang** at the time.
They urged me to **record them**,
saying: *"Only one skilled in **ordering figures (zhěngdùn)**—
something I have never seen—
should undertake this.
There will be about ten such banners."*

On the **fourth day of the intercalary tenth month of the [Great] Yonghe era**—
when **Master Yang** was twenty-three years old,
and **Emperor Xiànwén** had just become **Grand Tutor**,
though he had not yet been **Secretary of the Secretariat**—
*(perhaps this was the following year’s intercalary month as well)*

On the **twelfth day of the eleventh month**,
I dreamed of a **coffin and funeral implements** exposed with water.

On the **eighteenth day of the twelfth month**,
I dreamed of piercing **Xuánwǔ with an iron hairpin**.
*(This Xuánwǔ was likely the **Xuánwǔ of the tomb**—
not the tortoise and serpent I had kept.)*

On the **twenty-first day**,
I dreamed of seeing the **Son of Heaven**,
who appeared to be about sixteen or seventeen years old,
standing upon the **hall’s platform**.
*(This must have been during the reign of Emperor Kāng—
though I do not know which year.)*

Again, I dreamed of being in the same place,
where a **hanging mountain chest** released itself,
falling from a **precarious peak**.
From afar, I saw **Liú Shēng** conversing with someone.
From that point, I was to **return home**—
but the **road back was perilous**.
I obtained a **fine knot** and threaded it through a **handkerchief**.
I saw my **native city** offering support,
and thus I was able to **turn and retreat**.
*(The above six entries are all recorded by the Prefect himself from his dreams.)*

On the **ninth day of the tenth month**,
the **five officials of the kitchen** were ordered to **hunt in the southern mountains**.
*(This is also recorded by the Prefect himself—
where "ordered" should be read as **"pointed out"**.)

The decree pointed to the **oath of thunder** to pacify the residence and purify the dwelling.

**Yá** visited the **lady** (the empress).
The decree should use **double gold rings**.
You have none.
I shall prepare **exchange gifts** to repay the favor.
If **Hòu** has a **golden thread**, present it to the lady.
Say it is to repay the **officials and soldiers**.
When **Huá Gōngcāo** arrived,
he secretly whispered to the **newly wed** (omitting "newly wed" in the original),
*"Keep it secret, keep it secret!"*
If not, then present **two double gold rings** with a kneeling offering.
Do not hesitate, do not hesitate.
If you wish to obtain the treasures from the **imperial person’s** adornments, that would be best.
*(Huá Gōngcāo seems to be Huá Jiào.
Later, it mentions "Yang’s decree," which may be incorrect.
Hòu appears to be the **Tiger-Tooth Lady**.)*

Recently, I wrote to you in detail about the two **decrees** that should be issued.
That very night, there was an imperial edict stating:
*"We two officials and soldiers—
if the decree is not carried out,
our descendants will no longer serve as messengers."*

Within **Yang’s decree**, it states that a **hairpin** may be used.
The **younger lady** said that the hairpin is used to **guide and open pathways**—
it can certainly be used.
The newly wed has a **gold hairpin**,
which can be used as a substitute.
It can be stopped and threaded.
First, present it to the lady.
Then, present it to the **two spirits**.
You measure it accordingly.
You shall seek a **bell-shaped ornament** as per the earlier decree.

The newly wed may also use a **silver hairpin**.

If there is none,
then the **bell-shaped ornament** should be used.
I have stopped your letter awaiting the **decree**.
The **decree** should follow beneath it.
Do not make another separate one.

(This book touches upon prior matters.)
To obtain a **favorable and tranquil** state,
it says: *"We command you to study the **Inner Canon**.*
This is **preservation of life**.
You respond indifferently, **without reply**—
this is not proper.
Yet these are not ordinary teachings;
they all arise from **mysterious profundity**.
You should compose them as **root and branch**.
Your response should follow this intent.
If you speak nothing aloud,
it is **unacceptable**.
(The **Inner Canon** perhaps refers to the *Huangting Jing*—
or else it should be the **natural laws** within the nuptial chamber.)

Tao Xiu gave you **two hundred sheets**—
I retain **one hundred**.
(Checking the *Tao* register,
the **Chief Clerk’s wife’s** relatives
do not appear with the name *Xiu*.)

The **white rice** for the **axe** has already been prepared.
You shall **provide it**.
(This is for **dyeing** to make it **blue** {*to eat* **Zhen**}.)

*Yi* warned: *"You should be slightly less than perfect.*
**Guard against this**."
(*Yi* is **Changing the Chief Clerk’s wife**—
these seven items
are all from the **Chief Clerk’s and the Tiger-Tooth Lady’s** book.)

Right, the **Chief Clerk** Xu in his lifetime copied and recorded matters from the *Memoirs of Affairs* and dreams.
All that which was also recorded in the books given to his sons is concluded here.
As for what was recorded in the *True Spirit Canon*,
it has already been separately listed in the earlier volumes.
The **Master** himself entrusted his spirit,
casting his form into the **Eastern Grove of Shadows**.
He bathed in the **Leisurely Hill**,
yet strayed from my shared heart.
Each time he gazed east toward the **Azure Sea**,
he sighed at the swift passage of time.
When he turned west toward the **Cloudy Peaks**,
his sorrow welled from within.
He longed for his homeland,
but its barriers loomed ever higher.
He intended to return his body to the **Muddy Path**,
yet only twisted his feet and stilled his ears.

Then, with a tranquil mind and single thought,
he leaned upon the **Spirit’s Void**,
ascending rugged cliffs.
He raised his head to behold the **Mysterious**,
sealing his consciousness behind the **Threshold of Darkness**.
He wandered among the clouds,
and his **True Form** stood erect—
only then did he realize his body was not his own.
Thus, he abandoned his **corporeal shell** and returned to his **spirit**,
his heart free as the **wild winds**,
his path through the world forever severed.
He found joy in the **secluded groves**,
and all external hardships were blocked away.
With unwavering resolve,
his **sincere essence** was never wasted.
He then encountered a **brilliant teacher**,
received **marvelous techniques**,
and heard **pure lectures** of profound mysteries.
His **jade voice** revealed **cavernous secrets**.
Through **breath regulation**, his countenance became serene,
his **soul refined** and his **bones preserved**.
His **qi** surged and was smoothed away—
no longer was there any distinction between inner and outer.
(This is the method of **elixir ingestion** taught by Wang Shilong and others.)

Yet I regret that I met my **good teacher** too late.
My **nature** was hindered, and my **progress** was not early.
The **difficulties** of attaining the **Dao**,
the **arduous trials**—
these were already recorded by **True Lord** Dinglu,
and I have set them down as well.
The **venerable** and the **reliant**,
the **covering** and the **rescuing**—
their **virtue/power** cannot be altered by heaven and earth.
(It should read *yú* instead of *yù*. This is the **ordeal** spoken of by Dinglu.)

I hear that my younger brother has journeyed far to attain the **upper laws**,
(referring to the **Dao of Supreme Clarity**)
encountering the **True** and the **profound**,
(meaning the **Cloud Forest Descent**)
his mind observing the **Spirit’s Origin**,
(referring to the **Xie Method**)
and **refining the Five Spirits**.
He ascended the **Seven Gates’ towering grandeur**,
(meaning the **Flying Heaven** technique)
and savored the **Three Celestial Bodies** to accumulate **transformation**.
(referring to the **Sun, Moon, and Five Stars**)
His **spirit** fell void beyond the **clouds**,
and his **essence** lingered in the **Nine Abysses**.
This **Dao** is lofty,
(its **advancement** is sublime).
Not something my disciples could ever hope to hear.
It is also because my **foundation** is shallow,
and I have not yet reached it.

But those who pursue the **highest practices** must always beware of **peril**.
Those who find **delight** in the **Dao** often risk losing it.
The **clarity** of **fortune and misfortune** is applied here.
The **Dao** is close to those who **strive diligently**,
and the **spirit** returns to those who **cultivate sincerity**.
A **red heart** awaits the **True**—
if one harbors **a hundred distractions**,
narrowing one’s focus on the **Dao**,
even if one exerts oneself for a **hundred years**,
there will still be no hope.

The **Three Officials** face **urgent trials**—
I have spoken of this before.
(In the **heavy discourse** on dispelling and overcoming **ordeals**)
This was already explained to you earlier.

The **Seven Tests’ blessings** have been spread to posterity.
(As for **your brother’s merits**,
they will extend to the **Chief Clerk’s father and son**.)
The **fate’s course** is swift—
how could it not be?
Nearly completed yet ruined,
self-made and self-destroyed—
all these **thousand trials** arise from **evil influences**.

Think of the **good**—
how much **bitter effort** and **labor** to refine one’s **form**,
to **cultivate merits**—
yet all things drift like clouds.
What use is it?

The axe’s *shuāoshuāo*—
how enviable its fate!
Each fails to awaken—
yet must meet this trial.
Who but the axe alone
enjoys such lofty heights?
*(Sighing at its singular transcendence)*

The bonds of master and friend—
gain and loss, the objects of reverence—
leaning on shadows to seek the true,
all rests in this act.

I now dwell in spirit’s mountain retreat,
shading my form in deep forests,
plucking the reclusive herbs of the valleys,
*(handcrafting)* the elixir grasses to attain the true.
*(Lofty words of attainment—
somewhat exaggerated for effect.)*

Earlier, when the Dao was perfected, I would return to deceive trust.
Though not yet fully illuminated,
roughly resembling the likeness,
I also wished to rest in the hollow wilds,
visiting ancestral mounds.
Unplanned, I went—
hoping to see my younger brother once more.
Circumstances were brief,
and as I wrote, my longing deepened.
*(From my ancestor’s given name, Yìng,
I changed it to Yuǎnyóu—
this ten-character inscription honors my brother.)*

*(Note: The following entry—
After the Master faced the trials,
he served as Chief Clerk.
At that time, he was already in Gàizhú Mountain.
The record states:
In the Year of the Dragon, he would temporarily return to deceive the people’s trust.
Thus, this must be the correspondence from the years of the 3rd Heavenly Stem—Bing-Yin or Ding-Mao.)*

Serving as a minor official in the first year of the **Taihe** era (827 CE), I wore the **Six Jia Talismans** *(a method of the Lingfēi Six Jia School—
separate scriptures exist for this.)*
In the second year of **Taihe** (828 CE).

The **Yearly Star** was in **Ding-Mao**.
In the first month, I practiced the **Returning to the Origin Dao** *(this is the Method of Atonement; there is a separate scripture for it)*.

In the second month of **Taihe** (828 CE),
I performed the **Empty Constant** *(this is a distinct Flying Step method)*.

In the fourth month,
I wore the **Green Fang** *(this is the Green Fang’s nascent method; the world has yet to see its scripture)*.

In the seventh month,
I practiced the **Sun and Moon in the Heart Mud Pill Dao** *(the following five entries were recorded together on one sheet of paper)*.

Preserving the **Sun and Moon in the Mud Pill method**—
in the sixth month of **Taihe** (828 CE), I performed it *(previously noted as the seventh month; there must be an error in the characters—this is the method of wearing the Sun and Moon Fang)*.

In the fifth month of **Taihe** (829 CE),
I performed the **Dashing Between Two Scenes Dao** *(this is the method of the *Yùlín* School; though there are copies, no grand scripture has been seen. Again, the following two entries were inscribed separately on another sheet of paper in red ink, not connected to the previous records)*.

On the third night of the second month... *(two characters lost).*

It seems to be called **"Dreaming of Lady Zheng Bai"**.

The **Dao/Way** of exchange has those who are intimate within yet distant without.
Zheng’s modest concerns—
this is precisely the case now.
I should come together with **Mountain-Zeng’s** aunt.

*(Zheng refers to the mother of Dengyi’s wife.
She consorted with Lady Yiqian, hence the dream of the clerk to forge a bond across the misty ford.
**Mountain-Zeng’s** aunt remains unclear—
the exchange in dreams should also be in the second month of the second year.)*

On the twenty-seventh day of the fourth month, at midnight,
I dreamed of a woman
wearing upper and lower layers of green silk robes.
She met with me and introduced herself, saying,
*"I am the younger sister of Wang Mi-Shou."*

When we met,
it seemed as though we were in a mountain forest.
She said, *"Tomorrow, I may briefly step out beyond the West Gate.
The oxcart with a white cow and a leather headwrap for the driver is mine."*

Later, we parted and met again at **Gui Jie**.
She spoke and composed a poem as if bidding farewell:
*"Crossing the **Shuilu** ford,
Gathering herbs atop the central peak.
Unveiling the heart’s luminous thoughts,
Ethereal and boundless—no words to grasp.
I wish to wander with one of noble virtue.
With matched steeds, we ride the threads of fate.
What use are the dusty honors?
Swiftly, I urge you toward pure clarity.
If you can seize the subtle vision,
Inhaling and exhaling will prolong life."*

*(Wang Mi-Shou’s younger sister—
the **Zhonghou Lady**.
Since the clerk has not yet encountered her in truth,
he uses the dream to convey his meaning.
Yet the line about "dusty honors" suggests
she may not be the clerk’s intended.)*

On the fifth day of the sixth moon in the first year of **Taihe**,
I suddenly heard celestial **golden bells and drumming** from above.
Then, looking upward, I saw **rainbow-like** **colorful clouds**,
their **qi** form bright and refreshing—
spreading across the sky, moving straight from east to west.
In my mind, I thought it was the **female spirit** passing by.
Some called her **Yuanjun**.
Suddenly, she appeared as if walking along a road,
and in a flash—**as if a spark**—she had gone.

The **jade ax** then chased after the **clerk**,
but only saw a **palace carriage**,
followed by a **red-robed attendant**.
All returned to pay respects.
Beside the road stood one man,
in white robes like a **fortune-teller**.
He spoke, saying:
*"Your body is frail and cannot endure toil.
You should focus solely on your **Dao/Way**.
Do not mix in other matters.
If you do not focus, you will fall ill.
You do not yet believe—
but you will surely dream of this.
This man claims his surname is **Zhuó**."*

Toward the end of the seventh moon,
the **jade ax** dreamed of his body soaring upward,
and with a sudden clarity—**as if a great opening**—
it entered beneath a house.
Stacked beds faced south, and he sat there,
saying: *"I am the **harmonizing light of the sun**,
the **Dao/Way** that reverses the five stars’ course."*
*(These words come from the **Sutras for Dispelling Illusions**,
the speech of the **Highest Sovereign**.)*

A man stood facing east, extending his hand to unroll a scroll.
The images revealed were like painted landscapes—
mountains and valleys in vivid detail.
Below the scroll were written words,
and he conversed with the man holding the scroll for quite some time.

On the third evening of the eighth month,
he dreamed suddenly of a man in tattered robes,
tall and gaunt—
following a small boy, as if **hustling wares** *(a peddler’s cry, meaning like one calling out to sell things)*.
He sat and spoke with the Jade Ax.
Then he spoke of **Highest Dao/Way** matters.
The Ax was startled, sat upright in alarm.
After a moment, as he was about to enter the inner gate,
he expounded on the **Highest Dao/Way**.
He looked back at the small boy:
*"Do not let anyone see me outside the drum."*

The Ax asked, *"Is it the **Celestial Hook Music**?*
*(referring to the lofty melodies of the **Upper Pure** realm)*
The man replied, *"No.
I have come only to take what can be taken.
You yourself will surely obtain the **Celestial Hook Music**."*

The Ax then asked how many were needed for the **Celestial Hook**.
The answer came:
*"Ten for one **Celestial Hook**.
The Great Law extends even to ten thousand."*

*(He did not know it reached as high as a hundred thousand.)*
Then he awoke again, drifting back into sleep.
Once more he dreamed of a scroll—
the Jade Ax’s book first unfurled,
but only the later sections revealed **Lord Wang’s** affairs,
like the **Four Ministers’ Transmission**.
All were gathered above,
discussing Lord Wang’s years of **Dao/Way** study—
the visions and dialogues of his **Dao/Way** practice.
*"It will take two or three years,"*
he thought, with great reverence.

This man had not yet paid his respects
when he awoke.
At last, he saw the **Chief Clerk** also seated among them.

On the thirtieth night of the eighth month, **Taihe** Year 1,
he dreamed of receiving a scroll with four small volumes.
It was said to be the **Divine Mother’s** book,
or perhaps a **Transmission**.
All were bound with fine blue silk,
with red ends.
The pages were blank.

At the time, the **Master** was also present.
He then inscribed the **Jade Ax’s Transmission**—its preface—
upon a white wall outside the door,
its characters resembling **talismans**,
or like beastly forms.
The scroll’s wrapping did not resemble ordinary silk.
*(Recorded with care.)*
*(The Master refers to **Lord Yang**.)*

On the twenty-fifth night of the third month, **Taihe** Year 3,
the Jade Ax dreamed he was walking in the heavens.
White clouds swirled and coiled,
low-hanging yet not high.
Gazing upward through the clouds,
he saw an empty space
shaped like a mountain cave.
Moving eastward several steps,
he perceived a great **Dao/Way** to the northeast.
Following it, he entered a deep chamber—
like a stone hall.
A radiance emerged from within,
or perhaps a surging watery mist,
crowning the Jade Ax’s body.
He sat up abruptly,
yet felt no fear.

The mist then suddenly dispersed.
Within the chamber, a bed and mat were seen—
its utensils and furnishings most orderly.
In his mind, he deemed it the dwelling of a **numinous being**.
He bowed and kowtowed before the chamber,
then begged and entreated.
Inside, there was a hole.
The Jade Ax advanced further through it,
but the passage was narrow and constricted—
he could not proceed.
Yet in his resolve to advance,
suddenly he saw a man outside the chamber,
who spoke to the Jade Ax: *"You must not enter yet.
You shall soon reach the front."*
He bowed again, saluted, and withdrew.
Then he was seen escorted to the Dao/Way,
urged to receive the scriptures.
His words were earnest and meticulous:
*"You must diligently preserve this."*
The **Southern True Lady** dispatched three attendants
to guide the Jade Ax across the **Board Bridge**.
At first, he emerged
and saw within an ox-cart two youthful figures,
their heads exposed,
laughing and conversing with the man.
Before reaching his dwelling,
he awoke—
delighted, yearning for enlightenment,
as if the dream had conveyed a message.
*(Recorded with care.)*
*(The following seven entries are all self-recorded dream accounts.
At that time, my humble thoughts—
what difference did they make from today’s recluse?)*

The **8th day of the 3rd month**, the Jade Ax presented a memorial.

The Jade Ax spoke:
*"The Zheng [family] has returned. By imperial command,
Your Reverence still suffers from lingering drink-induced pain.
I dare not approach—my body trembles in reverence.
I pray Your Reverence’s meals are now without ailment.
This very day, I have received Your favor.
The tooth [i.e., the tooth-ache] has reached this point—
I wish to depart westward and arrive early.
Respectfully, I submit this memorial.
The Jade Ax bows twice."*

The Jade Ax spoke:
*"Your Reverence seeks the *Liujia* talisman.
It seems to be at the *Yá* [official’s] residence in Jùróng.
The Ax has not yet received word from the mountain.
I pray to examine it from the *Yá*’s collection.
Respectfully submitted.
*(This *Liujia* talisman is not a *Língfēi* [divine flight] talisman—
it must be in the *Yòuxuán Lù* [Left Mysterious Register].)"*

The Jade Ax spoke:
*"I received the *Liúbēi* gathering three days ago.
Your Reverence also composed poetry.
I trust the letter will soon be returned.
Respectfully submitted.
The salt tea arrives soon.
I pray for a gift of betel nut—I must consume it daily.
Respectfully submitted.
*(One must always have tea and betel nut—
this is also to dispel phlegm and dampness.
Thus it is said one may bathe frequently,
to wash away the illness when free from labor.
This letter’s weight and size differ slightly.
In this age, calling one’s father ‘Your Reverence’ is proper—
ancient rites were often thus.)"*

The Fourteenth Day of the Fourth Month, respectfully submitted:
The Jade Ax spoke:
*"The heat is gradually increasing.
I do not know how Your Reverence’s body fares—whether at rest or in motion.
I pray for a gift of **gradual-awakening tea** to dispel it.
Far from home, I tremble with fear—
I urgently request permission to depart.
This very day, I have received Your Reverence’s favor.
Respectfully submitted with the letter.
The Jade Ax bows twice.
The Jade Ax spoke:
‘I have **betel nut**. I pray for a gift of it.
For now, I temporarily borrow Xu Shen’s services to escort me until my return.
Respectfully submitted.’*

The Fifteenth Day of the Fourth Month, respectfully submitted:
The Jade Ax spoke:
*"Yesterday, Xu Shen promised to escort me immediately.
The heat is gradually increasing.
I do not know whether Your Reverence’s body is now at ease or otherwise.
I pray for a gift of **gradual-awakening tea** to dispel it.
Far from home, I tremble with longing.
The **Yá** remains as usual.
At the appointed time, I was granted permission to depart.
The Jade Ax has crudely received Your Reverence’s favor.
Respectfully submitted with Feng Ling’s letter.
The Jade Ax bows twice.
*(Xu is the elder brother of the **Shù**.)"*

The Eighteenth Day of the Fourth Month, respectfully submitted:
The Jade Ax spoke:
*"The heat is oppressive and stifling.
I do not know how Your Reverence’s body fares—whether at rest or in motion.
I pray for a gift of **awakening tea** to dispel it.
Chen Hui has arrived.
Your Reverence should come to the Jade Ax’s **nearby retreat** today.
Only Your Reverence’s arrival matters—
the rest are difficult to see.
I pray for safe travels.
Little Hist is within the hall.
To prevent him from venturing far,
or being delayed by the floods.
Respectfully submitted with Chen Hui’s letter.
The Jade Ax bows twice.
*(This is also the **Míngzhēn** Retreat**—**
only to keep outsiders from being delayed by the floods.
Little Hist is likely his name,
yet he still sends the young attendant inside to serve.
The noble cannot personally attend to such trivial matters.)"*

The Jade Ax spoke:
*"Xu and the **Yá** have also been granted temporary permission to return here.
I pray for safe travel.
Respectfully submitted."*

April 23rd, the day of the bowing memorial:
The Jade Ax speaks: *"By imperial command, I arrived last night.
The heat and chill have been soothed.
May Your Reverence’s body, nourished by food, remain unharmed.
I have yet to attend upon you in person.
My longing and reverence are laid out in the morning’s memorial.
Teng’s memorial has already arrived.
Respectfully submitted with the memorial.
The Jade Ax bows twice."*

The Jade Ax speaks: *"Yang’s younger brother, the official’s sibling, lies gravely ill.
I am deeply concerned.
I trust his recovery will soon be favorable.
Respectfully submitted."
(Previously, Yang’s letter spoke of his aged mother;
now it speaks of his brother—
only these two matters are made clear.
The rest of his kin are unknown.)*

April 28th, the day of the bowing memorial:
The Jade Ax speaks: *"Yesterday, by imperial command,
the chill was soothed and the heat abated.
May Your Reverence’s body remain unharmed.
The awakening tea has been removed,
yet the lingering chill persists.
Respectfully submitted with the memorial.
The Jade Ax bows twice."*

The Jade Ax speaks: *"The money has been given to the landlord.
Here, there is no longer any need to secretly entrust more than two *sheng*.
The wife of Hua Xin wishes for a small portion.
May it be divided among them—
she is also of long standing here.
Respectfully submitted."
(The mountain folk are poor and perilous,
and their separation is indeed profound.
Hua Xin’s wife is the same as the Yá’s wife.)*

May 4th, the day of the bowing memorial:
The Jade Ax speaks: *"With the arrival of the season, my gratitude deepens.
The damp heat troubles me.
I do not know how Your Reverence’s body fares.
The tea has not yet been fully removed,
and the lingering chill still burns.
Though the powdered medicine allows for slight sips of water,
this is still beneficial.
May all—great and small—remain unharmed.
Your Reverence is to descend on the fifth.
May this be fulfilled.
Respectfully sent with Fu Nan’s memorial.
The Jade Ax bows twice."*

The Jade Ax speaks: *"Chen Lu has arrived.
Your Reverence’s gift of preserved meat and steamed scallions
has reached the imperial capital.
I have already returned the bundle—
it was most welcome.
(Four characters lost.)
Respectfully submitted."
(From the 23rd, there have been three memorials in total.
The Prefect seems to have been lingering in the household below.)*

The Jade Ax speaks: *"I have received the official’s dispatch.
I fear passing through Jùróng.
I have not yet reached this Zhàn household.
The grain is not yet ripe.
Now I send Zhū Shēng to assist.
May Your Reverence order him to return with grain.
I will make it into rice for the capital.
Here, there is nothing suitable for pounding.
If he arrives,
I will immediately combine it.
I fear the medicinal herbs will dry out—
only with rice on board can the herbs be gathered.
Respectfully submitted."*

The Jade Ax speaks: *"Here, the pot is too small—
it can hold only one *hú* of grain.
It does not suit the steamer.
Moreover, glutinous rice requires a pot.
There are large pots here that can hold two *hú* or more.
May they be sent together with the medicines.
I do not see any objections.
The borrowed rice will be repaid at the county seat.
(Approximately ten characters missing in this line.)
Respectfully submitted."
(These requests for rice and a large pot
are all for preparing meals.
The grain is not yet ripe—
this should be in the ninth month.
This memorial was written while the Prefect was in the capital.)*

Eight memorials in total.
The Clerk, in the mountains, wrote to his father.
At the time, the Prefect was in the capital and the county seat.
All these are records of the Clerk’s worldly affairs,
and the memorials that have been preserved.
Ends here.
(There is also a farewell to Zhēnlíng,
detailed in the previous section.)

*True Announcements*, Volume Eighteen.
*True Announcements*, Volume Nineteen.
*Winged True Examination*, First Section.
*True Announcements* Summary.
*True Announcements* Symbolic Theme, First Section.
(This volume establishes the farewell and intent,
unfolds the resonant meaning,
discusses the numinous responses and correspondences,
and their mutual resonance—
divided into four volumes.)

*True Announcements* Bestowal of Fate, Second Section.
(This volume elucidates guidance in practice,
admonishes negligence,
and clarifies the division of uprightness.
It illuminates the causes of fortune and misfortune.)

*True Announcements*, Coordination of Auspicious Periods, Third Section.
(This volume establishes the precepts for cultivation,
the regulations for attire and conduct,
and emphasizes practical application.
It reveals the Dao according to circumstances.)

*True Announcements*, Investigation of Divine Pivots, Fourth Section.
(This volume delineates the traversal of mountains and waters,
proclaims the hidden abodes of the numinous,
measures the ranks and deeds of true immortals,
and clarifies what is lacking in governance—
divided into four volumes.)

*True Announcements*, Elucidation of Profound Mysteries, Fifth Section.
(This volume expounds upon the palaces of ghosts and gods,
their officials, departments, and lineages,
and clarifies the unextinguished nature of forms,
leaving no trace of good or evil—
divided into two volumes.)

*True Announcements*, Grasping the True and Assisting, Sixth Section.
(This volume records the Three Sovereigns’ earthly deeds,
their own writings, and correspondence—
not following the *True Announcements* convention.
Divided into two volumes.)

*True Announcements*, Winged True Examination, Seventh Section.
(This volume marks the true lineage,
verifies the essence of the primal source,
and encompasses all accounts of seclusion—
not following the *True Announcements* convention.
Divided into two volumes.)

Thus concludes the *True Announcements* in one compendium.
(Of its sixteen volumes, twelve are the True Person’s decrees,
and four are worldly records.)

In reverence, I trace the Dao scriptures—
the *Upper Clarity* of the highest grade,
attaining the utmost true immortality.
The *Lotus Sutra* of the Buddha’s wondrous law,
comprehending the singular vehicle.
The *Zhuangzi*’s Inner Chapters of the Immortal’s writings,
fathoming the realm of boundless mystery.

These three Dao suffice to encompass all phenomena,
embodying both the subtle and manifest,
each comprising twenty volumes.

At this time, the *Yù Xuán Fāng* and *Yù Jǐ*—the seven celestial officials—
were aligned to harmonize the eight directions.

The *Hidden Residence’s* method for ascending to true transcendence,
also structured in seven precepts.

Now, in recounting this *True Announcement*,
it again forms seven days—
the five-sevens tally,
and all cosmic principles are complete.

The True Person’s intent
differs from worldly conventions.
With reverence, I follow the *Fǎnwéi* and *Hòu*—
selecting their thematic correspondences,
and framing them in triplets—
just as the *Zhuangzi* does,
for it reflects the subtle teachings of the *Chángsāng* Master.

Common scholars, observing this, fail to grasp its purpose.

The *True Announcement* is the True Person’s own decree—
as if the Buddhist scriptures all state, *"Thus did the Buddha speak."*
Yet *Gù Xuánpíng* mistakenly termed it *"true traces."*
It should be called the True Person’s handwritten decrees,
or the True Person’s recorded deeds.

If taken as handwritten, the True Person could not have written in clerical script.
If taken as recorded deeds, then these traces do not belong to the True Person.
Moreover, at the time of their inscription, the True Person had not yet attained transcendence.
Since this contradicts their essence, it is unfit as a designation.

The *Southern Peak Lady’s Transmission* records the *Qīng* [*bamboo slips*] text:
*"In the year *Jiǎzǐ*, on the first day of the month, the hour *Xīnhài*."*

The *Xian Nong* feast began at dawn.
In the year *Jiǎyín*, *Yǔshuǐ* [羽水]—the *Yǔ* [羽] being rain—
arose with the *An* [安] and the *Qǐ* [启] of the year.
The transmission then commenced.

The child of the *Dao*-attained one
should practice the *Yùwén* [玉文] script.
Carefully tracing the *Jìn* [晋] calendar,
the *Aìdì* [哀帝] *Xīngníng* [兴宁] year of the *Tàisuì* [太岁] *Jiǎzǐ* [甲子],
the first day of the month, *Xīnhài* [辛亥] at the new moon.
(Though the auspicious date could be used to honor *Xian Nong*)
On the fourth day, *Jiǎyín* [甲寅], *Yǔshuǐ* [羽水] descended.
(Within the first month, *Yǔ* [羽] means rain.)

*Qǐ* [起] is *Xīng* [兴], *An* [安] is *Níng* [宁].
Thus, the name is veiled in ambiguity.
Therefore, by the *Xīngníng* [兴宁] year’s first month,
the *Nán Zhēn* [南真] had already descended and granted the *Yáng Jūn* [杨君] the scriptures.

Now, examining the records of the *Zhēnshòu* [真授],
the earliest year and month are:
the *Yǐchǒu* [乙丑] year, sixth month, twenty-first day,
when the *Dìnglù* [定录] was granted the inquiry.
From this month and day onward,
there were gradual descents and transmissions.

Furthermore, the *Zhōngshǐ* [中侍] *Fūrén* [夫人] revealed:
*"Let the bamboo slips be planted beside the northern lodgings,
to bring forth successors."*
She also said:
*"The *Fúhé* [福和] will bear two sons,
with great virtue destined to rule the world."*

This was during the time when *Jiǎnwén* [简文] was the *Xiàngwáng* [相王],
and due to his lack of sons, he made the request.
Thus, *Lǐ Fūrén* [李夫人] bore the *Xiàowǔ* [孝武] and the *Guìjì Wáng* [会稽王].

When *Xiàowǔ* [孝武] passed away at age thirty-five,
it was in the *Rénxū* [壬戌] year,
two years before *Jiǎzǐ* [甲子].
Thus, the *Zhēnshòu* [众真] had long been descending upon *Yáng Jūn* [杨君].

Again, in the *Yǐchǒu* [乙丑] year, sixth month,
the *Dìnglù* [定录] likened the scriptures to *Yáng Jūn* [杨君],
saying: *"I once obtained the writings of the previous year,
the eighth day of the third month."*
This should also be the *Guǐhài Jiǎzǐ* [癸亥甲子] year.

Moreover, *Yìng Lǜ* [愕绿华] descended in the *Shēngpíng* [升平] year,
which was the *Jīwǔ* [己未] year,
five years before *Jiǎzǐ* [甲子].
Though this descent was not for *Yáng Jūn* [杨君],
*Yáng Jūn* [杨君] knew of it and recorded it.

In the *Yǐchǒu* [乙丑] year,
the *Ān Fēi* [安妃] told *Yáng Jūn* [杨君]:
*"In twenty-two years,
a worthy ruler will ascend to the clouds and ride the dragon.
The *Shàngqīng* [上清] of the Northern Court
should be marked by the *Tàiyuán* [太元] year, *Dīngxū* [丙戌]."*

Thus, over twenty years of recorded transmissions,
many matters were not fully accounted for.
What remains today is but a few years.
Upon examining the beginning and end,
not a single detail is omitted.

Before the *Zhēnshòu* [众真] descended upon *Yáng Jūn* [杨君],
they had already commanded *Huá Jiào* [华侨] to transmit the *Yīnyì* [音意].
*Huá Jiào* [华侨] having been slandered and dismissed,
the *Zhēnshòu* [真] thus ordered *Yáng Jūn* [杨君] to take charge.
*Huá Jiào*’s [华侨] writings and traces did not emerge in his time.

Though *Èr Xū* [二许] were of noble bearing and refined talent,
their nature was incompatible with the *Shìjì* [世迹].
Thus, they did not attain the *Zhēnshòu* [真授].
Most of what was transmitted
was established for *Èr Xū* [二许],
all decreed by *Yáng Jūn* [杨君].
The *Shū* [疏] were shown to *Èr Xū* [二许].
Only the *Ān Fēi*’s [安妃] few records were *Yáng Jūn*’s [杨君] own.

Now, when people see the titles:
*"On such a day, such a month, such a lord [口受] granted to *Èr Xū* [二许] and *Yòu Yǐng* [右掾]..."*
They claim it was *Èr Xū* [二许] who personally received the *Yīnyì* [音旨].
This is far from the truth.

The books written by *Èr Xū* [二许]
are all copies of what *Yáng Jūn* [杨君] instructed them.

Moreover, *Yòu Yǐng* [右掾] recorded:
*"In the *Tàihé* [泰和] year, I practiced the *Dào* [道].
The two *Lù* [录] were received in the second year.
After the third year,
there were no further *Shū* [疏]."*

The *Zhōngshǐ*’s [中侍] calligraphy was not skilled,
and his transcriptions were few.
Today, one matter may have two or three versions,
all being *Èr Xū*’s [二许] repeated copies,
with no discrepancies.
Yet *Yáng Jūn*’s [杨君] writings all lack duplicates,
clearly indicating that only *Yòu Yǐng*’s [右掾] records were preserved.
*Yáng Jūn*’s [杨君] own records have vanished beyond measure.

After *Yáng Jūn* [杨君] departed, six or seven years passed,
but no traces of the *Zhōngshǐ* [长史] emerged.

Regarding the recorded years and months today,
the *Yǐchǒu* [乙丑] year has the most entries,
while the *Dǐngyín* [丙寅] and *Dīngwǔ* [丁卯] years each have several.
The first volume still retains some semblance of order,
but the remaining volumes have dates and events jumbled,
not fully aligned in sequence.

As for the recorded dates of transmissions,
most do not specify the year.
I have now arranged them sequentially,
assuming their order based on precedence.
Some matters are interrupted,
but not all must be accounted for.
When no year or month is recorded,
or when the recipient is not specified,
they are left as originally omitted.

The *Zhēnshòu* [真授]’s teachings on others’ likes and dislikes
were all inquiries made by the *Zhōngshǐ* [长史] on behalf of *Yáng Jūn* [杨君],
and the answers were given accordingly.
They were kept secret within *Èr Xū*’s [二许] circle,
and at that time, not all were necessarily aware.

Moreover, *Bìng Jǐn* [并衿] received the *Jǐngyáng Ān* [景阳安],
which was clearly and distinctly explained.
All the poems of *Xīng* [兴] with or without waiting,
as well as the *Cí* [辞], *Yù* [喻], *Fěng* [讽], and *Zhǐ* [旨],
were all descended by *Yúnlín* [云林] upon the *Pínxiān Hòu* [嫔仙侯].
Their meaning and purpose were also manifest.
The *Nán Zhēn* [南真] was the teacher who instructed,
while *Zǐwēi* [紫微] was the artisan of the lower teachings.
They were not bound by the *Liǔjié* [俦结] rules.
However, *Zhōnghòu Zhāolíng* [中侯昭灵] seemed to have a separate domain.
Since the matters were not all from one time,
the records are not entirely precise.

The other male *Zhēn* [真]
either accompanied or were assigned by those cited,
such as the two *Jūn* [君].
They were the primary leaders.
If people read these transmissions
without understanding this principle,
they will never grasp their true meaning.
Hence, I briefly mark the general intent,
which should be kept in strict confidence.

*Èr Xū* [二许] should have been practicing the scriptural arts.
Since they did not attain the *Zhēnshòu* [真授],
they had no way to see the scriptures.
Thus, the *Nán Zhēn* [南真] first instructed *Yáng Jūn* [杨君],
then ordered him to transmit.
The transmission then became the teacher.
That is why the *Zhōngshǐ* [长史] and *Yòu Yǐng* [右英] wrote:
*"The *Nán Zhēn* [南真] in pity,
last spring sent the teacher to instruct in the *Dòngfáng* [洞房]."*
Yet *Èr Xū* [二许] were separated by worldly conventions
and did not revere or respect the teachings.

Yang dared not presume to act on his own.
Having violated the true precepts,
he declared:
*"To receive the scriptures is to have a teacher—
should I then be ashamed of this?"*

Yet the *Nán Zhēn* [南真] was the *Xuán Zhōng* [玄中] teacher.
Thus, Yang and the *Zhǎngshǐ* [长史] alike called her the *Xuánshī* [玄师].
It was further stated:
*"The afflicted should first inform the *Xuánshī* [玄师].
Otherwise, their condition will not improve."*

Yet the letters from the *Zhǎngshǐ*, *Yòu Yǐng* [右英], and the assembled *Zhēn* [真]
merely expressed their trembling reverence—
this was but the form of a teacher’s manner,
not the true transmission itself.

Moreover, in Yang’s writings, there are many drafts marked with *"[人巉]"*—
these were all written during the reception of instructions.
Since they were hastily composed and later revised,
he would recall earlier words and then add or subtract accordingly.
For those who wrote carefully and neatly,
he would rewrite them entirely
to present to the *Zhǎngshǐ*.

Now, regarding the three *Jūn* [君]’s handwritten letters—
since their originals are no longer extant,
the texts have been indiscriminately copied without distinction.
Thus, each entry is annotated below.
If a letter’s authenticity as a true original is uncertain,
it is marked *"[某书]"* [so-and-so’s letter].
There are also four or five letters from different hands—
it is unclear whether they were written simultaneously by scribes
or later additions by others.
Since no names are attached and their authenticity cannot be verified,
they are all transcribed here,
with annotations distinguishing them as *"A," "B," "C," "D"* for verification.

Furthermore, within the texts, there are variations in handwriting—
additions, deletions, and corrections marked *"[人巉]"*—
most of which were made by *Èr Xǔ* [二许] and his descendants.
Some were to invoke auspicious names,
others to align with contemporary conventions,
but they were all baseless fabrications.
Yet they were unable to discern this,
and thus uncritically adopted them.
Since these are not originals,
there is fear of further confusion—
each alteration is therefore annotated.
If a text was personally revised by the original author,
no further distinction is made.

As for the handwriting of the three *Jūn* [君],
Yang *Jūn*’s script was the most refined—
neither too modern nor too archaic,
capable of both bold and delicate strokes.

Upon closer examination, though the three *Jūn* [君] imitated the *Xi* [希] *Yì* [邑] [法] script,
their strokes adhered to strict proportions,
yet their names were not renowned—
likely due to their humble origins,
and also being overshadowed by the two *Wáng* [王].
*Yáng Jūn*’s [杨君] calligraphy was a study of *Yáng*’s [掾],
with sharp and vigorous strokes,
particularly skilled at copying *sūtras* [经] and drawing *fú* [符].
It bore resemblance to *Yáng*’s [杨],
yet its bold and dynamic brushwork
was beyond human skill alone.
*Yù Bó* [郁勃] excelled in *zhāngcǎo* [章草] script,
but his *zhèngshū* [正书] was awkward and crude,
and his *fú* [符] lacked finesse—
thus he never copied *sūtras*.
In the past, *Yǐn Jū* [隐居] once observed *Zhāng Dào’ēn* [张道恩]’s ability to distinguish authentic script,
admiring his divine insight.
Now, upon seeing the three *Jūn*’s [君] works,
one could recognize their styles
by even a single stroke—
a revelation beyond mere skill.
It was as if Heaven granted this discernment,
allowing one to perceive their true nature.

Again, the three *Jūn*’s [君] calligraphic styles
differed from those of the present age.
They followed conventions of exaggerated elegance—
writing *guī* [龟] as *lóng* [龙], *xū* [虚] as *huá* [华],
displaying ornate attire (*xiànfú* [显服]),
and omitting the closing stroke (*yǐqián* [阙关]).
All three adhered to these norms.
As for *Yáng Fēi* [杨飞] (also *Yáng Wǒ* [掾飞]),
*Yáng Wǒ* [杨我] (also *Yáng Wǒ* [掾我]),
*Yáng Líng* [杨灵] (also *Yáng Líng* [长史灵], *Yáng Líng* [掾灵]),
*Yáng Zhēn* [杨真] (also *Yáng Zhēn* [长史真]),
and *Yáng Shī* [杨师] (also *Yáng Shī* [掾师]),
*Yáng È* [杨恶] (also *Yáng È* [长史恶]),
these were their self-imposed variations.

Moreover, they wrote *guǐmó* [鬼魔] as *mó* [摩],
*jìngjié* [净洁] as *shèngjié* [盛洁],
and *shèngzhù* [盛贮] as *qǐngzhù* [请贮].
Such deviations were broadly consistent—
though not all can be exhaustively recorded,
fearing later generations,
in transcribing these works,
would inevitably alter them to suit contemporary styles.
Thus, their examples are marked here,
so that successors may adhere to them diligently.

These discrepancies and similarities
are all thoroughly documented in *Dēngzhēn Yǐnjué* [登真隐诀].

Furthermore, among the three *Jūn*’s [君] scripts,
some characters were not properly formed—
by principle, they should be simplified (*yìng zhì yì* [应治易]),
yet their original traces must be preserved intact.
Alterations should not be made arbitrarily—
careless changes would only lead to confusion,
losing the authentic essence.
Thus, all suspect characters are now encircled in red (*jūguō* [朱郭]),
with annotations beneath them.

Additionally, the three *Jūn* [君] frequently wrote on *Jīngzhōu* [荆州] white paper,
which, over time and exposure,
sometimes became frayed or damaged at the edges.

Sometimes the paper would rot or tear away,
leaving fragments lost to time.
Earlier hands, unable to fully reconstruct the missing parts,
had merely patched them together.
Now, I have taken the original characters,
discarding the damaged sections entirely.
Where the text was lost—no longer the authentic hand—
even if later scribes filled in the gaps,
the original intent must be preserved.
Thus, I have recorded them as they are,
without further dissection.

Among the three *Jūn*’s works, some scrolls survive intact—
only those copied by the two *Xǔ* under Dao transmission.
(*The "Dūgōng Jì" is attributed to Yang Jí and his clerk’s hand,*
*and these too remain whole in form and content.*
*The rest—whether five sheets, three sheets,*
*or even a single scrap—*
*were later patched together by others,*
*not arranged in their original order.*
Now, I have extracted (*bá* [拔])
the dated entries and those of similar themes,
no longer following the sequence of the earlier scrolls.)

As for the collected sayings of the *Zhēn* masters,
each bears profound meaning—
some in verse, some in admonition,
interwoven in dialogue.
Yet their compiled manuscripts,
when categorized by topic,
lose their original coherence,
making their intent obscure.
Now, I have restored them to their original context,
grouping them by date, paper, and ink,
to preserve their natural flow.

Regarding the rituals of *Qǐjū Bǎoshén* and the *Míngtáng* dream-prayers,
the ten-odd recorded methods
are mostly copied from scriptures,
lacking proper beginnings or endings—
like the *Rìmáng* (日芒), *Rìxiàng* (日象), *Xuánbái* (玄白), or *Fúwù* (服雾) rites.
Yet they were never properly compiled,
leading to their neglect and loss.

Now I have compiled and annotated them all,
each according to its proper form.

As for those texts whose original manuscripts remain unseen,
and whose transmission—whether extant or lost—cannot be verified,
I dare not hastily omit or discard the inconsistencies and discrepancies recorded therein.
Instead, I have annotated my doubts in each case,
listing them under their respective entries.

Regarding the recorded lineages of the *Dònggōng* (洞宫) and the immortal clans of the various mountains,
I intended to cross-reference them with external texts for detailed annotation,
tracing their origins and lineages.
Yet fearing that such exposition might reveal their essence too plainly,
contrary to the hidden Dao,
I have only marked the discrepancies and ambiguities.

As for the *Gōngguǐ Guān* (宫鬼官),
their accounts may be disclosed as circumstances warrant.

Now, concerning the origins of this book,
the accounts of divine descents take precedence,
followed by the subsequent records of events.
The descent of the divine occurred at *Jiǔhuá*,
yet the compilation concludes with these matters last.
Moreover, the deeds of the Master were not yet at the level of the divine,
and thus should not be included in this section.
Yet the records of *Wáng Yòujūn* (王右军) and his descendants were mistakenly included,
contrary to proper order.
Now, I have placed the *Ānjì* (安记) first,
removed the *Xǔ Chuán* (许传),
and added them to the external texts under the category of immortals.
Only the letter the Master wrote to his younger brother after attaining immortality
remains in the later volumes.

The *Chángshǐ* (长史) letter pertains to the *Huáyáng* (华阳) affair,
while the *Huáyáng* affair is in response to the *Chángshǐ* letter.
Forcing them into two separate sections disrupts their natural sequence.
Now, I have restored them to their original order.

As for the recorded years of the two *Xǔ* (许),
they were inconsistent and erratic.
I have now carefully cross-checked them with the divine revelations,
and annotated the discrepancies in names,
appending them after this volume.

Regarding the writings of the Three Masters,
some were not directly received from the divine,
some were copies of secular texts,
some were personal dream records,
and some were private letters—
none of which should be mixed into the *Zhēn Gào* (真诰) canon.
Yet, as these texts are precious in ink and brushwork,
I have compiled them together as the sixty-first volume.

There remain a few additional entries,
which I have also included according to the same standard.

Within the *Zhēn Gào*,
all texts written in purple ink with large characters
were secretly transcribed by the recluse from the Three Masters’ handwritten copies,
interspersed with miscellaneous events,
each cross-referenced and categorized for verification.
Since these scriptures are not casually accessible,
their separation in the original examples would obscure their study.
Now, they are compiled together here for easier examination.

Among these six texts, those written in red ink with small characters
are all annotations by the recluse,
serving as supplementary notes.
The black-ink small characters remain the original text.

The beginning and end of the *Zhēn Jīng* (真经) trace its origin to the *Shàngqīng* divine scriptures,
which first emerged in the *Jìn* dynasty during the *Āiníng* era (哀帝兴宁二年),
when the *Tàisuì* (太岁) year was *Jiǎzǐ* (甲子).
The *Zǐxū Yuánjūn* (紫虚元君), the *Shàngzhēn Sīmìng* (上真司命),
and *Nányuè Wèi Furen* (南岳魏夫人) descended,
instructing their disciple, the *Lángyè* (琅琊) *Wáng Sītú* (王司徒),
the palace attendant *Yáng* (杨某),
to transcribe in clerical script and pass it to the military protector *Jùróng Xǔ* (句容许某),
along with the third son, the *Shàngjì Yuàn* (上计掾) *Xǔ* (许某).
The two *Xǔ* further transcribed and refined them,
practicing Dao and attaining transcendence.
The handwritten texts of the Three Masters that survive today
include over ten large and small scriptural transmissions,
mostly copied by the *Yuàn* (掾),
and over forty volumes of divine revelations,
mostly written by *Yáng* (杨).
(The *Lángyè Wáng* (琅琊王) was the *Jiǎnwén Dì* (简文帝) when he served as Crown Prince in the Eastern Palace.)

The *Chángshǐ Yuàn* (长史掾) established his residence at the rear of *Xiǎomáo* (小茅),
west-north of *Léipíng Shān* (雷平山).
There, he transcribed and refined the texts for practice.
He ascended in seclusion during the *Tàihé* era (泰和五年).

The *Chángshǐ* departed again in the first year of the *Tàiyuán* era (泰元元年).
His son, *Huáng Mín*, then seventeen years old,
collected and preserved the transcribed scriptures and secret bamboo records over the years.
