At that time, several volumes had also scattered among relatives and acquaintances—
these are the ones now held by *Jùróng*.
In the third year of *Yuánxīng* (元兴三年),
as turmoil raged in the capital region,
*Huáng Mín* carried the scriptures inward
*(the Chángshǐ’s father had once served as a county magistrate,
renowned for his virtue and benevolence;
the Chángshǐ’s eldest brother also resided there,
hence his refuge there)*,
and was supported by *Dōngchǎn Mǎ Lǎng*’s household
*(Lǎng was also called Wēngōng)*.
Lǎng’s junior cousin, *Hǎn*, assisted in their sustenance.
The people all recognized *Xǔ* the Master’s attainment of the Dao,
and his grandfather’s reputation as well,
so they honored him with filial reverence.
*Duàodǐ* *Dù Dàojū* of *Qián’táng*
*(the father of the *Shísì Jīngchǎn* resident)*
was wealthy and of noble standing,
and frequently invited them.
At that time, none of the others yet knew how to seek or examine the scriptures’ teachings—
they merely received and revered them.
By the *Yìxǐ* era (义熙中),
*Kǒng Mò* of *Lǔguó*, a devout adherent of Daoist teachings,
served as *Jìnan* Prefect.
After his dismissal, he returned to *Qián’táng*.

Hearing of *Xǔ Láng*—his ancestors having attained the Dao,
their scriptures preserved in full—
he went to visit *Xǔ*.
But *Xǔ* refused to meet him.

*Kǒng* knelt in obeisance, bowing repeatedly,
remaining for a span of ten moons,
offering gifts with earnest devotion,
his sincerity profound.
At last, *Xǔ* yielded and taught him.

*Kǒng* then ordered *Jìn’ān* Prefectural clerk *Wáng Xīng* to copy the texts.
(*Xīng* was diligent and skilled in calligraphy,
so the task was entrusted to him.)
When *Kǒng* returned to the capital,
he treasured only the records,
never practicing them himself.

During the *Yuánjiā* era, he was again appointed Governor of *Guǎngzhōu*.
After his death, his son *Xuī* passed away first.
*Xuī* was quick-witted and learned,
secretly perusing the texts.
He found the *Dà Dòng Zhēn Jīng* stating:
*"If recited ten thousand times,
one may attain immortality."*
He scoffed at this, declaring it absurd,
asserting that Daoist transcendence required elixirs and alchemical refinement of the form—
how could mere chanting yield feathered robes?

Moreover, other Daoists joined in disparaging the method,
some advising against keeping such texts.
Thus, in a moment of haste, they were burned away,
leaving no trace behind.
(*This was likely a hidden intent to prevent their spread in the world.*)
(*Later, Xuī [Guāng] and others were executed alongside Fàn Yè in a conspiracy.*)

*Wáng Xīng*, who had copied them for *Kǒng*,
privately made another set,
later intending to return east to study.

After crossing the Zhejiang River,
he was soon caught in a storm and drifted adrift,
only preserving a single copy of the *Huángtíng* text.
Wang Xing then chastised himself severely
and remained in the mountains,
gradually devoting himself to reading and recitation.
The mountain spirits burned his dwelling in flames.
Again, while studying on an open platform,
a sudden downpour ruined his scrolls and ink,
so he could not complete his repetitions.
Deeply aware of his transgression,
he severed all human ties,
recording only dates in his book
and trading grain to sustain himself.
His son, Dao Tai, served as a Jin’an maritime supervisor,
wealthy and generous,
often visiting to offer gifts
and sending two slaves in attendance.
Yet Xing accepted nothing
and remained steadfast in the mountains.

Thus, the two copies written by Kong and Wang
were all destroyed—
never circulating in the world.
(*This was likely due to Xing’s unauthorized copying and use,
leading to such consequences.*)

There was also Wang Lingqi,
a man of brilliant intellect and refined talent,
whose ambition was to propagate the Dao.
Seeing Ge Chaofu construct the *Língbǎo* framework,
with its teachings spreading widely,
he grew deeply resentful and envious.

Thus, Wang Lingqi went to Xu Cheng to request the transmission of the *Shangjing*.
Xu Cheng refused him.

Wang endured frost, dew, and snow,
nearly risking his life.

Moved by his sincerity, Xu Cheng finally granted it.
Upon receiving the scripture, Wang rejoiced and returned to study it deeply.
He realized the sacred law could not be openly propagated,
and the essential teachings could not be fully revealed.

Thus, he secretly added and altered passages,
embellishing them with elaborate rhetoric.
Following the titles of the Wang and Wei traditions,
he expanded and fabricated new works
to complete his collection.

He further exaggerated their authenticity,
elevating the Dao’s prestige.
In total, he produced over fifty additional texts.
Those eager to compete heard of his vast knowledge
and flocked to revere and learn from him.
As his transmissions spread widely,
branches and offshoots proliferated,
new and old versions became confused,
making them difficult to distinguish.

Unless one had seen the original scripture,
it was impossible to verify or discern the truth.
(*Some of his annotated copies still survive today.*)

Jú Shīsheng, the monk Biāo, and Gōng Bǒyù—
men of refined talent—
have said:
*"The world contains many men of brilliance and eloquence."*

Thus, he severed ties with all others.
I and King Lingqi set sail together from the capital.
By the time we reached the Dùnpò Gāng [Tonpogang],
I had already composed two volumes of the Upper Scripture—
truly astonishing!

Even before King Lingqi’s time,
the Upper Scripture had already been repeatedly corrupted.
Yang Xǐ of Hóngnóng, in the year *Gēngzǐ* of the fourth year of *Lóng’ān* (Hè),
reencountered me in hiding at Hǎilíng.
Among the twenty-odd chapters of the Upper Scripture—
some spanning several volumes—were not genuine.
He claimed that since his search for the scriptures,
twelve years had passed.
But this was after Yang Jun departed,
when I began my own work.
Thus, within the *Língbǎo* Scripture,
I incorporated what I had composed—
not all of it was created by King Lingqi alone,
but much of it was my own creation.

Now, throughout the world, this is what is transmitted and spread.
In the capital and the several prefectures of Jiangdong,
there is scarcely anyone who does not possess it.
Yet in the southern regions, it is still not widespread.
(*This must be because the Dao’s true subtlety cannot be openly proclaimed,
so it was arranged for King Lingqi to disseminate it in this manner.*)

Since King Lingqi monopolized these novel teachings,
the world revered and followed him.
Thus, he falsely claimed that the transmission came from the True One.

The original text was not the true one.
The *Língbǎo* Scripture’s volumes—though lost and scattered—
were seen in abundance by Xu Guang.
His faith was deep and his wealth plentiful,
his disciples numerous and prosperous.
Gold and silk filled his halls—
yet no one could fathom why.

Then he closed his own books,
and instead sought copies from King Lingqi.
Thus, the traces of both were spread together,
their voices in harmony, mutually praising.
So it came to pass that Xu Guang and King Lingqi
traveled in tandem—
true and false, their paths intertwined.
Following the current, they rode the wind,
and people came from a thousand *li* to seek them.

Later, there was a man named Cai Mai,
who also received these ten-odd volumes from Xu,
some of which were genuine.
He copied and transmitted them widely,
but their traces are no longer preserved.
(*Cai Mai practiced the Dao with sincerity,
yet the scriptures did not spread widely.*)

When Ma Lang saw the *Língbǎo* Scripture transmitted by Xu,
the number of volumes had increased.
He wished to receive them again,
and prepared offerings with deep faith,
planning to depart on a set day.
Suddenly, he dreamed of a jade basin descending from heaven,
shattering upon striking the earth.
Awakening, he pondered:
*"This scripture must be a heavenly treasure—
once on earth, it can no longer be used."*

Thus, he ceased his pursuit.
(*Though Ma Lang did not study diligently,
his reverence was profound.
His dream was extraordinary,
and his interpretation was wise—
he was surely a Daoist adept.*)

In the sixth year of *Yuánjiā*,
Xu Cheng wished to relocate to Jiāngtáng.
He sealed the original *Língbǎo* Scripture in a chest,
and entrusted it to Ma Lang’s purified chamber,
telling him:
*"This scripture bears the traces of the ancient sages.
Only I may retrieve it myself.
Even if letters arrive, do not give it to anyone."*

He then took with him the transmitted scriptures
and miscellaneous books—
ten-odd volumes in all—
and traveled to Dùjiā.
He stayed there for several months,
falling ill.

Xu Cheng feared that the request might not be fulfilled.
He sent someone to retrieve the scripture,
but Ma Lang treasured the books,
and also adhered to the original instructions.
His close kin, having received the imperial edict,
how could he dare to hand them over lightly?
Thus, he did not send the reply.

Then, Xu Cheng suddenly passed away.
The transmitted scriptures he had carried remained with the Dù family.
These are the very scriptures now found throughout the world.

Xu Cheng’s eldest son, Róng, returned to their homeland
to bring back his father’s remains.
After the mourning period was complete,
he went to Ma Lang to seek the scriptures.
Ma Lang was skilled in managing affairs,
but refused to give him the scriptures.
Xu Cheng, feeling ashamed,
no longer pressed for their retrieval,
and stayed on.

Later, Ma Lang taught the *Língbǎo* scriptures
to be recited and expounded,
and also wrote out the original texts.
He appended notes to each scripture, stating:
*"In such-and-such year and month,
the Immortal [某真人] transmitted the scriptures to Xu Cheng for his pilgrimage."*

(*At that time, most people knew that the Master had ingested elixirs and ascended into the mountains by the Daoist Way,
but they did not inquire into the deeds of the Prefect and his son.*)

Ma Lang also did not doubt the true meaning of the scriptures.
After several years, Xu Cheng’s son obtained two or three volumes of the original scriptures from Ma Lang,
and began to disseminate them.
(*These are the very scriptures that people like Wang Huì and others now possess.*)

In the twelfth year of *Yuánjiā*.

Afterward, he remained there.

He was then buried at White Mountain.
(*His younger brother was still alive, but he indulged in licentious extravagance, taking no interest in scriptural learning. This is why Master He was able to quickly obtain and copy the texts from the Ma family.*)

The Ma Lang and Ma Han reverently tended to the scriptural treasures.
When they transgressed against their parents,
they habitually employed two devoted servants—
(*one named Whitehead, the other Flathead*)
—to constantly attend the incense and lamps,
sweeping and polishing with devotion.
Often, divine luminosity and sacred qi manifested within their halls.
Lang’s wife was particularly adept at perceiving these wonders,
often seeing green-robed celestial maidens ascending and descending through the air,
their forms like soaring birds.
Thus, the Ma family grew wealthy and prosperous,
their wealth numbering in the millions.
When they grew old and died,
Lang’s son Hong, Hong’s brother Zhen,
and Han’s son Zhi and others
still continued in their ways.
In their later years, they turned to Buddhism,
and their devotion waned.

(*This was surely the result of the scriptures’ karmic influence manifesting.*)

Master He Daojing of Shanyin was a man of steadfast resolve from youth,
skilled in calligraphy and painting.
He had once wandered the mountains
and was taken into service by the Ma family,
who entrusted him with all matters of scripture and ritual.
Seeing the radiant and vivid talismans—
unlike any worldly texts—
in the eleventh year of *Yuánjiā*,
he began to copy them.
(*The character "Mo" is illegible here.*)
Since Ma Han resided in another estate,
He was also commissioned to compose several additional passages,
which were later combined into two volumes
and kept in Han’s household.
Later, He frequently exchanged copies of the original texts,
returning to live at his Eastern Villa on Qingtan Mountain.
There, he recorded the true events of the scriptures—
a few sheets in total.
But He was of coarse and sluggish nature,
unable to refine his lofty studies.
Later, most of these texts were lost,
though a few volumes remained.

Now, the scriptures are preserved in the household of his female disciple, **Zhang Yujing**, in the **Shihfeng Houfang** estate on **Mountain Zhang** (i.e., *Zhangshan*).

*(Zhang Yujing often used plain white paper for her work. She was coarse and unsophisticated, yet the recluse **Gu Ju** heard of her obtaining the scriptures and thus went to visit her. Upon arrival, he found her returning home from toiling in the fields with a hoe. Gu mistook her for a servant and asked whether the Master **He** was present. She replied that she did not know. Gu then returned to his own home and never saw her again. Gu lingered there for several days, feeling remorseful for his oversight, but ultimately did not seek further contact. People at the time universally regarded He’s vulgar nature and his failure to recognize talent as shameful, lamenting the loss of such an opportunity.)*

After He had divided and distributed the scriptures, he also revealed their teachings. **Ma Lang** was enraged and thus cast the bronze keys of the kitchen into molten copper, binding his household servants with an oath never to open them again. In the seventh year of *Dàmíng*, famine struck the **Three Wu regions**, and the county suffered from severe scarcity. **Lou Reshù Huiming**—who had previously been there—returned with his female disciple, **Zhang Yujing**, her attendant **Zhong Yishan**, and several others from the **Yanyuan** region to seek sustenance in this land. Since Lou was skilled in **talismans** and **Five Phases/Elements** (*Wǔxíng*) as well as **hostile destinies** (*sùmìng*), he also interpreted and elucidated their meanings.

Ma Hong once again devoted himself to the Dao, entering and exiting the Hall of Serenity with reverence. He witnessed the scripture kitchen firsthand and had already seen what He had recorded. His heart was deeply covetous of the treasures, yet the **golden oranges** (jīnjú) were strictly guarded, and there was no way to examine them thoroughly.

In the first year of *Jǐnghé*, he left the capital and ordered **Cāijìng Shìjì Zhēn** to open and retrieve the sealed texts from Jiaxing. Cāijìng, however, was reckless and unrestrained. Lou declared that the true scriptures should not be exposed to the world, so he selected and copied the genuine scriptures and genuine transmissions, along with about ten miscellaneous **oral transmissions** (*kǒushòu*), leaving them in Zhong’s care. Only the **unrestricted talismans** (*huòluò fú*) and about twenty small oral transmissions were taken, along with the two records **He** had **not personally inscribed** (*mòshǒu*), to be brought back to the capital. Cāijìng then presented them to Cāijìnghé, who briefly opened them in the **Huálín Garden** before entrusting them to the Daoists of the rear hall.

At the beginning of the *Qín* [*Tàishǐ*] era, Cāijìng attempted to take out his private annotations. **Lù Xiūjìng** then traveled south, establishing the **Chóngxū Hall**. He also took possession of the texts there. After Lù’s death, they were returned to **Lúshān**. Later, **Xú Shùbiāo** brought them down to the capital. Upon Xú’s death, they remained with Lù’s nephew, **Yùhuái Wén**. (Within these texts were the true transmissions written by the three masters. Later, they were carelessly bound together, divided into twenty-four sections. This occurred in the third year of *Jiànyuán*.)

The emperor commanded **Dòng Zhòngmín** to construct a **meritorious hall** at **Lúshān**. Dòng sought divine marvels, and **Xú** therefore divided one *Yáng* text into two volumes and gave them to Dòng. Upon returning to the capital, Dòng presented them to **Gāodì**. The emperor entrusted them to **Dài Qìng**, the scholar of the Five Classics. When Dài Qìng departed, he took the texts with him. After Xú’s death, his disciple **Lǐ Guǒzhī** again took one volume and the *Huò* [*Luò*] text away. Only twenty-one volumes remained, all of which were returned to the **Sealed Bright Platform**.

**Lóu Cóng** returned from the capital and settled there, then sought from **Zhōng** the true scriptures left behind. Zhōng refused to return them, so Lóu had them copied. After much time, he obtained several volumes. He bore a grudge against **Mǎ Hóng** and relocated to **Chángshān** in **Dōngyáng**. Later, Mǎ secretly came to take them but mistakenly obtained other scriptures. Meanwhile, some volumes were lost within Lóu’s collection. Still, one or two volumes should remain today. (The second volume was already returned to the Sealed Bright Platform.)

*Zhēn Yuè*, Volume Nineteen.
*Zhēn Yuè*, Volume Twenty.
*Yì Zhēn Jiǎn*, Section Two.

During the time of **Kǒng Yùcāo** (the humble one), **Dú Jūshì** (the recluse) took the scriptures and settled at **Nánshè Dàhú**.

Lóu Cóng initially, together with **Gù Huān**, **Qī Jǐng Xuán**, **Zhū Sēng Biāo**, and several others, jointly examined and assessed the scriptures. Gù had already copied some of the scriptures kept by Lóu, and though he only roughly recognized the true scriptures, they were still selected and sorted. In total, there were four or five volumes of *jīng* and *zhuàn*, and seven or eight *zhēn* (transmitted orally) texts. Some of these still remain in Dú Jūshì’s household. (The two volumes of *jīng* and the *zhēn* (transmitted orally) texts have already been returned to the Sealed Bright Platform.)

In the late years of the **Sòng Dàmíng** era, **Dài Fǎxīng**, elder brother of **Yán Xīng**, served as county magistrate and was also fond of the Dao. Along with the celestial masters of **Tiānmù Shān** in **Wúxīng**, he also obtained many copies of Dú’s scriptures. Lóu Cóng’s younger brother, **Dào Jì**, and **Fǎ Zhēn**, along with **Zhōng Xīng’s** daughter **Fù Guāng**, all obtained copies of the scriptures from Lóu and Zhōng. Though they exchanged them freely, none could perfectly replicate the originals, yet they often embellished and refined them, making the calligraphy smooth and elegant—utterly devoid of rigid structure or sharp edges. Their copied scriptures were also rife with errors.

By the year **Gēngwǔ**, the hidden retreat entered the Dao of **Dōngyáng**. Later scholars gradually refined their craft. **Pán Wén Shèng** of **Shānyīn**, **Dú Gāoshì** of **Jiǎngtáng**, **Jiǎng Hóngsù** of **Yìxīng**, and **Xǔ Língzhēn** of **Jùróng** were all skilled in this art. Though people today admire the calligraphy of the "Two Wangs," they fail to recognize the true scriptures. The authenticity of the scriptures stems from the hidden retreat’s hand—there is no need for all to be filled in by **Guō** (i.e., meticulously traced). A single stroke captures the essence, and the power nearly equals the original. As for the seals, regardless of size, they should all be filled in accordingly.

This ended in the **Qín Tài** era’s fourth year, when the scriptures were moved back to **Shǐníng Shān**. **Mǎ Zhìwǎn**, under pressure from the monks, converted to Buddhism and handed over dozens of Daoist scriptures to Zhōng—all of which **Hé Gōng** had originally written for his father. There were also miscellaneous scriptures by **Wáng Língqī**, but only four or five volumes and six or seven *zhēn* (transmitted orally) texts were in the true handwriting, unrelated to what Lóu had obtained. (The two volumes of scriptures, including the *zhēn* (transmitted orally) texts, have all been returned to the Sealed Bright Platform. After Zhōng’s death, the remaining scriptures should still be with his siblings, his daughter, and **Qī Jǐng Xuán.**)

The ancient **Chén Léi** of **Dōng Yáng** was a disciple of **Xǔ Zhǎngshǐ**. He was diligent and devout, and **Xǔ Zhǎngshǐ** often entrusted him with the duty of copying and overseeing sacred texts, frequently instructing him in their meaning. **Chén Léi** also transcribed some scriptures himself and obtained **Xǔ Zhǎngshǐ**’s personal copy of the *Bùqǐ Qīyuánxīng* (Seven Elements of the Step Diagram).

After **Xǔ Zhǎngshǐ** departed, **Chén Léi** returned to **Dōng Yáng**. In the **Yìxǐ** era’s thirteenth year, he joined forces with two nephews of **Wèi Xīn**, the Prefect of **Dōng Yáng** (from **Rènchéng**), in the alchemical practice of **hédān** (elixir fusion). Upon its completion, the three consumed it in succession, each experiencing extraordinary spiritual effects—ascending to transcendence, embodying transformation, and vanishing into **huàdiàn** (mystic retreat).

**Chén Léi** had a grandson named **[missing name]**, with the sobriquet **Chánglè**. Now residing at **Héngjiāng Qiáo** north of **Yǒngkāng**, he is a **Qīngshān Dàoshì** (Daoist of **Qīngshān**). The **Fán Xiān** (Immortal Fán) also frequently sought out the scriptures **Chén Léi** had transcribed. However, the *Bùqǐ* (Step Diagram) remains in his possession.

What is currently practiced is the original version itself. From this point onward, all scriptures found in his collection have their own distinct titles and volumes. If only a few scattered sections or one or two volumes remain, their original titles and chapter listings are now revealed as follows:

**Yángshū Língbǎo** (Scripture of the Soul’s Treasure) with five *fú* (talismans) and one volume—originally held by **Gě Cǎn** in **Jùróng**—from the **[missing year]** of the **Tàishǐ** era.

Gě showed these to **Lǔ Xiānsheng**. After Lǔ had expounded the true scriptures—the *Zhēnwén Chìshū*, the *Rénniǎo Wǔfú*, and other teachings—his practice spread widely. Though he no longer wished to display extraordinary miracles, he gave Gě silk offerings in return for the texts, carefully concealing them. When **Gù Gōng** heard of this and earnestly sought to see them, Lǔ refused. He passed them only to **Nányáng Sūn Yóuyuè** and his female disciple **Méi Lìngwén**. After Lǔ’s death, they returned to **Lúshān**. Later, **Xú Shūbiāo** intended to take them out, but he died before doing so, leaving them with **Lǔ Yùhuái Wén** (already returned to **Fēng Zhāotái**).

The *Yángshū Língbǎo* transmitted by **Wáng Jūn**, one volume—originally held by **Jùróng Gě Yǒngzhēn**—later passed to **Wáng Wénqīng’s** household, then to the **Máoshān Dàoshì Gě Jǐngxiān** (already returned to **Fēng Zhāotái**).

The *Yànshū Fēibù Jīng*, one volume—originally in the household of **Jùróng Yán [Chóngyǐ]**. In the **Dà Míng** seventh year (1429), during a famine with scarce grain, **Wáng Wénqīng** of his village fed Yán with money in exchange for the text, keeping it thereafter (already returned to **Fēng Zhāotái**).

The *Yànshū Xīyuè Gōng Jìnshān Fú* and the *Yángshū Zhōnghuáng Zhìhǔbào Fú*—two short volumes—originally obtained by **Shàngyú Wú Zhàbá** from **Xǔ Chéng’s** miscellaneous Daoist texts in a gourd (*Lúguā*). Wú gave these two volumes to **Yīzhě Xiānsheng Bóyù**, who resided in **Nánhè**. While wandering the mountains, he always carried them with him.

The **Yīzhě** [Xiānsheng] passed away. The texts remained with his disciple **Zhū Sēngbiāo**.

Later, the **Yīzhě’s** fifth brother’s grandson, named **Zhòngyǎn**, again obtained them from **Zhū** (already returned to **Fēng Zhāotái**).

**Wú Zhàbá** of **Shàngyú**, though of the **Jiémǐ** clan, was a man of talent. Initially, he became a Daoist priest. **Xǔ Chéng** gave him a gourd (*Lúguā*) of texts—all minor Daoist techniques and incantations for practical use. Later, he took Buddhist vows and dispersed them all to beggars, exhausting them. Afterwards, he abandoned Buddhism and returned to lay life, dying in obscurity. The texts (*jié*) were never traced thereafter.

The *Yànshū Tàisù Wǔshén Èrshísìshén Jīng*, along with the *Huíyuán Yǐndào Jīng* (one volume), and the *Bā Sù Yīnyáng Gē* (one volume)—all originally obtained by **Zhāng Língmín** of **Dōngyáng** when he left the capital. At the time, **Zhāng** did not recognize the true scriptures, only saying they were common Daoist texts. He returned to **Dōngyáng** and showed them to **Gù**, but **Gù** did not immediately take to the Dao. He kept them, dividing the *Huíyuán* into two volumes. Later, when **Zhāng** came to recognize them, he sought them out and now holds them. The second volume, the *Jǐng Gē*, **Zhāng** has already given to **Sūn Gōng** (already returned to **Fēng Zhāotái**). **Zhāng** said:

At that time, there were also several volumes of *Qū Sù Jīnzhēn* (or *Shè Jīnhuá*), now rotted and decayed. Since the true transmission was not yet understood, the scriptures were merely copied by their characters alone, and the original texts were all buried.

Among the *Yànshū* texts was a short volume of *Lièjì Huángsù Shū*, originally entrusted by Xu Chéng to his disciple Sū Dàohuì. Sū Dàohuì passed it to Shàngyú Hé Fǎrén, who then transmitted it to Zhū Sēngbiāo. Zhū Sēngbiāo offered it to Zhōng Fǎshī. Lóu Jūshì saw it and sought to obtain it—now it should still be in his possession.

*Zhāng* also copied the *Wèi Chuán Zhōnghuángjīng*, along with several sheets of the *Fùzhēn Shòu*. These were once kept by Shānráng Huìlǎng. After Ráng’s death, they should now be with his female disciple or fellow student, *Zhāng Língmín*. In Yǒngxīng, there was a family named Jiě, who in the past also provided offerings to Xu Láng. They also obtained some minor texts. Later, the female Daoist *Fán Miàoluó* of Jīngshān, through connections, acquired the *Yángshū* (a volume on *Gōngshì*). After Fán’s death, it was passed to her female disciple, Shěn Ōu, who then gave it to Kǒng Zǒng of Sìmíngshān. (He has since returned to *Fēng Zhāotái*.) The remaining texts from the Jiě family have now vanished without trace.

It is also said that in Shānyīn and Qiántáng, several families possess ancient scriptures—likely mixed with genuine texts. From the beginning, they have never been properly investigated. Those who are earnest in learning and devoted to the Dao should diligently seek them out. If any are found, even a glimpse would distinguish the precious from the worthless.

(Additionally, when Wèi Fūrén was in *Xiǎoxī* [or *Xiá*] in Kuàijī, she carried a *zhōngxiāng* [perfumed box] containing ritual robes and scriptures, which she personally offered.)

Afterward, he remained in Shānyīn, where the lineage still exists today. *(Yet it has not yet been properly sought and investigated.)*

The **True Genealogy of the Descendants** *(This is the account as it stands today, hence it may be called the **True Genealogy**)*. According to careful examination, **Xǔ Chángshǐ**’s sixth-generation ancestor was named Guāng, with the courtesy name Shǎozhāng. He was the fifth son of **Sītú Xǔ Jìng**. During the reign of Emperor Líng, his elder brother Xùn and Xùn’s son Xiàng both sought favor with the eunuchs and prospered in their power. Guāng feared calamity and, in the year **Yōngjiàn** of **Zhōngpíng** (Middle Peace), *i.e.*, the second year of **Zhōngpíng** (184 CE), he crossed the Yangtze River and settled in **Jùróng County**, **Dānyáng**, within **Dūxiāng**’s **Jíyáng Lí**. Later, during the early **Wú** dynasty, he served as **Guānglùxùn**. The tomb of **Xǔ Guānglù** is still there today.

At that time, his seventh-generation ancestor **Zhào** still resided in **Rǔnán’s Píngyù**. *(Métú Yún states that **Jùróng’s Zǐ’ā** is incorrect.)* The **True Annals** *(or **Zhēn Gāo**) say that **Chángshǐ**’s seventh-generation ancestor **Zhào**, with the courtesy name **Zǐ’ā**, had the merit of **Zhèn Huì** (Reviving Benevolence). However, upon checking the genealogical records, the seventh-generation ancestor’s name was **Jìng**, with the courtesy name **Hóngqīng**. During the **Hòuhàn** (Later Han) reign of Emperor Āndì, he held the post of **Guānglù** (Illustrious Official). In **Yǒngjiàn** (Eternal Construction) Year 1 of Emperor Shùndì, he was appointed **Sītú** (Grand Tutor). His name and courtesy name differ from the **True Annals**; the reason for this discrepancy remains unclear.

In **Yǒngchū** (Eternal Beginning) Years 2 and 3 of **Āndì**’s reign, there was a great famine. A *dòu* (peck) of rice cost 2,000 *wén* (copper cash), and people resorted to cannibalism. If **Zhào** indeed saved and revived 480 people, as the **True Annals** claim, it must have been during this time.

According to the **Hàn** (Han) dynastic **Gūnyí** (Official Rites), as recorded in **Cuī Yùhuàn’s** (Cui Yuhuan’s) memorial, **Xǔ Jìng** was over a hundred years old yet still held his official position. Thus, not only did his **yīndé** (shady virtue) benefit future generations, but his **zìyáng** (self-illuminating merit) also left a lasting mark upon the world. This is why his life was long and free from misfortune, and his position reached the heights of the **táidǐng** (imperial court).

In **Yǒngchū** (Eternal Beginning) Years 2 and 3 of **Āndì**’s reign, there was a great famine. A *dòu* (peck) of rice cost 2,000 *wén* (copper cash), and people resorted to cannibalism. If **Zhào** indeed saved and revived 480 people, as the **True Annals** claim, it must have been during this time.

According to the **Hàn** (Han) dynastic **Gūnyí** (Official Rites), as recorded in **Cuī Yùhuàn’s** (Cui Yuhuan’s) memorial, **Xǔ Jìng** was over a hundred years old yet still held his official position. Thus, not only did his **yīndé** (shady virtue) benefit future generations, but his **zìyáng** (self-illuminating merit) also left a lasting mark upon the world. This is why his life was long and free from misfortune, and his position reached the heights of the **táidǐng** (imperial court).

---

The son instructed the grandson, both serving as **Sānguó** (Three Dukes). **Guānglái** (光来) crossed the river to the south. The **yìshì** (deliberate succession) of the family was grandly upheld. Thus, they attained the **shénxiān** (immortal/transcendent).

(Shu **Sītú** (司徒) **Xǔ Jìng** (许靖), courtesy name **Wénxiū** (文休), was the eleventh-generation ancestor of the **Chángshǐ** (长史) clan. **Hàn** (Han) **Zhēngshì** (征士) **Xǔ Shào** (许劭), courtesy name **Zǐjiàng** (子将), was the tenth-generation ancestor. **Wú** (吴) **Chéngxiàng** (丞相) **Xǔ Yàn** (许晏), courtesy name **Xiàorán** (孝然), was the ninth-generation ancestor. All three descended from **Guāngwǔ** (光武) Emperor’s **Xǔ Jiāozhōu** (许交州) lineage in the **Hàn** (Han) dynasty. Later, the descendant of the **Jiāoxiàng** (交相) clan bore the name **Shèngqīng** (圣卿). The **Xǔ** (许) lineage originally sprang from **Jiāngshì** (姜氏), a clan of **Yāndì** (炎帝)’s time. During the reign of **Zhōu Wǔwáng** (周武王), **Xǔ Shū** (许叔) was granted the fief of **Xǔ** (许), now known as **Xǔchāng** (许昌) in **Yùzhōu** (豫州). By **Zhōu Jìngwáng**’s (周敬王) fifteenth year, it was destroyed by the **Zhèng** (郑). The family then migrated to **Chāngyī** (昌邑) in **Shānyáng** (山阳). They adopted the state as their surname, and later settled in **Jiāozhōu** (交州), finally moving to **Rǔnán Píngyú** (汝南平舆).

**Xǔ Jìng**’s (许靖) father was named **Fǎng** (方), a **yàn** (attendant) of the **Gōngfǔ** (imperial bureaucracy). **Xǔ Jìng**’s (许靖) fifth son was named **Guāng** (光), courtesy name **Shàozhāng** (少张), who served as **Shàngshū Láng** (尚书郎), **Jùlú Tàishǒu** (巨鹿太守), and **Shàofù Qīng** (少府卿). He crossed the river to the south.)

During the early **Wú** (吴) dynasty, he served as **Guānglù Xūn** (光禄勋).
*(His wife was of the Dài clan; they were interred together in present-day Jùróng’s Ānchéng Lǐ. The tomb was the first of the "井刀" [well-shaft] style, and locals still call it the "Tomb of Xǔ Guānglù." The mound and stele remain clearly visible today.)*

**Guāng**’s second son, **Xù Quē** (许阙), courtesy name **Jìyōu** (季优), was a man of talent and learning. He held the post of **Wú Shàngshū Láng** (吴尚书郎) and **Chángshuǐ Jiàowèi** (长水校尉).
*(His wife was of the Dài clan; they were interred together beside the first tomb.)*

**Quē**’s third son, **Xù Xiū** (许休), courtesy name **Wénliè** (文烈), cultivated the **Dao/Way** in leisure and refined his **qi**. Though offered the post of **zhōu pì biéjià** (州辟别驾), he declined.
*(His first wife was of the Huá clan from Jìnlíng; his second was a **Gě** of the same county—daughter of the **Shìzhòng Gě Xiàng** (侍中葛相). They were interred together beside the second tomb.)*

**Xiū**’s eldest son, **Xù Shàng** (许尚), courtesy name **Fǔfǔ** (符甫), was a man of talent and renown. In the third year of **Fènghuáng** (凤凰) under **Wú**, he was appointed **Zhōngshū Láng** (中书郎). He died at fifty.
*(His wife was of the **Táo** clan from the same commandery—the daughter of **Jīngzhōu Shìshǐ Táo Jùn** (荆州刺史陶浚). They were interred together beside the third tomb.)*

**Shàng**’s second son, **Xù Fù** (许副), courtesy name **Zhòngxiān** (仲先), was a **regenerated son** (庶生)—the father of the later **Chángshǐ** (长史). He was gentle, virtuous, and well-regarded by the state and commanderies. He served as **Jìn Yuāndì’s** (晋元帝) **Āndōng Cānjūn** (安东参军), then **Zhēngběi Cānjūn** (征北参军), and held the post of **Tàishǒu** of **Xià Pǔyì** (下丕邑). Later, he was appointed **Níngshuò Jiāngjūn** (宁朔将军) and, alongside **Kǒng Tǎn** (孔坦), campaigned against **Shěn Chōng** (沈充), earning the title **Xīchéng Xiànhòu** (西城县侯). He later served as a magistrate, known for his moral influence.

Xù Fù associated with the **Xiè Yì** brothers in scholarly exchanges. When **Sū Jùn’s** rebellion erupted, he again took his kin and fled. After the unrest settled, he was reinstated as **Fèngchē Dūwèi**. He died at seventy-seven.

*(His first wife was **Jìnlíng Huá** of the **Huá** clan, named **Zhuǎn**. She was the younger sister of **Yùshǐ Zhōngchéng Huá Yùqí**. His second wife was **Yìng Lái** of the **Yìng** clan, daughter of **Jìnglíng Tàishǒu Yìng Yànhuī**. Both were interred together in the great tomb to the north of the county.)*

Xù Fù had eight sons. The first was **Fèn**, whose given name was **Shǒu**, courtesy name **Xiàofāng**—a **regenerated son**. He possessed both literary and martial talent and was held in esteem. He was adopted by his uncle **Cháo** and served as **Hé Cìdào Cānjūn**. Later, he was falsely accused by his younger brother **Yíwú**, who succeeded him, and was executed by **Kāngdì** at thirty-six.

*(His wife was of the **Wáng** clan. They were interred together in the great tomb to the north of the county. His great-grandson was **Huìzhī**, who rose to the rank of **Sānfǔ**.)*

The second son, **Huǒzhào**, courtesy name **Xíngmíng**, was a **legitimate son** who inherited the title. He was well-versed in statecraft and possessed the foresight of his age. He also served as **Hé Cìdào Cānjūn**, then as **Nántái Shìyùshǐ**, and later as **Huáilíng Tàishǒu**.

Died at seventy-one. *(His wife was of the **Yóu** clan. Buried separately in Héliú Village, east of the county.)*

The third son, **Qún**, courtesy name **Tàihé**, was a legitimate son, bright and capable. He served as **Yú Tán Cānjūn**. He died at forty-four. *(His wife was of the **Lìyáng Shào** clan. Buried together in the great tomb to the north of the county.)*

The fourth son, **Mài**—that is, the aforementioned gentleman—
The fifth son, **Mǒu**—that is, the **Chángshǐ**—
Both were legitimate sons, recorded separately later.

The sixth son, **Mǎoxuán**, was a regenerated son, who died young. *(His mother was of the **Chén** clan.)*

The seventh son, **Shí Huò**, courtesy name **Yìxuán**, with the childhood name **Sìbó**, was a regenerated son. *(His mother was of the **Zhū** clan.)* He was adopted by his paternal uncle **Jié**. He was broad-minded and magnanimous, fond of learning. He served as **Húan Wēn’s Yángzhōu Cóngshì**, then as **Xiè Ān’s Wèijūn Cānjūn**. He accompanied **Xiè Xuán** in campaigning against **Fú Jiān**, earning merit. He was ennobled as **Dūxiāng Hóu**. Later, he held the posts of **Shàngshū Cāngbù Jiàbù Láng**, **Zhèngyuán Láng**, and **Tōngzhí Chángshì**. In his later years, he suffered from windstroke and lost his speech. He died in the second year of **Lóng’ān**, at seventy. *(His wife was of the **Xuānchéng Jǐ** clan. Buried together in the great tomb to the north of the county.)*

The eighth son, **Língbǎo**, was a regenerated son, who died young. *(His mother was also of the **Chén** clan.)*

He had four daughters:
The eldest, **Jiāng**, was a legitimate daughter, who died young.
The second, **Éhuáng**, was a legitimate daughter, who married **Huáng Yǎn**, the **Jiànkāng Lìng** of the same county.
The third, **Xiūróng**, was a regenerated daughter. *(Her mother was of the **Zhāng** clan.)*

The fourth daughter, **Huìróng**, was a legitimate daughter, who married **Jìnlíng Hóngshēng**, the **Āngù Lìng** of the same county. *(She was born alongside **Shí Huò**.)*

The younger brother, **Cháo**, styled **Yángxiān**, was renowned for his courage and bold **qi** *(as a man of virtue/power)*. He successively served as **Tàishǒu** of **Xiāngyáng**, **Xīnyě**, **Nányáng**, and **Wéngyáng**. Later, he conspired with **Gān Zhuó** to suppress **Wáng Dūn**. When the plot was uncovered, **Zhuó** was executed, and **Cháo** took his own life at the age of fifty-three. *(He was later buried in the great tomb to the north of the county. His wife was **Gě Dí**’s daughter, the sister of **Fèn**. Initially, he adopted **Fèn** as his son, but later had **Yíwú** and **Gāozǐ** by his own wife, both of whom also died young. He left no descendants.)*

The eldest brother, **Mài**, styled **Shūyuán**, with a childhood name of **Yìng**, cultivated purity and embraced the **Dao**. He lived in seclusion beyond the world. Hence, he later changed his name to **Yuǎnyóu**. He engaged in scholarly exchanges with **Wáng Yòujūn** and his son. When his son **Yóu** was born, he paid homage at **Sānzhī**’s shrine, pressing his hand upon the tablet to receive the **Six Jiǎ** and **Yīnyáng** symbols of the **Dao**. He was born in the year **Gēngshēn** of **Yǒngkāng** (i.e., the first year of **Yǒngkāng**), which corresponds to **Yǒnghé**’s fourth autumn in the **Pǔ** records.

「Retired from the world in the **Xīshān** of **Lín’ān**. At the age of forty-eight—this was the year **Yǒngníng** (i.e., **Xīn yǒu** of **Yǒngníng**), which would make him a year younger. Now, I take his own account as correct. At the time of his seclusion, he was forty-nine years old. He married the daughter of **Sūn Hóng** (字 **Yàndá**), of **Wú Jùn**, who was the granddaughter of **Piāojì Xiù** (i.e., **Mài**). After parting ways, he returned without descendants to his ancestral clan.

**Mài** attained the **Dao**. His deeds are recorded in the second volume, as referenced in the *Yùlù* during the **Shìshì** examinations. His records are already documented.

The **Chángshǐ** (i.e., **Mù**), styled **Sīxuán**, with another name **Mù**, was born in **Zhèngshēng**. As a child, he was well-known. He was refined and virtuous, embracing the **Dao** with scholarly grace, possessing vast learning and literary talent. The **Jiànwén Diàndà** (i.e., **Emperor Mu of Jin**) long held him in esteem, and he formed many bonds with contemporary sages and scholars. In his youth, he served as **Yùn zhǔng bù** and **Gōngcáo shǐ** of his native district. Though **Wáng Dǎo**, **Cài Mò**, and **Línchuān** sought to appoint him as **Cóngshì**, he declined. Later, he was selected as a **Tàixué Bóshì**, then served as **Yúmiáo Lìng**, **Shàngshū Láng**, **Jùnzhōng Zhèng**, **Hùfū Chángshǐ**, **Jǐshì Zhōng**, and **Sǎnqí Chángshì**. Though outwardly engaged in secular affairs, inwardly he cultivated **Zhēnxué**. He secretly transmitted the **Jiàojì**, adhering to the **Shàng Dào** and distributing what he had received. Thus, he became the **Shàngqīng Zhēnrén**. His rank ascended to **Hòubó**, and his position was listed among the **Qīngshì**. He governed as a **Xiānzhě**, aiding the **Shèngshèng** in their rule, and assisted the **Sages** in guiding the people.

According to the **Sīmìng**’s revelation in the year **Dīngmǎo** of **Tàihé** (i.e., **Yǒngxīng**’s second year, **Yǐchòu**), it was said that in the year **Bǐngzǐ**, he would depart. At that time, he was seventy-two years old. This would make his birth year **Yǒngxīng**’s second year, **Yǐchòu**, and his departure in **Tàiyuán**’s first year. However, the records state that he passed away in **Níngkāng** of **Xiàowǔ** (i.e., **Yǒnghé**’s fourth autumn), at the age of seventy-one. This would make his birth year **Guǐhài** of **Tài’ān**, which is two years more. Now, I take the **Zhēnxué** account as correct. (As **Gù** said, in the year **Níngkāng** of **Xiàowǔ**...)»

「Seven-two (i.e., seventy-two). Though he mingled with the common folk in outward affairs, inwardly he cultivated **Zhēnxué** (i.e., **true learning**). His wife was **Táowēi**’s daughter from the same district, named **Kēdòu**. She passed away during the **Xīngníng** era. He then entered the **Yì** (i.e., **Chángshān**) and received instruction in the **Yìguān** (i.e., **Palaces of Transformation**). (They were buried together in the old tomb two *lǐ* northwest of the county.)

He had three sons and one daughter by **Lǐngshǐ** (i.e., **Count Long**). The eldest son was named **Tiánchuān**, with the diminutive **Kuì**. He was born of a concubine. He served as the district **Gōngcáo** (i.e., **Clerk of the Public Records**). His wife was of the **Liú** clan. The second son was named **Fèngyóu**, who served as the district **Zhǔmù** (i.e., **Chief Clerk**). **Fèngyóu**’s son was **Dàofú**, with the courtesy name **Míngzhī**. **Míngzhī**’s youngest son was **Jìngtài**, with the courtesy name **Yuánbǎo**. He became the **County Magistrate of Hǎipíng**. For long he resided on **Yǔjǐng** Mountain in **Kuàijì**. He diligently followed the family tradition, transmitting the **Scriptures** (i.e., **Daoist texts**) through **Mòshǒu** (i.e., **handwritten copies**), no more.

**Jìngtài**’s wife was of the same district, the **Gě** clan. They had but one son, named **Língzhēn**, born in the year **Wùmǔ**. He still lives in **Kuàijì**. He also reveres the **Dao/Way** and its pursuits, skillfully composing **fúshū** (i.e., **auspicious talismans**).

From **Count Long** onward, only this sixth-generation grandson—**Língzhēn**—remains.

The middle son was named **Lián**, with the courtesy name **Fúhuī**. His diminutive was **Hǔyá**. He was born of a proper wife. He was earnest and trustworthy. He served as both the district **Zhǔmù** and **Gōngcáo**. **Xiè’ān** appointed him as **Hùfūjūn** (i.e., **General Who Guards the Army**), and later as **Gōngcáo**. He was also commissioned as **Magistrate of Yǒngkāng**, **Assistant to the Marshal of the Household**, and **Governor of Jìnkāng**. He did not assume office. He was later named **Fǔguó Sìmǎ** (i.e., **General of the Auxiliary Forces**). In the third year of **Yuánxīng** under Emperor **Āndì**, he died at home, aged sixty-eight. Thus, he was born in the year **Dīngyǒu** of **Xiánkāng**’s third year. (As **Gù** said: *He was born in the third year of Xiánkāng.*)」

His elder brother **Zǐyōng** (i.e., **Master Righteous**) was also of great error. His wife was the granddaughter of **Huá Yùqí** from **Jìnlíng**. She bore him a son named **Zǐróng**. (They were buried together in **Jiāngchéng**’s **Xīn’ān** village within the **Jiéyuán** boundary.)

His son, **Zǐchì** (with the courtesy name **Xuánzhēn**), was earnest and sincere, harmonious and reclusive. He served as both the district **Zhǔmù** and **Gōngcáo**. At the age of seventy-four, he died. He had four sons and grandsons, all of whom died early. Thus, there were no descendants left.

The younger son was named **Suìyǔ**, with the courtesy name **Dàoxiáng**. His diminutive was **Yùfǔ**. He was born of a proper wife. From his youth, he displayed the uprightness of **jī** and **zhāng** (i.e., the virtues of integrity and nobility). The **Zhǔngshǐ** (i.e., **Prefect of a Commandery**) admired his talent, and he was recommended as **Shàngjì Yuàn** (i.e., **Assistant Superintendent of Finance**), **Zhǔmù**, and **Gōngcáo**, though he never took up these offices. He was of refined and luminous bearing, yet he shunned worldly affairs like chaff and husks. Dwelling beneath **Léipíng Shān** (i.e., **Mountain of Tranquility**), he devoted himself diligently to his studies, always wishing to retire early to the **Dòngshì** (i.e., **Hollow Chamber**, a Daoist term for the body after death or the state of transcendence). He did not desire to linger long in the mortal world.

He then went to **Běidòng** (i.e., **Northern Grotto**) and declared his intent to depart. He resided in the **Fāngyú** mountain grotto and **Fāngyuán Guǎn** (i.e., **Hall of the Square Enclosure**). Often he would travel to the **Sìfāng Píngtái** (i.e., **Four-Square Terrace**), a sacred Daoist site. Hence, **Zhēnshì** (i.e., **True Edict**) said: *"The recluse, while alive, always took joy in dwelling here."* And **Yáng Jūn** wrote in his letter to the **Zhǔngshǐ**: *"I do not know if the recluse in **Fāngyú Shān** has already set aside a seat for me in a dwelling that is easy to move to."* After his death, sixteen years later, in the year **Dīngmǎo** of **Tàihé**, he was promoted to **Shàngqīng Xiān Gōng** (i.e., **High Clarity Daoist Official**) and **Shàngxiàng Dìchén** (i.e., **Supreme Attendant of the Imperial Court**). According to the records, he was thirty years old at the time, though he did not remember the year of his passing.

Cross-referencing the two registers, in the year **Dīngmǎo** of **Tàihé** (i.e., **Tàihé’s second year**), he was twenty-seven. Thus, he was born in the year **Xīnmǎo** of **Xiánníng’s seventh year**. (As **Gù** said: *"He was born in the sixth year of Xiánhé. Again, it is said that the **Sītú** (i.e., **Grand Tutor**) appointed him as **Yuàn**, but this was not true."*)

From the third year of **Tàihé** onward, there were no further traces of him. According to the records, he died at thirty, which would be the year **Gēngwǔ**. (Again, **Zhēnshì** said: *"He followed **Zhāng Zhènán** in his nightly ascension."*)

---
*(Note: "玉奇" (Yùqí) and "岁羽" (Suìyǔ) appear to be names; "珪璋标挺" (jī zhāng biāo tǐng) refers to the uprightness of the *jī* and *zhāng* vessels, symbols of virtue in ancient Chinese culture. "洞室" (Dòngshì) and "洞" (Dòng) are Daoist terms for transcendence or the state after death. "四平方台" (Sìfāng Píngtái) is a specific Daoist site, often associated with immortality practices.)*

While the method of **Zhāng Zhènán’s** nightly ascension remains unverified, the elders recount that **Yuàn** was found upon the northern stone altar in the **Běidòng**—there he burned incense, paid homage, and lay prostrate without rising. On the following dawn, his form appeared as in life. This altar still stands today, unaltered, proving his early transcendence and withdrawal from mortal affairs. (Further details are recorded in the second volume.)

His wife was the daughter of **Huáng Yǎn**, the **Jiànkāng** prefect, who was the son of **Gū Xiānghuáng**—her name was **Jìngyí**. She bore **Huáng Mín**, who was then sent back to his family. Later, she separated and remarried to **Dài Qí**, the prefect of **Yuànlíng**, by whom she had a daughter named **Sùxiān** (a concubine’s child). This daughter married **Huá Yīng**, son of the **Yuèqí** cavalry officer from **Jìnlíng**, whose name was **Guǎng**.

**Yuàn’s** son, **Huáng Mín** (courtesy name **Xuánwén**), was born in the fifth year of **Shēngpíng** (a year of the **Xīnyǒu**), when **Yuàn** was twenty-one. He served as a county magistrate’s assistant, was recommended as a **xiàolián** (filial and incorruptible scholar), held the post of **Sīnóng Chéng**, and later became a **Nánmán** military officer and the prefect of **Línzhú**. He died in the sixth year of **Yuánjiā** in the **Sòng** dynasty, at the age of sixty-nine. His wife was the daughter of **Gě Wàn’ān**, the prefect of **Xīyáng** (who was the second grandson of **Bàopǔ Zǐ**). **Huáng Mín’s** eldest son was **Róng** (or **Yùzhī**), who died in the twelfth year of **Yuánjiā**.

The lineage is unknown in age. There was a daughter named **Dàoyù** (道育). Born in the **Dīngyǒu** (丁酉) year of **Lóng’ān** (隆安) Year One. In the **Jiǎwǔ** (甲午) year of **Xiàojiàn** (孝建) Year One of the **Sòng** (宋) dynasty, she died on **Rén** (任) Mountain (also called **Tǔdì** (土棣) Mountain). *(The world called her the Elder Mistress of Xǔ (许大娘). Her body lay in a stone tomb, unburied, yet often emitted fragrant qi.)*

**Huáng Mín’s** younger son was named **Qìng** (庆). In the **Jiǎwǔ** (己酉) year of **Tàishǐ** (泰始) Year Five of the **Sòng** (宋) dynasty, he also died on **Rén** (任) Mountain (also called **Tǔdì** (土棣) Mountain). Unknown in age, there was a daughter named **Shén’ér** (神儿), also known as **Qiónghuī** (琼辉). Born in the **Jiǎsì** (己巳) year of **Yuánjiā** (元嘉) Year Six, she died in the **Bīngyín** (丙寅) year of **Yǒngmíng** (永明) Year Four of the **Qí** (齐) dynasty. *(The world called her the Younger Mistress of Xǔ (许小娘). Many Daoists in the Eastern Gate recognized her.)*

To the right, these are the descendants I inherited from the **Chángshǐ** (长史). Now only **Jué Xuán’s** (揆玄) great-grandson **Língzhēn** (灵真) remains.

**Yáng Jūn** (杨君), whose name was **Xī** (羲), was born in the ninth month of the **Gēngyín** (庚寅) year of **Chéng Dì’s** (成帝) **Xiánhé** (咸和) Year Five. Originally, he seemed to be from **Wú** (吴). Later, he settled in **Jùróng** (句容). When **Língzhēn** (灵真) passed away, he still had his mother and younger brother alive. **Yáng Jūn** (杨君) was a man of purity and virtue, with a graceful appearance and pleasant demeanor. He excelled in conversation and laughter, as well as in calligraphy and painting. As a youth, he loved learning and reading books, being well-versed in the classics and histories. His nature was profound and dignified, with a deep and sincere disposition. Even as a child, he possessed an extraordinary insight. Though his age differed greatly from that of the late **Chángshǐ** (长史), he early formed a bond with the **Shénmíng** (神明) [Divine]. **Chángshǐ** (长史) recommended him to **Xiàng Wáng** (相王), who appointed him as a **Gōngfǔ Shèrén** (公府舍人) to serve personally. After **Jiǎnwén** (简文) ascended the throne, **Yáng Jūn** (杨君) was no longer seen in public. *(**Gù Yún** (顾云) said: "He was **Jiǎnwén’s** (简文) teacher." Others said he was a **Bóshì** (博士).*

Yang was ten years younger than Emperor Jiǎnwén. *(All feared this might not be true.)*

According to the *Zhēn Yùn* (真诰), he should have departed in the year *Tàiyuán* 11 (太元), in the year *Bīngxū* (丙戌). It was also said: *"If one cannot endure wind and fire, one should cultivate the Dao of the sword’s sharpness and master the art of termination."* Thus, he likely passed away early—it need not have been in *Bīngxū*.

Upon receiving his true office, his duties were roughly as described by Jiǔhuá (九华): to assist the *Dōnghuá* (东华), fulfilling the role of *Sīmìng* (司命). He oversaw and governed the spirits of *Wúyuè* (吴越), both gods and ghosts, all under his dominion.

Yang first received the *Zhōnghuáng* (中黄) *Zhìhǔbào* (制虎豹符) in the year *Yǒnghé* 5 (永和), *Jǐyǒu* (己酉). In the sixth year, *Gēngxū* (庚戌), he again received the *Língbǎo* (灵宝) *Wǔfú* (五符) from *Wèi Fūrén* (魏夫人), the eldest son of *Liú Pú* (刘璞). At that time, he was twenty-one years old. In the year *Xīngníng* 3 (兴宁), *Yǐchǒu* (乙丑), the *Zhēn* (真) descended upon him *(through oral transmission)*. He was thirty-six years old. The place of the descent was not fixed—sometimes in the capital, sometimes in his home, or in his mountain retreat.

The mountain retreat was still the **Léipíng Shān** residence of **Xǔ Chángshǐ** (许长史). Yang Heng often came to serve as his assistant—not from his own mountain dwelling.

To the right: the affairs of **Master Yang**—roughly as described above. Must be passed down and remembered further.

According to the *Zhēn Gào* (真诰), it is said: *"Fèng cháo gāo mù, sù yī shān"* (凤巢高木，素衣衫然者)—this refers to **Chángshǐ’s** given name, **Kàng** (况).
*"Zēng Shēn chū tián"* (曾参出田)—this refers to **Liéhé’s** (离合) courtesy name.
**Xǔ Xiān Hóu** (许仙侯) and **Xǔ Qīng** (许卿)—these denote his attainment of the **True Rank** (得真位).
**Jǐshì Chángshì** (给事常侍)—his secular office in this life.

It is also said: *"Xǔ Zhāo"* (许朝)—this is **Chángshǐ’s** uncle from **Nányáng** (南阳).
*"Yín shòu bái chǐ"* (寅兽白齿)—this refers to **Hǔyá** (虎牙). It is also simply called *"Yín shòu"* (寅兽), *"Yín kè"* (寅客), or *"Xǔ Hǔ"* / *"Xǔ Yá"* (许虎/许牙).
**Xǔ Zhǔbù** (许主簿)—this is the **Hǔyá’s rank**.
**Huá Xīnfù** (华新妇)—this is **Hǔyá’s wife**, seemingly named **Hòu** (厚), also called **Xǔ Hòu** (许厚).
**Huá Hóu** (华侯) and **Huá Shūlì** (华书吏)—these are **Hǔyá’s wife’s younger brothers**.
*"Qióng Rèn"* (琼刃)—this is a metaphor for **Yuàn’s** (掾) childhood name, corresponding to *"Ruò Fēng"* (若锋) in the *Qīng Lù* (青录).
*"Qǐwàng rén fēi"* (企望人飞)—this is the official title of **Liéhé Yuàn** (离合掾).

It is also said: *"Xǔ [Quǎn Tuō] zǐ"* (许｛犬托｝子)—this seems to be **Kuí’s** (揆) childhood name.
*"Yì Qiān Fūrén"* (易迁夫人) and *"Dǒu"* (斗)—these refer to **Yuàn’s mother**, **Táo Kē** (陶科).
*"Wù yōu sì bó zhī guǐ"* (勿忧嗣伯之诡)—this is **Chángshǐ’s younger brother’s** childhood name.
*"Lái zǐ suī shàn yú ěr"* (来子虽善于尔)—this refers to **Chángshǐ’s stepmother**.
*"Dāng nài Zhāng zě hé"* (当奈张者何)—this refers to **Chángshǐ’s concubine**.

*"Huáng É"* (黄娥)—this is **Yá Yuàn’s mother-in-law**, originally named **Éhuáng** (娥皇), who married into the **Huáng** (黄) family, hence *"Huáng É"*.
*"Yǎn xiǎo zǐ ěr"* (演小子耳)—this is **Huáng Yǎn** (黄演), **Éhuáng’s** son-in-law.
*"Chì Sūn"* (赤孙)—this refers to **Hǔyá’s son**.

**Three ascended** (登升者): *(Master, Chángshǐ, and Yuàn)*
**Five who transcended the world** (度世者): *(Hǔyá, Huáng Mín, Róng Dì, Dàmā, Xiǎomā).*

The search for **Hǔyá** revealed that he attained immortality and surpassed the *Rénchén* limit—this must be the fate of one who transcends the world. The others left no visible trace.

**Huáng Mín** transmitted the Dao’s teachings and scriptures, yet his lineage perished. There were strange omens, suggesting he might have foreshadowed his own example. The two others may also reappear among later descendants. If indeed seven generations mark the limit, then the blessed offspring of the Immortal True One shall truly be the bearers of this blessing.

**Chángshǐ’s wife**, daughter of **Táo Wēi**, though she entered the **Yì Qiān** family, likely inherited the fortune of the **Táo** lineage alone—this need not concern the five of the **Xǔ** clan’s count.

There is mention of **Lǐ Dōng**—a ritual wine-pourer often employed by the **Xǔ** family, who was also **Master’s** teacher. His family hailed from **Qǔ’ē**. **Dōng** received the title of *Left Divine Sacrificial Wine-Pourer* from the Celestial Master **Jíyáng**. **Huá Xiáng**, a noble scion of **Jìnlíng**, tended to secular prayers. Initially, he was skilled in spirit-medium arts, often dreaming of feasting with the divine. Each time, he would sleep soundly without awareness, only to wake in a drunken stupor, vomit scattered about. The vulgar deities frequently employed his talents and directed his actions—before and after, numbering over ten such instances.

The **Zhēn** lineage’s son, **Zhuìzhōng**, was the one who bore this blessing—
　　the **Chángshǐ**’s wife, **Táo Wēi**’s daughter. Though she entered the *Yì* sect, it was feared that this fortune was her own inheritance from the **Táo** family alone—no need to count among the five of the **Xǔ** clan’s descendants.

There is mention of **Lǐ Dōng**—a ritual wine-pourer often employed by the **Xǔ** family, who was also **Master’s** teacher.
　　His family hailed from **Qǔ’ē**. **Dōng** received the title of *Left Divine Sacrificial Wine-Pourer* from the Celestial Master **Jíyáng**.

**Huá Xiáng**, a noble scion of **Jìnlíng**, tended to secular prayers. When he transgressed, he was immediately punished for it. **Xiáng** grew resentful and vexed, and thus entered the Dao. He gained mastery over the ghostly arts, and gradually, true immortals began to visit him in dreams. At first, it was only in sleep, but after years, he would see their forms at midnight. When **Péi Qīnglíng** and **Zhōu Zǐyáng** arrived, they all sent him to relay their messages to the **Chángshǐ**. Yet **Xiáng** was of a hasty and reckless nature, often failing to convey the divine decrees accurately, and was thus reprimanded. He was then replaced by **Yáng Jūn**. Later, **Xiáng** became the magistrate of **Jiāngchéng** County (or **Jiāngshèng**), and his family settled there. Today, all the **Huá** of **Jiāngchéng** (or **Jiāngshèng**) are his descendants.

（The **Huá** and **Xǔ** clans were related by marriage, hence the **Chángshǐ**’s letter to **Péi Qīnglíng**, urging him to extend warm invitations. If, as in the previous chapter, there was a warning to preserve life, then **Xiáng** would have been held guilty. Now, the tradition of **Zhōu Zǐyáng**’s transmission is what **Xiáng** established, hence its connection to the **Zhēn** revelations. Thus, the twenty-second scroll of the **Zhēn** revelations is linked to this account.）
